### `2026-04-01T03:29:49.383Z`

> Base directory for this skill: /Users/lane/.claude/plugins/cache/claude-plugins-official/superpowers/5.0.6/skills/brainstorming
> 
> # Brainstorming Ideas Into Designs
> 
> Help turn ideas into fully formed designs and specs through natural collaborative dialogue.
> 
> Start by understanding the current project context, then ask questions one at a time to refine the idea. Once you understand what you're building, present the design and get user approval.
> 
> <HARD-GATE>
> Do NOT invoke any implementation skill, write any code, scaffold any project, or take any implementation action until you have presented a design and the user has approved it. This applies to EVERY project regardless of perceived simplicity.
> </HARD-GATE>
> 
> ## Anti-Pattern: "This Is Too Simple To Need A Design"
> 
> Every project goes through this process. A todo list, a single-function utility, a config change — all of them. "Simple" projects are where unexamined assumptions cause the most wasted work. The design can be short (a few sentences for truly simple projects), but you MUST present it and get approval.
> 
> ## Checklist
> 
> You MUST create a task for each of these items and complete them in order:
> 
> 1. **Explore project context** — check files, docs, recent commits
> 2. **Offer visual companion** (if topic will involve visual questions) — this is its own message, not combined with a clarifying question. See the Visual Companion section below.
> 3. **Ask clarifying questions** — one at a time, understand purpose/constraints/success criteria
> 4. **Propose 2-3 approaches** — with trade-offs and your recommendation
> 5. **Present design** — in sections scaled to their complexity, get user approval after each section
> 6. **Write design doc** — save to `docs/superpowers/specs/YYYY-MM-DD-<topic>-design.md` and commit
> 7. **Spec self-review** — quick inline check for placeholders, contradictions, ambiguity, scope (see below)
> 8. **User reviews written spec** — ask user to review the spec file before proceeding
> 9. **Transition to implementation** — invoke writing-plans skill to create implementation plan
> 
> ## Process Flow
> 
> ```dot
> digraph brainstorming {
>     "Explore project context" [shape=box];
>     "Visual questions ahead?" [shape=diamond];
>     "Offer Visual Companion\n(own message, no other content)" [shape=box];
>     "Ask clarifying questions" [shape=box];
>     "Propose 2-3 approaches" [shape=box];
>     "Present design sections" [shape=box];
>     "User approves design?" [shape=diamond];
>     "Write design doc" [shape=box];
>     "Spec self-review\n(fix inline)" [shape=box];
>     "User reviews spec?" [shape=diamond];
>     "Invoke writing-plans skill" [shape=doublecircle];
> 
>     "Explore project context" -> "Visual questions ahead?";
>     "Visual questions ahead?" -> "Offer Visual Companion\n(own message, no other content)" [label="yes"];
>     "Visual questions ahead?" -> "Ask clarifying questions" [label="no"];
>     "Offer Visual Companion\n(own message, no other content)" -> "Ask clarifying questions";
>     "Ask clarifying questions" -> "Propose 2-3 approaches";
>     "Propose 2-3 approaches" -> "Present design sections";
>     "Present design sections" -> "User approves design?";
>     "User approves design?" -> "Present design sections" [label="no, revise"];
>     "User approves design?" -> "Write design doc" [label="yes"];
>     "Write design doc" -> "Spec self-review\n(fix inline)";
>     "Spec self-review\n(fix inline)" -> "User reviews spec?";
>     "User reviews spec?" -> "Write design doc" [label="changes requested"];
>     "User reviews spec?" -> "Invoke writing-plans skill" [label="approved"];
> }
> ```
> 
> **The terminal state is invoking writing-plans.** Do NOT invoke frontend-design, mcp-builder, or any other implementation skill. The ONLY skill you invoke after brainstorming is writing-plans.
> 
> ## The Process
> 
> **Understanding the idea:**
> 
> - Check out the current project state first (files, docs, recent commits)
> - Before asking detailed questions, assess scope: if the request describes multiple independent subsystems (e.g., "build a platform with chat, file storage, billing, and analytics"), flag this immediately. Don't spend questions refining details of a project that needs to be decomposed first.
> - If the project is too large for a single spec, help the user decompose into sub-projects: what are the independent pieces, how do they relate, what order should they be built? Then brainstorm the first sub-project through the normal design flow. Each sub-project gets its own spec → plan → implementation cycle.
> - For appropriately-scoped projects, ask questions one at a time to refine the idea
> - Prefer multiple choice questions when possible, but open-ended is fine too
> - Only one question per message - if a topic needs more exploration, break it into multiple questions
> - Focus on understanding: purpose, constraints, success criteria
> 
> **Exploring approaches:**
> 
> - Propose 2-3 different approaches with trade-offs
> - Present options conversationally with your recommendation and reasoning
> - Lead with your recommended option and explain why
> 
> **Presenting the design:**
> 
> - Once you believe you understand what you're building, present the design
> - Scale each section to its complexity: a few sentences if straightforward, up to 200-300 words if nuanced
> - Ask after each section whether it looks right so far
> - Cover: architecture, components, data flow, error handling, testing
> - Be ready to go back and clarify if something doesn't make sense
> 
> **Design for isolation and clarity:**
> 
> - Break the system into smaller units that each have one clear purpose, communicate through well-defined interfaces, and can be understood and tested independently
> - For each unit, you should be able to answer: what does it do, how do you use it, and what does it depend on?
> - Can someone understand what a unit does without reading its internals? Can you change the internals without breaking consumers? If not, the boundaries need work.
> - Smaller, well-bounded units are also easier for you to work with - you reason better about code you can hold in context at once, and your edits are more reliable when files are focused. When a file grows large, that's often a signal that it's doing too much.
> 
> **Working in existing codebases:**
> 
> - Explore the current structure before proposing changes. Follow existing patterns.
> - Where existing code has problems that affect the work (e.g., a file that's grown too large, unclear boundaries, tangled responsibilities), include targeted improvements as part of the design - the way a good developer improves code they're working in.
> - Don't propose unrelated refactoring. Stay focused on what serves the current goal.
> 
> ## After the Design
> 
> **Documentation:**
> 
> - Write the validated design (spec) to `docs/superpowers/specs/YYYY-MM-DD-<topic>-design.md`
>   - (User preferences for spec location override this default)
> - Use elements-of-style:writing-clearly-and-concisely skill if available
> - Commit the design document to git
> 
> **Spec Self-Review:**
> After writing the spec document, look at it with fresh eyes:
> 
> 1. **Placeholder scan:** Any "TBD", "TODO", incomplete sections, or vague requirements? Fix them.
> 2. **Internal consistency:** Do any sections contradict each other? Does the architecture match the feature descriptions?
> 3. **Scope check:** Is this focused enough for a single implementation plan, or does it need decomposition?
> 4. **Ambiguity check:** Could any requirement be interpreted two different ways? If so, pick one and make it explicit.
> 
> Fix any issues inline. No need to re-review — just fix and move on.
> 
> **User Review Gate:**
> After the spec review loop passes, ask the user to review the written spec before proceeding:
> 
> > "Spec written and committed to `<path>`. Please review it and let me know if you want to make any changes before we start writing out the implementation plan."
> 
> Wait for the user's response. If they request changes, make them and re-run the spec review loop. Only proceed once the user approves.
> 
> **Implementation:**
> 
> - Invoke the writing-plans skill to create a detailed implementation plan
> - Do NOT invoke any other skill. writing-plans is the next step.
> 
> ## Key Principles
> 
> - **One question at a time** - Don't overwhelm with multiple questions
> - **Multiple choice preferred** - Easier to answer than open-ended when possible
> - **YAGNI ruthlessly** - Remove unnecessary features from all designs
> - **Explore alternatives** - Always propose 2-3 approaches before settling
> - **Incremental validation** - Present design, get approval before moving on
> - **Be flexible** - Go back and clarify when something doesn't make sense
> 
> ## Visual Companion
> 
> A browser-based companion for showing mockups, diagrams, and visual options during brainstorming. Available as a tool — not a mode. Accepting the companion means it's available for questions that benefit from visual treatment; it does NOT mean every question goes through the browser.
> 
> **Offering the companion:** When you anticipate that upcoming questions will involve visual content (mockups, layouts, diagrams), offer it once for consent:
> > "Some of what we're working on might be easier to explain if I can show it to you in a web browser. I can put together mockups, diagrams, comparisons, and other visuals as we go. This feature is still new and can be token-intensive. Want to try it? (Requires opening a local URL)"
> 
> **This offer MUST be its own message.** Do not combine it with clarifying questions, context summaries, or any other content. The message should contain ONLY the offer above and nothing else. Wait for the user's response before continuing. If they decline, proceed with text-only brainstorming.
> 
> **Per-question decision:** Even after the user accepts, decide FOR EACH QUESTION whether to use the browser or the terminal. The test: **would the user understand this better by seeing it than reading it?**
> 
> - **Use the browser** for content that IS visual — mockups, wireframes, layout comparisons, architecture diagrams, side-by-side visual designs
> - **Use the terminal** for content that is text — requirements questions, conceptual choices, tradeoff lists, A/B/C/D text options, scope decisions
> 
> A question about a UI topic is not automatically a visual question. "What does personality mean in this context?" is a conceptual question — use the terminal. "Which wizard layout works better?" is a visual question — use the browser.
> 
> If they agree to the companion, read the detailed guide before proceeding:
> `skills/brainstorming/visual-companion.md`

### `2026-04-01T06:51:02.956Z`

> wait, one last thing, is our prompt standard compatible with this document? Policy on the use of Generative Artificial Intelligence for NLnet-funded projects
> 
> The policy came into force on: December 8, 2025
> This latest version is valid as of: January 26, 2026.
> Version control
> Date     Version     Comment
> December 8, 2025     Version 1.0     Initial release
> December 8, 2025     Version 1.0     Corrected one typo ("artifical") & fixed link to the footnote
> January 26, 2026     Version 1.1     Adjustments based on feedback from grantees
> 
> Generative Artificial Intelligence (GenAI) tools are rapidly emerging across the ecosystem. While we recognize the interest – and at times share the curiosity – surrounding these technologies, it is essential that they be used responsibly, transparently, and in full accordance with our established principles.
> 
> This policy describes how GenAI— including tools such as large language models and code assistants—may be used in applications submitted to NLnet and in all work delivered within NLnet-funded projects, covering both the preparation of proposals and the execution of funded activities. The policy concerns GenAI specifically and does not regulate other forms of automation.
> Foundation of the policy
> 
> This policy is grounded in longstanding principles that apply to all NLnet-funded work. From these fundamental principles we have deduced what we consider common sense consequences with regards to the use of GenAI.
> 
> Fundamental principles
> 
>     FLOS licence: All projects must be free/libre/open source: all scientific outcomes must be published as open access, and any software and hardware developed must be published under a recognised free and open source licence in its entirety.
>     No misrepresentation: Grantees and applicants should not claim work as their own, if it is not. This has always been true and GenAI doesn’t change that.
>     Project quality: Grantees are expected to deliver project outcomes to the best of their ability. Tools may assist but do not replace human responsibility for correctness, clarity, and reproducibility.
> 
> Use of GenAI in the application process
> 
> We encourage applicants to trust their own skills and write their own proposals. That being said, applicants may use GenAI tools in preparing applications, but any such use must be disclosed. This includes drafting, translation, or summarisation. It applies both to written proposals and to materials provided during interactive evaluation. Disclosure allows evaluators to understand how the proposal was produced and ensures fairness.
> How to disclose?
> 
> If GenAI is used in the application process a prompt provenance log must be maintained. This log should list:
> 
>     the model used,
>     dates and times of prompts,
>     the prompts themselves,
>     the unedited output.
> 
> Instructions about how to submit the prompt log for applications are provided on the proposal form.
> Use of GenAI in project development
> 
>     Grantees must ensure that all submitted work can be legally published under a FLOS licence. This includes verifying that GenAI-assisted outputs do not reproduce copyrighted or incompatible material.
>         Example: when using a code assistant, check the assistant’s terms of use, and ensure that outputs are not reconstructed from copyrighted sources.
>         Example: Under EU law, purely AI-generated outputs without substantial human intellectual contribution are not eligible for copyright protection [Note]. In any case, outcomes purely generated by AI are not allowed to be submitted as work eligible for payment (as part) of the grant.
> 
>     Grantees must not present AI-generated content as if it were their own human-authored work.
>         Explanation: When we provide a grant to a person to develop a project, we expect that person to do the work. They should not outsource the work to another person while pretending they did it themselves. Similarly, grantees should not deliver GenAI outcomes and pretend it was their own human effort. Human contributors remain accountable for accuracy, originality, and integration of GenAI-supported work.
> 
>     Use of GenAI must not reduce the quality, clarity, reliability, or reproducibility of the work.
>         Explanation: Tools may assist, but human responsibility for quality remains. Human contributors are are expected to understand and be able to explain design and code decisions.
> 
>     It is allowed to work on the topic of GenAI itself within the scope of a grant, but only if this is explicitly part of approved work.
>         Explanation: We are not against GenAI, so applicants should not hesitate to submit proposals that involve work on GenAI. However, work on GenAI only falls within scope if it is part of the approved project plan.
> 
> Transparency & logging for project development
> 
> Use of GenAI should be disclosed and transparent. For any substantive use of GenAI that materially affects outputs, public disclosure is required, making it available to both users and contributors.
> 
>     The general stance toward the use of GenAI within a project should be disclosed and transparent for the public by providing a broad description.
>         Example: A codebase declares, typically in its ‘readme’, broadly how GenAI is used, e.g. for logic/boilerplate/tests/documentation/…. This can help set expectations for both users and contributors.
>         Example: A project publishes its own policy for contributors, outlining its do's and don't with regards to the use of GenAI.
> 
>     Generated content should be marked as such. When adding (partially) generated code, make sure the provenance is clear for each such contribution. Specify which model was used, (including version), and how it was used. Provide the used prompts/interactions and resulting output, or a summary thereof.
>         Example: When using git, distinguish commits that add generated code and include the used model and prompts in the commit message. Consider choosing a development tool that helps you create commits, and auto-fills the relevant information. See [Note 2] for example commits.
>         Make sure to provide the information in a logical place where it can easily be found. Avoid hosting it on third-party platforms that require a log-in or may disappear over time.
> 
>     If GenAI is not used for generating code but only for tasks like testing or creating documentation, it suffices to provide a general description of the use in the ‘readme’. More detailed logging on a per commit basis is preferred but not required.
> 
> Alternative methods for logging
> 
> The goal of disclosure is to inform NLnet, users and contributors about the extend to which GenAI was used to generate project results. If you prefer to use different methods for logging with equivalent results, this can be acceptable too. Use common sense to determine such equivalence and make sure you are able to answer questions about the use of GenAI from our team.
> Exceptions for grantees with active projects during the publication of the policy
> 
> We ask everyone to make an effort to uphold our request for transparency and logging. However, we also understand that for grantees with ongoing projects, this is a new requirement while work was already underway. Grantees of ongoing projects who feel that none of disclosure options offered above will work for them, can propose a personalised plan for transparency to their contact person at NLnet.
> An ongoing project in this context, is a project for which the Memorandum of Understanding was signed before December 8, 2025, the date when this policy came into force. Note that this is project-based and does not apply to returning grantees with projects starting after this date.
> 
> Note that for ongoing projects, logging is not required retroactively. It applies to milestones which were started after this policy came into force (December 8, 2025).
> Non-Compliance
> 
> Failure to comply with the above policy may result in rejection of the proposal or ultimately in the termination of the running grant.
> Scope of this policy
> 
> This policy explicitly deals with GenAI only (such as Large Language Models). NLnet is a strong proponent of automation and of deterministic and reproducible generation of source code, formal and symbolic proofs, etc. based on specifications and scientific and engineering rigour. Similarly, it does not in any way seek to prevent the use of other forms of machine learning, fuzz testing or other beneficial use cases. However, when in doubt please contact us.
> Note 1
> 
> See: Generative AI and Copyright page 93, a report requested by the European Parliament's Committee on Legal Affairs. The entire quote reads:
> "Given this framework, it follows that purely AI-generated outputs—those created automatically by an AI system without substantial human intervention—are not eligible for copyright protection in the EU. Such outputs are considered to fall into the public domain, making them freely available for anyone to use, reproduce, or adapt without seeking permission or providing attribution. The legal and commercial implications of this are significant. For creators and companies investing in AI systems that generate music, art, or text, there is no proprietary right over the final output unless a human has contributed in a way that meets the “intellectual creation” standard."
> Note 2
> 
> Example commits:
> 
>     Author: Harry Hacker hh@example.org
>     Date: Sun Jan 18 10:32:15 2026
>     Fix compliance tests
>     Fix several mistakes in generated code, make it compile; manually verify each test with RFC123 specification.
>     Author: Harry Hacker with CodeLLM-3.4 hh@example.org
>     Date: Sun Jan 18 10:52:08 2026
>     Generate compliance tests
>     Prompt: Generate tests for compliance with RFC123 messages.
>     Output: (this commit)

### `2026-04-02T05:13:53.912Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a7f0b08b8bf349770</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01Hvu7NQq1wM2ZkJtJhb8QtS</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/421eb12c-3b30-4168-9218-3902f0c0fd4b/tasks/a7f0b08b8bf349770.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Session-type: par CLL vs EAct, enum branching" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have everything I need. Let me compose the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> # CLL (par) vs EAct vs pane-session: Formal Relationship and Adoption Analysis
> 
> ## 1. CLL vs EAct: Complementary, Not Competing
> 
> **Verdict: Complementary at distinct layers. CLL governs the channel discipline (binary, point-to-point protocol shapes). EAct governs the actor/handler-store discipline (how one actor interleaves multiple sessions). pane already uses them this way informally; the question is whether par's specific CLL encoding offers concrete improvements over pane-session's current encoding.**
> 
> ### The formal relationship
> 
> Wadler's "Propositions as Sessions" (JFP 2014, Theorems 2 and 3) established the correspondence: propositions of CLL (with MIX) correspond bijectively to binary session types, and CLL cut corresponds to process composition. Specifically:
> 
> - A tensor B (A x B) = Send A then continue as B
> - A par B (A^perp par B) = Recv A then continue as B  
> - A plus B = internal choice (select)
> - A with B = external choice (branch)
> - Negation = duality
> 
> par implements this directly. Its `Recv&lt;T, S&gt;` is tensor (A x B) and `Send&lt;T, S&gt;` is par (A^perp par B) -- note the reversal from pane-session's conventions, which follow the more standard session type reading where Send is tensor. This is a cosmetic difference in par's logic-first naming.
> 
> EAct (Fowler et al., "Safe Actor Programming with Multiparty Session Types") operates at a different level. EAct is based on MPST (Honda/Yoshida/Carbone, POPL 2008), which is the *multiparty* extension. The key principles (KP1-KP5, EAct SS1.2) address problems that CLL doesn't: reactivity (KP1), no explicit channels at the actor API surface (KP2), multiple simultaneous sessions per actor (KP3), inter-session interaction (KP4), and failure handling (KP5).
> 
> The relationship is layered:
> 
> 1. **Channel discipline (CLL / par / pane-session)**: Governs what a single binary channel can express. Send/Recv/Select/Branch/End. Duality. Protocol shapes. This is where par lives.
> 
> 2. **Actor discipline (EAct)**: Governs how an actor (pane's looper + handler store) interleaves work across multiple sessions. EAct's Theorem 4.3 (Progress, restated as Theorem `thm:progress` in the extended version) shows that the event-driven programming style -- only one handler runs at a time, handlers don't block -- is sufficient for deadlock freedom *across sessions*, provided each individual protocol is compliant (Definition `Compliance`, SS4). The compliance condition on individual protocols is exactly what the CLL channel discipline provides.
> 
> 3. **Separation logic (DLfActRiS)**: Governs reasoning about channel ownership transfer and connectivity graphs. This is where the affine gap analysis lives.
> 
> **For pane**: CLL (par's approach) handles layer 1. EAct handles layer 2. They compose because EAct's compliance premise for individual protocols (Definition `Compliance`: safe + deadlock-free runtime type environments) can be discharged by CLL's cut-elimination theorem -- any protocol expressible as a CLL proof term is compliant. pane can use CLL-derived channel types for the binary protocols while relying on the EAct-style event-driven handler pattern for cross-session deadlock freedom.
> 
> ### What this means concretely
> 
> pane-session's `Chan&lt;S, T&gt;` already implements CLL-derived binary session types. The question isn't "CLL or EAct" but "does par's *specific encoding* of CLL offer anything pane-session doesn't have?" That's the subject of the remaining sections.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. Enum-Based Branching for IPC
> 
> **Verdict: Conditionally sound. Adoptable with a `SessionEnum` derive macro that assigns stable discriminants and generates serialization. The pattern is strictly superior to binary `Select&lt;L, R&gt;` nesting for protocols with 3+ branches. The key constraint is that serialization must happen at the choice boundary, not inside the enum variants.**
> 
> ### What par does
> 
> par's branching works by sending enum values where variants carry session endpoints:
> 
> ```rust
> enum Operation {
>     CheckBalance(Send&lt;Amount&gt;),
>     Withdraw(Recv&lt;Amount, Send&lt;Result&lt;Money, InsufficientFunds&gt;&gt;&gt;),
> }
> // choose: atm.choose(Operation::CheckBalance) -&gt; Dual&lt;Send&lt;Amount&gt;&gt; = Recv&lt;Amount&gt;
> ```
> 
> The `choose` method (exchange.rs) works by forking: `S::Dual::fork_sync(|session| self.send1(choice(session)))`. The caller picks a variant, par creates a fresh channel pair, puts one endpoint into the enum, sends the enum, and returns the dual endpoint. This is the CLL additive rule encoded via multiplicative + fork -- additive choice decomposed into "send a session endpoint that represents the chosen branch."
> 
> ### Why it works in-process but not naively over IPC
> 
> par's choose works because the session endpoint inside the enum variant is *moved* through the oneshot channel. No serialization. For IPC (pane-session), you can't send a `Chan&lt;S, T&gt;` inside an enum -- channels contain transport handles (file descriptors, sockets), which aren't serializable.
> 
> ### The IPC adaptation
> 
> For pane-session, enum-based branching must decompose into two wire operations:
> 
> 1. **Send a discriminant tag** (the enum variant, without the session payload)
> 2. **Continue the existing channel** with the session type from the chosen variant
> 
> This is isomorphic to what pane-session already does with `Select&lt;L, R&gt;` and tag bytes, but with N-ary enums instead of binary nesting.
> 
> A `SessionEnum` derive macro would look like:
> 
> ```rust
> #[derive(SessionEnum)]
> #[repr(u8)]
> enum Operation {
>     #[session_tag = 0]
>     CheckBalance,                    // continuation: Send&lt;Amount, End&gt;
>     #[session_tag = 1]  
>     Withdraw,                        // continuation: Recv&lt;Amount, Send&lt;Result&lt;Money, Error&gt;, End&gt;&gt;
> }
> ```
> 
> The derive generates:
> 
> ```rust
> // For the selector (internal choice / Select side):
> impl&lt;T: Transport&gt; Chan&lt;Choose&lt;Operation&gt;, T&gt; {
>     pub fn choose_check_balance(self) -&gt; Result&lt;Chan&lt;Send&lt;Amount, End&gt;, T&gt;, SessionError&gt; {
>         self.transport.send_raw(&amp;[0u8])?;
>         Ok(self.advance())
>     }
>     pub fn choose_withdraw(self) -&gt; Result&lt;Chan&lt;Recv&lt;Amount, Send&lt;Result&lt;Money, Error&gt;, End&gt;&gt;, T&gt;, SessionError&gt; {
>         self.transport.send_raw(&amp;[1u8])?;
>         Ok(self.advance())
>     }
> }
> 
> // For the offerer (external choice / Branch side):
> enum OperationOffer&lt;T: Transport&gt; {
>     CheckBalance(Chan&lt;Recv&lt;Amount, End&gt;, T&gt;),     // dual continuations
>     Withdraw(Chan&lt;Send&lt;Amount, Recv&lt;Result&lt;Money, Error&gt;, End&gt;&gt;, T&gt;),
> }
> 
> impl&lt;T: Transport&gt; Chan&lt;Offer&lt;Operation&gt;, T&gt; {
>     pub fn offer(self) -&gt; Result&lt;OperationOffer&lt;T&gt;, SessionError&gt; {
>         let tag = self.transport.recv_raw()?;
>         match tag.as_slice() {
>             [0] =&gt; Ok(OperationOffer::CheckBalance(self.advance())),
>             [1] =&gt; Ok(OperationOffer::Withdraw(self.advance())),
>             _ =&gt; Err(SessionError::Io(io::Error::new(...))),
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Static guarantees
> 
> - **Compile-time exhaustiveness**: Rust's `match` on the `OperationOffer` enum forces the offerer to handle every variant. This is the same guarantee par gets, and the same guarantee pane-session's binary `offer!` macro provides via nested Offer::Left/Right matching.
> - **Stable discriminants**: `#[repr(u8)]` + explicit `#[session_tag]` attributes prevent reordering from changing wire format. This addresses one of the fragilities the optics design brief identifies with Be's `FindMatch` dispatch (item 2, SS3).
> - **Typestate continuation**: Each variant maps to a specific continuation type. The type system enforces that after choosing `Withdraw`, the channel is in state `Recv&lt;Amount, ...&gt;`, not `Send&lt;Amount, ...&gt;`.
> 
> ### Duality
> 
> The duality must be computed per-variant. If the selector's continuation for variant `V` is `S_V`, the offerer's continuation for `V` is `Dual&lt;S_V&gt;`. The derive macro generates dual types automatically. This is exactly par's pattern -- `choose` returns `S::Dual` -- but with serialization instead of fork.
> 
> ### What's gained over the status quo
> 
> pane-session currently encodes N-ary choice as right-nested binary: `Select&lt;A, Select&lt;B, C&gt;&gt;`. The `choice!` macro hides this. The offer side uses nested `offer!` which expands to nested `match` on `Offer::Left/Right`. This works but has two costs:
> 
> 1. **Wire cost**: An N-ary choice sends up to N-1 tag bytes (left/right traversal of the binary tree). With enum-based branching, it's always exactly 1 byte.
> 2. **Readability**: The enum variant names are self-documenting; `Offer::Left(Offer::Right(c))` is not.
> 
> Both of these are genuine improvements. The wire cost matters for high-frequency protocol interactions.
> 
> ### Invariants for soundness
> 
> 1. Discriminant tags must be stable across versions (wire compatibility). The `#[session_tag]` attribute makes this explicit.
> 2. The continuation type for each variant must be specified in the macro input, not inferred. This prevents silent breakage when the enum changes.
> 3. Duality generation must be tested per-variant (unit tests that `Dual&lt;Dual&lt;S_V&gt;&gt; == S_V`).
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. Queue for Streaming
> 
> **Verdict: Sound and directly adoptable. par's `Queue&lt;T, S&gt;` pattern maps cleanly to IPC with one critical addition: backpressure, which par doesn't need (in-process, unbounded mpsc) but pane does (cross-process, finite buffers).**
> 
> ### What par does
> 
> par's Queue is a recursive session type:
> 
> ```rust
> enum Queue&lt;T, S: Session&gt; {
>     Item(T, Dequeue&lt;T, S&gt;),
>     Closed(S),
> }
> type Dequeue&lt;T, S&gt; = Recv&lt;Queue&lt;T, S&gt;&gt;;
> type Enqueue&lt;T, S&gt; = Send&lt;Queue&lt;T, Dual&lt;S&gt;&gt;&gt;;
> ```
> 
> The enqueue side sends either `Item(value, continuation)` or `Closed(session)`. The dequeue side receives and matches. Pushing is non-blocking (the continuation channel is created by fork). Closing transitions to the continuation session `S`.
> 
> ### Wire protocol for IPC
> 
> For pane-session, the Queue pattern becomes:
> 
> ```
> // Wire: tag byte + payload
> // 0x00 + postcard(value)     -&gt; Item, continue as Queue&lt;T, S&gt;
> // 0x01                       -&gt; Closed, continue as S
> ```
> 
> The session type:
> 
> ```rust
> // Recursive via enum -- same pattern as par but with Serialize/DeserializeOwned bounds
> pub struct StreamSend&lt;T, S&gt;(PhantomData&lt;(T, S)&gt;);  // Enqueue side
> pub struct StreamRecv&lt;T, S&gt;(PhantomData&lt;(T, S)&gt;);  // Dequeue side
> 
> impl&lt;T: Serialize, S&gt; Chan&lt;StreamSend&lt;T, S&gt;, Transport&gt; {
>     pub fn push(mut self, item: T) -&gt; Result&lt;Chan&lt;StreamSend&lt;T, S&gt;, Transport&gt;, SessionError&gt; {
>         self.transport.send_raw(&amp;[0x00])?;
>         self.transport.send_raw(&amp;postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;item)?)?;
>         Ok(self.advance())  // same type -- recursive
>     }
>     
>     pub fn close(mut self) -&gt; Result&lt;Chan&lt;S, Transport&gt;, SessionError&gt; {
>         self.transport.send_raw(&amp;[0x01])?;
>         Ok(self.advance())
>     }
> }
> 
> impl&lt;T: DeserializeOwned, S&gt; Chan&lt;StreamRecv&lt;T, S&gt;, Transport&gt; {
>     pub fn pop(mut self) -&gt; Result&lt;StreamItem&lt;T, Chan&lt;StreamRecv&lt;T, S&gt;, Transport&gt;, Chan&lt;S, Transport&gt;&gt;, SessionError&gt; {
>         let tag = self.transport.recv_raw()?;
>         match tag.as_slice() {
>             [0x00] =&gt; {
>                 let bytes = self.transport.recv_raw()?;
>                 let item: T = postcard::from_bytes(&amp;bytes)?;
>                 Ok(StreamItem::Item(item, self.advance()))
>             }
>             [0x01] =&gt; Ok(StreamItem::Closed(self.advance())),
>             _ =&gt; Err(SessionError::Io(...)),
>         }
>     }
> }
> 
> pub enum StreamItem&lt;T, C, S&gt; {
>     Item(T, C),
>     Closed(S),
> }
> ```
> 
> ### Duality
> 
> `Dual&lt;StreamSend&lt;T, S&gt;&gt; = StreamRecv&lt;T, Dual&lt;S&gt;&gt;` and vice versa. The duality is straightforward and correct -- sending items is dual to receiving items, and closing transitions to dual continuations.
> 
> ### Backpressure
> 
> par's `Enqueue::push` is non-blocking because it creates a fresh channel pair (fork). In-process, this means unbounded buffering. For IPC, this is unacceptable -- a fast producer can exhaust the receiver's memory.
> 
> pane-session's underlying transport is synchronous blocking I/O with calloop integration. `send_raw` blocks when the kernel buffer is full (unix sockets) or when TCP flow control kicks in (TCP). This gives implicit OS-level backpressure. But application-level backpressure (e.g., "at most 64 items queued before the sender must wait") needs explicit support.
> 
> Options:
> 1. **Rely on OS buffer pressure** (current approach for all send operations). Sufficient for modest throughput.
> 2. **Add an ack-back protocol**: after every N items, the receiver sends an ack. This changes the session type to include periodic `Recv&lt;Ack&gt;` steps. Sound but adds latency.
> 3. **Window-based**: similar to TCP windowing. The stream negotiates a window size at open, and the sender pauses when the window fills. This is out-of-band relative to the session type.
> 
> For pane's use cases (observer notifications, clipboard change streams, filesystem event streams), OS-level backpressure is likely sufficient. The event rate is human-interaction-speed, not bulk data transfer.
> 
> ### Application to pane
> 
> The Queue pattern directly serves:
> - **Observer notifications**: `StreamSend&lt;PropertyChange, End&gt;` from observed pane to watcher. Close on unsubscribe.
> - **Clipboard change streams**: `StreamSend&lt;ClipboardEvent, End&gt;` from clipboard service.
> - **Filesystem event streams**: `StreamSend&lt;FsEvent, End&gt;` from pane-notify.
> 
> The continuation `S` after `Closed` is valuable -- it allows a clean shutdown protocol (e.g., `Closed` transitions to `Send&lt;UnsubscribeAck, End&gt;` so the watcher knows the subscription is fully torn down).
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. Server Pattern for pane-headless
> 
> **Verdict: The design pattern is sound and applicable, but the implementation must be substantially reworked for cross-process use. par's Server relies on in-process mpsc channels and closure-based scope isolation -- neither of which survives IPC. The *ideas* (connect/resume/suspend lifecycle, per-connection data, scope separation) transfer; the *code* doesn't.**
> 
> ### What par does
> 
> par's Server module (from Balzer &amp; Pfenning's coexponentials, "Client-server sessions in linear logic," LICS 2021, https://dl.acm.org/doi/10.1145/3473567) has three components:
> 
> 1. **Server&lt;Connect, Resume, ConnectionData&gt;**: Central loop. Calls `poll()` to receive connection initiations and resumptions. Calls `suspend()` to create Connection handles.
> 2. **Proxy&lt;Connect&gt;**: Client-facing handle for initiating connections. Cloneable (via closure, not `Clone` trait -- scope isolation).
> 3. **Connection&lt;Resume&gt;**: Handle for resuming a suspended conversation. Created by `suspend()`, consumed by `resume()`.
> 
> The critical invariant: **no two of Server, Proxy, Connection share a scope.** This is enforced by passing them into closures rather than returning them. This prevents deadlocks where one component blocks waiting for another that's in the same thread.
> 
> ### What pane-headless already has
> 
> pane-headless (`pane-server`) already implements a multi-client server:
> - Accepts connections (unix + TCP)
> - Runs handshake protocol (session-typed via `Chan&lt;S, T&gt;`)
> - Manages per-client sessions with identity and state
> - Uses calloop for event-driven dispatch
> 
> The handshake is already session-typed. The active phase uses typed enum messaging (`ClientToComp`/`CompToClient`) over the extracted transport.
> 
> ### What par's pattern adds
> 
> The key gap in pane-headless that par's Server addresses is **session suspension and resumption**. Currently, once a pane connects and completes the handshake, the connection stays active until the pane exits. There's no concept of "suspend this conversation and resume it later."
> 
> For distributed pane (mobile clients, laptop lid close, WiFi transitions), session suspension is critical. The `ReconnectingTransport` (already implemented) handles *transport-level* reconnection, but the *protocol-level* concept of "suspend a multi-step conversation, persist state, resume later" is missing.
> 
> par's pattern gives this structure:
> - `suspend()` captures the conversation state (what protocol step we're at) and creates a resumption token
> - `resume()` re-enters the server's event loop at the saved protocol step
> - `ConnectionData` holds per-client state across suspensions
> 
> ### IPC adaptation challenges
> 
> 1. **Scope isolation**: par enforces "no two parts in the same scope" via closures. In pane-headless, the server loop is a single calloop event loop. The Connection handles are distributed to remote processes. Scope isolation is *architectural* (the server is a different process than the client) rather than *lexical* (closures). This is actually stronger -- process isolation is harder to violate than lexical scoping.
> 
> 2. **Proxy cloning**: par's `Proxy::clone` uses a closure to prevent two proxies in the same scope. For pane, the "proxy" is the network address of the server. Any client that knows the address can connect. This is the standard distributed analog -- the server's listen address is the proxy.
> 
> 3. **Connection serialization**: par's `Connection&lt;Resume&gt;` contains a closure (`Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(Resume::Dual) + Send&gt;`). This can't be serialized. For pane, the connection token must be a serializable identifier (UUID, session token) that maps to server-side state.
> 
> 4. **Resume protocol**: When a remote client reconnects, the server must verify identity and locate the suspended session state. This is an additional protocol step that par doesn't need (in-process, identity is implicit).
> 
> ### What pane should adopt
> 
> The *lifecycle model*: Connect -&gt; Active -&gt; Suspend -&gt; (Connection token held by client) -&gt; Resume -&gt; Active -&gt; ... -&gt; Disconnect.
> 
> The *session type* for this lifecycle:
> 
> ```rust
> // Connection protocol
> type ConnectProtocol = Send&lt;PeerIdentity, Recv&lt;Result&lt;SessionToken, ConnectionRefused&gt;, End&gt;&gt;;
> 
> // Resumption protocol  
> type ResumeProtocol = Send&lt;SessionToken, Recv&lt;Result&lt;ResumeAck, SessionExpired&gt;, End&gt;&gt;;
> 
> // The server internally maps SessionToken -&gt; (ConnectionData, suspended protocol state)
> ```
> 
> The scope isolation guarantee transfers architecturally: the server never holds a client's Connection token (it holds the server-side state that the token references), and the client never has direct access to the Server's event loop.
> 
> ### Deadlock analysis
> 
> par's scope isolation prevents a specific deadlock: calling `resume()` while holding the `Server`, causing `resume` to block waiting for `poll()` which can't run because the Server is in the same thread. In pane-headless, this deadlock is impossible because `resume()` is a network call from a remote process -- the server's calloop will receive it on the next iteration regardless. The BLooper model (one thread, one message queue, sequential processing) in each pane provides the same independence that par's scope isolation provides within a single process.
> 
> This aligns with EAct Theorem `thm:progress` (Progress): the event-driven style where handlers don't block guarantees that the server can always poll for new events, and clients can always send resume requests.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 5. The Crash-Safety Gap
> 
> **Verdict: The panic behavior is NOT fundamental to par's CLL encoding. It's an implementation choice. par's CLL logic would be equally valid with Result returns. The panic happens in exactly two places (`expect("sender dropped")` and `expect("receiver dropped")`) and both can be replaced with `Err(Disconnected)` without breaking any CLL property. What's lost is the *static guarantee* that channels are used -- but that guarantee was already absent (Rust is affine, not linear).**
> 
> ### Where par panics
> 
> In `exchange.rs`:
> 
> ```rust
> impl&lt;T, S: Session&gt; Recv&lt;T, S&gt; {
>     pub async fn recv(mut self) -&gt; (T, S) {
>         loop {
>             match self.rx.await.expect("sender dropped") {  // PANIC
>                 Exchange::Send(x) =&gt; break x,
>                 Exchange::Link(r) =&gt; self = r,
>             }
>         }
>     }
> }
> 
> impl&lt;T, S: Session&gt; Send&lt;T, S&gt; {
>     fn link(self, dual: Self::Dual) {
>         self.tx
>             .send(Exchange::Link(dual))
>             .ok()
>             .expect("receiver dropped")  // PANIC
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> And in `server.rs`, `Proxy::connect` and `Server::suspend` both use `try_send(...).expect("server/receiver dropped")`.
> 
> ### Why par panics
> 
> par's documentation says "Session end-points **must not be dropped.**" This is enforcing a *linear* (not affine) discipline by crashing on violation. The idea: if you accidentally drop an endpoint, the program crashes immediately rather than silently deadlocking. This is a design preference, not a logical necessity.
> 
> The CLL cut rule (which `fork_sync` implements) creates two endpoints that must both be consumed. If one is dropped, the dual endpoint's `recv()` would await forever (the oneshot channel is cancelled). par converts "await forever" into "panic immediately" as a fail-fast.
> 
> ### Why pane can't use panic
> 
> pane-session is designed for IPC between separate OS processes. If a client process crashes, segfaults, is killed by the OOM killer, loses network connectivity, or has its laptop lid closed, the server-side endpoint sees a dropped channel. This is a *normal operational event*, not a programming error. Panicking the server would take down all other clients. This is the fundamental reason pane has its own session type implementation, correctly identified in `lib.rs` line 9.
> 
> ### Adapting par's design to use Result
> 
> Every panic point in par corresponds to a oneshot channel cancellation. The adaptation:
> 
> ```rust
> // par's current code
> self.rx.await.expect("sender dropped")
> 
> // Result-returning version
> self.rx.await.map_err(|oneshot::Canceled| SessionError::Disconnected)?
> ```
> 
> This preserves all of par's session type properties:
> - **Protocol adherence** (Wadler, "Propositions as Sessions," JFP 2014, Theorem 3: correspondence between proofs and processes): Still holds. A well-typed program that doesn't drop endpoints still follows the protocol. The only change is what happens when an endpoint *is* dropped.
> - **Duality correctness**: Unaffected. The dual relationship is a property of the types, not the runtime error handling.
> - **fork_sync / link semantics**: fork_sync still creates dual endpoints. link still wires them. The only difference is the failure mode.
> 
> What's lost: par's panic-on-drop serves as a runtime *approximation* of linearity. When you switch to Result, dropped endpoints produce errors rather than crashes, which means a buggy program can silently ignore SessionError::Disconnected and effectively drop a session without crashing. pane compensates with `#[must_use]` on Chan and on Result, which produces compiler warnings. This is the same affine gap analysis from DLfActRiS (SS3.1, Theorem 1.2): linearity is required for deadlock freedom from types alone; pane's runtime recovery (`Disconnected` errors, `ReplyFailed` on Drop) is the engineering response.
> 
> ### Ferrite comparison
> 
> Ferrite (Chen, Balzer, Toninho, ECOOP 2022, SS4.2 Theorem 4.3) achieves protocol fidelity in Rust by using a judgmental embedding -- sessions are not returned to the caller but passed into continuations, preventing drop. Ferrite's approach would work for pane, but requires a fundamentally different API shape (CPS instead of typestate). Given pane's goal of a BeOS-familiar kit API, the typestate + Result approach is the right trade-off, as correctly assessed in the DLfActRiS review (`docs/superpowers/dlfactris-review.md`, path (a): "Accept affinity + runtime recovery").
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary of Recommendations
> 
> | Pattern | Adopt? | What to adapt | Key invariant |
> |---------|--------|--------------|---------------|
> | Enum branching | Yes | `SessionEnum` derive macro with stable discriminants, 1-byte tags | Discriminants must be wire-stable across versions |
> | Queue/streaming | Yes | `StreamSend&lt;T, S&gt;` / `StreamRecv&lt;T, S&gt;` with continuation | OS-level backpressure sufficient for pane's event rates |
> | Server/Proxy/Connection | Adopt the lifecycle model | Connect/Suspend/Resume with serializable session tokens | Process isolation replaces lexical scope isolation |
> | CLL foundation | Already in use | pane-session's types ARE CLL-derived (acknowledged in lib.rs line 13) | No change needed |
> | Crash safety via Result | Already in use | par's panics are not fundamental; pane's approach is correct | `#[must_use]` + Drop cleanup compensates for affine gap |
> 
> **Attribution**: Any adopted patterns should cite: "Adapted from par (Michal Strba, https://github.com/faiface/par), a classical linear logic session type library for Rust." The Server pattern additionally cites Balzer &amp; Pfenning, "Client-server sessions in linear logic," LICS 2021.
> 
> **Key files reviewed**: `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-session/src/types.rs`, `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-session/src/dual.rs`, `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-session/src/macros.rs`, `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-session/src/error.rs`, `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-session/src/framing.rs`, `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-session/src/transport/mod.rs`, `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-session/src/transport/memory.rs`, `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/docs/superpowers/dlfactris-review.md`, `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/docs/optics-design-brief.md`, `/Users/lane/gist/safe-actor-programming-with-multiparty-session-types/eventactors-extended.tex`.</result>

### `2026-04-02T05:37:01.898Z`

### `2026-04-02T06:08:27.651Z`

> forward this for deliberation to the agents: Here are specific refinements to lock down before coding begins, organized by risk level. These close the gaps between the theoretical model and the implementation reality.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 1. Linear Discipline & Obligation Semantics (Critical)
> 
> **Fix the `Chan` Drop Gap**
> The spec notes that `Chan` lacks a Drop impl, creating a hangup window during handshake. **Decision:** `Chan<S, T>` **must** implement `Drop` to send a `ProtocolAbort` frame (distinct from `End`) that frees the peer's session thread immediately, not at TCP timeout. Document the wire frame: `[0xFF][0xFF]` (double-reserved as abort).
> 
> **ReplyPort Lifetime Constraint**
> Clarify that `ReplyPort` (and all obligation handles) are **short-lived**: they must be consumed or dropped before the handler method returns `Flow::Continue`. If a handler stores `ReplyPort` in `self`, the reply is deferred until pane destruction, blocking the requesting pane indefinitely.  
> *Refinement:* Add `#[must_use] impl !Send for ReplyPort` (if possible in future Rust) or a lint that detects storage in handler struct fields. For v2, explicitly document: "Storing obligation handles in handler state is a protocol hang."
> 
> **Commit Error Handling**
> Define `ClipboardWriteLock::commit(self, ...)` (takes `self` by value). On failure, the lock is consumed (no retry). The `Result<(), CommitError>` includes `Disconnected`, `LockRevoked`, `ValidationFailed`. Drop compensation (Revert) fires only if `commit` was never called.
> 
> **AppPayload Safety**
> Close the smuggling loophole. Change the requirement from `AppPayload: Send + 'static` to `AppPayload: Clone + Send + 'static`. Obligation handles (ReplyPort, etc.) are intentionally `!Clone`. This prevents accidental inclusion even by pane-app developers. Document: "App messages must be values; obligations cannot cross this boundary."
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 2. Multi-Server Topology & Token Semantics (Critical)
> 
> **Token Scope**
> Clarify that `token: u64` is **per-Connection**, not global. A pane connected to three servers has three independent token namespaces. `Cancel { token }` is routed to the Connection that issued the original request. The `Messenger` embeds this routing context.
> 
> **Cross-Connection Deadlock (I8)**
> Make I8 enforceable: "Debug builds maintain a thread-local `CURRENT_CONNECTION` id. If a handler calls `send_and_wait` on a `Messenger` whose Connection id differs from `CURRENT_CONNECTION`, the runtime panics with `CrossConnectionDeadlock`." This turns the advisory rule into a testable invariant.
> 
> **Service Map Resolution**
> Define precedence:  
> 1. `$PANE_SERVICE_OVERRIDES` (temp dev config)  
> 2. Pane's own manifest (for bundled services)  
> 3. `$PANE_SERVICES` (user config)  
> 4. `/etc/pane/services.toml` (system)  
> 
> Clarify that headless panes omit the `[compositor]` section but must still specify `[clipboard]` if they use it.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 3. Protocol Wire Format & Evolution (High)
> 
> **Message Enum Growth**
> Decide now: Add a `ServiceNotification { service_id: u8, payload: Vec<u8> }` variant to the base `Message` enum. Service-specific notifications (ClipboardChanged, etc.) move into this wrapper instead of flattening into the top-level enum. This prevents the "AppDefs.h constant explosion" problem and keeps the base protocol stable as services evolve.  
> *Trade-off:* Filters cannot see service notifications without parsing, but that's correct—filters operate on universal lifecycle/display events, not service specifics.
> 
> **FilterAction Clarification**
> Change `FilterAction::Pass(Message)` to `FilterAction::Pass` (unit). The message is passed through unchanged by ownership. `Transform(Message)` carries the new value. The debug assertion that the passed message is unchanged is then unnecessary.
> 
> **Version Negotiation Detail**
> Specify that `DeclareInterest` includes `min_version` and `max_version`. The server responds with the selected version or `InterestDeclined`. Per-service version selection allows clipboard v2 to coexist with clipboard v1 handlers during upgrades.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 4. Session Resumption & State Management (High)
> 
> **Clarify Resumption Semantics**
> Replace ambiguous text with:  
> "Upon resume, the client receives `Ready` as if new. The client **must** re-send `DeclareInterest` for all required services; the server may optimize by recognizing previously-held locks, but the protocol requires re-declaration. Stateful obligations (ReplyPort, ClipboardWriteLock) never survive suspension; they trigger Drop compensation at the moment of transport disconnect."
> 
> **Stream Draining**
> Add: "Streams must be explicitly closed before suspending. If a pane suspends with an open `StreamSend`, the server closes it with `StreamError::Suspended`, triggering Drop compensation on any buffered items."
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 5. API Ergonomics & Type Safety (Medium)
> 
> **Ghost State for Tokens**
> Address Open Question #2 now. Introduce `RequestToken<T>` phantom type:  
> ```rust
> pub struct RequestToken<T> { raw: u64, _phantom: PhantomData<T> }
> ```  
> `send_request<T>(msg: T) -> RequestToken<T>`  
> `reply_received<T>(token: RequestToken<T>, payload: Box<dyn Any>) -> Result<T, TypeMismatch>`  
> This eliminates the `downcast()` boilerplate and makes the handler's state machine type-safe.
> 
> **Geometry Definition**
> Define `Geometry` precisely:  
> - `x, y`: Logical pixels (scale-independent)  
> - `width, height`: Logical pixels  
> - `scale_factor: f32`: Physical = Logical × scale_factor  
> - `physical_size() -> (u32, u32)` helper  
> 
> This resolves HiDPI ambiguity immediately.
> 
> **Command Surface Resolution**
> Resolve Open Question #1: Move `command_executed` and `completion_request` to a new `ScriptingHandler: Handler` trait. DisplayHandler requires user input events (key, mouse); ScriptingHandler requires programmatic command surface. A headless pane can impl ScriptingHandler without DisplayHandler. This completes the "headless agent with API" story.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 6. Error Handling & Termination (Medium)
> 
> **Actor Failure (Err) vs Flow**
> Specify the looper behavior on `Err`:  
> "If a handler method returns `Err(e)`, the looper immediately initiates termination: no further events are dispatched, the handler is dropped (triggering obligation compensation), and `PaneExited { reason: ExitReason::Error(e) }` is sent to the server. `Flow::Stop` is graceful; `Err` is crash."
> 
> **Filter Chain Ordering**
> Specify: "Filters are applied in registration order. `add_filter` appends to the chain. Filters can be removed by dropping the returned `FilterHandle` (which sends `RemoveFilter` to the looper)."
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 7. Implementation Sequencing Notes (Add to Spec)
> 
> Add a "Implementation Phases" appendix that explicitly defers non-core items:  
> - **Phase 1 (Core):** Single-server, headless, no suspension, no streaming.  
> - **Phase 2 (Distribution):** Multi-server, TLS, session resumption.  
> - **Phase 3 (Scale):** Streaming observers, batch coalescing optimizations.  
> 
> This prevents premature optimization and keeps the v2 implementation focused on validating the linear discipline.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Summary Checklist
> 
> Before coding starts, the spec should explicitly state:
> 
> - [ ] `Chan` Drop sends `ProtocolAbort` frame  
> - [ ] Tokens are per-Connection; `Cancel` routed accordingly  
> - [ ] `AppPayload` requires `Clone`  
> - [ ] `ReplyPort` is short-lived (method scope)  
> - [ ] `FilterAction::Pass` takes no message; `Transform` takes the replacement  
> - [ ] `ServiceNotification` wrapper variant exists for service events  
> - [ ] `Geometry` uses logical pixels with scale_factor  
> - [ ] `ScriptingHandler` trait exists for headless command surface  
> - [ ] `RequestToken<T>` replaces raw `u64` in API  
> - [ ] Debug builds enforce I8 (no cross-Connection blocking)  
> 
> These refinements close the ambiguity windows that traditionally kill protocol projects: resource leaks during crashes, undefined behavior under suspension, and type-unsafe token matching. They preserve the theoretical commitments while making the implementation actually possible.

### `2026-04-02T19:22:20.005Z`

> we now have a new perspective to consider. look at this paper:
> gist/practical-refinement-session-type-inference/
> 
> and consider the below analysis:
> 
> ---
> 
> Good question. The paper speaks to several of the issues pretty directly, and in one case it reframes the problem in a way that's more interesting than the fix I originally suggested.
> 
> ## Request/reply: the typed hole has a session-typed solution
> 
> This is where the paper hits hardest. The `reply_received(&mut self, proxy: &Messenger, token: u64, payload: Box<dyn Any + Send>)` signature is exactly the kind of thing refinement session types eliminate. The paper's `∃n.A` quantifier type expresses "I will send a witness and then continue at a type indexed by that witness." Translated to pane's request/reply pattern:
> 
> ```
> RequestReply[T] ≜ ∃token. Send<Request[token], Recv<Reply[token, T], End>>
> ```
> 
> The token isn't ghost state floating in `self` — it's a bound variable in the session type. The reply's type is _indexed by the same token_, so the correlation is enforced by the protocol structure, not by manual matching in the handler. The paper's `∃R` rule (Section 5) shows how the inference engine handles exactly this: when you send a witness `e`, the constraint `n = e` is added to the governing context `C`, and all subsequent typing sees that binding.
> 
> What this means concretely for pane: instead of `send_request` returning a `RequestToken<T>` that the handler stores and manually correlates in `reply_received`, the session type for the request/reply interaction could carry the expected reply type _in the protocol_. The looper's dispatch wouldn't need the `Box<dyn Any + Send>` escape hatch — it would know the reply type from the session structure. The `Handles<P>` machinery already does monomorphized dispatch; extending it to cover request/reply would close the one hole where the typed dispatch system currently goes soft.
> 
> The practical question is whether this can be done without making the common case (fire-and-forget messaging) heavier. The paper's two-stage approach suggests yes — you'd infer the structural types first (which messages flow where), then layer the refinement indices (which tokens correlate with which replies) as a second pass. The structural stage is decidable; the refinement stage uses Z3 but is feasible with the polynomial template optimization.
> 
> ## SERVICE_ID: subtyping makes the relationship explicit
> 
> The paper's subtyping formalism resolves the ambiguity I flagged about Display and Lifecycle both having `SERVICE_ID: 0`. In the paper's terms, the base protocol on service 0 is:
> 
> ```
> BaseProtocol ≜ ⊕{lifecycle_msgs... , display_msgs...}
> ```
> 
> A headless pane's connection speaks a _subtype_ of this — the label set containing only lifecycle messages. A display pane speaks the full type. The paper's `st⊕` rule says `⊕{ℓ : Aℓ}_{ℓ∈L} <: ⊕{m : Bm}_{m∈M}` when `L ⊆ M` — fewer labels means subtype. So the headless protocol is a subtype of the display protocol, and both live on service 0 because they're the same session at different refinement levels, not two protocols sharing an ID by accident.
> 
> This also gives you a formal basis for capability negotiation. The `DeclareInterest` handshake is essentially the server and client agreeing on which subtype of the full protocol they'll speak. The paper's `st?` rule — `?{ϕ}.A <: ?{ψ}.B` when `ϕ → ψ` — models exactly this: the capability constraint `ϕ` on the declared interest must imply the constraint `ψ` the server requires.
> 
> The split I suggested (Protocol vs WireProtocol) might still be worth doing for Rust ergonomics, but the _conceptual_ issue dissolves: it's not two protocols on one ID, it's one protocol with a subtyping lattice.
> 
> ## Protocol versioning gets a formal backbone
> 
> The `DeclareInterest { expected_version: u32 }` is an arithmetic refinement on the protocol type. In the paper's notation:
> 
> ```
> Service[v] ≜ ?{v ≥ min_supported ∧ v ≤ max_supported}. ActiveProtocol[v]
> ```
> 
> The server checks the refinement constraint; if it holds, the session continues at `ActiveProtocol[v]`, which is itself indexed by the version. The paper's inference engine would deduce the version constraints from the message shapes — if a v2 message has a field that v1 lacks, the refinement `v ≥ 2` gets inferred automatically.
> 
> This turns your "range negotiation deferred to Phase 2" open question into something with a clear formal treatment. When the version becomes a refinement index rather than a runtime field, the session type itself enforces that you don't send v2 messages on a v1 connection. The `st?` subtyping rule handles the compatibility direction: a server supporting `v ∈ [1,3]` can accept clients declaring `v = 2` because `v = 2 → v ≥ 1 ∧ v ≤ 3`.
> 
> ## Suspension and stale RequestTokens: the refinement makes staleness visible
> 
> This is where the paper reframes my concern rather than just answering it. I flagged that `RequestToken<T>` is liminal — not an obligation handle (no Drop compensation), but it represents an expectation about a future event that suspension invalidates.
> 
> In the paper's framework, a RequestToken is a channel whose session type transitions on suspension:
> 
> ```
> ActiveToken[t] ≜ Recv<Reply[t, T], End>
> SuspendedToken[t] ≜ ?{false}. Recv<Reply[t, T], End>
> ```
> 
> The `?{false}` refinement (constraint is ⊥) means the continuation is unreachable — the `st⊥` rule makes any type a subtype under a false constraint. So the token _exists_ but its continuation type is vacuously satisfied. The paper calls this the "impossible branch" pattern and handles it explicitly in program reconstruction (Section 6.1): the system inserts an `impossible` marker for branches that can't be taken.
> 
> For pane, this suggests: on suspension, outstanding RequestTokens should transition to a "poisoned" state where `extract()` returns `None` and `reply_received` is never called for them. Not through Drop compensation (they're not obligations), but through explicit invalidation when the connection enters suspension. The handler gets `ready()` again on resume, and any stored tokens from the previous session are dead. The paper's formalism makes this explicit in the type — a pane implementation would make it explicit in the runtime state.
> 
> ## The two-stage architecture validates pane's phasing
> 
> The paper's most practically relevant finding for pane's implementation plan: separating structural type checking from refinement checking isn't just an optimization, it's architecturally sound. Their Theorem 2 proves that if the structural stage accepts and the refinement stage finds a satisfying assignment, the combined result type-checks.
> 
> Pane's Phase 1 (single-server, no versioning, validate the linear discipline) is the structural stage. Phase 2 (multi-server, version negotiation, capability constraints) adds the refinements. The paper proves this separation doesn't lose soundness — you can validate the protocol structure first and layer arithmetic constraints later without worrying that the refinements will invalidate the structural guarantees.
> 
> The transitivity optimization (Section 6.1) is also directly applicable. When a message routes through App's ServiceRouter from one Connection to another, the intermediate type at the routing boundary can be eliminated the same way the paper eliminates intermediate type variables. This matters for Phase 2 performance — the multi-server topology introduces intermediate types at every Connection boundary, and without transitivity elimination, the constraint set would grow with the number of servers.
> 
> ## What the paper doesn't address
> 
> Two of my original issues sit outside the paper's scope:
> 
> **I8 enforcement** (debug-panic vs release-log) is a runtime property about blocking cycles in the connectivity graph, which is closer to the DLfActRiS work you already cite in the optics brief. The Ueno & Das paper is about protocol structure, not scheduling.
> 
> **MouseMove coalescing** is a real-time systems concern that no session type paper addresses — it's about event delivery policy, not protocol correctness.
> 
> But for the protocol-level issues — the request/reply hole, the SERVICE_ID ambiguity, version negotiation, and suspension semantics — this paper provides a formal framework that's both sound and (as their benchmarks show) practical enough to implement. The polynomial template trick for Z3 is particularly encouraging: it means you don't need full dependent types to get useful refinements, just linear arithmetic over protocol indices. That's exactly the complexity level pane's version and capability constraints live at.
> 
> **Paper reference**: Ueno, Das. "Practical Refinement Session Type Inference (Extended Version)." arXiv:2602.06715v1, Feb 2026. Artifact: <https://doi.org/10.5281/zenodo.18155032>

### `2026-04-02T19:42:46.978Z`
