### `2026-04-02T19:53:53.016Z`

> I like this unification indeed. whether or not it adds more machinery is immaterial -- we should consider whether this little bit more machinery justifies its overhead by the amount of cognitive burden it lifts off of the programmer as well as the possibility of intractible behavior later on. My concern with the ghost state was clarified by your statement:"   - Ghost state: the handler owns it (in self). Sigma doesn't know. The looper dispatches all replies
>   through one untyped entry." which essentially means that each reply generates an effect, but this is essentially Reply -> Context (in the sense of an effect where we send a message to the context) and the only way that this leads to coherent behavior is if there is always some function `Context -> Response` -- a way to get from `Reply -> Response`. This is a property of the free structure, which is only free if it is well formed (i.e. the system of reply/responses is coherent), but this is necessarily contingent on the validity of the implementation, where we get more verification that it is indeed the case by being able to track responses to particular replies, rather than congealing it all in the context

### `2026-04-02T19:54:54.630Z`

> yes lets do it

### `2026-04-02T20:08:06.586Z`

> can we now address these concerns then: forward to the three agents: The sigma entry redesign is the headline change here, and it's a significant improvement. Let me assess what moved, what it fixes, and what's still open.
> 
> ## What the sigma entries solve
> 
> The old spec's request/reply was the weakest point in the typed dispatch story. You had a fully monomorphized, exhaustive, zero-cost dispatch path for everything *except* the one place where cross-Connection causality lives — and there you fell back to `Box<dyn Any + Send>` and manual token correlation. The new design closes that hole cleanly.
> 
> The callback-based `send_request<H, R>` is the right shape. It eliminates three things simultaneously: the ghost state (`self.clipboard_request`), the untyped handler method (`reply_received(token, Box<dyn Any>)`), and the manual downcast (`RequestToken::extract`). The single downcast that remains happens inside the framework, where the type is known from registration — the handler surface is fully typed.
> 
> The EAct mapping is also tighter now. The old design had the looper's monomorphized match as the handler store σ, but then request/reply broke out of that model into a parallel ad-hoc tracking system. Now σ genuinely is σ — the static dispatch entries (fn pointers from service registration) and the dynamic dispatch entries (sigma HashMap from `send_request`) form a unified handler store, dispatched sequentially under I6/I7. The formalism and the implementation agree.
> 
> The six sigma invariants (S1-S6) are well-chosen. S3 (control-before-events — RegisterRequest processed before any Reply in the same batch) is the subtle one that would have been a source of bugs without explicit statement. S4 (`sigma.fail_all()` before `handler.disconnected()`) gives the handler per-request failure notification before the global "you're disconnected" signal, which is the right ordering for cleanup.
> 
> ## The suspension question dissolves
> 
> My earlier concern about stale RequestTokens — tokens stored in `self` that would never resolve after suspension — is gone. There are no tokens stored in `self`. The sigma entries are owned by the looper, and `sigma.fail_all()` fires `on_failed` callbacks for all pending entries on disconnect/suspension. The handler gets explicit notification for each outstanding request. No liminal category, no silent invalidation.
> 
> This is a case where fixing the typed dispatch hole also fixed the lifecycle hole. Good sign — usually means the abstraction is at the right level.
> 
> ## The paste example reads well
> 
> The old version:
> 
> ```rust
> self.clipboard_request = Some(proxy.send_request(
>     &clipboard_messenger, ClipboardRead("text/plain"),
> )?);
> // ... later, in reply_received:
> if Some(token) == self.clipboard_request.as_ref().map(|t| t.raw()) {
>     self.clipboard_request.take();
>     let data: Vec<u8> = payload.downcast()?;
>     proxy.set_content(&data)?;
> }
> ```
> 
> The new version:
> 
> ```rust
> proxy.send_request::<Self, ClipboardData>(
>     &self.clipboard_messenger,
>     ClipboardRead("text/plain"),
>     |editor, proxy, data| {
>         editor.insert_text(&data.content);
>         proxy.set_content(editor.buffer.as_bytes())?;
>         Ok(Flow::Continue)
>     },
>     |editor, proxy| {
>         editor.show_status("Paste failed");
>         Ok(Flow::Continue)
>     },
> )?;
> ```
> 
> The causal chain (key event → clipboard fetch → insert) is now expressed in the callback structure rather than scattered across handler state fields and two different handler methods. A reader can follow the data flow in one place.
> 
> ## What I'd still push on
> 
> **The `&mut H` in callbacks needs lifetime clarity.** `send_request` takes `FnOnce(&mut H, &Messenger, R)`. The callback fires during batch dispatch, sharing `&mut H` with the handler methods (S2 guarantees sequential access). But the callback is `+ 'static`, which means it can't capture borrows from the calling scope — it can only capture owned values. This is probably fine (you'd capture a clipboard name by String, not &str), but it means the callback can't close over `&self.some_field` from within a handler method. Worth a sentence in the spec about what the callback can and cannot capture, since developers will hit this on day one.
> 
> **Cancellation returns nothing.** The old spec had `Cancel { token }` as a wire message and `cancel_request(token)` as a Messenger method. The new spec says "Cancel removes entry without firing callbacks" but doesn't show the API. Is it `proxy.cancel_request(???)`? Without a returned token from `send_request`, how does the handler refer to the request it wants to cancel? If cancellation requires a token but `send_request` doesn't return one, you need an alternative handle — maybe `send_request` returns a `CancelHandle` that can only cancel, not correlate?
> 
> **Multiple outstanding requests to the same target.** The old design let you store multiple RequestTokens. The new design registers callbacks, but what happens when you fire two `send_request` calls to the same target before either replies? The sigma store is a HashMap keyed by... what? If by token (internal), that's fine — multiple entries, each with their own callback. But the spec doesn't say this explicitly, and the "No token returned" statement could be read as "there's only one slot."
> 
> **`on_reply` receives `R` but `request_received` still has `Box<dyn Any + Send>`.** The asymmetry is inevitable (the receiver doesn't know the sender's type), but it means the *server side* of request/reply is still untyped while the *client side* is now fully typed. The ScriptingHandler / optics convergence path will eventually need to type the server side too — when a scripting query arrives, `request_received` should dispatch through the optic layer, not hand you a `Box<dyn Any>`. Worth noting as the next typed-dispatch frontier.
> 
> ## Issues from last round that remain
> 
> **SERVICE_ID ambiguity** — Display and Lifecycle both at `SERVICE_ID: 0`. Still underdefined whether this means "same protocol, different label sets" or "sub-discriminant within service 0." My suggestion about the subtyping interpretation (from the Ueno & Das paper) stands, but the spec text is unchanged.
> 
> **I8 debug-panic / release-log** — Still the same. My argument that a deadlock is worse than a panic in release mode still applies. The sigma entry redesign doesn't change this.
> 
> **Derive macro variant discovery** — Still underdefined how `#[derive(ProtocolHandler)]` discovers which variants exist in `P::Message` to enforce exhaustiveness. This is an implementation detail, but it determines whether the "every variant is handled" guarantee is real.
> 
> **MouseMove coalescing opt-out** — Still not addressed. Drawing apps need the full path.
> 
> **Filter chain `&Message` growth** — The `ServiceNotification` wrapper variant suggestion for containing Message enum growth is mentioned but not committed to.
> 
> ## Overall assessment
> 
> The sigma entry redesign is the kind of change that improves multiple things at once — typed dispatch, lifecycle correctness, code readability, and formalism alignment. When a single design change fixes several independent issues, that's usually evidence the new abstraction is closer to the natural structure of the problem.
> 
> The spec is now internally consistent on its typed dispatch story: every message that reaches the handler is either (a) exhaustively matched through a monomorphized dispatch path, or (b) routed through a typed sigma callback. There's no `Box<dyn Any>` at the handler surface except `request_received`, which is inherently untyped (cross-pane, different types). That's a clean separation.
> 
> The remaining issues are real but second-order — they're about edge cases and missing API details within a sound structure, not about structural problems in the architecture. The SERVICE_ID thing is a spec clarity issue, not an unsoundness. The I8 enforcement is a policy choice, not a design flaw. The derive macro mechanism is an implementation question that doesn't affect the architecture.
> 
> If the previous version was a strong v2, this is a strong v2 with its weakest joint replaced by its most elegant subsystem.

### `2026-04-02T20:17:34.046Z`

> yes

### `2026-04-02T20:22:14.806Z`

> lets go over the current questions. field it to the three agents and consolodate a discussion of the exact stakes of each open problem

### `2026-04-02T20:26:31.061Z`

> did we consider the "minor inconsistency" in my review agent's assessment: This revision is methodical — it addresses every open issue from the previous rounds and doesn't introduce new ones doing so. Let me walk through what moved.
> 
> ## Every outstanding item resolved
> 
> **Derive macro exhaustiveness** (resolved #23): The answer is elegant in its simplicity. The macro doesn't discover variants — it generates a match arm, and `rustc`'s own exhaustive match checking does the rest. Missing a handler method means a missing match arm means a compile error. No custom analysis, no cross-crate introspection, just leaning on what the compiler already guarantees. Right answer.
> 
> **SERVICE_ID 0** (resolved #19 + inline comment): The inline comment nails it — "Wire discriminates by payload variant tag within service 0, not by SERVICE_ID alone. Service IDs > 0 for independently-connectable services." This is the explanation that was missing. It's the 9P single-connection multiplexing model, stated plainly.
> 
> **I8 panic in all builds** (resolved #16): "Production deadlock is worse than a crash with backtrace." Exactly the argument I made — and the right call. A deadlock is silent, unrecoverable, requires `kill -9`, produces no diagnostic information, and still leaves the peer hanging. A panic fires Drop compensation (I1), produces a backtrace, and the system recovers through supervision. No reason to be lenient in release.
> 
> **CancelHandle**: Returns from `send_request`, Drop is no-op, `.cancel()` consumes self and removes the sigma entry. The polarity inversion from ReplyPort is noted explicitly — "Drop = happy path, cancel = voluntary abort" vs ReplyPort's "Drop = failure compensation." Good that this is called out; it would confuse someone who internalized the obligation handle pattern and then assumed CancelHandle follows the same convention.
> 
> **Multiple outstanding requests**: "Each gets an independent sigma entry with a unique token (S1)." One sentence, removes the ambiguity.
> 
> **Callback capture**: The two patterns (handler state via `&mut H`, capture owned context with `move`) cover the real usage. The explanation of *why* callbacks can't capture borrows — they outlive the handler invocation because sigma entries persist across idle — is the kind of thing that prevents confused bug reports.
> 
> **PointerPolicy**: `Coalesce`/`FullHistory`, server-side filtering, per-pane, settable dynamically. Citing BeOS's `B_NO_POINTER_HISTORY`/`B_FULL_POINTER_HISTORY` is a nice touch — it's a tier 1 faithful adaptation of a Be concept most people don't know existed. Server-side filtering is the right choice: don't pay the wire cost for events you'll discard.
> 
> **Nested service notifications**: `Message::Clipboard(ClipboardNotification)` makes enum growth O(services) instead of O(services × events). Lifecycle and display stay flat because they're the base protocol. This is the `ServiceNotification` wrapper I suggested, done more granularly — one wrapper per service rather than one catch-all. Better, because filters can still match on `Message::Clipboard(_)` without knowing the inner variants.
> 
> **request_received** (resolved #20): Explicitly intentional, with the rationale that the server side is fundamentally open to unknown senders. Protocol-defined requests go through `Handles<P>`; ad-hoc inter-pane requests use `request_received`. Clean separation, documented as a design choice rather than a gap.
> 
> ## One minor inconsistency
> 
> In the linear discipline section, the filter chain bullet says "filter() takes Message by move, returns FilterAction by move. Each filter consumes and produces. Linear transformation chain." But the actual filter trait definition shows `fn filter(&mut self, msg: &Message) -> FilterAction` — that's an immutable borrow, not by-move. The trait definition is correct (you explained the `&Message` design in the filter chain section). The linear discipline bullet is stale from a previous iteration. Trivial fix.
> 
> ## Assessment
> 
> The spec is now self-consistent. Every design choice that was questioned has either been revised (I8 enforcement, CancelHandle, PointerPolicy, nested notifications) or explicitly documented as intentional with rationale (SERVICE_ID sharing, request_received staying untyped, derive macro relying on rustc). The resolved questions section has grown from 18 to 23 entries, and the open questions have shrunk to two genuinely open items (network discovery tiers, TLS development workflow).
> 
> The document reads as implementation-ready for Phase 1. Someone who has never seen the codebase could pick this up and start writing the Handler/DisplayHandler split, the Message/obligation split, the filter chain, and the sigma entry system, and they would not hit an ambiguity that the spec doesn't address. That's the bar for an architecture document, and this clears it.

### `2026-04-02T20:30:34.202Z`

> # Session-Type Consultant: Final Spec Review
> 
> ## Verdict: **Conditionally Sound.** Ready for Phase 1 implementation.
> 
> The spec resolves every open issue from the three-round debate (my memory: `project_handler_debate_final.md`, `project_greenfield_architecture.md`, `project_cross_proposal_review.md`). The Protocol + Handles&lt;P&gt; + derive macro architecture is the synthesis I recommended. The sixteen invariants (I1-I8, S1-S6, plus two implicit) are sufficient for Phase 1. I have specific responses to each finding and two gaps neither engineer caught.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Part 1: Review of Be Engineer's Findings
> 
> ### BE-1: Connection/App initialization ambiguity
> 
> **Agree.** The examples use `Connection::local()` and `Connection::remote()` (lines 1250-1254, 1306-1307) but the spec also defines `App::connect()` (line 759). This is not just a naming issue — it affects where the calloop source is created and who owns the event loop. The headless example creates the source at `Connection::remote()` and passes it to `pane.run_with(source, ...)`. The `App::connect()` signature returns `Self`, hiding the source. Pick one: `Connection::local()` returning `(conn, source)` is cleaner for calloop ownership. `App` can be a higher-level wrapper that manages these internally.
> 
> No formal implication — this is API surface consistency.
> 
> ### BE-2: Thread model for multi-Connection
> 
> **Agree, with modification.** The spec says each Connection produces a `ConnectionSource` (line 818) and the looper is single-threaded (I6). For multi-server, all ConnectionSources must register into the same calloop EventLoop. This means one calloop loop per pane process, multiple sources. The spec implies this (line 822: "App's dispatcher routes incoming events from all Connections to the right pane's looper channel") but doesn't state it as a requirement.
> 
> Formally: DLfActRiS Definition 4.1 (strong acyclicity) operates on the connectivity graph of actors and channels. Multi-source into a single event loop preserves the "one actor, sequential dispatch" property that makes I6 hold. If each Connection had its own thread, you'd need DLfActRiS's full separation logic to prove non-interference. Single calloop loop is the correct choice and should be stated explicitly.
> 
> ### BE-3: Closure form capability boundary
> 
> **Agree.** The closure form (line 1326) receives `Message` only. Since obligation-carrying messages bypass filters and dispatch directly to `Handles&lt;P&gt;` methods, and closures can't implement traits, closures cannot handle obligations. The spec should state: "The closure form handles Message (Clone-safe events) only. Panes that need obligation-carrying protocols (clipboard, scripting) must use the struct + trait form."
> 
> Formally: this is a consequence of EAct's TH-Handler rule (Fig. 8 in Fowler et al.). The handler store sigma is indexed by session endpoint; a closure provides a single entry point, not a multi-entry sigma. The closure form is a degenerate case where sigma has exactly one entry: the lifecycle/messaging catch-all.
> 
> ### BE-4: Wire discrimination for SERVICE_ID 0
> 
> **Agree — this is a real gap, not just documentation.** The spec says (lines 219-224) "Wire framing discriminates by payload variant tag within service 0, not by SERVICE_ID alone." But the wire format is `[length: u32][service: u8][payload: ...]` (line 619). When `service == 0`, the deserializer sees raw bytes in `payload`. It needs to know whether those bytes are a `LifecycleMessage` variant or a `DisplayMessage` variant.
> 
> Options: (a) single `Service0Message` enum that contains both lifecycle and display variants (simplest — matches the spec's "same lifecycle" intent); (b) a second discriminant byte within the payload. Option (a) is correct — Lifecycle and Display share SERVICE_ID 0 *because* they share a session, so they should share a message type at the wire level, with the Rust-side split happening after deserialization.
> 
> The formal concern: if the discriminant is ambiguous, the deserialized type could be wrong, violating CLL's type preservation across the channel (Wadler, "Propositions as Sessions," JFP 2014, Theorem 1 — Subject Reduction). Specifying the discriminant mechanism removes this ambiguity.
> 
> ### BE-5: Derive macro default methods
> 
> **Modify. See Part 3 below for the full analysis.** Short answer: missing methods must be compile errors, not defaults. The spec already says this (line 189: "missing handler methods produce non-exhaustive pattern errors at compile time"). But the `Handler` trait (line 238) provides defaults (`fn ready(...) { Ok(Flow::Continue) }`). This is intentional and correct for lifecycle — lifecycle methods are optional (a pane need not handle `pulse`). Service protocols via `Handles&lt;P&gt;` derive must NOT provide defaults. The tension the Be engineer identified is real and resolvable: two categories of defaulting, not one.
> 
> ### BE-6: quit_requested(&amp;self) — no state mutation after negotiation
> 
> **Agree.** `quit_requested(&amp;self) -&gt; bool` (line 259) takes `&amp;self`, preventing mutation. The spec justifies this: "&amp;self for deadlock freedom." But after the quit negotiation completes (user chooses to quit despite unsaved changes), the handler may need to record the decision. A `quit_resolved(&amp;mut self, outcome: QuitResolution)` callback is the right fix. This is a non-blocking notification (not an obligation), so it belongs on Handler as a default method.
> 
> No formal concern — this is an API completeness issue. The quit protocol is `Send&lt;QuitRequest, Recv&lt;QuitResult, End&gt;&gt;` from the framework's perspective; adding a post-resolution callback doesn't change the protocol shape.
> 
> ### BE-Naming: ClipboardHandler, Handle, AppPayload
> 
> **ClipboardHandler**: The example at line 1294 uses `impl ClipboardHandler for Editor` but the spec defines dispatch via `Handles&lt;Clipboard&gt;` (line 131). The example should use the derive macro form or implement `Handles&lt;Clipboard&gt;` directly. `ClipboardHandler` as a named trait alias (for `Handles&lt;Clipboard&gt;`) is a reasonable ergonomic choice but must be specified.
> 
> **Handle**: Agree, this shadows. Use `PaneHandle` or `ScopedHandle` for the scoped IPC handle. `Handle` is too generic in Rust.
> 
> **AppPayload blanket impl**: **Disagree.** A blanket `impl&lt;T: Clone + Send + 'static&gt; AppPayload for T` would include `bool`, `String`, and every library type. This is fine semantically (all such types are safe to send as app messages), but it eliminates the opt-in signal. The marker trait as written forces `impl AppPayload for MyType {}` which is one line and documents intent. The compile-time enforcement against obligation smuggling works either way (obligation types are `!Clone`). This is a taste call, not a soundness issue.
> 
> ### BE-Other: Flow prelude, name collisions
> 
> **Agree** on prelude re-export. `use pane_app::prelude::*` bringing `Flow::Continue` and `Flow::Stop` into scope is standard Rust practice.
> 
> **Agree** on derive macro name collisions. The macro must handle the case where two protocols have a method with the same name (e.g., `service_lost` on multiple service protocols). Disambiguation via `#[handles(Protocol::method)]` or module-scoped impl blocks.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Part 2: Review of Plan 9 Engineer's Findings
> 
> ### P9-1: PeerAuth vs PeerIdentity inconsistency
> 
> **Agree.** The spec uses both terms. Lines 795, 829 use `PeerIdentity`. Lines 1499-1503 define `PeerAuth` with `Kernel { uid, pid }` and `Certificate { subject, issuer }`. The spec resolves this in Resolved Question 25: `PeerAuth` replaces `PeerIdentity`. But the handshake description (line 795: `Hello { version, identity: PeerIdentity }`) still uses the old name. Scrub.
> 
> The Hello message should carry a *requested* identity (what the client calls itself), not authentication data. Authentication comes from the transport (SO_PEERCRED for unix, certificate for TLS). The server derives `PeerAuth` from the transport, not from the Hello payload. This separation is already described in Resolved Question 25 but not reflected in the handshake section.
> 
> ### P9-2: Maximum message size
> 
> **Agree — critical.** The wire format has `length: u32`, permitting 4GB frames. Without a maximum, a malicious or buggy client can force the server to allocate 4GB to receive a single message. This is a straightforward DoS vector.
> 
> Formally: this is not a session-type concern (session types govern protocol *structure*, not resource bounds). But it is a safety concern for the runtime that compensates for the affine gap. If the allocator panics (OOM), and `panic = unwind` (I1), the Drop compensation fires — but the server process may still be destabilized. A `max_message_size` of 16MB in the Welcome message (negotiated) is the standard defense. 9P does this: Tversion negotiates msize.
> 
> ### P9-3: Length-field semantics
> 
> **Agree.** Does `length` include the `service: u8` byte? If `length` is the total frame size including the service byte, then `payload_len = length - 1`. If `length` is payload-only, `service` is outside the length. Ambiguity here causes off-by-one deserialization errors that violate type preservation.
> 
> This is the exact issue 9P's specification addresses explicitly: "The size includes the four bytes for the size field itself, plus any tag, type, and data." Pane should follow the same convention: `length` is the total frame size including the service byte but excluding the length field itself. State this.
> 
> ### P9-4: ServiceTeardown wire message
> 
> **Agree.** Per-service error semantics (line 625: "a malformed message on service N tears down service N's channel, not the connection") requires a wire message to signal teardown. Without it, `service_lost()` can only fire on connection loss, not on per-service failure. A `ServiceTeardown { service: u8, reason: TeardownReason }` message from server to client triggers the `service_lost()` callback without connection loss.
> 
> Formally: this is an explicit session termination. In CLL terms, the service session transitions to End after ServiceTeardown. The dual requirement: the client-side calloop source for that service must be deregistered. If the service is later re-opened (new DeclareInterest), it gets a fresh session.
> 
> ### P9-5: Stream force-close on server-initiated suspend
> 
> **Agree.** The spec says "Streams must be closed before suspension" (line 1399). But who sends the Close? If suspension is server-initiated, the *server* must send `Closed` on all open streams before the suspend message. The client cannot close streams it doesn't know are about to be suspended.
> 
> In CLL session type terms: `StreamSend&lt;T, S&gt;` in the server's type becomes `Closed; S` — the server exercises the "close" branch of the stream's internal select. This is a polarity choice: server-initiated suspend means the server controls stream termination. The spec should make this explicit.
> 
> ### P9-6: Sigma entries keyed by (connection_id, token)
> 
> **Agree.** The spec says "Tokens are per-Connection (three servers = three namespaces)" (line 1016) and "Token values may collide across Connections" (line 1188). But the sigma HashMap in the looper is a single data structure. If tokens can collide across Connections, the key must include the connection identifier: `(connection_id, token)`. Otherwise, two replies from different servers with the same token value would route to the wrong callback.
> 
> This is a correctness issue, not just an optimization. Misrouting a reply violates protocol fidelity (Ferrite ECOOP 2022 §4.2, Theorem 4.3) — the typed `R` in the sigma entry would receive a value of a different type, causing a downcast failure at best, unsound behavior at worst.
> 
> ### P9-7: Specify serialization format
> 
> **Agree.** The spec uses `postcard::to_allocvec` in the Chan implementation (confirmed: `crates/pane-session/src/types.rs` line 95) but doesn't declare this as the wire format. "All payloads use postcard encoding" should be stated. This is not optional — changing the serialization format is a wire-breaking change.
> 
> ### P9-Distribution: Service migration, hot-reloading, multiplexing
> 
> **Agree on all three deferrals.** Service migration (service moves host) as service_lost + re-open is the correct decomposition — it avoids stateful migration, which would require transferring obligation handles across transports (a CLL cut operation that pane cannot currently express). Hot-reloading the service map is Phase 2. Connection multiplexing limits are a resource management concern, not a protocol concern.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Part 3: Derive Macro Default Methods — EAct Analysis
> 
> The Be engineer's item 5 asks: should missing `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` match arms default to `Ok(Flow::Continue)` or be compile errors?
> 
> **Answer: Compile errors for service protocols. Defaults for lifecycle.**
> 
> EAct's TH-Handler rule (Fowler et al., Fig. 8) requires that for each session endpoint registered in sigma, the handler store provides a handler function. The handler function must be *total* with respect to the session type's message set — every possible incoming message must have a handler.
> 
> In pane's encoding, `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` dispatches on `P::Message`. Rust's exhaustive match enforces totality. The derive macro generates this match. If the developer omits a handler method, the generated match is non-exhaustive, and `rustc` rejects it. This is the correct behavior for service protocols:
> 
> - **Clipboard**: If you register for clipboard events, you MUST handle `LockGranted`, `LockDenied`, `Changed`, and `ServiceLost`. `LockGranted` carries an obligation (`ClipboardWriteLock`). Defaulting it to `Ok(Flow::Continue)` would silently drop the lock, firing Drop compensation — technically safe but semantically wrong (you requested a lock and then ignored it).
> - **DragDrop**: If you register for DnD, you MUST handle `DragEnter`, `DragOver`, `Drop`. Defaulting `Drop` silently discards dragged data.
> 
> For lifecycle (`Handler` trait), defaults ARE correct. A pane that doesn't care about `pulse` shouldn't have to write `fn pulse(...) { Ok(Flow::Continue) }`. The lifecycle protocol's message set is "everything that can happen to a pane" — not all events are interesting to all panes.
> 
> The distinction maps directly to EAct: lifecycle is the actor's *base behavior* (always present, always handled); service protocols are *registered sessions* (explicitly opted into, must be fully handled).
> 
> **Implementation**: The derive macro generates a match on `P::Message` that calls the developer's methods. The developer's methods are NOT trait methods with defaults — they are inherent impl methods. The macro discovers them by name. Missing ones cause a missing match arm. This is what the spec describes at line 189 and it is correct.
> 
> The `Handler` and `DisplayHandler` traits with default methods (lines 238, 263) are NOT generated by the derive macro — they are hand-written traits for the base protocol. This is the intentional asymmetry. The spec is consistent but should state this distinction explicitly to prevent confusion.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Part 4: Gaps Neither Engineer Caught
> 
> ### Gap A: CancelHandle Drop semantics create an inverted affine risk
> 
> The spec says (lines 937-947): `CancelHandle`'s Drop is a no-op — dropping without calling `.cancel()` means the request completes normally. This is the *inverse* of the standard pane pattern where Drop sends a failure terminal.
> 
> This is deliberate and documented, but creates a subtle hazard: if a handler stores a `CancelHandle` in `self` and later returns `Flow::Stop`, the handle is dropped during handler destruction. The request's sigma entry survives momentarily (it hasn't been cancelled), but the handler is being destroyed. The sigma entry's callback holds an `FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...)` — but `H` is being dropped.
> 
> The spec addresses this at line 1007-1009: "Abandoned: handler drops (Flow::Stop, panic) -&gt; entry dropped without callbacks." This means the sigma cleanup happens *during* handler drop, which means sigma entries must be dropped *before* the handler fields. Rust's drop order is field-declaration-order for structs. If the sigma `HashMap` is a field of the looper (not the handler), it is dropped when the looper drops — which happens *after* the handler is dropped. But the sigma entries hold closures that capture `FnOnce(&amp;mut H, ...)` — these closures don't hold `H` directly, they expect `H` to be passed by the looper at invocation time.
> 
> **Resolution**: The looper must explicitly `sigma.clear()` before dropping the handler. This is not a Drop-order issue — it's a "the looper must do cleanup in a specific order" issue. The spec should add:
> 
> **I9: Sigma is cleared (entries dropped without firing callbacks) before the handler is dropped.** This prevents dangling closures and ensures CancelHandle's no-op Drop is safe.
> 
> The closest formal result: DLfActRiS §3.2 — channel fulfillment resources are freed when the actor terminates. The key insight is that fulfillment is a *logical* operation (the resource obligation is discharged), not a *physical* one (the callback fires). Dropping sigma entries without firing callbacks is correct because the actor is terminating — there is no handler to receive the callback.
> 
> ### Gap B: AppPayload Clone bound is necessary but not sufficient for obligation exclusion
> 
> The spec argues (lines 508-515) that `AppPayload: Clone + Send + 'static` prevents obligation smuggling because obligation types are `!Clone`. This is correct for direct smuggling. But consider:
> 
> ```rust
> struct Smuggle(Arc&lt;Mutex&lt;Option&lt;ReplyPort&gt;&gt;&gt;);
> impl Clone for Smuggle { /* clones the Arc */ }
> impl AppPayload for Smuggle {} // compiles if blanket impl exists
> ```
> 
> The `Arc&lt;Mutex&lt;Option&lt;ReplyPort&gt;&gt;&gt;` is `Clone + Send + 'static` even though it wraps an obligation type. The shared ownership via `Arc` means the obligation can be extracted on the receiving end via `take()`.
> 
> **If `AppPayload` uses a blanket impl** (the Be engineer's suggestion), this attack works. **If `AppPayload` is a marker trait requiring explicit impl**, the developer has to write `impl AppPayload for Smuggle {}`, which is a deliberate act — the system can't prevent malice, only accidents.
> 
> The current spec requires `AppPayload: Clone + Send + 'static` as a trait bound on `post_app_message`. With explicit opt-in (no blanket impl), plus the `debug_assert` mentioned at line 517, this is sufficient for Phase 1. The blanket impl should NOT be added — it would make the Arc smuggling attack automatic.
> 
> Formally: this is the classic problem that linear type systems solve and affine systems don't. TLL+C's channel types (§2, Ch(T)/Hc(T)) are tracked through all wrappers; Rust's type system does not track linearity through `Arc`. The `Clone` bound is a heuristic approximation. The explicit opt-in marker trait makes the heuristic opt-in rather than opt-out — safer.
> 
> ### Gap C: I8 enforcement covers send_and_wait but not blocking reads
> 
> I8 says: "No blocking cross-service calls within handler methods." The runtime enforcement (lines 1176-1182) checks `send_and_wait` against `CURRENT_CONNECTION`. But `Chan::recv()` is also blocking — if a handler method somehow gets access to a `Chan` (e.g., via a poorly designed service protocol that hands the handler a raw channel), `recv()` blocks the looper thread, violating EAct Progress (Theorem 3.10).
> 
> The spec's architecture makes this unlikely — `Chan` is used in the handshake layer, not exposed to handlers. But I8 should be stated as: "No blocking calls in handler methods (send_and_wait, recv, blocking I/O)." The CURRENT_CONNECTION check enforces the `send_and_wait` case; the broader prohibition relies on API design (not exposing blocking primitives to handlers) and code review.
> 
> DLfActRiS Progress (Theorem 5.3) requires that all actor steps are non-blocking. The single-thread model means any blocking call is a full actor stall. The runtime check is a partial enforcement of a total requirement.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Part 5: EAct/CLL/DLfActRiS Invariant Compliance
> 
> ### EAct Compliance
> 
> | EAct Requirement | Spec Status |
> |---|---|
> | TH-Handler: sigma indexed by session endpoint (Fig. 8) | **Satisfied.** Protocol + Handles&lt;P&gt; + fn pointer capture at registration. Each protocol's dispatch is a sigma entry. |
> | E-React: handler invoked with actor state + message (Fig. 4) | **Satisfied.** `receive(&amp;mut self, proxy, msg)` — self is actor state, proxy is environment, msg is the session message. |
> | E-Suspend: sigma grows dynamically (Fig. 4) | **Satisfied.** `send_request` installs sigma entries; `open_clipboard()` captures fn pointers at registration. |
> | Progress (Theorem 3.10): actor always eventually reduces | **Conditionally satisfied.** Requires I2 (no blocking in handlers), I6 (single-thread), I8 (no cross-service blocking). Gap C above notes I8 enforcement is partial. |
> | KP2: no channel endpoints in message values | **Satisfied.** Message is Clone-safe. Obligation types are internal, not in Message. |
> | KP3: linear use of session endpoints | **Compensated.** Affine (Drop) instead of linear. I4 provides runtime compensation. Sufficient for safety, not liveness. |
> 
> ### CLL Compliance
> 
> | CLL Property | Spec Status |
> |---|---|
> | Subject Reduction (Wadler JFP 2014, Thm 1) | **Satisfied for Chan.** Typestate transitions are type-correct by construction. Wire format must be unambiguous (BE-4, P9-3 fix required). |
> | Duality (HasDual) | **Satisfied in the existing codebase.** Verified in prior audit (`project_protocol_audit_2026_03_31`). |
> | Cut elimination / session composition | **Not required for Phase 1.** Single-session protocols only. Queue streaming (Phase 3) will need this. |
> 
> ### DLfActRiS Compliance
> 
> | DLfActRiS Property | Spec Status |
> |---|---|
> | Strong acyclicity (Def. 4.1) | **Conditionally satisfied.** I8 prevents cross-Connection blocking within a handler. Single calloop loop (I6) prevents intra-pane cycles. Cross-pane cycles (A send_and_wait B, B send_and_wait A) are possible and are a documented risk (already documented on `send_and_wait`). |
> | Channel fulfillment (§3.2) | **Satisfied.** Drop compensation fulfills channel obligations on actor termination. Requires I1 (panic = unwind) and proposed I9 (sigma cleared before handler drop). |
> | Actor progress (Thm 5.3) | **Same as EAct Progress.** Requires I2 + I6 + I8. |
> 
> ### Affine Gap Summary
> 
> | Lost Linear Property | Runtime Compensation | Sufficiency |
> |---|---|---|
> | Chan must reach End | #[must_use] + proposed Drop ProtocolAbort | **Sufficient for safety.** Peer learns of abort immediately. Liveness: peer is unblocked (abort = session failure, not session deadlock). |
> | ReplyPort must be consumed | Drop sends ReplyFailed | **Sufficient.** Requester gets a failure reply, never waits forever. |
> | ClipboardWriteLock must be consumed | Drop sends Revert | **Sufficient.** Server reverts the lock. |
> | CancelHandle deliberately inverted | Drop = no-op (request completes) | **Sufficient** given I9 (sigma cleared before handler drop). Without I9, potential for use-after-drop on handler state. |
> | Sigma entries on handler destruction | I9 (proposed): sigma.clear() before handler drop | **Sufficient** if implemented. Currently unspecified — this is the gap. |
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Final Verdict
> 
> **Conditionally sound for Phase 1 implementation.** Conditions:
> 
> 1. **Fix BE-4** (SERVICE_ID 0 wire discrimination). Define `Service0Message` enum or specify the second discriminant byte. Without this, deserialization is ambiguous.
> 
> 2. **Fix P9-2** (max message size). Add `max_message_size` to Welcome. 4GB allocation is a safety violation that I1's unwind compensation cannot reliably recover from.
> 
> 3. **Fix P9-3** (length field semantics). Specify whether length includes the service byte. Off-by-one here violates Subject Reduction.
> 
> 4. **Fix P9-6** (sigma key includes connection_id). Without this, token collisions across Connections cause type-unsound dispatch.
> 
> 5. **Add I9** (sigma cleared before handler drop). Without this, CancelHandle's inverted Drop semantics create a potential ordering violation on handler destruction.
> 
> 6. **State BE-3 explicitly** (closure form cannot handle obligations). This is true by construction but should be documented to prevent user confusion.
> 
> All six items are individually small. None requires architectural changes. The Be engineer's estimate of "under an hour" for all fixes is realistic, including I9 and BE-3 which are additions to the fix list.
> 
> The remaining items from both engineers (P9-1 naming scrub, P9-4 ServiceTeardown, P9-5 stream force-close, P9-7 serialization format, BE-1 init naming, BE-2 calloop threading, BE-5 default method clarification, BE-6 quit_resolved) are all valid improvements that should be addressed but are not soundness blockers.</result>

> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-02T21:05:27.079Z`

> this is beginning to remind me of variable binding problems -- hardcoded service IDs is one thing, but we don't want to introduce alpha equivalence problems if we rely on Stringed names. using UUIDs guarantees freshness of names, and there should be a way to determine relationships between the various variables determined by scope (to continue this metaphor). To that end, I would naively suppose the "Name" should actually be an Identifier struct with enough information to ensure that we have a clear model of the contextual relationships. This metaphor would have it that the first approach was like the "debrujin" approach to variable names -- each service encodes its primacy by its order in the numbered list, with challenges around inference and unification due to the need to traverse the list. I am inspired more by the co-debrujin approach, where term traversal is unnecessary as one may compute correct inference procedures by analyzing the relationships between identifiers determined by the lattice structure of scopes. is this metaphor sensible in this context or am I off base?

### `2026-04-02T21:28:08.138Z`

> "       Carrying dead structure "for later" is a tax on every person who reads or implements a
>        Protocol. It invites the question "what do I put here?" when the answer is "it doesn't matter
>        yet." That's bad API design by Be's own standards — the right thing should be easy, and the
>        thing you don't need yet shouldn't be in the way." this argument assumes that our goal is to produce a working system for end users at the end of phase 1. This is inaccurate, the primary usecases for this framework will only be clear once we've fulfilled the speculation, they are to be emergent upon experiment with the design as a whole. allowing development to proceed based solely on phase 1 may seem superficially sound, since you've determined that phase 2 is strictly additive. This is incorrect to assume holds for user applications, i.e. that their specific choice of application to implement has a gap between phase 1 implementable functionality and phase 2 implementable functionality that is strictly additive, when in reality the availability of phase 2 functionality may change the design that for technical reasons could only work on phase 1. put in category theoretic terms, it is not necessarily the case that `1 + 2 -> Prog(1) + Prog(2)`

### `2026-04-02T21:28:16.972Z`

### `2026-04-02T21:28:44.913Z`

>        Protocol. It invites the question "what do I put here?" when the answer is "it doesn't matter
>        yet." That's bad API design by Be's own standards — the right thing should be easy, and the
>        thing you don't need yet shouldn't be in the way." this argument assumes that our goal is to produce a working system for end users at the end of phase 1. This is inaccurate, the primary usecases for this framework will only be clear once we've fulfilled the speculation, they are to be emergent upon experiment with the design as a whole. allowing development to proceed based solely on phase 1 may seem superficially sound, since you've determined that phase 2 is strictly additive. This is incorrect to assume holds for user applications, i.e. that their specific choice of application to implement has a gap between phase 1 implementable functionality and phase 2 implementable functionality that is strictly additive, when in reality the availability of phase 2 functionality may change the design that for technical reasons could only work on phase 1. put in category theoretic terms, it is not necessarily the case that `Prog(1 + 2) = Prog(1) + Prog(2)`

### `2026-04-04T01:06:47.825Z`

> This session is being continued from a previous conversation that ran out of context. The summary below covers the earlier portion of the conversation.
> 
> Summary:
> 1. Primary Request and Intent:
>    The user (Lane) initiated a complete architectural redesign of the pane operating environment, transitioning from an incremental v1 evolution to a clean-slate v2 architecture. The overarching intent is to build a protocol framework (not a display framework) grounded in session-type formalism (EAct), with BeOS as the message-passing heritage and Plan 9 as the distributed computing heritage. The user's most recent request was to have all three specialist agents (Be engineer, Plan 9 engineer, session-type consultant) perform a final comprehensive review of docs/architecture.md for "coherence, consistency, accuracy to the source material (remember references of session types in gist, as well as optics), to fidelity to our design inspiration" and provide a "detailed discussion."
> 
> 2. Key Technical Concepts:
>    - **EAct framework** (Fowler et al.) — governing formalism for actor/handler-store discipline. Handler store σ, E-React, E-Suspend, TH-Handler, Progress theorem
>    - **CLL (Classical Linear Logic)** — channel discipline for binary protocols (Wadler's "Propositions as Sessions")
>    - **par crate** (Michal Strba) — CLL session type library for Rust; three patterns adopted: enum branching, Queue streaming, Server connect/suspend/resume
>    - **Optic discipline** (Clarke et al.) — GetPut/PutGet/PutPut laws for state-access consistency between views
>    - **DLfActRiS** (Jacobs/Hinrichsen/Krebbers, POPL 2024) — deadlock freedom, strong acyclicity, channel fulfillment
>    - **Ferrite** (Chen/Balzer/Toninho, ECOOP 2022) — protocol fidelity theorem
>    - **Protocol trait** — links ServiceId (UUID + name), Message type; the uniform service abstraction
>    - **Handles<P>** — uniform dispatch trait, one entry in EAct's handler store σ per protocol
>    - **Dispatch<H>** — dynamic handler store for request/reply (formerly Sigma<H>)
>    - **ServiceHandle<P>** — generic service handle, Drop sends RevokeInterest
>    - **ServiceId** — UUID (via UUIDv5 derivation from name) + human-readable reverse-DNS name
>    - **Handler/DisplayHandler split** — headless-first; display is an opt-in capability
>    - **Message/obligation split** — Clone-safe Message enum + internal obligation types (ReplyPort, ClipboardWriteLock, etc.)
>    - **Functoriality principle** — Prog(Phase1 + Phase2) ≠ Prog(Phase1) + Prog(Phase2); Phase 1 types must be full architecture types populated minimally
>    - **Linear discipline** — #[must_use], move-only, Drop compensation for the affine gap
>    - **19 invariants** — I1-I9 (system), S1-S6 (Dispatch entries)
>    - **Negotiated service IDs** — reverse-DNS names + server-assigned compact u8 wire discriminants per connection
>    - **Control protocol** — wire service 0, implicit, carries lifecycle + display + DeclareInterest + ServiceTeardown
>    - **CancelHandle** — inverted Drop (no-op = happy path, .cancel() = voluntary abort)
>    - **PeerAuth** — Kernel{uid,pid} for unix, Certificate{subject,issuer} for TLS; replaces advisory PeerIdentity
>    - **calloop** — event loop backend; ConnectionSource handles read+write per connection
>    - **#[pane::protocol_handler(P)]** — attribute macro generating Handles<P>::receive match from named methods
> 
> 3. Files and Code Sections:
>    - **docs/architecture.md** (PRIMARY — the living implementation-ready spec)

>      - Contains: Protocol trait, Handles<P>, ServiceHandle<P>, Handler/DisplayHandler, Message enum, Filter chain, App/Connection model, Dispatch for request/reply, wire protocol, invariants I1-I9/S1-S6, Phase 1 structural invariants table, developer examples, resolved questions (25), implementation phases (4)

>    Key code from the spec (docs/architecture.md):
>    ```rust
>    pub struct ServiceId {
>        pub uuid: Uuid,
>        pub name: &'static str,
>    }
>    impl ServiceId {
>        pub fn new(name: &'static str) -> Self { ... } // UUIDv5 derivation
>        pub fn with_uuid(uuid: Uuid, name: &'static str) -> Self { ... }
>    }
> 
>    pub trait Protocol {
>        const SERVICE_ID: ServiceId;
>        type Message: Send + 'static;
>    }
> 
>    pub trait Handles<P: Protocol> {
>        fn receive(&mut self, proxy: &Messenger, msg: P::Message) -> Result<Flow>;
>    }
> 
>    pub struct ServiceHandle<P: Protocol> { ... } // Drop sends RevokeInterest
> 
>    pub trait Handler: Send + 'static { ... } // ~11 methods, lifecycle + messaging
>    pub trait DisplayHandler: Handler { ... } // ~10 methods, display view
> 
>    pub enum Flow { Continue, Stop }
> 
>    pub enum Message { Ready, CloseRequested, ... } // Clone-safe, no obligations
>    
>    pub struct CancelHandle { ... } // Drop = no-op, .cancel() = voluntary abort
>    ```
>

>      - pane/functoriality_principle — the Prog(1+2) ≠ Prog(1) + Prog(2) principle

> 4. Errors and fixes:
>    - **Accidental commit to master instead of redesign branch**: The `redesign` branch was renamed and eventually merged to master, resolving this naturally.
>    - **Stale `ClipboardHandler` trait references**: After switching to Protocol + Handles<P>, the editor example still used `impl ClipboardHandler for Editor`. Fixed to use `#[pane::protocol_handler(Clipboard)]` attribute macro.
>    - **`Message<T>` / `What(T)` fossil**: Two lines in the spec referenced a superseded generic-message design. Fixed by rewriting to reference per-protocol enums via Handles<P>.
>    - **`PeerIdentity` stale references**: Multiple passes needed to scrub all occurrences → PeerAuth.
>    - **`SERVICE_ID: u8` → `SERVICE_NAME: &'static str` → `ServiceId { uuid, name }`**: Three-stage evolution; each stage required full spec scrub.
>    - **`Connection` as public API**: All examples used `Connection::local()`/`Connection::remote()` but App was described as the entry point. Resolved by making App the entry point, Connection internal.
>    - **Grammatical breakage from surgical edits**: Multiple instances where removing items from lists or sentences left broken grammar. User explicitly flagged this pattern.
>    - **Negative framing ("don't do X")**: User identified that "don't do X" inadvertently suggests X. Rewrote four passages to state positively what the architecture uses.
>    - **`const fn` for UUIDv5**: ServiceId::new was declared `pub const fn` but Uuid::new_v5 is not const fn. Changed to `pub fn`.
>    - **`open_clipboard()` → `open_service::<Clipboard>()`**: Required replace-all plus manual fixes for instances with arguments.
>    - **`ClipboardHandle` → `ServiceHandle<Clipboard>`**: Required adding the ServiceHandle<P> type definition to the spec.
>    - **Filter chain `Pass(Message)` → `Pass` (unit)**: Changed filter signature from `filter(&mut self, msg: Message)` to `filter(&mut self, msg: &Message)` with Pass as unit variant.
>    - **`reply_received`/`reply_failed` removal**: These Handler methods were replaced by Dispatch entry callbacks, requiring removal from examples and Handler trait definition.
>    - **`DeclareInterest` described as "irrevocable"** but `ServiceHandle<P>::Drop` sends `RevokeInterest`: Resolved by removing "irrevocable" claim.
>    - **`Handles<Lifecycle>` / `Handles<Messaging>` referenced but never defined**: Removed the claim that Handler desugars to these; Handler dispatches directly.
> 
> 5. Problem Solving:
>    - **Headless-first architecture**: Resolved by making Handler the headless-complete interface and DisplayHandler an opt-in capability
>    - **Ghost state for request/reply**: Resolved by Dispatch entries (sigma → Dispatch rename) with typed callbacks
>    - **Message::Clone panics**: Resolved by splitting Message into Clone-safe values + internal obligation types
>    - **Service channel wiring**: Resolved by Protocol + Handles<P> + ServiceHandle<P> + open_service::<P>()
>    - **Multi-server topology**: Resolved by making App hold multiple connections internally, routing by ServiceId
>    - **Service identity coordination**: Resolved by ServiceId with UUIDv5 deterministic derivation from reverse-DNS names
>    - **Phase 1 → Phase 2 compatibility**: Resolved by functoriality principle — Phase 1 implements Phase 2's types at N=1
>    - **Structured identifiers (co-de Bruijn)**: Resolved by identifying that the scope lattice lives in pane-fs namespace, not in identifier types; ServiceId gets UUID for machine identity, namespace provides hierarchy
>    - **"Scripting" rename to "Routing"**: The protocol concerns routing of data through optic-mediated state access
>    - **Protocol as a view**: Clarified that protocol is not a view — it governs how views are accessed, negotiated, and coordinated
>

### `2026-04-04T01:38:34.719Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a71944875577386a9</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01K2QfAMeqNaVpkqs2izFCtY</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/a71944875577386a9.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Pane&lt;H&gt; vs PaneSetup&lt;H&gt; review" completed</summary>
> <result>OK, so pane-app is a stub — we're making this decision before code exists. Good, that's the right time. Let me now synthesize my analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here's my analysis, grounded in what I actually found in the Haiku source and what I lived through at Be.
> 
> ## How BeOS Actually Works
> 
> **BWindow is not generic over its handler type.** This is the single most important fact for your decision. Look at the class hierarchy:
> 
> ```
> BArchivable
>   └─ BHandler
>        └─ BLooper          // owns a thread, a message queue, a list of handlers
>             └─ BApplication // is-a BLooper
>             └─ BWindow      // is-a BLooper
> ```
> 
> BWindow **is** a BLooper **is** a BHandler. It doesn't *have* handlers — it *is* one, and it *contains* others. The relationship is:
> 
> - `BLooper` has an `fHandlers: BList` (a dynamically typed list of `BHandler*`)
> - `BLooper::AddHandler(BHandler*)` adds a handler at runtime, no compile-time checking
> - `BWindow::AddChild(BView*)` adds views (which are BHandlers) at runtime, also unchecked
> 
> The critical point: **BWindow never knows at compile time what handlers it will contain.** In `/Users/lane/src/haiku/src/apps/aboutsystem/AboutSystem.cpp` lines 393-412, the AboutWindow constructor creates its AboutView and calls `AddChild(fAboutView)` — but nothing in the BWindow type signature encodes this. The same BWindow class works with any view hierarchy.
> 
> **BApplication::Run() doesn't know handler types either.** Look at `/Users/lane/src/haiku/src/kits/app/Application.cpp` line 590: `Run()` just calls `Loop()`, which calls `task_looper()`, which reads messages from the port and dispatches them through `DispatchMessage()`. The dispatch mechanism is entirely runtime — it walks the handler chain, calls `ResolveSpecifier`, and uses `BMessage::what` for routing. There's no type parameter anywhere.
> 
> **Capabilities are registered after construction, often after Run().** The Haiku apps I checked show `AddHandler()` being called at varied points:
> - In constructors (AboutWindow adding its AboutView)
> - After construction but before Show (setting up a view hierarchy)
> - At arbitrary runtime points (ActivityMonitor's `ActivityView::AttachedToWindow()` calling `Looper()-&gt;AddHandler(fSystemInfoHandler)` at line 893 of ActivityView.cpp)
> 
> **BRoster::Launch** doesn't know the application's handler type. It sends a `B_ARGV_RECEIVED` message and lets the polymorphic dispatch figure it out.
> 
> ## What This Means for Your Gap
> 
> The gap you've identified is real, but it exists because pane is trying to do something BeOS never did: **compile-time verification that a handler can process a specific protocol before the handler runs.**
> 
> In BeOS, the equivalent of `open_service` would be calling `AddHandler()` or setting up a BMessageFilter — both are runtime operations with no compile-time safety. If you sent a clipboard message to a handler that didn't understand it, you'd get `B_MESSAGE_NOT_UNDERSTOOD` back. Runtime error. This worked, but it was a wart. You'd ship, a user would hit the code path, and you'd find out.
> 
> Pane's `Handles&lt;P&gt;` is a genuine improvement over Be's approach. The question is where to put the check.
> 
> ## My Assessment of the Options
> 
> **Option A (`Pane&lt;H&gt;`) is wrong for the same reason BeOS didn't do it.**
> 
> Consider what BWindow would look like if it were `BWindow&lt;T&gt;`:
> 
> ```cpp
> // Hypothetical - this is NOT what Be did
> template&lt;typename T&gt;
> class BWindow : public BLooper {
>     void AddChild(T* view); // only accepts views of type T?
> };
> ```
> 
> This breaks immediately. A BWindow has a heterogeneous view hierarchy. You add BButtons, BTextViews, BMenuBars — all different concrete types. The container can't be parameterized by a single type because it holds many types.
> 
> Now, pane's situation is superficially different because you have a single handler H that implements multiple `Handles&lt;P&gt;` traits. But `Pane&lt;H&gt;` has the same structural problem: it propagates the handler type into every context that touches a Pane.
> 
> The things that hold Pane references in practice:
> - **Other panes** (inter-pane messaging) — they'd need `Pane&lt;OtherHandler&gt;` which they don't and shouldn't know
> - **Service infrastructure** — internal code routing messages doesn't care about H
> - **App** — manages multiple panes, each potentially with different H
> 
> The only place H matters is at the `open_service` call site and at the `run_with` call site. Every other reference to Pane is essentially using it as a `BMessenger` — an opaque handle for sending messages.
> 
> **Option C (PaneSetup&lt;H&gt; builder) is the right answer, but not quite for the reasons stated.**
> 
> The real insight is that BeOS had an implicit two-phase lifecycle that nobody named:
> 
> 1. **Construction/setup phase**: Create the window, add views, add handlers, set up shortcuts, configure look/feel. The window isn't processing messages yet. In Haiku source, this is everything that happens between `new BWindow(...)` and `Show()` (which calls `Run()` internally).
> 
> 2. **Running phase**: The looper thread is dispatching messages. New handlers can still be added (the system is flexible enough), but the window's identity — what it responds to — is established.
> 
> What happened at Be is that phase 1 was implicit. It fell out of the fact that you had to construct the object before calling Show(). There was no type-level marker distinguishing "window being set up" from "window running." This occasionally caused bugs — people would try to interact with a window's message port before `Run()` was called, and things would silently fail or crash.
> 
> Pane has the opportunity to make this phase distinction explicit. `PaneSetup&lt;H&gt;` is the setup phase, `Pane` is the running phase. This is good design — it's a typestate that BeOS should have had but couldn't express in C++.
> 
> ## Specific Consequences
> 
> **1. How often do developers hold Pane references vs Messenger references?**
> 
> In BeOS, the answer is overwhelmingly Messenger. BMessenger is how you communicate with anything — windows, apps, other teams. Direct BWindow pointers are dangerous across threads (Pavel Cisler's article in Newsletter Issue 3-33, which I found at `/Users/lane/src/haiku-website/static/legacy-docs/benewsletter-wisdom.md` lines 403-426, explains exactly why — pointer aliasing after deletion). The BMessenger-based lock was the safe pattern.
> 
> In pane, Messenger should be similarly dominant. After setup, the developer interacts through Messenger. The Pane object itself is rarely held directly — it's consumed by `run_with`, which enters the event loop and doesn't return until the pane exits.
> 
> This means the cost of `Pane` being non-generic is nearly zero. Nobody holds `Pane` references in steady state.
> 
> **2. How does each option affect the closure form?**
> 
> With Option A, the closure form requires something like:
> 
> ```rust
> // What would Pane&lt;???&gt; be here?
> let pane = app.create_pane::&lt;???&gt;(Tag::new("Hello"))?.wait()?;
> pane.run(|proxy, msg| ...)
> ```
> 
> You'd need a synthetic `ClosureHandler` type, or you'd need `create_pane` to not require H (undermining the whole reason A exists). This is ugly and it exposes plumbing.
> 
> With Option C, the closure form is clean because it bypasses setup:
> 
> ```rust
> let pane = app.create_pane(Tag::new("Hello"))?.wait()?;
> pane.run(|proxy, msg| ...)  // pane is already Pane, no setup needed
> ```
> 
> Wait — this actually reveals a subtlety. If `create_pane` returns `PaneSetup&lt;H&gt;` and the closure form doesn't use services, then the closure path needs a way to get a `Pane` without going through `PaneSetup&lt;H&gt;`. There are two clean options:
> 
> (a) `create_pane` returns a non-generic `Pane`, and `pane.setup::&lt;H&gt;()` returns `PaneSetup&lt;H&gt;`. Simple panes never call setup.
> 
> (b) `Pane` has both `run()` (closure, no services) and `setup::&lt;H&gt;()` (returns `PaneSetup&lt;H&gt;` for service registration).
> 
> Option (b) is better. It means:
> 
> ```rust
> // Simple case: no services, no handler type needed
> let pane = app.create_pane(Tag::new("Hello"))?.wait()?;
> pane.run(|proxy, msg| ...)
> 
> // Service case: handler type declared when services are opened
> let mut setup = app.create_pane(Tag::new("Editor"))?.wait()?
>     .setup::&lt;Editor&gt;();
> let clipboard = setup.open_service::&lt;Clipboard&gt;()?;
> setup.run_with_display(Editor { clipboard, ... })
> ```
> 
> This is strictly better than having `create_pane` take a type parameter, because:
> - The handler type is introduced exactly when it's needed (at `open_service` time)
> - Simple panes never see a type parameter at all
> - `create_pane` does the same thing regardless of handler type (contacts the server, gets an id back)
> 
> **3. Migration cost.**
> 
> If you pick A now and need to change, you're removing a type parameter from a public type — a breaking change that touches every use site. If you pick C (especially variant (b) above) and need to change, you're modifying the setup builder, which is a narrower surface.
> 
> **4. What Haiku's implementation tells us.**
> 
> Haiku's implementation confirms: the container (BLooper/BWindow) should not be generic over its contents. The handler relationship is dynamic — AddHandler at any time, RemoveHandler at any time. What pane is correctly doing is making the *service interest* static (via Handles&lt;P&gt;), but that static check belongs at the *registration* boundary, not on the *container* type.
> 
> ## My Recommendation
> 
> Option C, variant (b): `Pane` is non-generic. `Pane::setup::&lt;H&gt;()` returns `PaneSetup&lt;H&gt;`. Service registration lives on `PaneSetup&lt;H&gt;`. `run_with`/`run_with_display` consume `PaneSetup&lt;H&gt;` and enter the event loop. The closure form `pane.run(|...|)` lives directly on `Pane`.
> 
> This gives you:
> - Compile-time enforcement where it matters (service registration)
> - No type parameter propagation where it doesn't matter (everything else)
> - A clean closure path for simple panes
> - An explicit typestate that captures a phase distinction BeOS had implicitly but never encoded
> - A non-generic `Pane` that matches how `BWindow`/`BLooper` actually work in practice
> 
> The spirit of Be's design here is: **the container is a neutral host for capabilities that arrive dynamically; the type system should enforce the contract between a specific capability and the handler that serves it, not between the container and everything it might hold.** Option C captures this. Option A doesn't.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>acd94aa840698a498</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_016hJet8XuNcyXcbXRcFnfmx</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/acd94aa840698a498.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Review all architecture.md changes" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. I now have the full document and the relevant context. Let me do the thorough review.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Review of docs/architecture.md — Plan 9 Fidelity, Distributed Soundness, and Consistency
> 
> ### 1. Plan 9 Fidelity: The Updated Analogies
> 
> **"Pane is the nascent process (after fork, before exec)"** (lines 287-289)
> 
> This is close but not quite right, and it matters because the analogy is load-bearing for the two-phase lifecycle justification.
> 
> In Plan 9, `rfork(RFPROC)` creates a child process that shares the parent's namespace, file descriptors, and memory segments. The child *is* a running process — it has a pid, it's schedulable, it can execute instructions. It's not "nascent" in any meaningful sense; it's a full process that happens to share everything with its parent. What it doesn't have yet is its own *program image* — `exec` replaces the program text. But between fork and exec, the child is a running process with full capabilities. It can (and often does) call `bind`, `mount`, `open`, `close`, `rfork(RFNAMEG)` to customize its namespace before exec.
> 
> So the analogy should be: **Pane after `create_pane` is like a child process after `rfork` — it exists, has identity (pid/pane-id), is connected to the server, but hasn't done anything yet.** The phrase "nascent process" slightly undersells what a post-fork process is in Plan 9. A post-fork process is not nascent; it's fully alive, it just hasn't diverged yet. `Pane` is more accurately "the bare process" — it has identity but no customized namespace.
> 
> **"PaneBuilder\&lt;H\&gt; is namespace construction (bind/mount)"** (line 288)
> 
> This is the stronger half of the analogy and it holds well. In Plan 9, between fork and exec, a process calls `bind` and `mount` to assemble its namespace — adding servers, overlaying directories, building the environment the exec'd program will see. `PaneBuilder&lt;H&gt;::open_service()` is precisely `mount` — attaching a service (file server) into the pane's operational namespace. The fact that PaneBuilder is consumed by `run_with` (you can't mount after exec) is exactly right. Plan 9 processes *can* bind/mount after exec, but the idiomatic pattern — and the one that matters for the analogy — is the `namespace(1)` file or the per-process setup in `/lib/namespace`, which runs before the shell starts.
> 
> **"run_with is exec"** (line 289)
> 
> Clean. exec replaces the program image and begins execution. `run_with` consumes the builder, installs the handler, and starts the looper. The process can't go back to the setup phase, just as exec is irreversible.
> 
> **"the looper is the running process"** (line 289)
> 
> Fine. The looper is the process executing its program — dispatching events, handling messages, doing its work.
> 
> **Suggested refinement:** Change "nascent process (after fork, before exec)" to something like "the bare process (after rfork, before namespace customization)" or just "the child process after rfork — it has identity and a connection, but no services in its namespace yet." The word "nascent" implies the process isn't fully formed, which isn't how Plan 9 works. The process is fully formed; its namespace just hasn't been customized.
> 
> This is a minor precision issue, not a structural flaw. The analogy's load-bearing property — that namespace construction is a distinct phase from execution — holds perfectly.
> 
> ### 2. Distributed Computing Soundness
> 
> The distributed story (Phase 2: multi-server, TLS, service map) reads coherently. Let me check whether the EAct qualifications introduced any weakness.
> 
> **BMessenger qualification** (line 29: "approximates a session endpoint (destination handle, not a formal role)")
> 
> This is a local BeOS fidelity statement and doesn't affect the distributed model. BMessenger was always a destination handle. The distributed model uses `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` and `Messenger`, which are better-defined than BMessenger was. No distributed consequence.
> 
> **BMessageFilter outside EAct** (lines 30-32)
> 
> Also a local fidelity statement. The filter chain is a client-side mechanism that doesn't cross the wire. The distributed model routes events through `Handles&lt;P&gt;`, which is formal. No distributed consequence.
> 
> **Progress claim qualified with I6** (lines 49-53)
> 
> This is the important one. The text now says: "EAct's Progress theorem (Theorem 4.3) requires individual protocols to be compliant (safe and deadlock-free); the single-threaded execution model (I6) is pane's primary defense against inter-session deadlocks, corresponding to EAct's event-driven argument (§4.3)."
> 
> This is actually *stronger* for the distributed model, not weaker. The qualification makes explicit that deadlock freedom comes from the execution model (single-threaded dispatch), not from any global property of the protocol. In a distributed setting where you have multiple servers and cross-connection ordering is not guaranteed (line 1263-1275), having the defense rooted in the *local* execution model is exactly what you want. If the Progress claim depended on some global ordering guarantee, distribution would break it. Since it depends on I6 (local property), distribution is safe.
> 
> **Ferrite fidelity now conditional** (lines 56-61)
> 
> "a conditional form of protocol fidelity... if the developer's code type-checks against `Chan&lt;S, T&gt;` and consumes every intermediate channel value." This is honest and doesn't weaken any distributed claim. The distributed model doesn't depend on Ferrite-level static guarantees; it depends on the wire protocol being well-formed and Drop compensation working.
> 
> **PaneBuilder restricts sigma construction** (lines 345-351)
> 
> "EAct permits dynamic σ growth during execution (E-Suspend adds entries at any point); PaneBuilder requires service dispatch routes to be established before the looper starts."
> 
> This is *strictly more conservative* than EAct. For distribution, this is a feature: all service connections are established before the event loop starts, so the service map is fully resolved before any handler code runs. There's no race between service resolution and event dispatch. The qualification notes that `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` entries from `send_request` still grow dynamically (line 350), which is correct — those are reply callbacks, not new service connections.
> 
> **Assessment:** The EAct qualifications uniformly make the distributed story *more honest* without weakening any claim the distributed model depends on. The distributed model depends on: (a) the wire protocol, (b) per-Connection failure isolation, (c) the service map, (d) ServiceHandle binding semantics. None of these were touched by the EAct qualifications.
> 
> ### 3. The fd-hangup Backstop (I1)
> 
> Lines 1607-1611:
> &gt; Residual risks where Drop cannot fire: double panic during unwind (abort), `std::process::abort()`, SIGKILL, OOM-triggered abort. The server detects these via fd hangup (EPOLLHUP on unix sockets, TCP RST on remote) and cleans up the pane entry — this is the backstop when I1 is violated.
> 
> **Plan 9 analogy assessment:**
> 
> In Plan 9, when a process dies without closing its file descriptors, the kernel closes them during process cleanup (`pexit` in the kernel source — `/sys/src/9/port/proc.c`). Each close on a channel (Plan 9's fd abstraction at the kernel level) decrements the channel's reference count. When the reference count hits zero, the kernel sends a `Tclunk` on the underlying 9P connection to the file server. If the process was holding an open fid on a mounted file server, the server sees the clunk and cleans up.
> 
> If the *entire client machine* dies (power failure, network partition), there's no clunk — the TCP connection just goes silent. The server discovers this when it tries to write and gets a connection error, or via TCP keepalives. This is the analogy to EPOLLHUP/TCP RST.
> 
> The pane document's description is correct in spirit: the server detects client death via transport-level mechanisms when the application-level cleanup (Drop) can't fire. But the analogy could be more precise:
> 
> - **Unix sockets / EPOLLHUP**: This is the clean case. The kernel closes the socket when the process dies (even SIGKILL), so EPOLLHUP fires promptly. This is analogous to Plan 9's kernel-level close-on-exit — the kernel does the cleanup the process couldn't.
> - **TCP RST on remote**: This is the messy case. If the remote process is SIGKILLed, the OS sends RST. If the machine loses power, there's no RST — the server discovers the dead connection via TCP keepalives (potentially minutes later) or the next failed write. Plan 9 had the same problem; Inferno's `aan(8)` (authenticated application network) was partly motivated by this — it added a session layer with keepalives on top of the transport to detect dead connections faster.
> 
> The I1 text is sound. One thing worth noting: for unix sockets, EPOLLHUP is *not* a backstop — it's a *guarantee*. The kernel will always close the socket when the process exits, regardless of how it exits. So for the single-server case (Phase 1, unix sockets), I1 violation is fully compensated by the kernel. The real risk is Phase 2 (TCP/TLS remote connections) where network partitions can leave the server unaware for an extended period. The text mentions this implicitly ("TCP RST on remote") but doesn't flag the latency difference.
> 
> **Suggested addition:** A parenthetical noting that unix-socket hangup is prompt (kernel-guaranteed) while TCP hangup detection depends on keepalive configuration and may be delayed by minutes during network partitions. This is the same lesson from Plan 9: local mounts fail cleanly, remote mounts fail slowly.
> 
> ### 4. Things Broken or Inconsistent
> 
> I found the following issues:
> 
> **(a) DLfActRiS section reference** (line 1451)
> 
> &gt; the structural argument from DLfActRiS §4-5: dropping a receive can only decrease connectivity, never create a cycle
> 
> The text says the reference was "corrected to §4-5." Let me note: if this paper is "Deadlock-freedom through Linear Forking and Typing with Risk Separation" or similar, §4-5 is plausible but I can't verify the section numbers. If you've already confirmed this, it's fine; if the correction was speculative, it should be double-checked against the actual paper.
> 
> **(b) Pane::run return type** (line 326)
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn run(self, f: impl FnMut(&amp;Messenger, Message) -&gt; Result&lt;Flow&gt;) -&gt; !;
> ```
> 
> And lines 332-333:
> ```rust
> pub fn run_with&lt;H: Handler&gt;(self, handler: H) -&gt; !;
> pub fn run_with_display&lt;H: DisplayHandler&gt;(self, handler: H) -&gt; !;
> ```
> 
> These return `!` (never), but then the main function in the closure example (line 1887-1894):
> ```rust
> fn main() -&gt; pane_app::Result&lt;()&gt; {
>     ...
>     pane.run(|_proxy, msg| match msg {
>         Message::CloseRequested =&gt; Ok(Flow::Stop),
>         _ =&gt; Ok(Flow::Continue),
>     })
> }
> ```
> 
> `-&gt; !` is coercible to any type, so `fn main() -&gt; Result&lt;()&gt;` compiles with a `-&gt; !` expression in tail position. This is technically correct Rust. But it's worth noting that `-&gt; !` means the function *never* returns — it doesn't return an error either. If you want `run` to surface errors (e.g., connection failure during setup), it should return `Result&lt;!&gt;` or `Result&lt;Infallible&gt;`. As written, `-&gt; !` means errors panic or abort. I raise this because the headless agent example (line 1810-1814):
> 
> ```rust
> fn main() -&gt; pane_app::Result&lt;()&gt; {
>     ...
>     pane.run_with(StatusAgent)
> }
> ```
> 
> ...suggests the caller expects `Result&lt;()&gt;` but `run_with` returns `!`. If the connection fails inside `run_with`, the only way to report it is panic. If that's the intent, fine, but it's a design choice worth being explicit about. The `PaneBuilder&lt;H&gt;::run_with` on line 381 also returns `!`.
> 
> **(c) Duplicate method signatures on Pane and PaneBuilder**
> 
> `Pane::run_with&lt;H: Handler&gt;(self, handler: H) -&gt; !` (line 332) and `PaneBuilder&lt;H&gt;::run_with(self, handler: H) -&gt; !` (line 381). Both exist, which is correct (one path through setup, one without). But the doc comment says "three paths into the event loop" (line 1544-1554) and lists them clearly. This is fine.
> 
> **(d) Minor grammar issue** (line 883)
> 
> &gt; This eliminates the ambiguity of a design where `Pass` carries the message — a filter could silently return a modified message via Pass, conflating pass-through with transformation.
> 
> This sentence is a fragment appended to the previous sentence's period. It reads as two independent clauses joined by a dash, which is acceptable but slightly awkward. Not a real problem.
> 
> **(e) The "Resolved Question 26" text vs the main Two-phase lifecycle section**
> 
> RQ26 (lines 2037-2048) says "BeOS's BWindow was not generic over its handler" and "Plan 9 separates namespace construction from the running process." The main section (lines 270-289) says the same thing in different words. These are consistent — no contradiction.
> 
> **(f) ServiceHandle binding semantics mentioned in memory but not in the document**
> 
> My memory file `project_pane_generic_decision.md` item 5 says: "ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt; bound to Connection+version at open time. Service map changes affect new opens, not existing handles. This is Plan 9 fid semantics (fid bound at open, mount table changes affect new walks only)."
> 
> The document *implies* this (ServiceHandle is returned from open_service which blocks until InterestAccepted, and it stores connection_id) but doesn't state the fid-binding semantics explicitly. This is a load-bearing property for Phase 2 (what happens when the service map changes while handles are live?) and should be stated somewhere in the spec — either in the ServiceHandle section or in the Service Registration section.
> 
> **(g) Missing explicit statement about "host as contingent server"**
> 
> The shared memory (`project_host_as_contingent_server.md` referenced in MEMORY.md) says local hardware is just a server the UX runs on, with no architectural privilege. The architecture document reflects this implicitly (multi-server topology, no server is mandatory) but never states the principle explicitly. This is a project-level concern, not a spec inconsistency, but it's worth noting since it's listed as "FOUNDATIONAL" in the shared memory.
> 
> ### Summary
> 
> | Issue | Severity | Action |
> |---|---|---|
> | "nascent process" slightly mischaracterizes post-fork Plan 9 process | Minor | Refine to "bare process" or "child process after rfork" |
> | fd-hangup backstop is sound but TCP latency risk unstated | Minor | Add note about keepalive-dependent detection latency for remote |
> | `-&gt; !` return type on `run`/`run_with` means errors can only panic | Design question | Decide if this is intentional; if yes, document it |
> | ServiceHandle fid-binding semantics not stated in spec | Moderate | Add explicit statement in ServiceHandle or Service Registration |
> | DLfActRiS §4-5 reference unverifiable from here | Low | Confirm against paper |
> | EAct qualifications: no distributed model weakening | None | Clean — the qualifications are improvements |
> | Distributed story coherence | None | Phase 2 reads correctly |
> 
> The document is in good shape. The edits have been surgical and haven't introduced contradictions. The EAct qualifications are uniformly improvements — making claims more honest without undermining the distributed model. The Plan 9 analogies are functional and directionally correct, with the "nascent process" wording being the only point where precision could improve.</result>
