> ---
> 
> ## Research Report: rumpsteak-aura
> 
> ### 1. What is rumpsteak? What is rumpsteak-aura?
> 
> **Original rumpsteak** (https://github.com/zakcutner/rumpsteak) was a 2021 research prototype by Zak Cutner at Imperial College London. Single crate, 354 lines of Rust, published once as v0.1.0. Marked "work in progress." It provided multiparty session types for async Rust with deadlock-freedom guarantees via MPST and asynchronous subtyping.
> 
> **rumpsteak-aura** (https://github.com/hxrts/rumpsteak-aura) is a fork by Sam Hart (GitHub: hxrts) under the "Aura" project umbrella. It's a significant extension: the original was a flat session-type runtime; the fork adds a full choreographic programming layer with a DSL, automatic projection from global types to local types, an effect handler system for transport abstraction, and formal verification via Lean 4. The aura-project/rumpsteak-aura GitHub org repo returns 404 (empty org), but the actual code lives at hxrts/rumpsteak-aura.
> 
> **Lineage**: rumpsteak -&gt; rumpsteak-aura -&gt; telltale (same author, v10.0.0, actively maintained as of today). Telltale appears to be the successor crate name, with a bytecode VM, protocol machine, transport crate, and runtime.
> 
> ### 2. Session-type formalism
> 
> **MPST (Multiparty Session Types)**, not CLL. This is the key distinction from pane-session.
> 
> The formalism is based on:
> - **"A Very Gentle Introduction to Multiparty Session Types"** (Yoshida &amp; Gheri) -- the standard MPST reference
> - **"Precise Subtyping for Asynchronous Multiparty Sessions"** (Ghilezan et al., POPL 2021) -- async subtyping that allows message reordering while preserving causal dependencies
> - **"Mechanised Subject Reduction for Multiparty Asynchronous Session Types"** (ECOOP 2025) -- a mechanized Coq proof fixing flaws in Honda et al.'s original MPST formulation, adding an "unstuckness" property
> 
> The core types in `rumpsteak-types` are `GlobalType` and `LocalTypeR`, with explicit roles, labels, and payload sorts. The `rumpsteak-theory` crate implements projection, merge, duality, sync/async subtyping, bounded recursion, coherence predicates, and async step semantics. All algorithms are designed to match Lean definitions exactly.
> 
> **Critical difference from pane-session**: pane-session uses the Caires-Pfenning/Wadler correspondence (CLL -- classical linear logic), which gives binary sessions with duality as negation. Rumpsteak uses MPST, which starts from a global type and projects to multiple local types. These are fundamentally different frameworks:
> - CLL: binary, duality is structural (Send/Recv swap), composition via cut
> - MPST: n-ary, projection from choreography, merge for non-participants, subtyping for optimization
> 
> ### 3. Relation to other Rust session-type libraries
> 
> | Library | Formalism | Arity | Async | Transport | Status |
> |---------|-----------|-------|-------|-----------|--------|
> | **pane-session** | CLL (Caires-Pfenning) | Binary | Sync (calloop) | Pluggable (unix, tcp, memory) | Active |
> | **par** | CLL | Binary | Sync | In-memory | Unmaintained |
> | **session-types-rs** | Binary ST | Binary | No | In-memory | Stale |
> | **Ferrite** | SILL (linear logic) | Binary | Async | In-memory | Academic |
> | **rumpsteak** (original) | MPST | Multiparty | Async (futures) | Sink/Stream | Abandoned (2021) |
> | **rumpsteak-aura** | MPST + choreography | Multiparty | Async (tokio/wasm) | Effect handlers | Active |
> | **telltale** | MPST + choreography + VM | Multiparty | Async | Production transports | Active (v10) |
> 
> Rumpsteak-aura is the only Rust library doing choreographic programming with a DSL and automatic projection. Ferrite is the closest academic competitor for formal rigor but is binary and focused on linear logic.
> 
> ### 4. Approach to key concerns
> 
> **Branching**: MPST-style. Global `Choice` has a choosing role and labeled branches. Projects to `Select` (chooser sends label) and `Branch` (others receive label). Non-participants get merged projections. Also supports `LocalChoice` (no communication, purely local decision). This is richer than pane-session's binary `Select&lt;L, R&gt;` / `Branch&lt;L, R&gt;` which offers exactly two options via tag bytes.
> 
> **Streaming**: No dedicated streaming primitive. Recursion via `Rec`/`Var` (explicit mu-binders). Loops with optional conditions (`Count(n)`, custom boolean, infinite). No equivalent to pane-session's potential streaming extension.
> 
> **Failure handling**: The aura fork adds `Sealable` trait and `SessionError::Sealed` / `ReceiveError::Sealed` variants. `SessionGuard` detects protocol violations on drop (debug-mode panic if session dropped without completing). Transport errors propagate through `SessionError::Channel`. This is structurally similar to pane-session's `SessionError::Disconnected` but less crash-safe -- pane-session's explicit design goal of "recv returns Err, not panic" is cleaner.
> 
> **Transport abstraction**: Two layers. The base rumpsteak session types use `Sink`/`Stream` from futures (any async channel). The choreographic layer adds `ChoreoHandler` trait with `send`/`recv`/`choose`/`offer` methods, decoupling protocol logic from transport entirely. `InMemoryHandler` for testing, `RumpsteakHandler` for production (accepts `SimpleChannel` pairs or custom sink/stream). WASM support via `wasm-bindgen-futures`. This is more abstract than pane-session's `Transport` trait (which deals with raw bytes) but also more complex.
> 
> ### 5. Maintenance and maturity
> 
> - **First published**: Nov 10, 2025. **Latest**: v0.9.1, Jan 17, 2026.
> - **14 releases in ~2 months** -- rapid iteration.
> - **998 downloads** total, 409 recent. Low adoption.
> - **Line counts (v0.9.1)**: 710 lines of Rust, **29,973 lines of Lean**. The Lean formalization dwarfs the Rust implementation.
> - **GitHub**: hxrts/rumpsteak-aura has 1 star. The parent project "aura" (hxrts/aura, "P2P choreography") has 10 stars.
> - **The author has already moved on to "telltale"** (v10.0.0), which adds a bytecode VM, protocol machine, simulator, and dedicated transport and runtime crates.
> - **Lean verification**: Partial. Core merge and subtyping reflexivity are proven. Subject reduction, progress, non-participant correctness, and merge composition are **axiomatized but not proven**. The verification pipeline is real but the proofs have significant gaps.
> 
> **Assessment**: Pre-alpha quality, single-developer project, active but rapidly evolving (already superseded by telltale). The Lean work is ambitious but incomplete.
> 
> ### 6. Academic papers
> 
> The project references three papers:
> 1. **"A Very Gentle Introduction to Multiparty Session Types"** -- Yoshida &amp; Gheri (standard MPST intro)
> 2. **"Precise Subtyping for Asynchronous Multiparty Sessions"** -- Ghilezan et al., POPL 2021 (the async subtyping algorithm)
> 3. **"Mechanised Subject Reduction for Multiparty Asynchronous Session Types"** -- ECOOP 2025 (at https://drops.dagstuhl.de/entities/document/10.4230/LIPIcs.ECOOP.2025.31), with mechanized Coq proof at github.com/Tirore96/subject_reduction
> 
> The original rumpsteak by Cutner doesn't have a published paper, though it came from Imperial College London's MRGG (Mobility Reading Group and Groupoid) which is Nobuko Yoshida's group.
> 
> ### Relevance to pane
> 
> **What's potentially useful:**
> - The choreographic DSL concept (define protocol globally, project to local types) is interesting for pane's multi-kit protocols where you want to specify an interaction between Application Kit and Interface Kit globally rather than writing both sides manually. However, pane's architecture is binary sessions between two endpoints, not multiparty.
> - The Lean verification pipeline (export Rust types to JSON, verify in Lean) is a pattern worth studying for pane's own formal verification ambitions.
> - The async subtyping algorithm (from POPL 2021) could be relevant if pane ever needs to verify that one protocol is a subtype of another (protocol evolution).
> 
> **What doesn't apply:**
> - MPST is a fundamentally different formalism from CLL. Pane-session's strength is that binary sessions compose via cut (the linear logic composition principle) -- you don't need a global choreography because the protocol is guaranteed correct by construction from binary interactions. MPST requires a global specification and projection, which is a heavier methodology.
> - The effect handler indirection layer adds complexity that pane doesn't need. Pane-session's direct `Transport` trait with raw bytes is simpler and sufficient for the desktop environment use case.
> - The Lean verification is impressive in ambition but the key safety theorems (subject reduction, progress) are axiomatized, not proven. Pane doesn't gain confidence by depending on unproven axioms.
> - The crate is pre-alpha, single-developer, low adoption, and already being superseded by telltale.
> 
> **Bottom line**: Rumpsteak-aura is an interesting research project exploring MPST + choreographic programming + Lean verification in Rust. It occupies a different design space from pane-session (multiparty vs binary, MPST vs CLL, choreographic vs compositional). There's nothing in it that would improve pane-session's architecture -- pane's binary CLL-based approach is the right choice for desktop IPC where protocols are between two parties. If pane ever needs multiparty coordination (e.g., a three-way handshake between app, compositor, and input server), that's when MPST theory becomes relevant, but even then the choreographic approach would be a design methodology to adopt, not a library to depend on.</result>

> <result>I have more than enough data to write the full report. Here is the comprehensive analysis.

> ---
> 
> ## Telltale Research Report
> 
> ### 1. What telltale provides
> 
> Telltale is a **three-surface project**: a Rust library for composable multiparty session-typed choreographies, an extensive Lean 4 proof system, and a three-paper series on MPST theoretical contributions. It's at **v10.0.0** as of April 1, 2026 (13 releases since Feb 25, 2026 -- roughly one major version every 3 days).
> 
> **GitHub repo:** https://github.com/hxrts/telltale (public, MIT, 2 stars, 0 forks, actively pushed today April 3)
> 
> **Authors:** Sam Hart (hxrts) and Zak Cutner (zakcutner) -- the same pair from rumpsteak.
> 
> ### 2. Sub-crates (12 workspace members)
> 
> From the workspace `Cargo.toml` and `docs/105_code_organization.md`:
> 
> | Crate | Role |
> |---|---|
> | **telltale** (root) | Facade: re-exports session types (Send/Recv/Select/Branch/End), `tell!` macro, language APIs |
> | **telltale-types** | Foundation: `GlobalType`, `LocalTypeR`, `Label`, `PayloadSort`, content-addressing (BLAKE3) |
> | **telltale-theory** | Pure algorithms: projection, merge, duality, well-formedness, bounded recursion, subtyping, coherence |
> | **telltale-language** | Shared frontend: Pest-based DSL parser (`.tell` files), AST, projection, codegen |
> | **telltale-macros** | Proc macros: `tell!`, `session`, `Role`, `Roles`, `Message` |
> | **telltale-machine** | Protocol machine: bytecode compiler, VM execution, scheduler, semantic objects, coroutines, buffers |
> | **telltale-runtime** | Choreography runtime: `ChoreoHandler`, effect interpretation, transport abstractions, WASM support |
> | **telltale-simulator** | Deterministic testing: `SimulationHarness`, fault injection, network modeling, replay, checkpoints |
> | **telltale-bridge** | Rust-to-Lean bridge: JSON export/import, schema versioning, trace comparison, parity enforcement |
> | **telltale-transport** | Production transport: TCP with length-prefix framing, IPv4/IPv6, env-based discovery |
> | **telltale-lints** | Custom linting via syn/quote |
> | (not published separately: `telltale-choreography`, `telltale-vm`, `telltale-lean-bridge` appear as related crates on lib.rs) |
> 
> ### 3. Architecture: The Bytecode VM and Protocol Machine
> 
> The architecture has **five layers**:
> 
> 1. **DSL + Parsing** -- `tell!` macro or `.tell` files parsed via Pest into `Choreography` AST
> 2. **Projection** -- Global protocol projected to `LocalType` per role
> 3. **Code Generation** -- Local types compiled to Rust session types + typed effect interfaces
> 4. **Effect Handler Runtime** -- Async interpreter with pluggable transports (`ChoreoHandler`)
> 5. **Protocol Machine + Simulation** -- Bytecode VM with scheduler, bounded buffers, deterministic middleware
> 
> **The bytecode VM** compiles `LocalTypeR` into instructions. The instruction set has 6 families:
> - **Communication:** `Send`, `Receive`, `Offer`, `Choose`
> - **Session lifecycle:** `Open`, `Close`
> - **Effect/guard:** `Invoke`, `Acquire`, `Release`
> - **Speculation:** `Fork`, `Join`, `Abort`
> - **Ownership/knowledge:** `Transfer`, `Tag`, `Check`
> - **Control:** `Set`, `Move`, `Jump`, `Spawn`, `Yield`, `Halt`
> 
> The **protocol machine** (`ProtocolMachineKernel`) is the **sole authority over semantic progression**. It runs coroutines with bounded buffers, scheduler policies (Cooperative, RoundRobin, Priority, ProgressAware), and observable traces. A threaded variant (`ThreadedProtocolMachine`) supports certified-wave parallel execution with fallback to canonical one-step semantics when wave certificates are invalid.
> 
> ### 4. Choreographic Programming: telltale vs rumpsteak-aura
> 
> The `tell!` macro is the primary surface. Protocols are written once, globally:
> 
> ```rust
> tell! {
>     protocol ThreeBuyers uses AlicePlanner, SellerPricing, BobBudget under Replay =
>       roles Alice, Bob, Seller
>       Alice -&gt; Seller : Request(i32)
>       Seller -&gt; Alice : Quote(i32)
>       Seller -&gt; Bob : Quote(i32)
>       Alice -&gt; Bob : Contribution(i32)
>       choice Bob at
>         | Confirm =&gt; ...
>         | Quit =&gt; ...
> }
> ```
> 
> Key advances over rumpsteak-aura:
> - **Typed effect boundaries**: `effect` declarations with explicit semantic metadata (class, progress, region, agreement, reentrancy) generate Rust traits that host code implements
> - **Protocol machine execution path**: in addition to the generated session-type path, protocols can run through the bytecode VM with deterministic replay
> - **Simulation infrastructure**: `telltale-simulator` provides fault injection, field environments, property checks, and cross-target conformance
> - **Conservation framework**: six conserved properties (evidence, authority, identity, commitment, structure, premise)
> - **Execution profiles**: protocols declare profiles (`Replay`, `fairness`, `eventual`, `admissibility`, etc.) that gate runtime behavior
> 
> ### 5. Transports
> 
> Currently **TCP-only** for production:
> - Length-prefixed message framing (4-byte big-endian u32 + payload)
> - IPv4, IPv6, dual-stack
> - Environment-based endpoint resolution (`TELLTALE_ALICE_ENDPOINT=...`)
> - Factory pattern with retry/backoff
> - TLS is declared as a future feature flag
> 
> Testing transports: `InMemoryHandler` (futures channels), `RecordingHandler` (event log), `NoOpHandler`.
> 
> The transport is **behind a trait boundary** (`ChoreoHandler` for choreography layer, `EffectHandler` for protocol-machine layer), so custom transports are straightforward.
> 
> ### 6. Branching, Recursion, Failure, Verification
> 
> **Branching:** `choice Role at | Label =&gt; ...` with optional guards. Projects to `Select`/`Branch` local types. `Offer`/`Choose` bytecode instructions with jump tables.
> 
> **Recursion:** `rec label ... continue label` for explicit recursion. `loop decide by Role` (desugars to choice+recursion). `loop repeat N`, `loop while "pred"`, `loop forever`. All standard MPST recursion through `Mu`/`Var` in local types, `Jump` in bytecode.
> 
> **Failure:** Typed failure is first-class:
> - `EffectFailureKind` taxonomy: denied, timeout, cancellation, stale authority, invalid evidence, unavailable, invalid input, determinism, topology disruption, contract violation
> - Explicit observable events: `TimeoutIssued`, `CancellationRequested`, `Cancelled`, `FailureBranchEntered`, `SessionTerminal`
> - Speculation with `Fork`/`Join`/`Abort` bytecode instructions
> - `timeout ... on timeout ... on cancel ...` in the DSL
> - Evidence guards: `when check Effect.op(...) yields witness`
> 
> **Verification:** Protocol proof status is exposed at runtime via `Protocol::proof_status` and `Protocol::sessions`. Four tiers: full spec, session projectable, protocol-machine executable, proof only.
> 
> ### 7. Lean 4 Verification Status
> 
> This is where telltale massively surpasses rumpsteak-aura. The Lean tree has **656 files, 134,173 lines** organized in 10 library layers:
> 
> | Layer | Files | Lines | Focus |
> |---|---|---|---|
> | SessionTypes | 48 | 10,040 | Global/local types, de Bruijn, participation |
> | SessionCoTypes | 78 | 16,302 | Coinductive EQ2, bisimulation, duality, async subtyping |
> | Choreography | 89 | 19,361 | Projection, harmony, blindness, embedding, erasure |
> | Semantics | 14 | 2,359 | Operational semantics, determinism, deadlock freedom |
> | Classical | 16 | 2,193 | Transported queueing/stochastic theorems |
> | Distributed | 59 | 7,266 | FLP, CAP, quorum, Nakamoto, reconfiguration |
> | Protocol | 170 | 40,133 | Async buffered MPST, coherence, preservation, monitoring |
> | Runtime | 176 | 34,561 | Protocol machine model, Iris backend, resource algebras, WP |
> | IrisExtraction | 3 | 830 | Ghost state and program logic extraction |
> 
> The three papers establish mechanized metatheory for async buffered MPST:
> - **Paper 1:** Operational coherence invariant for compositional preservation proofs
> - **Paper 2:** Quantitative Lyapunov bounds and decidability results
> - **Paper 3:** Harmony theorem for dynamic reconfiguration
> 
> All results are claimed to be **mechanized in Lean 4** (with `just escape` checking axiom/sorry budgets). The verification covers:
> - Lean semantics and theorems
> - Rust-to-Lean core protocol-machine runtime correspondence
> - First-party handler and transport contract boundaries
> - Artifact correspondence pipeline
> 
> Explicitly **excluded** from formal claim: Rust macro expansion, the parser, compiler/third-party integration paths. Proof holes tracked by `just escape`.
> 
> This is a qualitative leap over rumpsteak-aura's "axiomatized but key theorems not proven" status. The Lean proofs use **paco** (parametrized coinduction), **mathlib**, and **iris-lean** (Iris separation logic).
> 
> ### 8. Line Counts, Maturity, Maintenance
> 
> From crates.io linecounts for v10.0.0:
> - **Lean:** 92,497 code lines (27,265 comments) in 660 files
> - **Rust:** 486 code lines in the root crate (but ~59K across workspace per lib.rs)
> - **Shell:** 7,506 lines in 48 scripts
> - **TeX:** 4,506 lines in 3 paper files
> 
> From GitHub languages API: **5.93 MB Rust, 5.88 MB Lean** -- roughly equal weight.
> 
> **Maintenance cadence:** Intensely active. 20+ commits on April 3 alone. 13 crate releases in 5 weeks (Feb 25 to Apr 1). 10 major versions in that period. This is pre-stabilization churn -- the author is clearly pushing hard toward a paper submission or release milestone.
> 
> **Maturity assessment:** The documentation is remarkably thorough (35+ doc files), the Lean proof stack is substantial, and the architecture is well-layered. But 10 major semver bumps in 5 weeks is a red flag for API stability. This is very much active development, not a stable dependency.
> 
> ### 9. Suitability for Layered Use (The Key Question)
> 
> **Architecture for composability -- good news:**
> 
> The crate structure is deliberately layered:
> - `telltale-types` is standalone (no workspace deps) -- could be used as a shared type vocabulary
> - `telltale-theory` depends only on `telltale-types` -- pure algorithms, no runtime
> - `telltale-language` depends only on `telltale-types` -- pure parsing/projection/codegen
> - The `tell!` macro generates standard session types (`Send`, `Recv`, `Select`, `Branch`, `End`) that match the classical MPST vocabulary
> 
> The root `telltale` crate's `lib.rs` exports exactly the primitives you'd want:
> ```rust
> pub struct Send&lt;'q, Q: Role, R, L, S: FromState&lt;'q, Role = Q&gt;&gt;
> pub struct Receive&lt;'q, Q: Role, R, L, S: FromState&lt;'q, Role = Q&gt;&gt;
> pub struct Select&lt;'q, Q: Role, R, C&gt;
> pub struct Branch&lt;'q, Q: Role, R, C&gt;
> pub struct End&lt;'q, R: Role&gt;
> ```
> 
> These are generic over `Role` (which is a trait with `Route&lt;R&gt;` for routing), and the channels are generic over `Sink`/`Stream`. The `Role` trait and `Route&lt;R&gt;` pattern means you can plug in your own transport.
> 
> **Concerns for the three-tier architecture:**
> 
> 1. **It's not "use the projection and ignore the rest."** The protocol machine, effect system, and simulator are deeply integrated. The `tell!` macro generates not just session types but also effect interfaces, proof status metadata, execution profiles, and annotation records. You'd be using a fraction of what it generates.
> 
> 2. **The session types it generates are its own types, not par's types.** `telltale::Send` is not `par::Send`. The generated code uses `telltale`'s own channel abstractions (`Role` trait + `Route&lt;R&gt;` pattern + `Sink`/`Stream`). Bridging to par's CLL session types would require a translation layer.
> 
> 3. **Transport is trait-bounded but telltale-flavored.** `ChoreoHandler` and `EffectHandler` are the integration points. Plugging in pane-session's IPC transport would mean implementing these traits, which is doable but ties you to telltale's handler model.
> 
> 4. **The theory crate is clean.** `telltale-theory` is the most obviously extractable piece. Pure projection, merge, duality, subtyping algorithms over `GlobalType`/`LocalTypeR` from `telltale-types`. If you wanted to use telltale only for MPST global protocol specification + projection + verification, you'd depend on `telltale-types` + `telltale-theory` + `telltale-language` (for parsing the DSL). That's viable.
> 
> **Verdict for the three-tier model:**
> 
> The ideal use case would be:
> - **telltale-types + telltale-theory + telltale-language**: MPST global specs, projection to local types, verification connection
> - **par**: Binary session types as the local execution model
> - **pane-session**: IPC transport layer
> 
> This is architecturally possible because `telltale-language` projects to `LocalType` values that could be translated to par's `Send`/`Recv`/`Select`/`Branch` types. The translation from telltale's `LocalType` (which is standard MPST local type syntax) to par's CLL types is the bridge you'd need to build. The Lean verification story would cover the global-to-local projection; par would cover the local binary session correctness; pane-session would cover the transport.
> 
> The **risk** is that telltale is moving so fast (10 major versions in 5 weeks) that any integration point could break. The types crate is stable (it's the Lean-correspondent foundation), but the language/theory APIs are evolving rapidly.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a3b115f182040d6e8</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01JXoXgVk2SV4Qn7VGb9yAXU</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/a3b115f182040d6e8.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Was Clone needed in BeOS messaging?" completed</summary>
> <result>Here are the facts.
> 
> ## 1. BMessage was fully copyable
> 
> - **Copy constructor**: `BMessage(const BMessage&amp; other)` -- line 62 of Message.h, implemented at line 171 of Message.cpp. Delegates to `operator=`.
> - **`operator=`**: Deep copy -- `malloc`s new buffers, `memcpy`s the header, fields, and data. Explicitly clears delivery metadata flags (`REPLY_REQUIRED`, `IS_REPLY`, `WAS_DELIVERED`, `PASS_BY_AREA`) from the clone. Comment at line 210 notes "BeOS R5 seems to keep the reply info" -- so Haiku intentionally diverges here by stripping delivery state from copies.
> - **Flatten/Unflatten**: Serialization to/from byte buffers or `BDataIO` streams -- a different kind of "clone" that goes through wire format.
> 
> No `Archive`/`Unarchive` on BMessage itself -- that's the `BArchivable` protocol, which BMessage is *not* a subclass of. BMessage is the *container* used by `BArchivable::Archive()`.
> 
> ## 2. Was cloning used in practice?
> 
> Yes, routinely:
> - **Sending always copies.** `BLooper::PostMessage()` takes `const BMessage*` and the message gets flattened into the port. The receiver reconstructs from the flat buffer. The sender retains its original.
> - **Scripting specifier chains.** You'd build up a BMessage with specifiers and send it. Copies happened naturally.
> - **Forwarding.** A handler could copy a message, modify the `what` field or add data, and post it elsewhere.
> - **Template patterns.** You'd keep a "template" BMessage and copy it before filling in per-instance data.
> - **Reply chains.** `BMessage::SendReply()` copies internally to flatten/send.
> 
> ## 3. Could BMessage contain non-copyable resources?
> 
> **No, by design.** BMessage's data model was pure values:
> - Scalars (int8-int64, float, double, bool)
> - Strings, Points, Rects
> - `entry_ref` (device + directory inode + name -- not an fd)
> - `BMessenger` (team + port + token -- a copyable address, not ownership)
> - `BFlattenable` (serialized to bytes on add)
> - Nested `BMessage`
> 
> The one wart: **`AddPointer()`/`FindPointer()`** -- stores a raw `void*`. This copies the pointer value, not what it points to. It was always understood that pointer data was only valid within the same address space and was essentially unmanaged. It existed for convenience in local-only messaging and was not recommended for cross-team communication.
> 
> BMessage could **not** carry file descriptors, port ownership, shared memory handles, or any resource with move semantics. If you needed to share a resource, you'd pass an identifier (a path, an `entry_ref`, a `BMessenger`, a port ID) and the receiver would open/acquire its own handle.
> 
> ## 4. Filters operated on borrowed references, never cloned
> 
> Look at `_ApplyFilters` (Looper.cpp line 1362): the filter chain gets a `BMessage*` -- a mutable pointer to the looper's *own* message. The same pointer flows through every filter in the chain. Filters can:
> - Mutate the message in place (add/remove/replace fields)
> - Redirect to a different handler (via the `BHandler**` out-param)
> - Return `B_SKIP_MESSAGE` to drop it
> 
> No cloning happens in the filter path. There's exactly one BMessage instance, owned by the looper, and filters borrow it.
> 
> ## Implications for pane
> 
> The Be model worked because **BMessage was a pure value type.** Copyable, flattenable, no resource ownership. This made the whole messaging system simple: messages could freely cross address spaces, get queued, cloned, logged, forwarded, without worrying about ownership transfer.
> 
> Your `ClipboardWriteLock` problem is a real tension. Be didn't have this problem because **Be never put obligations in messages.** Obligations were represented as *separate interactions* -- you'd send a message, get a reply, and the reply would carry data, not a lock. The clipboard, for example: `BClipboard::Lock()` was a local method call that acquired a lock on the clipboard looper. You'd then modify the clipboard data and `Commit()`. The lock was never a thing that traveled in a message.
> 
> So the honest answer is: **Be's design says your move-only obligation variants shouldn't be in the same type as your value messages.** Be maintained that separation naturally (messages were values, locks/resources were held locally). If pane wants `Message: Clone`, that's consistent with what Be actually did -- but then obligation-carrying things need to be a separate mechanism, not message variants. They're different animals.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a38b84c28f328c824</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01KvXqgDjxWCf5qQAZg66hjf</tool-use-id>

> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Review architecture-next.md" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have good context. Let me do the systematic review.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Review: architecture-next.md
> 
> Reviewed against five criteria. Line numbers reference the spec. I'll group by your requested categories.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 1. Plan 9 Fidelity — Heritage Notes
> 
> **Lines 87-88 — Bottom-up composition comment: accurate.**
> &gt; "Plan 9 was bottom-up too: each file server spoke 9P correctly, composition emerged from the namespace. No global choreography."
> 
> Correct and well-stated. This is precisely the argument from Pike's "The Use of Name Spaces in Plan 9" (1992) and the practical reality of how composition worked. No notes needed.
> 
> **Lines 56-61 — Optic discipline / Plan 9 comment: accurate but could be sharper.**
> &gt; "`/dev/snarf`, rio's per-window synthetic files, per-process namespaces — optic projections."
> 
> This is reasonable as shorthand, but technically `/dev/snarf` is not an optic projection in any precise sense — it's a flat file that implements read/write directly. The *optic* analogy works better for the relationship between `/proc/N/status` (a read-only lens onto kernel state) or rio's `/dev/wsys/N/` hierarchy (a prism: the window exists or it doesn't, and accessing it gives you a full interface). `/dev/snarf` is really just shared mutable state with no lens structure — a single file you read and write, no GetPut/PutGet laws apply (two writes don't commute, there's no "get-then-put-back" identity). I'd either drop snarf from the optic examples or note it's the degenerate case.
> 
> **Suggestion for line 57:** Change `/dev/snarf` to `/proc/N/status` — that's a genuine read-only lens (GetPut holds trivially for read-only access). Or keep snarf but call it "state shared via filesystem interface" rather than grouping it with optic projections.
> 
> **Lines 350-352 — PaneBuilder = namespace construction comment: good.**
> &gt; "Plan 9: Pane = bare process after rfork. PaneBuilder = namespace construction (bind/mount). run_with = exec. The looper = the running process."
> 
> Accurate and helpful. This maps precisely. One refinement: in Plan 9, a child process inherits the parent's namespace (the `RFNAMEG` flag in `rfork(2)` controls whether to share or copy). Here, `PaneBuilder` constructs a fresh namespace rather than inheriting one. That's fine — it's a different model — but the comment could note the difference. Currently it reads like a tighter analogy than it is.
> 
> **Lines 418-421 — ServiceHandle fid semantics comment: accurate and important.**
> &gt; "Plan 9 fid semantics: bound at open, mount table changes affect new walks only."
> 
> This is exactly right. In Plan 9, once a fid is walked to a file, subsequent changes to the mount table don't affect existing fids — only new `walk(2)` calls see the new topology. This is the right semantics for `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` and the comment nails it.
> 
> **Lines 448-449 — Messenger fid principle comment: needs refinement.**
> &gt; "Plan 9 fid principle: the handle IS the name."
> 
> This is imprecise. In 9P, a fid is not the name — a fid is a *binding* to a specific server file created by `walk`. The name (path) is used during walk to produce the fid; afterward, the fid carries no name. The principle is closer to: "the handle replaces the name" or "resolution happens once, at open time; the result is a direct binding." The current phrasing might lead someone to think fids carry path information, which they don't.
> 
> **Lines 517-518 — Cancel / 9P Tflush comment: accurate.**
> &gt; "advisory, same semantics as 9P Tflush"
> 
> Correct. `Tflush` in 9P is advisory — the server may have already processed the request. The client must handle both "request completed" and "request flushed" responses. Same semantics here.
> 
> **Lines 579-580 — Ehangup per-connection comment: accurate.**
> &gt; "Plan 9: Ehangup per-connection. Failure surfaces at the use site, not as a global event."
> 
> Correct. This is exactly how Plan 9 `Ehangup` works — the next operation on a hung-up connection returns the error string "hangup". No SIGHUP-style signal.
> 
> **Lines 205 — Comment about qid.path analogy: reasonable but imprecise.**
> &gt; "Plan 9: ServiceId's UUID is analogous to qid.path (stable, machine-comparable); the name is the directory entry."
> 
> `qid.path` is a server-local identifier — unique within a single file server, not globally. A UUID is globally unique. The analogy is structurally similar (stable numeric identity behind a textual name) but the scope differs. Not wrong, but worth noting the scope difference if anyone looks at this closely.
> 
> **Missing heritage note: line 473 — wire framing.**
> 
> The framing `[length: u32][service: u8][payload]` is worth a Plan 9 note. 9P uses `[size[4] type[1] tag[2] ...]` — very similar structure. The `service: u8` discriminant plays a role analogous to 9P's `tag[2]` for multiplexing, though the semantics differ (9P tags identify outstanding requests for interleaving; service IDs route to different protocol handlers). A short note would help readers familiar with 9P understand the mapping.
> 
> **Missing heritage note: line 509 — PeerAuth.**
> 
> The transport-level identity model deserves a Plan 9 note. In Plan 9, `Tauth`/`Rattach` establish identity at connection setup, and factotum handles the authentication conversation *outside* the application. pane's approach — deriving `PeerAuth` from transport properties (SO_PEERCRED, TLS certificates) — is the same separation-of-concerns principle but pushed even further: the application doesn't even see an auth conversation. Worth noting this is factotum's principle taken to its logical conclusion.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 2. Distributed Computing Soundness
> 
> **Service map (lines 557-570): sound but underspecified for real deployment.**
> 
> The service map is a static TOML file. This works for development and simple deployments. But for the multi-server story (lines 547-554) to hold in production, you need:
> - How does the service map get distributed to clients? If it's baked into the application, topology changes require redeployment.
> - What happens when a service moves? The spec says `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` bindings survive service map changes (fid semantics, line 419) — good — but there's no mechanism for *discovering* that a service has moved.
> - Precedence chain `$PANE_SERVICE_OVERRIDES &gt; manifest &gt; $PANE_SERVICES &gt; /etc/pane/services.toml` is reasonable. But "manifest" isn't defined anywhere else in the spec.
> 
> I don't think the spec needs a full service-discovery design today, but it should explicitly state that the service map is *the minimal viable mechanism* and that dynamic discovery is a future concern. Currently it reads like the finished design.
> 
> **Per-connection failure isolation (lines 574-580): sound.**
> 
> This is the correct model. Each connection is independent. Losing the compositor doesn't affect clipboard; losing clipboard doesn't affect display. The handler continues with remaining capabilities. This matches Plan 9's per-mount-point failure isolation exactly.
> 
> One thing to verify at implementation time: when a connection drops, the spec says `fail_connection()` fires for Dispatch entries keyed to that Connection (line 715, S4). Make sure this also handles the case where *no* Dispatch entries exist for the failed connection — the handler should still get a `ServiceLost` on the relevant `Handles&lt;P&gt;` even if no requests were outstanding.
> 
> **Cross-connection ordering (lines 583-587): correctly stated, but "unified batch linearizes" needs care.**
> 
> The spec says events across connections are not causally ordered, and the unified batch linearizes within each dispatch cycle. This is correct in principle, but the word "linearizes" might overstate the guarantee. Within a batch, you have a total order imposed by the implementation (calloop poll order, arrival order, etc.), but that order is not *meaningful* — it's arbitrary. The handler shouldn't depend on cross-connection order within a batch.
> 
> **Suggestion for line 585:** Add a sentence: "The batch imposes a total order for dispatch purposes, but cross-connection ordering within a batch is implementation-defined and must not be relied upon."
> 
> **Multi-server topology (lines 547-554): the story holds.**
> 
> The key architectural move — App resolves servers from the service map, each connection is a calloop source, the developer sees uniform abstractions — is sound. This is essentially "mount different servers into different parts of the namespace" in Plan 9 terms. The developer doesn't manage connections; they open services.
> 
> One gap: the spec doesn't discuss what happens when two services resolve to the *same* server. Do you get one connection or two? This matters for connection-count efficiency and for failure correlation (if the server goes down, do both services fail atomically?). Plan 9 had a clear answer: `import(4)` gave you one connection to a remote server, and multiple mounts over it shared that connection. pane should state its policy.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 3. Namespace / pane-fs Implications
> 
> **Message: Clone (lines 166-176): enables namespace projection correctly.**
> 
> The `Clone` requirement on `Message` is the right call for namespace event streams. pane-fs needs to project message traffic into the filesystem (observers reading from synthetic files). If messages are move-only, the observer has to intercept (consume) the message, breaking the primary delivery path. With `Clone`, the namespace layer can clone messages for projection without disturbing the primary dispatch. This is exactly the pattern: filter chain sees `&amp;Message`, can clone for logging/projection, original continues to handler.
> 
> The spec clearly states obligation handles are NOT `Message` variants (line 406-411) — correct, since those are move-only and should never appear in namespace projections. The namespace sees values; obligations are private to the handler.
> 
> **ServiceId as namespace key (lines 183-200): sound.**
> 
> `ServiceId` with UUID + name maps cleanly to pane-fs paths. A pane's namespace would look like `/pane/&lt;id&gt;/&lt;service&gt;/...` where `&lt;service&gt;` is derived from `ServiceId.name`. The UUID provides stable identity for programmatic access; the name provides human-readable paths. This is the qid/name duality from 9P, adapted correctly.
> 
> **Filter chain as namespace observation point (lines 598-620): correct position.**
> 
> Filters operate on `&amp;Message` (Clone-safe values), which means a pane-fs projection layer can be implemented as a filter. It sees all value messages, can clone them into a synthetic filesystem buffer, and passes them through. Obligation handles bypass filters (line 611), which means they're invisible in the namespace — correct, since they represent private state-machine transitions that shouldn't be externally observable.
> 
> **Missing: how pane-fs observes service registration.**
> 
> The spec describes `DeclareInterest` / `InterestAccepted` as the service negotiation protocol (lines 486-498), but doesn't discuss how pane-fs knows which services a pane has. For the namespace to project a pane's capabilities (`/pane/&lt;id&gt;/clipboard/`, `/pane/&lt;id&gt;/display/`), something needs to track accepted services. This is probably obvious at implementation time (the dispatch table knows), but it's worth noting that the dispatch table's contents define the pane's namespace structure.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 4. The Three Error Channels
> 
> **Lines 92-115: reads well.** The table is clear, the formal mappings are precise, the BeOS comment confirms the model has heritage.
> 
> A few notes on the exposition:
> 
> **Line 94, "CLL ⊕ branching" for Protocol channel:** Technically accurate — ⊕ (internal choice, `Select`) is how the server decides to send an error response vs a success response. But a reader might expect the formal rule column to name the mechanism rather than the type constructor. Consider "CLL Select (⊕)" to be more immediately parseable.
> 
> **Line 99, "E-Reset" for Flow::Stop:** EAct's E-Reset is "return to idle state with value." `Flow::Stop` is more like "terminate." The mapping works if you read E-Reset as "handler returns control to the runtime," but strictly, E-Reset in Fowler et al. returns the actor to its initial state for the next message. `Flow::Stop` exits the loop entirely. The paper's actual termination is `E-Raise` (failure) or external shutdown. I'd either note that E-Reset here means "handler yields control" (not "returns to idle") or map `Flow::Stop` to the actor-shutdown case described in §4.3 of Fowler et al.
> 
> **Lines 106-111, "Control" and "Crash" descriptions:** Clean and precise. The principle that the looper never sees handler-domain errors (line 98) is well-stated.
> 
> **One thing that reads slightly ambiguous, line 94:** `ServiceLost` appears in the Protocol channel row. But `ServiceLost` doesn't go through a `ReplyPort` — it's delivered as a `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` message. It belongs in the Protocol channel conceptually (it's about a protocol relationship ending), but the mechanism column listing "ReplyPort, ServiceLost" groups two things with different dispatch paths. A reader might think `ServiceLost` goes through `ReplyPort`. Consider splitting:
> 
> | Channel | Mechanism | Formal rule | Audience |
> |---------|-----------|-------------|----------|
> | Protocol (request) | ReplyPort error/drop | CLL ⊕ | Requesting participant |
> | Protocol (session) | ServiceLost message | session end | Service participants |
> 
> ...or just add a parenthetical clarifying that `ServiceLost` is a `Handles&lt;P&gt;` message, not a `ReplyPort` delivery.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 5. Missing from the Distributed/Namespace Perspective
> 
> **A. Connection identity and pane identity across servers.**
> 
> The spec says `Pane` has an `id: Id` (line 294), but doesn't discuss who assigns this ID or whether it's globally unique. In a multi-server topology, a pane connects to multiple servers. Does each server see the same pane ID? Is the ID assigned by the primary server (compositor) and presented to other servers during handshake? The `Welcome` message (line 508) returns `instance_id` — is that the server's instance ID or the pane's?
> 
> In Plan 9, each process had a unique PID assigned by the kernel. When you exported a process's namespace to a remote machine via `exportfs(4)`, the remote saw that process's fids, not its PID — identity was connection-local. pane needs to decide: is pane identity global (one ID, presented to all servers) or connection-local (each server assigns its own handle)?
> 
> **B. No discussion of reconnection or session resumption.**
> 
> The spec covers connection failure (lines 574-580) and cleanup (lines 714-721), but says nothing about reconnection. If the clipboard server goes down and comes back, does the handler need to re-run `open_service`? Is that even possible after `run_with` has consumed the `PaneBuilder`?
> 
> This is the `aan(8)` gap from Plan 9. `aan` was a network session layer that transparently reconnected after outages — it kept the mount alive across temporary disconnections. pane doesn't need transparent reconnection (that's the "transparent latency" antipattern), but it needs a story for *explicit* reconnection. Currently, a lost service is lost forever for the lifetime of the pane.
> 
> Even just "reconnection is out of scope; a pane that needs a lost service must exit and re-launch" would be a useful statement.
> 
> **C. No mention of how headless panes are discovered.**
> 
> The spec opens with "the headless server is the base case" (line 10) and the example (lines 773-796) shows a headless agent. But there's no mechanism for other panes to *find* this agent. The `Routing` protocol is declared (line 921) but its message type is undefined.
> 
> In Plan 9, discovery was simple: `exportfs` served a namespace, and the client mounted it. The path in the namespace *was* the discovery mechanism. For pane, the namespace equivalent is pane-fs: `/pane/&lt;id&gt;/`. But the spec doesn't say how a new pane knows which IDs exist.
> 
> This is probably intentional deferral (the `Routing` protocol is the answer), but it's worth a brief note in the multi-server section saying how discovery is expected to work.
> 
> **D. No TLS client certificate story.**
> 
> Line 591 says `PeerAuth::Certificate { subject, issuer }` for TLS, and line 592 says plaintext TCP is not supported. But there's no discussion of mutual TLS. Are both sides authenticated? Server-only TLS (the common case) gives you `PeerAuth::Certificate` for the *server*, but the server sees the client as anonymous. For meaningful access control on services, you need client certificates too.
> 
> Plan 9's factotum handled mutual authentication: both sides proved identity during `Tauth/Rattach`. The spec should state whether mTLS is expected for remote connections and what the server sees as client identity when TLS is server-only.
> 
> **E. Batch coalescing across connections (line 688-690).**
> 
> "Coalescing within the batch (mouse events, etc.)" — this needs a cross-connection constraint. Coalescing should only merge events from the *same* connection and service. Merging mouse events from two different input sources (two different servers providing Input) would be wrong. The spec probably intends this but doesn't state it.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Minor / Editorial
> 
> - **Line 309:** `pub fn run(self, f: impl FnMut(&amp;Messenger, Message) -&gt; Flow) -&gt; !;` — the closure takes `Message` by value. But `Message` here seems to be a concrete type (the closure form's message type), not the `Message` trait from line 174. This could confuse readers. Is this `LifecycleMessage`? A specific `ClosureMessage` enum? Should be explicit.
> 
> - **Line 375:** `HashMap&lt;(ConnectionId, u64), Entry&gt;` — the `u64` is presumably the request token. Worth naming it: `HashMap&lt;(ConnectionId, Token), Entry&gt;` or a brief note.
> 
> - **Line 659:** `exit(0)` after `Flow::Stop` — is this `std::process::exit`? That bypasses Drop for everything *except* the handler (which was already dropped on line 658). Worth noting this is intentional and that in-flight I/O on other connections is abandoned. `std::process::exit` does not run destructors for stack-allocated values or thread-local storage.
> 
> - **Line 925-928:** "Lifecycle and Display are bundled in the Control protocol (wire service 0, implicit)." This means Display doesn't go through `DeclareInterest`. But earlier (line 487) it says "Display declared in handshake (Hello's interests list)." These two statements are consistent but the relationship should be explicit: Display is special-cased because it's negotiated during handshake, not via `DeclareInterest`.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Confidence
> 
> High confidence on the Plan 9 heritage accuracy (I wrote some of this code). High confidence on the distributed model soundness — the architecture is clean. Medium confidence on the pane-fs projection story — it works in principle but the spec is deliberately silent on implementation details, so I'm extrapolating. The three error channels read well; my nitpicks are expository, not structural.
> 
> The biggest gap from a distributed perspective is item 5B (reconnection story) and 5A (cross-server pane identity). These don't need to be *solved* in this spec, but they should be acknowledged as open questions or explicitly deferred.</result>

> </task-notification>

> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Review architecture-next.md" completed</summary>
> <result>Good, I now have thorough knowledge of both specs and the actual BeOS implementation. Let me compile the review.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here's my review. Line numbers reference `architecture-next.md`.
> 
> ## 1. BeOS Heritage Fidelity
> 
> **Heritage annotation line 52-54 — accurate.** "BLooper = EAct actor. BHandler chain = sigma. The formalism proves why Be worked and reveals where it was unsound (dispatch re-entrancy, untyped messages, no coverage checking)." This is correct. BLooper::task_looper() (Looper.cpp:1162) is exactly this: read from port, drain queue, dispatch sequentially. The unsoundness call-outs are real — BHandler::MessageReceived took `void*`-equivalent `BMessage*` with `what` as the only discriminant, no coverage check.
> 
> **Heritage annotation line 113-115 — mostly accurate but imprecise.** "BeOS had exactly these three: SendReply(error) for protocol, QuitRequested() → bool for control, thread death for crash. Handler methods returned void." The claim checks out against the Haiku headers: `MessageReceived` returns `void`, `QuitRequested` returns `bool`, `SendReply` is on BMessage. The imprecision: `QuitRequested() -&gt; bool` is your control channel 2, but it's not quite the same as returning `Flow`. `QuitRequested` was a _query_ method (const-like semantics, the looper asked "may I quit?"), not a return value from dispatch. In BeOS, dispatch didn't return anything — the looper decided what to do next by checking `fTerminating`. pane's `Flow::Stop` returned from `receive()` is structurally different: it's the handler _telling_ the looper to stop, not the looper _asking_ if it can. Worth noting that the translation improves on Be's design — the QuitRequested/PostMessage(B_QUIT_REQUESTED) dance was one of the more confusing parts of the API.
> 
> **Missing heritage note: filter chain (line 598-621).** The filter chain section has no heritage annotation. BMessageFilter was a significant part of BHandler — every BHandler had its own filter list, and BLooper ran `_TopLevelFilter()` before `DispatchMessage()` (Looper.cpp:1250). The fact that pane's filters operate on `&amp;Message` (Clone-safe values only) while obligations bypass them is a genuine improvement over Be, where the filter saw the raw BMessage* including any embedded reply ports. A short annotation here would clarify why the bypass exists and that it's a correction of a Be-era smell.
> 
> **Missing heritage note: open_service blocking (line 631).** "Blocking — pre-looper, I2/I8 don't apply." This is where you're implicitly referencing the BApplication construction sequence: BApplication's constructor connected to app_server synchronously, ran the handshake, and blocked until registration completed, all before Run() started the message loop. Same structural guarantee. Worth a one-liner.
> 
> **Heritage annotation line 349-352 — accurate.** The Plan 9 analogy (Pane = bare process, PaneBuilder = namespace construction, run_with = exec) is sound. No BeOS annotation here, which is fine — Be didn't have this two-phase structure. BWindow's constructor did everything.
> 
> ## 2. API Ergonomics
> 
> **Line 98-100: "No Result in the handler API. Handler methods return Flow."** This is the biggest API change from the current spec. The current spec has `anyhow::Result&lt;Flow&gt;` everywhere. The new spec eliminates Result entirely from handler signatures. This is cleaner — it matches BeOS (void returns) and forces errors to be handled locally or via panic. Good call. But two concerns:
> 
> (a) **Line 309: `pane.run(self, f: impl FnMut(&amp;Messenger, Message) -&gt; Flow) -&gt; !`** — the closure returns `Flow`, not `Result&lt;Flow&gt;`. Combined with `-&gt; !` on `run_with`, this means there's no way for `main()` to use `?` for pre-run failures. The current spec returns `pane_app::Result` from `run_with`, making `main() -&gt; pane_app::Result` work with `?` on `App::connect`, `create_pane`, and `open_service`. The new spec's `-&gt; !` means either (1) App::connect and create_pane still return Result and you handle errors before run_with, or (2) they panic. The spec says `App::connect` "panics on failure" (line 536), which is a different philosophy from the current spec's `Result&lt;Self&gt;`. This needs to be made explicit: if the run methods diverge (`-&gt; !`), what is the error story for pre-run failures? If it's all panics, say so.
> 
> (b) **Line 459: `post_app_message` returns `Flow`.** This is odd. In what world does posting a message produce a lifecycle decision? In the current spec it returns `Result&lt;()&gt;`. The other Messenger methods (set_content, set_pulse_rate, set_pointer_policy) also return Flow (lines 457-458), but these are _actions_ the handler takes, not _decisions_ about continuing. set_content failing shouldn't mean "stop the looper." These should either return `()` (fire-and-forget, errors panic or are silent) or `Result&lt;()&gt;` (caller decides). Returning Flow from an action method confuses the two domains.
> 
> **Line 334: `open_service` returns `Option&lt;ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;&gt;` not `Result`.** The current spec returns `Result&lt;ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;&gt;` where `Err` includes declined-with-reason. The new spec's `Option` loses the reason. If a service is declined due to version mismatch vs. unknown service, the developer can't distinguish. The resolved question (line 955-956) says "None = service unavailable, Some = confirmed binding" — but "unavailable" has several causes. Consider at least logging the decline reason internally, or keeping Result.
> 
> **Lines 888-893: Closure form uses `Message::CloseRequested`.** But `Message` is defined as the trait (line 174), not as an enum. The closure example treats it as an enum with variants. There's a name collision: `Message` is both the trait bound (line 174) and apparently an enum in the closure API (line 888). The current spec defines `pub enum Message { Ready, CloseRequested, ... }` explicitly. The new spec defines `pub trait Message` but the closure example pattern-matches on it. This is a contradiction — you need to pick one, or use a different name for one of them.
> 
> ## 3. Completeness — What's Missing from the Current Spec
> 
> **PaneError / Result type.** The current spec (lines 764-803) defines `PaneError { Disconnected, Handler(anyhow::Error), Infra(anyhow::Error) }` and `pub type Result&lt;T = ()&gt;`. The new spec eliminates this entirely — no process-level error type, no `main() -&gt; Result`. Combined with `-&gt; !` on run methods, this means the entire error-propagation story for setup is gone. This is the most significant omission.
> 
> **Termination semantics.** The current spec (lines 1828-1851) has a detailed walkthrough of what happens on Flow::Stop and Err(e). The new spec's catch_unwind boundary (lines 652-667) covers the crash case, but the graceful shutdown sequence (stop dispatching -&gt; drop handler -&gt; notify server) is only partially covered in the destruction sequence (lines 714-720). Missing: the explicit statement that the looper stops dispatching further events from the batch before dropping the handler.
> 
> **Obligation handle lifetime convention.** The current spec (lines 1781-1793) discusses the convention that obligation handles should be consumed within the handler invocation that receives them, and that storing them in `self` defers resolution. The new spec doesn't mention this. It's an important developer-facing guidance note.
> 
> **send_and_wait details.** The current spec (lines 1809-1826) has thorough coverage of the synchronous blocking variant including thread-local enforcement, CURRENT_CONNECTION, and per-Connection token namespaces. The new spec (lines 723-727) mentions it briefly. The current spec's detail is needed for implementors.
> 
> **Application-defined protocols.** The current spec (lines 658-683) shows how developers define their own Protocol with custom Message enums for looper-local dispatch. The new spec doesn't cover this. It's an important developer experience feature — it's how apps do structured internal messaging.
> 
> **PointerPolicy.** The current spec (lines 632-654) defines Coalesce/FullHistory. Not in the new spec. Minor but it was a resolved design decision.
> 
> **Streaming (Queue pattern).** The current spec (lines 1163-1188) covers wire format, backpressure semantics, and the stream-close-before-suspension interaction. The new spec mentions Queue/Dequeue in the type vocabulary (lines 137-138) but doesn't cover the wire format or backpressure semantics.
> 
> **Session suspension/resumption.** The current spec (lines 1190-1224) has detailed coverage. The new spec doesn't mention it at all. Fair if this is intentionally deferred, but it should be stated.
> 
> **max_message_size negotiation.** Current spec (lines 1350-1358) covers the Hello/Welcome negotiation. New spec's handshake (lines 507-508) shows `max_message_size` in Hello but doesn't explain the negotiation (min of both sides).
> 
> **Functoriality principle.** The current spec (lines 78-96) and the Phase 1 Structural Invariants table (lines 2167-2197) are absent from the new spec. The new spec has no phases, which is fine, but the principle that you build the full data structures from day one and populate at N=1 is a hard-won design principle. Losing it means an implementor might take shortcuts the full architecture can't tolerate.
> 
> ## 4. Internal Consistency
> 
> **Message trait vs Message enum (lines 174 vs 888-893).** As noted above, `Message` is defined as a trait on line 174 but used as an enum in the closure example on line 888. These can't both be called `Message`.
> 
> **ServiceHandle methods return Flow (line 442).** `request_lock(&amp;self) -&gt; Flow` doesn't make sense — a service handle method requesting a lock is an action, not a lifecycle decision. In the current spec these return `Result&lt;()&gt;`. Same issue as `Messenger::set_content -&gt; Flow`.
> 
> **Lifecycle as Protocol (lines 902-906) vs Control bundling (lines 926-929).** Lines 902-906 define Lifecycle as `Protocol { SERVICE_ID, Message = LifecycleMessage }`. Lines 926-929 say "Lifecycle and Display are bundled in the Control protocol (wire service 0, implicit)." But if they're bundled in ControlMessage, why do they also have separate SERVICE_IDs? The current spec resolves this clearly: Lifecycle and Display are logical protocols (separate Handles&lt;P&gt; dispatch paths) but share the Control wire protocol. The new spec states this but the Protocol impls with separate SERVICE_IDs create the impression they're separate wire channels.
> 
> **EAct Progress theorem citation.** Line 68 cites "Thm 3.10" while the current spec (line 49) cites "Theorem 4.3". One of these is wrong. Looking at the EAct paper structure, §4 is the global progress section, so Theorem 4.3 from the current spec is likely correct. Line 68 should be verified.
> 
> **Handler trait: `quit_requested(&amp;self) -&gt; bool` (line 246).** This returns `bool`, not `Flow`. All other Handler methods return `Flow`. The inconsistency is inherited from BeOS (`QuitRequested()` returned `bool`), and it's the right call — `quit_requested` is a query, not a dispatch method. But it's worth a one-line comment explaining why it breaks the pattern, or an implementor will think it's a mistake.
> 
> **Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; blanket impl (line 254-264).** The blanket `impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; for H` means every Handler automatically handles Lifecycle. But Handler has `quit_requested(&amp;self) -&gt; bool`, which doesn't match the `-&gt; Flow` return type of `receive`. How does the blanket impl handle the `quit_requested` method? It's not a LifecycleMessage variant (can't be — returns bool, not Flow). This needs clarification: is `quit_requested` called outside the normal dispatch path, or is there a LifecycleMessage::QuitRequested variant that the blanket impl maps to quit_requested and converts the bool to Flow?
> 
> ## 5. Implementor Confusion Points
> 
> **Line 44: "Handles\&lt;P\&gt; impls + Dispatch\&lt;H\&gt; entries".** The EAct mapping table uses `\&lt;P\&gt;` and `\&lt;H\&gt;` — these backslash-escaped angle brackets look like they're meant for Markdown rendering, but they appear inconsistently. Some places use plain `&lt;P&gt;` (line 215), others use `\&lt;P\&gt;` (line 44). Not a big deal for humans but it'll render inconsistently.
> 
> **"Two dispatch surfaces" (lines 224-230).** This is the first mention of value dispatch vs obligation dispatch — a critical concept. But the two surfaces aren't clearly connected to the filter chain bypass until line 611. An implementor reading top-down won't understand why there are two surfaces until 400 lines later. Consider a forward reference.
> 
> **catch_unwind boundary (lines 652-667) uses `exit(0)` and `exit(101)`.** These are process exits, not returns. But the new spec's `Pane::run_with` signature is `-&gt; !`, which means it never returns. Is this a `std::process::exit()` call? If so, that's a hard exit — Drop impls on stack frames _above_ the looper won't fire. The code shows `drop(handler)` before `exit()`, which handles the handler's obligations, but anything in `main()` above `run_with` (like file handles, network connections) won't be cleaned up. The current spec's `-&gt; Result` approach is safer because it returns normally, letting the caller's stack unwind. This is a meaningful tradeoff that should be discussed.
> 
> **Line 536: `App::connect` panics on failure.** Combined with `-&gt; !` on run methods, the error model is: pre-run failures panic, handler errors panic, the only structured exit is Flow::Stop. This is more opinionated than the current spec. For a library (which pane-app is), panicking on connection failure is unusual — it prevents the caller from doing graceful degradation. BeOS's BApplication constructor _did_ panic on failure (well, it called `debugger()`), so there's heritage precedent, but it was widely regarded as a wart.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary
> 
> The new spec is tighter and better grounded in formal foundations (the CLL/EAct layering is clearer). The three error channels are the cleanest innovation — that framing genuinely clarifies what was implicit in BeOS. The heritage annotations are accurate where they exist.
> 
> The main risks are:
> 
> 1. **Message name collision** — trait and enum can't share the name
> 2. **Error model gap** — the -&gt; ! / panic-on-connect philosophy needs to be stated and defended, or the Result-based error model from the current spec should be preserved
> 3. **Flow returned from action methods** — set_content, post_app_message, request_lock shouldn't return Flow
> 4. **Missing sections** — application-defined protocols, obligation handle lifetime guidance, streaming/backpressure, send_and_wait details, PaneError/Result type, functoriality principle
> 5. **EAct theorem number** — verify 3.10 vs 4.3
> 
> The design decisions are sound. The new spec needs about 20% more material to be implementation-ready, mostly recovered from sections of the current spec that were cut during the rewrite.</result>

> <result>The review is above in full. The two moderate issues are:
> 
> 1. **Destruction invariant gap (lines 714-721):** on_failed callbacks during `dispatch.fail_connection()` execute with `&amp;mut H` before handler drop. If an on_failed callback calls `send_request`, it installs a new Dispatch entry that will be silently cleared by handler drop without its callbacks ever firing. Either prohibit send_request during destruction (add an invariant), or document that entries created during destruction are abandoned per the "Abandoned" lifecycle state (line 387).
> 
> 2. **E-Reset misdescription (line 47):** "Handler returns Flow::Stop (back to idle)" — E-Reset fires on any handler return, not just Flow::Stop. Every handler invocation that completes without panic is E-Reset. The parenthetical makes it sound like E-Reset is specifically the stop path.
> 
> The §4.3 citation on line 70 should be corrected to §4.2.2 — that's where the event-driven inter-session deadlock argument lives (lines 2928-2931 of the paper). Everything else in the EAct mapping table, CLL correspondence, error channels, invariants, and Message bounds checks out.</result>

> 
> **First-pass fixes: verification**
> 
> 1. **Cross-connection ordering** (lines 608-615). Fixed. The language is now precise: within-connection FIFO, cross-connection implementation-defined within a batch, send_request callbacks for cross-connection causality. This is correct and matches how 9P connections compose (each connection is an independent reliable stream; inter-connection ordering is the application's problem). No issue.
> 
> 2. **/dev/snarf replaced.** The clipboard protocol now uses the Handles\&lt;Clipboard\&gt; mechanism with obligation handles (ClipboardWriteLock, commit, request_lock). The Plan 9 /dev/snarf file interface survives only in the pane-fs layer, which is correct — FUSE synthesizes the file; the kit API is channel-based. Fixed properly per the Phase 3 decision (async channel, synchronous only in pane-fs).
> 
> 3. **Heritage notes.** I see Plan 9 comments on wire framing (lines 482-484), PeerAuth/factotum (lines 521-527), filter chain BeOS note (lines 626-628), open_service / fid semantics (lines 421-425), namespace analogy on PaneBuilder (lines 355-357). These are accurate and terse. No inflation.

> 4. **Open questions section** (lines 1083-1098). Present with three specific questions. Covers pane identity, reconnection, same-server routing. Good.
> 
> 5. **Session suspension** (lines 1054-1069). Present with explicit acknowledgment that obligations do not survive suspension, tokens expire server-side, multi-server is per-Connection independent. The aan(8) analogy is stated with the correct caveat — pane's suspension is explicit, not transparent.
> 
> 6. **Messenger fid note** (lines 453-456). Refined: "like a fid — resolution happens once at open time; the result is a direct binding, not a name." Accurate characterization.
> 
> All six items addressed. No regressions from the fixes.
> 
> ---
> 
> **New issues from the fixes: none found.** The open questions section doesn't introduce contradictions. The session suspension section is consistent with the obligation handle discipline.
>

> 
> **Completeness for an implementor — remaining gaps**
> 
> Three things an implementor would need that aren't here:
> 
> **A. msize negotiation semantics incomplete (minor).** Lines 1073-1080 say Hello/Welcome negotiates max_message_size and the effective limit is the minimum. Good. But it doesn't say what happens when a *service-level* message exceeds the negotiated msize mid-session. The framing section says "server rejects frames exceeding this" but doesn't specify the error: is it a connection-level tear-down, a per-service error, or a silent discard? 9P's answer was clear: Rflush or Rerror, connection stays up. This matters for implementors because a buggy service sending an oversized message shouldn't kill the entire connection if other services share it.
> 
> **B. DeclareInterest version negotiation underspecified (minor).** Lines 502-509 show `expected_version: u32` and `version: u32` in the response. But there's no statement about what happens on version mismatch. Does the server downgrade? Does it decline? Is InterestDeclined the only outcome for a version the server can't satisfy? One sentence would close this.
> 
> **C. Open Question 1 (Pane identity) has downstream consequences not acknowledged.** Lines 1085-1087 correctly flag who assigns pane Id. But this question also affects: ServiceHandle binding (line 429 has `connection_id` — what if the same pane talks to two servers?), Dispatch keying (line 380 uses `(ConnectionId, Token)`), and PaneExited broadcast (which pane Id does the server use?). Flagging the question is good; noting which types are blocked on the answer would help implementors know where to put placeholders.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Plan 9 fidelity**
> 
> No fidelity concerns. The analogies drawn are accurate:
> 
> - ServiceId UUID / qid.path (line 207): correct parallel — stable, machine-comparable identity.
> - open_service / fid binding at walk time (lines 421-425): correct — mount table changes affect new walks, not existing fids.
> - PeerAuth / factotum separation (lines 521-527): correctly stated that pane goes further than factotum (application doesn't see auth conversation at all).
> - Session suspension / aan(8) (lines 1066-1069): correct with the important caveat about explicit vs transparent.
> - Ehangup per-connection (lines 605-606): correct.
> 
> The one analogy I'd refine slightly is line 355-357: "Pane = bare process after rfork. PaneBuilder = namespace construction (bind/mount). run_with = exec." In Plan 9, rfork creates the process *and* its initial namespace simultaneously (RFNAMEG flag controls whether namespace is shared or copied). The Pane/PaneBuilder split more closely resembles rfork(RFNAMEG) (new namespace) followed by a sequence of bind/mount calls, then exec. The current comment elides the RFNAMEG step. This is cosmetic — an HTML comment, not user-facing — but if someone reads it who knows Plan 9, they'll notice.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Bottom line:** First-pass issues are resolved. Document is implementor-ready for Phase 1 (connection, handshake, looper, lifecycle, display). Gaps A and B are each one sentence. Gap C is bookkeeping for when Open Question 1 is resolved. The Plan 9 heritage notes are accurate and proportionate.</result>

> 
> ## Second-Pass Review: architecture-next.md
> 
> **Overall verdict:** Conditionally sound. The first-pass moderate issues are addressed. The new sections introduce one moderate issue (filter chain object safety) and two minor issues.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### First-Pass Fix Verification
> 
> **1. E-Reset now covers both Flow variants.** FIXED. Line 47: `E-Reset | Handler returns Flow (back to idle — both Continue and Stop)`. Line 96: `Flow::Stop / Continue`. The prior misdescription that E-Reset only applied to Flow::Stop is gone.
> 
> **2. Section citation corrected from §4.3 to §4.2.2.** FIXED. Line 73: `§4.2.2, Progress`. Correct per EAct paper (Progress for event-driven actors, lines 2928-2931).
> 
> **3. Destruction invariant gap documented.** FIXED. Lines 757-765: explicit prohibition — on_failed callbacks "must NOT call send_request (new entries created during destruction are abandoned per the 'Abandoned' lifecycle — cleared without callbacks when the handler drops in step 2)." This is the right approach: document the constraint + define the behavior if violated (abandoned, not silent). The "Abandoned" lifecycle (line 391) is the backstop. Convention, not static enforcement, but the failure mode is safe (entries dropped, no callbacks, no unsoundness).
> 
> **4. Token named in HashMap.** FIXED. Line 380: `HashMap&lt;(ConnectionId, Token), Entry&gt;`.
> 
> **5. Display/Control bundling explicit.** FIXED. Lines 974-978: "Lifecycle and Display are bundled in the Control protocol (wire service 0, implicit — declared in the handshake's interests list, not via DeclareInterest). Both have Protocol impls with ServiceIds for type-system dispatch, but they share the Control wire channel." This resolves the prior confusion about whether I12 applies to Display.
> 
> All five first-pass issues addressed.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### New Section Analysis
> 
> #### Application-Defined Protocols (lines 982-1014)
> 
> Sound. The key claim is "Application protocol messages are local to the looper, never cross the wire" (line 1011). This means they bypass pane-session entirely — no Chan&lt;S, T&gt;, no serialization roundtrip, no duality required. They're intra-process typed message dispatch using the same Handles&lt;P&gt; trait machinery. The Protocol trait still requires `type Message: Message` (which has Serialize + DeserializeOwned bounds, line 176), so these *could* cross the wire if needed later without protocol changes. No formal issue.
> 
> #### Obligation Handle Lifetime (lines 1018-1032)
> 
> **Sound, honestly documented.** The spec acknowledges this is convention, not enforcement. Storing a ReplyPort in `self` is type-safe — Drop compensation fires at handler destruction. The liveness degradation (requester blocks until handler destruction) is real but correctly characterized as a liveness issue, not a safety issue. This matches the affine gap analysis: Rust's affine types give safety-via-Drop, not liveness-via-forced-consumption.
> 
> The spec notes ReplyPort is `Send` for worker thread use. This is fine — the obligation handle's invariant is single-use (move semantics), not single-thread.
> 
> #### Streaming and Backpressure (lines 1036-1052)
> 
> **Minor issue (M1): "Streams must be closed before session suspension" has no enforcement mechanism.** Line 1049: "A resumed session with a desynchronized stream is a protocol error." But who checks? If suspension is server-initiated (line 1057), the server may suspend while a Queue is mid-stream. The spec needs to say either: (a) server waits for stream close before suspending, (b) suspension forcibly closes open streams (which end the stream fires), or (c) the client is responsible for closing streams when it receives a suspend signal. Without this, the composition of streaming + suspension is underspecified.
> 
> The backpressure design (push() returns Err(Backpressure), doesn't block) is correct for I2.
> 
> #### Session Suspension and Resumption (lines 1054-1069)
> 
> **Sound within stated scope.** "Stateful obligations do NOT survive suspension — they fail via Drop compensation" (line 1059) is the correct call. This avoids the reconnect unsoundness I flagged in the prior protocol audit (project_protocol_audit_2026_03_31.md) — obligations are session-scoped, not connection-scoped. Re-declaration of interests on resume (line 1061) means the resumed session is a fresh negotiation, which avoids protocol version skew.
> 
> The per-Connection independence (line 1063) is correct for multi-server — one server suspending doesn't affect others. No formal issue.
> 
> #### Maximum Message Size (lines 1074-1081)
> 
> Sound. min(client, server) is the standard negotiation pattern. The length field excluding itself is a clarity note that prevents off-by-four bugs.
> 
> #### Open Questions (lines 1083-1098)
> 
> Question 2 (reconnection) is correctly deferred — the current model (PaneBuilder consumed, no mid-session open_service) is structurally sound because it means service bindings are immutable after run_with. If mid-session reconnection were added, it would need to inject new Dispatch entries into a running looper, which would be a significant complexity increase.
> 
> Question 3 (same-server service routing) is architecturally interesting but has no formal implications either way.
> 
> #### Functoriality (lines 1101-1117)
> 
> Design principle, not formal content. The BeOS lesson is apt.
> 
> #### send_and_wait (lines 1119-1130)
> 
> **Minor issue (M2): "Same-Connection cycles... remain a runtime hazard, documented as a mutual-deadlock risk."** This is honest documentation, but the spec should state whether the framework detects this. A sends send_and_wait to B through server S, B sends send_and_wait to A through server S: classic deadlock. The single-thread model (I6) means the blocked thread can't service the incoming request. Detection is possible (check if the reply token maps to an in-flight send_and_wait on the same connection), but not mentioned. If detection isn't planned, the spec should say "detected: no" so implementors know.
> 
> This is distinct from the I8 check (looper thread-local panic), which only catches the trivial case of calling send_and_wait from within a handler on the same pane.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Filter Chain: &amp;dyn Message / Box&lt;dyn Message&gt; / Clone
> 
> This is the question you specifically flagged. Let me be precise.
> 
> **The `Message` trait as defined (line 175-176) is NOT object-safe.**
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Message:
>     Serialize + DeserializeOwned + Clone + Send + 'static {}
> ```
> 
> `Clone` requires `Self: Sized` (its signature is `fn clone(&amp;self) -&gt; Self`). A trait with a `Clone` supertrait is not object-safe. Therefore:
> 
> - `&amp;dyn Message` is **ill-formed** — you cannot create a trait object from `Message`.
> - `Box&lt;dyn Message&gt;` is **ill-formed** — same reason.
> - `fn filter(&amp;mut self, msg: &amp;dyn Message)` will not compile.
> - `FilterAction::Transform(Box&lt;dyn Message&gt;)` will not compile.
> 
> **This is a compile error, not a subtle soundness issue.** The filter chain as currently specified cannot exist in Rust.
> 
> **Fix options:**
> 
> 1. **Clone-boxing pattern.** Define a separate object-safe trait (`DynMessage`) with a `fn clone_boxed(&amp;self) -&gt; Box&lt;dyn DynMessage&gt;` method. Blanket-impl `DynMessage` for all `T: Message`. The filter chain operates on `&amp;dyn DynMessage` / `Box&lt;dyn DynMessage&gt;`. This is the standard Rust pattern (cf. `dyn Error`). Downside: filters can't downcast to concrete types without `Any`.
> 
> 2. **Typed filters.** Make filters generic: `trait MessageFilter&lt;P: Protocol&gt;` with `fn filter(&amp;mut self, msg: &amp;P::Message) -&gt; FilterAction&lt;P::Message&gt;`. Filters are per-protocol, registered at open_service time (which the spec already suggests: "Per-service filter hooks," line 648). This preserves concrete types and avoids object-safety entirely. Downside: no cross-protocol filters.
> 
> 3. **Add `Any` to the filter surface.** The filter receives `&amp;dyn Any` and downcasts. Ugly but functional. DeserializeOwned is also not object-safe, so this compounds.
> 
> Option 2 (typed filters) aligns best with the per-service registration the spec already describes. The base filter chain on line 733 ("Base filter chain sees Message variants") would need to be protocol-typed too.
> 
> **Severity: moderate.** This blocks implementation of the filter chain. The rest of the spec is unaffected.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Summary of Issues
> 
> | # | Severity | Section | Issue |
> |---|----------|---------|-------|
> | M1 | Minor | Streaming | Stream close before suspension has no enforcement mechanism — who initiates? |
> | M2 | Minor | send_and_wait | Same-Connection cycle deadlock: no detection specified |
> | M3 | **Moderate** | Filter Chain | `Message` trait is not object-safe (`Clone` supertrait). `&amp;dyn Message` and `Box&lt;dyn Message&gt;` won't compile. |
> 
> No EAct/CLL claims in the new material that are incorrect. The new sections don't make novel formal claims — they extend the existing framework operationally.
> 
> **Implementation-readiness:** The spec is implementation-ready for everything except the filter chain, which needs a resolution to M3 before coding. The rest (obligation lifetime, streaming, suspension, app-defined protocols, send_and_wait) can proceed as written.
> 
> Let me update my memory with this review.</result>
