> ## Second-Pass Review: architecture-next.md
> 
> ### Fix Verification
> 
> All 10 first-pass items confirmed addressed:
> 
> 1. **E-Reset** — Fixed at line 47. Correct.
> 2. **Section ref** — "§4.2.2, Progress" at line 73. Correct.
> 3. **Message name collision** — Closure form uses `LifecycleMessage` (line 314, 936). Blanket impl dispatches `LifecycleMessage` (line 260). Clean.
> 4. **Action methods return ()** — `set_content`, `post_app_message`, `request_lock` all show no return type. Correct.
> 5. **Destruction invariant** — Lines 760-763 document the constraint and explain why (abandoned lifecycle). Correct.
> 6. **Pre-run error story** — Lines 558-567 with heritage note. Correct, and the Haiku source at Application.cpp:539 confirms the `exit(0)` behavior.
> 7. **/proc/N/status** — Line 58. Correct Plan 9 reference.
> 8. **New sections** — Application-defined protocols (line 982), obligation handle lifetime (line 1018), streaming/backpressure (line 1034), session suspension (line 1055), max_message_size (line 1073), open questions (line 1083), functoriality (line 1104), send_and_wait details (line 1119). All present.
> 9. **Heritage notes** — Filter chain (line 628), open_service blocking (line 660), wire framing (line 483), PeerAuth (line 521). All present.
> 10. **Minor fixes** — quit_requested (line 249), Messenger fid note (line 455), exit(0) (line 708-713), Token named (line 803), Display/Control bundling (line 974-977). All present.
> 
> ### New Issues
> 
> **1. Heritage error: "BHandler::QuitRequested()" — should be BLooper** (line 249)
> 
> The comment says `BHandler::QuitRequested() -&gt; bool`. In Haiku (and original BeOS), `QuitRequested()` is on `BLooper`, not `BHandler`. Verified: `headers/os/app/Handler.h` has no `QuitRequested`; `headers/os/app/Looper.h:74` declares `virtual bool QuitRequested()`. Minor but an implementor checking the reference will be confused.
> 
> **2. `TimerToken` is an orphan** (line 409)
> 
> Listed in the obligation handles table but connected to no API. `set_pulse_rate` returns `()`. Either:
> - `set_pulse_rate` should return `TimerToken` (drop cancels the pulse), or
> - `TimerToken` is for a future general-purpose timer API (e.g. `set_timer`) not yet described, or
> - It shouldn't be in the table yet.
> 
> The current state is a spec gap — an implementor would ask "where does this come from?"
> 
> **3. `AppPayload` trait undefined** (line 465)
> 
> `post_app_message&lt;T: AppPayload&gt;` uses a bound that's never defined. What are the bounds? Presumably `Message`-like minus the Clone requirement? Or identical to Message? If it's just `Message`, say so. If it's different, define it.
> 
> **4. `PropertyInfo` undefined** (line 251)
> 
> `supported_properties` returns `&amp;[PropertyInfo]` but `PropertyInfo` is never defined. This is the scripting/automation entry point — one of the six core BeOS principles. The spec currently has exactly one line about it with an undefined type. An implementor has nothing to work with here.
> 
> For heritage context: BeOS had `property_info` structs with name, commands (GET/SET/COUNT/CREATE/DELETE), specifiers (DIRECT/INDEX/REVERSE_INDEX/RANGE/NAME), and usage strings. `GetSupportedSuites` returned BMessage with property_info arrays. The scripting protocol was substantial — it needs at minimum a forward reference to where it will be specified, or a sketch of what `PropertyInfo` contains.
> 
> **5. `request_received` obligation callback is commented out** (lines 254-256)
> 
> ```rust
> // fn request_received(&amp;mut self, proxy: &amp;Messenger, service: ServiceId,
> //     msg: Box&lt;dyn Any + Send&gt;, reply: ReplyPort) -&gt; Flow;
> ```
> 
> This is the only place showing how a pane *receives* a request from another pane (as opposed to *sending* one via `send_request`). It's in a comment. Is this part of the spec or aspirational? The handler-as-server story depends on it. An implementor needs to know whether this ships in v1.
> 
> **6. `ControlMessage` never defined** (line 492)
> 
> "ControlMessage — lifecycle, display, connection-management variants" but never shown. The spec defines `LifecycleMessage` and `DisplayMessage` as separate Protocol Message types, then says they're bundled into `ControlMessage` on the wire. How does demultiplexing work at the framing layer? The service byte is 0 for both — what discriminates Lifecycle from Display payloads inside wire service 0?
> 
> **7. Destruction sequence trigger is under-specified** (line 757)
> 
> "Handler returns Flow::Stop or panic caught" — these are the two triggers shown. But `disconnected()` also returns Flow, and its default is `Flow::Stop`. Is the destruction sequence for "primary connection lost" the same sequence? ExitReason::Disconnected exists but the destruction sequence description doesn't mention it as a trigger. From the catch_unwind code block (line 696), `Flow::Stop` from any handler callback triggers exit — so yes, `disconnected` returning `Stop` would trigger it. But this should be explicit since it's the same sequence through a different path.
> 
> **8. `Handles&lt;Display&gt;` bound placement inconsistency** (lines 320, 343-344)
> 
> On `Pane::run_with_display` (line 320): `H: Handler + Handles&lt;Display&gt;` — both bounds on the method.
> On `PaneBuilder::run_with_display` (line 343): `where H: Handles&lt;Display&gt;` — only Display, because Handler is already on the impl block.
> 
> This is correct Rust, but an implementor might wonder: if Display is bundled into Control and declared in the handshake, why does `run_with_display` not also require `open_service::&lt;Display&gt;()` first? The spec says Display is "declared in the handshake's interests list, not via DeclareInterest" (line 975-976) — meaning the `run_with_display` terminal method must internally handle Display registration as part of the handshake. This internal mechanism is never described. Worth a one-line note.
> 
> ### Missing Heritage Notes
> 
> **A. BLooper lock** — The spec doesn't mention that BeOS required Lock()/Unlock() to safely access a BLooper from another thread. pane's single-thread dispatch (I6) + Messenger eliminates this entirely. This is a genuine improvement worth a heritage note — implementors familiar with Be should know they don't need the locking dance.
> 
> **B. BHandler chain** — BeOS had `SetNextHandler()` creating a chain for message dispatch (handler couldn't process it, passed to next). pane replaces this with per-protocol dispatch (each `Handles&lt;P&gt;` is a separate entry point). The handler chain was a source of bugs in BeOS — order-dependent dispatch, unclear ownership. Worth noting as a deliberate departure.
> 
> **C. Detached handlers** — In BeOS, BHandlers could be moved between BLoopers. pane's model (one Handler struct, multiple `Handles&lt;P&gt;` impls) makes this impossible by construction. Another deliberate improvement.
> 
> ### Implementation Readiness Assessment
> 
> The spec is implementation-ready for the core path: App, Pane, PaneBuilder, Handler, Handles\&lt;P\&gt;, Flow, Messenger, Dispatch, the looper, and obligation handles. An engineer could write the looper dispatch loop from this document.
> 
> Not implementation-ready for:
> - **Scripting/properties** — `PropertyInfo` is a stub
> - **Wire demux of Control** — `ControlMessage` internal structure undefined
> - **Inbound request handling** — `request_received` is a comment
> - **Timer API** — `TimerToken` is dangling
> - **Application messages** — `AppPayload` bounds undefined
> 
> These are all deferrable — they're not on the critical path for a first running system. But they should either be sketched or explicitly marked as "deferred, see PLAN.md" so an implementor knows they're not missing something.</result>

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a7a872ad6dad268b3</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01BCs5iZvb8fT9pKFF5QA9Em</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/a7a872ad6dad268b3.output</output-file>

> <summary>Agent "Review three-crate implementation" completed</summary>

> 
> ---
> 
> ## Review: Three-Crate Architecture
> 
> I've read every `.rs` file across all three crates. Here are the findings, organized by your five review axes.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 1. Plan 9 Fidelity
> 
> **ServiceId: Missing the UUID.** The architecture doc specifies `ServiceId { uuid: Uuid, name: &amp;'static str }` with UUIDv5 derived from the name. The implementation has `ServiceId { name: &amp;'static str }` only. This is the exact violation the functoriality principle (architecture.md lines 79-97) warns against: shipping a Phase 1 type that omits structure needed later. When federation arrives and two servers might use different human names for the same protocol, there is no machine-comparable identity. Plan 9's qid.path exists precisely for this reason — the numeric identity survives across mount points where the directory entry name may differ.
> 
> **Severity: high.** This is the one structural deviation from the spec that will cause a breaking change later.
> 
> **Handshake maps well to Plan 9.** The Hello/Welcome exchange is structurally sound — it's the `Tversion`/`Rversion` of 9P. Client declares version + max message size + interests; server responds with bindings. The session-typed encoding (ClientHandshake/ServerHandshake as par duals) is cleaner than 9P's raw tag matching while preserving the same exchange structure.
> 
> **ServiceHandle fid semantics are correct.** The comment about "bound at open, mount table changes affect new walks only" is exactly right. The `#[must_use]` + Drop-revokes-interest pattern is a faithful translation of fid clunk. No issues here.
> 
> **Missing: no Tauth equivalent.** The handshake has no authentication step. In 9P, `Tauth` precedes `Tattach` — the client establishes an auth fid, runs the auth protocol through it, then attaches using the resulting ticket. The prior decision (memory: PeerIdentity goes in ClientHello) noted this should be Optional&lt;PeerIdentity&gt;. It's not present at all yet. Not blocking for local-only, but the slot needs to exist for the functoriality argument to hold.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 2. Distributed Soundness
> 
> **Transport::send_raw / recv_raw are synchronous and panic.** This is fine for the in-process MemoryTransport and correct under par's CLL model. But the architecture doc says handlers return `Result&lt;Flow&gt;`, while the implementation has `Handler` methods returning bare `Flow` and `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` returning bare `Flow`. The three-channel error model (per prior decisions) says errors split into protocol/control/crash channels. The current code has no error channel at all — every failure is a panic.
> 
> For multi-server: the Transport trait needs a fallible variant or the handshake must transition from session-typed (panic-on-disconnect) to active-phase (Result-based) explicitly. The `into_transport()` method on Chan exists, which is the right escape hatch — the handshake uses session types, then the active phase uses the raw transport with framing. But nothing after `into_transport()` exists yet.
> 
> **session_id is u8 in ServiceBinding.** This limits a single connection to 256 multiplexed services. 9P uses a 16-bit tag space (65536 concurrent requests) and a 32-bit fid space. u8 is probably fine for service discriminants (you won't have 256 protocols), but document that this is the service discriminant, not a request multiplexer. Request multiplexing (the equivalent of 9P tags) is handled by `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;`'s Token, which is u64 — that's correct.
> 
> **instance_id is String in Welcome.** The prior decision says hostname + generation counter. The current code just has `String`. No structure. When you need to parse it for the namespace (`/pane/remote/&lt;host&gt;/`), you'll be doing string splitting. Consider making this a struct now.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 3. ServiceId Box::leak
> 
> The `Box::leak` trick in `ServiceId::Deserialize` is **acceptable with caveats**.
> 
> The argument "ServiceIds are a small, fixed set" is correct for legitimate peers. But: any peer that sends you a ServiceId gets a `Box::leak` allocation. A malicious or buggy peer that sends N distinct ServiceId strings causes N permanent allocations. This is a bounded DoS vector.
> 
> Plan 9's approach to this was different: qid.path is a u64, no allocation at all. The server assigns it, the client stores it as a number. The human name lives in directory entries, not in the protocol identity.
> 
> **Recommendation**: When the UUID arrives (finding #1), use the UUID as the wire identity (16 bytes, no allocation) and the name as a local lookup. The `Box::leak` trick becomes unnecessary. Until then, the leak is tolerable for development but should not ship. At minimum, add a `MAX_SERVICE_ID_LENGTH` check before the leak and an interning table (HashSet&lt;&amp;'static str&gt;) so duplicates don't leak multiple times.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 4. Wire Protocol Readiness
> 
> **Postcard is a good choice.** Compact, no-std compatible, deterministic encoding. Correct for a protocol that needs to be fast over unix sockets and legible enough for debugging with a hexdump.
> 
> **No framing layer.** The `lib.rs` header mentions `[length][service][payload]` framing, but there's no implementation. `Transport::send_raw` takes `&amp;[u8]` with no length prefix — `MemoryTransport` uses mpsc channels where message boundaries are implicit. A real socket transport will need length-prefix framing. This is expected for the current state but worth noting: the framing design should be nailed down before the first real transport.
> 
> Specific concern: 9P uses a 4-byte length prefix at the front of every message, including the length field itself in the count. Simple, battle-tested. I'd recommend the same: `[u32 length][u8 service_discriminant][payload]` where length includes itself. The service discriminant routes to the right deserializer. This matches what the architecture doc describes.
> 
> **No version negotiation semantics.** Hello sends `version: u32` and ServiceInterest sends `expected_version: u32`, but there's no defined behavior for version mismatch. 9P's `Tversion`/`Rversion` has a clear rule: the server responds with the highest version it supports that's &lt;= what the client asked for. If it can't, it responds with "unknown". Define this now — it's a one-line semantic rule that prevents ambiguity in multi-server deployments where servers may run different versions.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 5. Namespace (pane-fs) Readiness
> 
> **Property&lt;S,A&gt; is the right primitive.** Lens-backed named properties that satisfy GetPut/PutGet/PutPut — this is exactly what you need for `/pane/&lt;n&gt;/attrs/&lt;name&gt;`. The optic laws guarantee that reading a property through pane-fs and reading it through the handler are consistent. This is one of the cleanest things in the codebase.
> 
> **But Property takes S by value.** `view(&amp;self, state: S) -&gt; A` and `set(&amp;self, state: S, value: A) -&gt; S` both consume state. For pane-fs reads (which happen on the looper thread with `&amp;self` access to the handler's state), you need `view(&amp;self, state: &amp;S) -&gt; A`. Otherwise every pane-fs read requires cloning the entire handler state. The fp-library Lens may force this signature, but it's worth checking whether there's a `view_ref` or whether you need a wrapper that takes `&amp;S` for the namespace projection path.
> 
> **No ctl file pattern.** Plan 9's per-device/per-process control interface is a writable `ctl` file that accepts text commands. `/pane/&lt;n&gt;/ctl` with commands like `close`, `focus`, `set-title &lt;string&gt;` is the natural write interface for pane-fs. The architecture doc mentions route_command in the Routing protocol, which serves this role. But there's no type in pane-proto for it yet. The Routing protocol's RouteQuery and route_command are the ctl file — make sure they're designed as line-oriented text commands, not structured RPC. Text commands compose across machine boundaries (you can `echo close &gt; /pane/42/ctl` from a remote shell); structured RPC requires protocol knowledge.
> 
> **No service map / mount table type.** The architecture references "service map" and describes how DeclareInterest resolves through it, but there's no representation of the mount table — the mapping from ServiceId to Connection that the server maintains and that pane-fs would expose at something like `/pane/services/`. This is the Plan 9 mount table (`/dev/mtpt` or the per-process mount state accessed via `/proc/&lt;pid&gt;/ns`). Not urgent, but it's the piece that connects handshake bindings to the namespace.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Summary of Findings (by priority)
> 
> | # | Issue | Severity | Action |
> |---|-------|----------|--------|
> | 1 | ServiceId missing UUID (functoriality violation) | High | Add UUID field, add `service_id!` proc-macro or `lazy_static` |
> | 2 | Handler/Handles return bare Flow, not Result&lt;Flow&gt; | High | Align with architecture doc's `anyhow::Result&lt;Flow&gt;` |
> | 3 | Handler methods missing `&amp;Messenger` parameter | High | Align with architecture doc signatures |
> | 4 | Box::leak unbounded on deserialization | Medium | Add length check + intern table; dissolves when UUID arrives |
> | 5 | No framing layer for real transports | Medium | Define `[u32 len][u8 svc][payload]` before first socket transport |
> | 6 | instance_id is unstructured String | Medium | Make struct `{ hostname: String, generation: u64 }` |
> | 7 | No auth slot in Hello | Medium | Add `auth: Option&lt;AuthToken&gt;` placeholder |
> | 8 | Property takes S by value (clone cost for pane-fs reads) | Medium | Check fp-library for ref-based view, or add wrapper |
> | 9 | No version negotiation semantics defined | Low | Document "server picks min(client, server)" rule |
> | 10 | No ctl file / mount table types in pane-proto | Low | Design these when Routing protocol is implemented |
> 
> Findings 1-3 are spec drift — the code doesn't match the architecture doc. Findings 4-8 are distributed-readiness gaps. Findings 9-10 are future work that should be tracked but doesn't block current development.</result>

> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-05T04:57:01.561Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>aa21ca2a4a52c7d32</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01XQoXAkucxitTr5j9M2tAQi</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/aa21ca2a4a52c7d32.output</output-file>

> <summary>Agent "Review three-crate implementation" completed</summary>
> <result>Now let me verify one key thing — the spec says `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` takes `proxy: &amp;Messenger` but the implementation doesn't. Let me also check whether `Handler` methods take `proxy` in the spec vs implementation.
> 
> I've now read every file. Here's my review.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Review: pane Three-Crate Architecture
> 
> ### 1. BeOS Fidelity
> 
> **What maps well:**
> 
> The crate split is structurally sound and mirrors Be's organizational logic, even if the exact mapping isn't one-to-one with Be's kit structure. Be had the App Kit (BLooper, BHandler, BMessage, BMessageFilter, BMessenger) as a cohesive layer. pane-proto captures the *type contract* portion of this — the parts that had no runtime dependency. pane-app captures the *runtime* portion. pane-session is genuinely new territory; Be had no session-typed IPC layer because app_server communication was bespoke link-level messaging over ports, not a general protocol framework.
> 
> The `Handler` trait with named lifecycle methods is a clean translation of BHandler/BLooper. The key heritage decisions are correct:
> 
> - `close_requested` returns `Flow`, `quit_requested` returns `bool` — this mirrors BLooper's split between QuitRequested() (a query) and the actual quit pathway.
> - The blanket `impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; for H` is the right unification. In BeOS, lifecycle messages were just `what` codes dispatched through MessageReceived(). pane makes this explicit and typed, which is an improvement.
> - `MessageFilter&lt;M&gt;` with typed-per-protocol filters fixes a genuine Be wart: BMessageFilter saw raw BMessage* including embedded reply ports. The separation of obligations from filterable messages is noted in the doc comments and is correct.
> 
> **What's missing or divergent:**
> 
> The spec's `Handler` trait has methods the implementation lacks:
> 
> | Spec has | Implementation has |
> |---|---|
> | `ready(&amp;mut self, proxy: &amp;Messenger)` | `ready(&amp;mut self)` |
> | `pane_exited(&amp;mut self, proxy: &amp;Messenger, pane: Id, reason: ExitReason)` | absent |
> | `request_received(&amp;mut self, proxy: &amp;Messenger, ...)` | absent |
> | `supported_properties(&amp;self) -&gt; &amp;[PropertyInfo]` | absent |
> 
> The `proxy: &amp;Messenger` parameter is absent from *every* Handler and Handles&lt;P&gt; method in the implementation. This is the single biggest spec divergence — the spec is clear that the handler receives the Messenger from the looper at dispatch time. This matters because in BeOS, every MessageReceived() call had implicit access to the looper via `Looper()` on the BHandler. The Messenger is pane's equivalent of that ambient context, and without it, handlers have no way to send messages, set content, or manage timers during dispatch.
> 
> The `pane_exited` callback is Be's `B_SOME_APP_QUIT` mechanism — it's how the observer pattern (BHandler::StartWatching/SendNotices) maps to pane. Its absence means there's no way for one pane to react to another pane's exit. This isn't blocking for the skeleton, but it should be called out as a known omission.
> 
> `request_received` is the scripting/automation entry point — BeOS's `ResolveSpecifier` chain. The Property&lt;S,A&gt; optics machinery in pane-proto exists to support this, but there's no dispatch surface that connects properties to incoming requests. The gap is between the optic-backed state access (which exists) and the scripting protocol that reads/writes through those optics (which doesn't).
> 
> **Heritage notes:** The doc comments are generally good about citing Be concepts. I'd add one note to `ServiceId`: in BeOS, MIME signatures (`application/x-vnd.Be-TRAK`) served as ServiceId equivalents. The spec calls for UUIDs (UUIDv5 from reverse-DNS); the implementation uses bare `&amp;'static str`. That's a divergence worth tracking — see below.
> 
> ### 2. API Ergonomics
> 
> **Good:**
> 
> - The builder pattern (`Pane → setup::&lt;H&gt;() → PaneBuilder&lt;H&gt; → open_service → run_with`) is clear and type-safe. The phase separation prevents opening services after dispatch begins, which was a real hazard in BeOS (you could add handlers to a running looper, which introduced ordering bugs).
> - `#[must_use]` on Pane, PaneBuilder, ServiceHandle, TimerToken, Chan — correct application of the affine discipline.
> - `Flow::Continue` / `Flow::Stop` is simpler and more honest than returning void (BeOS) or Result (typical Rust). The three-error-channel model is well-motivated.
> - The blanket `impl&lt;T: Serialize + DeserializeOwned + Clone + Send + 'static&gt; Message for T` means developers never write `impl Message for X`. The trait is a genuine marker. Clean.
> 
> **Concerns:**
> 
> - **No Messenger in handler callbacks.** As noted above, a handler currently has no way to interact with the framework during dispatch. In BeOS, `BHandler::Looper()` was always available. Without `proxy: &amp;Messenger`, even a simple "update content in response to a message" is impossible. This needs to be the next thing fixed.
> 
> - **Property takes ownership of S.** `view(&amp;self, state: S)` and `set(&amp;self, state: S, value: A) -&gt; S` consume the state. For a lens backing a filesystem read, this means cloning the entire handler state for every property access. In BeOS, the scripting protocol operated on `&amp;self`/`&amp;mut self` references. The current signature will either force excessive cloning or require the state to be behind `Rc&lt;RefCell&lt;&gt;&gt;`, which defeats the point. The lens should operate on `&amp;S` (for view) and `&amp;mut S` (for set), not owned `S`. This is a real ergonomic problem if properties are accessed frequently through the namespace.
> 
> - **FilterAction&lt;M&gt; requires Clone on Transform.** The Message trait already requires Clone, so `Transform(M)` works. But `filter` takes `&amp;M` and returns `FilterAction&lt;M&gt;`, meaning the filter must clone the message to transform it. This is actually correct — it matches BeOS where filters could modify the message (the filter got a `BMessage*` and could mutate it in-place, but pane's functional style is better). Just noting it's a conscious cost.
> 
> ### 3. Structural Issues
> 
> **ServiceId: spec says UUID, implementation says bare string.**
> 
> The spec is explicit: `ServiceId { uuid: Uuid, name: &amp;'static str }` with UUIDv5 from reverse-DNS. The implementation is `ServiceId { name: &amp;'static str }` — no UUID. The Deserialize impl leaks memory (`Box::leak`) which the comment acknowledges is bounded, but this is a red flag for any context where ServiceIds arrive from untrusted sources. A malicious peer could force unbounded leaks by sending unique strings.
> 
> The spec's UUID approach avoids both problems: comparison is by UUID (fixed-size, no allocation), and the name is debugging metadata. I'd prioritize aligning with the spec here. The leak-on-deserialize is a time bomb.
> 
> **pane-app does not depend on pane-session.**
> 
> The Cargo.toml for pane-app says `# pane-session will be added when par-backed channels are built`. This means:
> - No connection type exists in pane-app
> - No wire framing
> - No looper integration with calloop
> - Dispatch&lt;H&gt; exists but can't actually fire — there's no transport to receive replies from
> 
> This is fine as a skeleton, but Dispatch&lt;H&gt; is tested with in-memory callbacks that bypass the wire entirely. The test shows the dispatch map works, but doesn't test the critical path: deserialized reply → type-erased Box&lt;dyn Any&gt; → downcast → callback. The `Box&lt;dyn Any + Send&gt;` in `fire_reply` is correct for the type-erased storage, but the downcast step (which will happen at the looper layer) is untested.
> 
> **Chan&lt;S,T&gt; panics on disconnect — spec says Result.**
> 
> The spec (line 162) says "Crash safety: all operations return `Result&lt;_, SessionError&gt;`, never panic on peer disconnect." But `Chan&lt;S,T&gt;::send` panics: `expect("session send: serialization failed")` and `Transport::send_raw` panics on broken pipe. The spec explicitly says pane-session fixes par's panic-on-disconnect. The implementation follows par's model instead.
> 
> This is a real structural issue. The whole crash-safety argument depends on Chan returning Result, not panicking. If Chan panics, the catch_unwind boundary at the looper becomes the crash safety mechanism, and the session type guarantees degrade. The spec's model is better — fix Chan.
> 
> **Handshake types live in pane-session, reference pane-proto.**
> 
> This is correct. The dependency direction is right: pane-session depends on pane-proto for ServiceId. Handshake types (Hello, Welcome, ServiceInterest, ServiceBinding) are wire-protocol concerns, not type contracts, so they belong in pane-session. Good.
> 
> **LifecycleMessage is in pane-proto, ControlMessage is spec-only.**
> 
> The spec describes a ControlMessage enum that wraps LifecycleMessage, DisplayMessage, and connection management messages. The implementation only has LifecycleMessage. This is fine for now — ControlMessage is a wire concern that belongs in pane-session when the wire protocol is built.
> 
> ### 4. Test Coverage
> 
> **What's tested well:**
> 
> - Handles&lt;P&gt; dispatch (basic receive, Flow::Stop, Message bounds)
> - Handler defaults and blanket Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; (exhaustive variant coverage)
> - MessageFilter (consume, pass, matches prefilter)
> - Property optic laws (GetPut, PutGet, read-only)
> - Chan&lt;S,T&gt; over MemoryTransport (send/recv, terminal ops, multi-step, branching via Result)
> - Handshake roundtrip
> - Dispatch&lt;H&gt; (insert/fire, consumption, cancel, fail_connection, clear, token uniqueness)
> - PaneBuilder (open_service, duplicate panic, compile-time Handles bound)
> 
> **What's missing:**
> 
> 1. **No test for Messenger.** It's a stub, but the AppPayload blanket impl should be tested.
> 
> 2. **No cross-crate integration test.** A test that creates a Handler, opens a service via PaneBuilder, receives a message through Handles&lt;P&gt;, and returns Flow — exercising the full type-level machinery across all three crates. The individual crate tests are good but don't prove composition.
> 
> 3. **No test for Filter + Dispatch interaction.** The spec says "Base filter chain sees Message variants. Service events dispatch through Handles&lt;P&gt;." Testing that a filter can Transform a message before it hits Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive would verify the pipeline.
> 
> 4. **No test for obligation handle Drop compensation.** ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt; Drop should send RevokeInterest. TimerToken Drop should cancel. These are stubs now, but the pattern should be tested even if the transport is mocked.
> 
> 5. **Property PutPut test is missing.** GetPut and PutGet are tested. PutPut (two writes collapse to the last) is the third lens law and isn't tested.
> 
> 6. **No negative test for Chan panic-on-disconnect.** If Chan is supposed to panic (current implementation) or return Result (spec), this behavior should be tested explicitly.
> 
> 7. **No test for MemoryTransport disconnection.** What happens when one side of the MemoryTransport is dropped and the other tries to send/recv? The mpsc channel will return Err, which becomes a panic. This should be a `#[should_panic]` test.
> 
> ### 5. Spec Alignment — Divergence Summary
> 
> | Spec | Implementation | Severity |
> |---|---|---|
> | Handler/Handles methods take `proxy: &amp;Messenger` | No proxy parameter | **High** — handlers can't interact with framework |
> | `ServiceId { uuid, name }` with UUIDv5 | `ServiceId { name }` bare string, leaks on deser | **Medium** — design principle violation (functoriality) |
> | Chan returns Result, never panics on disconnect | Chan panics via expect() | **High** — undermines crash safety argument |
> | Handler has `pane_exited`, `request_received`, `supported_properties` | Missing | **Low** — known future work |
> | `App::connect(signature)` as entry point | No App type | **Low** — pane-app is the app framework, App is next |
> | `service_id!()` proc macro for const construction | `ServiceId::new()` const fn | **Low** — macro is sugar |
> | `LifecycleMessage` includes `PaneExited(Id, ExitReason)` in blanket | Only Ready/CloseRequested/Disconnected/Pulse | **Low** — PaneExited variant needed when observer pattern added |
> | Property lens operates on references | Property consumes owned state | **Medium** — ergonomic problem for frequent access |
> 
> ### Overall Assessment
> 
> The skeleton is sound. The crate boundaries are correct. The type discipline (Message as marker trait, Handles&lt;P&gt; for uniform dispatch, Handler as sugar, Flow for control) is the right decomposition. The EAct mapping is genuine, not just terminology — Dispatch&lt;H&gt; actually implements the E-Suspend/E-React lifecycle with proper one-shot semantics.
> 
> The two high-severity items are:
> 1. Add `proxy: &amp;Messenger` to Handler and Handles&lt;P&gt; methods. Without this, the framework is a dispatch mechanism with no way to respond.
> 2. Fix Chan to return Result instead of panicking. The spec's crash safety argument depends on it.
> 
> The ServiceId UUID gap is worth fixing before any wire protocol work, because the wire format bakes in the identity representation.
> 
> From a Be perspective: this is tracking the right heritage. The Handler/Handles&lt;P&gt;/Flow structure is a genuine improvement over BHandler/BLooper's untyped MessageReceived + void return. The filter separation (obligations bypass filters) fixes a real BeOS bug. The builder pattern prevents the mid-run handler addition races that plagued Be apps. The parts that are missing (scripting, observer pattern, App) are correctly identified as future work, and the type contracts in pane-proto are shaped to receive them.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Re-review against current spec" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. All 27 tests pass. I've now read every source file and the complete architecture spec. Here's the re-review.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Re-Review: Three-Crate Implementation vs. Current Spec
> 
> ### 1. Actual divergences from the current spec
> 
> **A. `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` and `Handler` methods are missing `proxy: &amp;Messenger`**
> 
> This is the single most significant divergence. The spec says:
> 
> ```rust
> fn receive(&amp;mut self, proxy: &amp;Messenger, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
> fn ready(&amp;mut self, proxy: &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow;
> ```
> 
> The implementation has:
> 
> ```rust
> fn receive(&amp;mut self, msg: P::Message) -&gt; Flow;
> fn ready(&amp;mut self) -&gt; Flow;
> ```
> 
> This is the Messenger crate boundary issue you flagged, not accidental drift. I'll address it in section 2.
> 
> **B. `ServiceId` is `&amp;'static str`, not `{ uuid: Uuid, name: &amp;'static str }`**
> 
> The spec is explicit (line 185-201):
> 
> ```rust
> pub struct ServiceId {
>     pub uuid: Uuid,
>     pub name: &amp;'static str,
> }
> ```
> 
> With a `service_id!` proc-macro for compile-time UUIDv5 from the reverse-DNS name. The implementation has only a `&amp;'static str` wrapper. The spec's own design principles section (line 1180-1187) calls this out directly: "a type that omits structure creates patterns that can't cleanly accommodate the full design." The Deserialize impl already leaks strings to get `'static` — adding UUID doesn't change that cost, it just adds the machine-comparable identity the spec says is load-bearing.
> 
> **C. Handler is missing `pane_exited`, `supported_properties`, `request_received`**
> 
> The spec's Handler trait includes:
> 
> - `pane_exited(&amp;mut self, proxy: &amp;Messenger, pane: Id, reason: ExitReason) -&gt; Flow`
> - `supported_properties(&amp;self) -&gt; &amp;[PropertyInfo]`
> - `request_received(&amp;mut self, proxy: &amp;Messenger, service: ServiceId, msg: Box&lt;dyn Any + Send&gt;, reply: ReplyPort) -&gt; Flow`
> 
> The implementation has only the four lifecycle methods plus `quit_requested`. This is probably intentional staging (PropertyInfo and ReplyPort don't exist yet), but worth tracking as known work.
> 
> **D. `PaneBuilder` is missing `dispatch_table: Vec&lt;ServiceDispatchEntry&gt;`**
> 
> The spec shows `dispatch_table` and `registered_services` as separate fields. The implementation has only `registered_services: HashSet&lt;ServiceId&gt;`. The dispatch table is where fn-pointer capture happens at `open_service` time — it's the static part of sigma. This gap means the looper can't dispatch service messages yet. Known TODOs cover this.
> 
> **E. `Pane` is missing `run_with_display` and `PaneBuilder` is missing `run_with_display`**
> 
> The spec has both. Currently only `run_with` exists. Minor — Display protocol doesn't exist yet.
> 
> **F. `App` doesn't exist**
> 
> The spec shows `App::connect(signature)` and `app.create_pane(tag)`. There's no App type in the implementation. The Pane struct is constructed directly in tests. This is the entry point for the whole system — it needs to exist before any real integration work.
> 
> **G. `ControlMessage` enum doesn't exist**
> 
> The spec shows a `ControlMessage` enum that multiplexes lifecycle, display, and connection management on wire service 0. The handshake exists in pane-session, but the active-phase control protocol is absent.
> 
> **H. pane-session's Chan returns nothing on error — it panics**
> 
> The spec says (line 32-34): "pane-session reimplements par's type vocabulary for IPC (serialization, crash safety via Result not panic, transport abstraction)." But the implementation's Transport trait panics on disconnect (matching par's model, not the IPC adaptation). The spec literally says the difference between par and pane-session is "Result not panic." The Transport trait should return `Result&lt;_, SessionError&gt;`, with Chan propagating errors. This is a genuine design flaw — the spec is clear about the intent and the implementation contradicts it.
> 
> Wait — you said in your prompt that "the CURRENT spec says Chan panics on disconnect (par's CLL model)." Let me re-check. Line 161-163 of the spec: "Crash safety: all operations return `Result&lt;_, SessionError&gt;`, never panic on peer disconnect." But also the spec's resolved questions (line 1216-1217): "par is complete. pane-session reimplements the type vocabulary for IPC. No crate dependency (runtime models incompatible — par panics, pane returns Result)."
> 
> The spec says pane-session returns Result, not panic. The implementation panics. If you're telling me the intent is to panic, then the spec text at lines 161-163 and 1216-1217 needs updating. As written, the spec and the implementation disagree. This needs a decision: which one is wrong?
> 
> ### 2. The Messenger crate boundary tension
> 
> The spec shows `proxy: &amp;Messenger` on both `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` and all `Handler` methods. But:
> 
> - `Handler` is defined in `pane-proto` (no runtime, no IO)
> - `Messenger` is defined in `pane-app` (runtime layer)
> - `pane-proto` cannot depend on `pane-app`
> 
> Three options:
> 
> **Option A: Move the Messenger type declaration to pane-proto.** Define a trait or minimal struct in pane-proto that Messenger implements. Something like:
> 
> ```rust
> // pane-proto
> pub trait Proxy: Send + Clone + 'static {
>     fn set_content(&amp;self, data: &amp;[u8]);
>     fn set_pulse_rate(&amp;self, duration: Duration) -&gt; TimerToken;
> }
> 
> pub trait Handler&lt;P: Proxy&gt;: Send + 'static { ... }
> pub trait Handles&lt;Proto: Protocol, P: Proxy&gt; { ... }
> ```
> 
> This pollutes the type contracts with a generic parameter that threads through everything. BeOS didn't have this problem because BLooper/BHandler/BMessenger were all in the same kit (libbe.so). The separation into layers creates it.
> 
> **Option B: Handler doesn't take proxy. The looper provides it.** Keep Handler/Handles exactly as they are now — no proxy parameter. The looper holds both the Messenger and the Handler. After dispatch returns Flow, the handler has already done whatever it needed to (mutations to self). If the handler needs to send messages, it captures a reference or channel to the Messenger in its own state at construction time.
> 
> This is actually what BeOS did for BHandler — `MessageReceived` took `BMessage*` only, and the handler called `Looper()-&gt;PostMessage()` or used its own `BMessenger` that it acquired separately. The proxy parameter in the spec is sugar that BHandler didn't have.
> 
> **Option C: Messenger is a concrete type in pane-proto, but opaque.** Move `Messenger` to pane-proto as a struct wrapping a trait object:
> 
> ```rust
> // pane-proto
> pub struct Messenger(Box&lt;dyn ProxyInner&gt;);
> trait ProxyInner: Send { ... }
> ```
> 
> pane-app provides the real implementation. pane-proto defines the interface.
> 
> My recommendation: **Option B is cleanest for now.** It matches what you've already built. The handler struct owns whatever outbound channels it needs (it gets them during PaneBuilder setup). The looper calls `handler.receive(msg)` and checks the Flow. If you later want the convenience of `proxy: &amp;Messenger`, Option C works without changing the crate dependency direction. But don't block on it — Option B is correct and ships.
> 
> ### 3. The ServiceId UUID gap
> 
> The spec's functoriality principle explicitly says don't build a bare-string ServiceId that you have to upgrade later. The implementation has exactly the bare-string ServiceId the principle warns against. However, this is a self-contained change: add a `uuid: Uuid` field, write a `service_id!` proc-macro that computes UUIDv5 from the name, update the Serialize/Deserialize impls. No downstream API changes — users already write `ServiceId::new("com.pane.lifecycle")`, they'd write `service_id!("com.pane.lifecycle")` instead.
> 
> Is it top priority? It depends on what's next. If you're about to define wire-protocol matching (InterestAccepted carries `service_uuid: Uuid`), then yes — you need the UUID before the wire format crystallizes. If you're still working on the actor framework, it can wait one cycle. But not longer. The spec is right: bare strings shape ecosystems around strcmp.
> 
> ### 4. Property takes S by value
> 
> `view(&amp;self, state: S) -&gt; A` takes S by value. `set(&amp;self, state: S, value: A) -&gt; S` takes S by value and returns S.
> 
> This is driven by fp-library's profunctor encoding — `Lens::from_view_set` takes `Fn(S) -&gt; A` and `Fn((S, A)) -&gt; S`, which requires ownership. The tests clone the state before calling `view` to compare afterward.
> 
> Is this a real problem? It depends on the state type. For small structs (EditorState with a cursor and a buffer), cloning is fine. For large handler states with multiple Vec/String fields, cloning on every namespace read is wasteful. But:
> 
> 1. The namespace projection is read by pane-fs, which is an external process reading via the socket. It's already serializing. The clone cost is dominated by serialization.
> 2. If the state is behind an `Rc` or similar, cloning is cheap.
> 3. The by-value signature is the honest one for a pure lens. A `&amp;self` getter that returns a reference would require the lens to know about borrowing, which profunctor optics don't naturally do.
> 
> My read: this is **fine for now** and potentially fine forever. The alternative (mutable-reference lens, or a separate getter trait that returns `&amp;A`) is a different design with different tradeoffs. If profiling shows clone cost matters, you can add a `view_ref` method alongside view that takes `&amp;S -&gt; &amp;A` as a simpler getter, without changing the lens abstraction.
> 
> ### 5. Missing tests
> 
> The existing 27 tests cover the type contracts well. What's missing:
> 
> **Critical (blocks next work):**
> 
> - **No test for Chan error behavior.** Either Chan should return Result (spec says so) and tests should verify error propagation on disconnect, or the spec needs updating and you need tests that verify the panic-on-disconnect model works with catch_unwind at the looper boundary. Either way, the error model for session channels is untested.
> - **No test for the destruction sequence.** The spec defines a 4-step destruction sequence (fail_connection, clear, drop handler, notify server). Dispatch has the primitives (`fail_connection`, `clear`), but there's no test that runs them in order and verifies obligation compensation fires. This is the most safety-critical path in the system.
> 
> **Important (needed soon):**
> 
> - **No integration test for PaneBuilder -&gt; open_service -&gt; Handles&lt;P&gt; dispatch.** The builder test calls `open_service` but never actually dispatches a message through the registered service. The fn-pointer capture and dispatch path is unexercised.
> - **No test for ServiceId deduplication across connections.** The HashSet check is there, but the wire-level behavior (what happens when two services resolve to the same connection) is untested.
> - **No test for LifecycleMessage serialization roundtrip.** The Message blanket impl covers the bounds, but nobody tests that `LifecycleMessage::Ready` survives postcard encode/decode. This matters because the enum discriminant encoding is the wire format.
> 
> **Nice to have:**
> 
> - Property optic law tests for PutPut (only GetPut and PutGet are tested).
> - FilterAction::Transform path (only Consume and Pass are tested).
> - Dispatch entry with on_reply that returns Flow::Stop (verifying the looper would honor it).
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Summary of real issues, ranked
> 
> 1. **Chan error model contradiction** — spec says Result, code panics. Needs a decision.
> 2. **Messenger/proxy parameter gap** — Option B (current code) is correct; spec needs updating OR Option C if you want the ergonomic proxy.
> 3. **ServiceId missing UUID** — spec's functoriality principle says do it now.
> 4. **Handler missing 3 methods** — `pane_exited`, `supported_properties`, `request_received`. Staged, but track it.
> 5. **No App type** — entry point for the whole system.
> 6. **Destruction sequence untested** — the most safety-critical path has no coverage.
> 7. **Property by-value** — fine, not a problem.</result>

### `2026-04-05T05:35:03.779Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>ae23a19ed9349a581</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_0114zaxqHxUCJiKsPEuFPM7A</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/ae23a19ed9349a581.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Review par bridge design" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. I have a clear picture. Here's the review.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Plan 9 analogy: closer to devmnt than exportfs
> 
> The bridge thread is **not** exportfs. exportfs(4) is a *server* — it receives 9P requests, walks a real namespace, and synthesizes replies. The bridge thread doesn't walk anything or serve a namespace. It's closer to **devmnt** (`/sys/src/9/port/devmnt.c`), the kernel device that sits between a process doing read/write on a mounted file descriptor and the 9P wire.
> 
> devmnt's job:
> - Process calls `read()` on a mounted fd → devmnt serializes an Tread → writes to the connection → reads the Rread → returns bytes to the process
> - One kernel proc per outstanding 9P transaction (Plan 9 used reader/writer splitting with `mntqlock`, but conceptually: translate between internal interface and wire format)
> 
> Your bridge does exactly this. The handler calls `send(Hello)` through par's in-process oneshot. The bridge thread catches that, serializes with postcard, writes to Transport, reads the response, deserializes, feeds it back into par's oneshot for the handler's `recv()`. That's devmnt to a T.
> 
> The key structural parallel: **devmnt was also protocol-specific**. It knew the 9P message sequence — it packaged Tag/Fid, matched replies to outstanding requests, handled the flush protocol. It wasn't a generic byte pump.
> 
> ## 2. Protocol-specific bridges: right for now, wrong at scale
> 
> For handshake, a one-off `bridge_client_handshake` function that hard-codes the Hello/Welcome sequence is perfectly correct. The handshake is a short fixed exchange — two messages, done.
> 
> For the active phase with N service protocols, writing a separate bridge function per protocol will not scale. The active phase is an open-ended sequence of messages multiplexed over one connection, not a fixed exchange. You'll have:
> - Multiple concurrent service sessions, each with their own par session type
> - Messages arriving in arbitrary order
> - Need to demux by session_id from ServiceBinding
> 
> The real question is what the active-phase bridge looks like. Two options:
> 
> **Option A: one bridge function per service protocol.** Each service gets its own bridge thread that owns a sub-transport (a demuxed channel). A dispatch thread reads from the wire, demuxes by session_id, and routes to the appropriate service bridge. This is analogous to how Plan 9 handled multiple mount points — each devmnt was independent but shared the underlying connection through tag-based demux.
> 
> **Option B: one generic bridge driven by a message enum.** The active-phase wire protocol uses a tagged union (session_id + payload). The bridge deserializes the envelope, routes to the appropriate par channel. This collapses the dispatch and bridge into one thread.
> 
> I'd lean toward **Option B** for the active phase. The handshake bridge is protocol-specific because the handshake *is* the protocol at that point. Once you're multiplexed, the bridge becomes a router. This is what devmnt actually looked like once you had multiple outstanding requests — it was a dispatcher with a tag table, not a separate thread per file operation.
> 
> But the current handshake-specific bridge function is the right shape for what it does. Don't over-generalize prematurely.
> 
> ## 3. Threading: one per connection is fine
> 
> Plan 9: one kernel proc per mount point in devmnt, which in practice meant one per connection. A typical Plan 9 terminal had maybe 5-10 active connections (cpu, factotum, plumber, font server, maybe a few file servers). Desktop Plan 9 never had hundreds.
> 
> For pane on a desktop: you'll have one connection to the compositor, maybe a handful of service connections. Call it 3-10. One OS thread per connection is completely fine. You're not building an HTTP server.
> 
> The more relevant concern is the `block_on` inside the bridge thread. Right now, `futures::executor::block_on(server.recv())` blocks the entire bridge thread waiting for one par oneshot. That's correct for a two-message handshake. For the active phase, a single `block_on` per message would serialize all communication on that connection. You'd want the bridge thread to select over multiple par channels and the transport simultaneously. But that's an active-phase design problem, not a handshake problem.
> 
> One thread per connection: endorsed. This is not where your complexity budget should go.
> 
> ## 4. Disconnect: the failure propagation is wrong
> 
> Right now the chain is:
> 
> ```
> Transport panics → bridge thread panics → oneshot Sender dropped → 
> handler's recv() panics ("sender dropped")
> ```
> 
> This is **two panics for one failure**, and it puts you on channel 3 (crash) of the three-channel model when you should be on a combination of channels 1 and 2.
> 
> From the three-channel error model:
> - **Protocol channel**: ServiceLost for service disconnects
> - **Control channel**: Flow::Stop for lifecycle
> - **Crash channel**: panic, for corrupt state
> 
> A transport disconnect is not corrupt state. It's a normal event in a distributed system. In Plan 9 terms, devmnt didn't panic when the connection dropped — it set `Ehangup` on the mount point and returned it from subsequent operations. The mount point was *marked dead*, and each subsequent use got an error (fail at use site, exactly per your service disconnect model).
> 
> What the bridge should do on transport failure:
> 
> 1. **Transport returns an error instead of panicking.** Change `send_raw`/`recv_raw` to return `Result&lt;(), TransportError&gt;` / `Result&lt;Vec&lt;u8&gt;, TransportError&gt;`. The current doc comment even says "Panics on broken connection" — that's the thing to fix.
> 
> 2. **Bridge catches the transport error and drops the par channel cleanly.** When the bridge thread encounters a transport error, it should drop its par endpoint (the `server`/`client` value) without panicking. This causes the handler's `recv()` future to resolve to `Err(Canceled)` — par's oneshot already has this path (line 127 of exchange.rs: `self.rx.await.expect("sender dropped")`).
> 
> 3. **That `.expect("sender dropped")` in par is the real problem.** Par panics on channel drop because CLL semantics require linearity — dropping is protocol violation. But transport disconnect IS how a connection ends. You need a way for the handler to observe channel closure without panicking. This probably means wrapping par's recv in something that converts `Canceled` to a `Result::Err(Disconnected)` rather than letting par's `.expect()` fire.
> 
> The clean version:
> 
> ```
> Transport error → bridge catches, drops par endpoint → 
> handler's next recv() returns Err(Disconnected) → 
> handler returns Flow::Stop → ExitReason::Disconnected
> ```
> 
> This puts disconnect on channel 2 (control), surfaced through channel 1 (protocol) at the use site. No panics anywhere. That's the Ehangup model.
> 
> The fact that par's `.expect("sender dropped")` panics on channel drop is a design tension between CLL's linearity (channels must be fully consumed) and network reality (connections die). You'll need a principled answer for this — either a `try_recv` variant, or a wrapper that catches the panic, or a par upstream change. This is the single most important thing to resolve before the active phase.
> 
> **Confidence: high on the diagnosis, medium on the exact fix** — the right solution depends on what par's API allows and whether you want to fork/patch par or wrap it.</result>

### `2026-04-05T05:41:51.733Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>afd57f6fd23fab0c5</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01FGDTYXarYuWtz84hAVW8yQ</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/afd57f6fd23fab0c5.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Option A vs D for disconnect" completed</summary>
> <result>No Drop impls. So when a Send or Recv is dropped without being consumed, the oneshot channel drops, and the other side gets `Canceled` / a failed send — which par handles with `expect("sender dropped")` or `expect("receiver dropped")`. These are panics.
> 
> Now I have everything I need. Let me give the recommendation.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Recommendation: Option D.** Transport returns Result; bridge catches errors and either propagates through par or converts to a setup-failure return. Here's why.
> 
> ### The Plan 9 analogy you asked about
> 
> In Plan 9, `mount(2)` is a syscall that takes an already-connected fd and performs the Tversion/Rattach exchange over it. If the connection dies during Tversion (the kernel sends Tversion, reads back nothing because the fd is dead), `devmnt.c` returns an error to the caller. Specifically, `mntversion()` in `devmnt.c` calls `mountrpc()` which calls `mountio()` — and `mountio()` does the actual read. If the read fails, it returns the error up through `mountrpc()` → `mntversion()` → the mount syscall, which returns -1 to userspace with an error string. The process is alive and well. It can try another server, print a message, whatever it wants.
> 
> `mount()` never panicked the process. It never killed the process. It returned an error code. The process had full agency over what to do next.
> 
> The Ehangup behavior you're thinking of — that's what happens *after* mount succeeds, when you have a mounted channel and the server disappears. The next `read()`/`write()` on a file in that mount returns Ehangup. That maps cleanly to your active-phase disconnected() callback and the fail-at-use-site pattern you already decided on.
> 
> These are genuinely different categories:
> 
> | Phase | Plan 9 | pane equivalent |
> |-------|--------|-----------------|
> | Setup (mount/Tversion) | syscall returns error | handshake failure |
> | Active (read/write) | Ehangup on next op | calloop → disconnected() → Flow::Stop |
> 
> ### The concrete problem with Option A
> 
> Look at what actually happens if the transport dies during the handshake under the current code. The bridge thread calls `transport.recv_raw()`, which panics (line 20-21 of transport.rs: `expect("peer disconnected")`). That panic kills the bridge thread. The bridge thread holds the par `ServerHandshake` (or `ClientHandshake`) endpoint. When the bridge thread's stack unwinds, par's `Send` or `Recv` is dropped without being consumed. No Drop impl in par (I checked — no Drop impls anywhere in par's source). The oneshot `Sender` or `Receiver` just drops. On the handler side, when it calls `.recv().await`, it hits `expect("sender dropped")` at exchange.rs line 127. That's a panic on the handler's thread.
> 
> So right now, under Option A, what actually happens is:
> 
> 1. Transport dies
> 2. Bridge thread panics via `.expect("peer disconnected")`
> 3. Bridge thread unwinds, par channel drops
> 4. Handler side panics via `.expect("sender dropped")`
> 5. catch_unwind at looper boundary catches it → ExitReason::Failed
> 
> The problem: **ExitReason::Failed means "handler panicked (caught by catch_unwind)"** — it semantically means the handler's *logic* is broken. But this isn't a logic bug. This is a transport failure during setup. You've collapsed two different failure modes into one exit reason, and the panic message (`"sender dropped"`) tells you nothing about *why* the transport died. You've also made every handshake failure look like a program bug in logs and in PaneExited broadcasts.
> 
> Worse: if pane-headless eventually supports retry-on-different-server (which your Phase 2 multi-server plan implies), Option A forecloses that. You'd need to catch_unwind *around the handshake specifically*, parse the panic payload to distinguish "transport died" from "handler logic bug" (which is not reliably possible), and then somehow recover. That's strictly harder than returning a Result.
> 
> ### What Option D looks like concretely
> 
> Three changes:
> 
> **1. Transport trait returns Result:**
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Transport: Sized + Send {
>     fn send_raw(&amp;mut self, data: &amp;[u8]) -&gt; io::Result&lt;()&gt;;
>     fn recv_raw(&amp;mut self) -&gt; io::Result&lt;Vec&lt;u8&gt;&gt;;
> }
> ```
> 
> **2. Bridge functions return Result instead of raw session endpoints:**
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn bridge_client_handshake(
>     mut transport: impl Transport + 'static
> ) -&gt; Result&lt;ClientHandshake, HandshakeError&gt; {
> ```
> 
> The bridge thread catches transport errors. If `recv_raw` returns Err, the bridge sends a protocol-level error through par (if the par channel is still live) or simply drops the par endpoint (which causes the handler side to get `Canceled`). Either way, the *bridge_client_handshake* function returns an error to its caller rather than letting a panic propagate.
> 
> Actually — let me be more precise. `bridge_client_handshake` returns the par endpoint immediately (because fork_sync returns synchronously). The bridge thread runs concurrently. So the error doesn't surface at the return site; it surfaces when the handler does `.recv()` on the par channel.
> 
> This means the cleanest version of Option D is:
> 
> **The bridge thread catches transport errors and drops the par endpoint cleanly. The handler's `.recv()` gets Canceled. But instead of par's `expect("sender dropped")` panicking, pane-session wraps par's recv in a checked version that returns Result.**
> 
> ```rust
> /// Checked recv that converts par channel cancellation to an error
> /// instead of panicking. Used during handshake phase only.
> pub async fn checked_recv&lt;T, S&gt;(recv: Recv&lt;T, S&gt;) -&gt; Result&lt;(T, S), HandshakeError&gt;
> where T: marker::Send + 'static, S: Session
> {
>     // Can't easily do this with par's current API — recv() calls
>     // expect() internally.
> }
> ```
> 
> Hmm. Par's recv does `.expect("sender dropped")` internally. You can't intercept that without either:
> - Forking par to return Result (you're vendoring it anyway — it's complete per the author)
> - Using `catch_unwind` around the recv specifically
> 
> **3. The honest middle ground:**
> 
> The bridge thread catches transport errors. It drops the par endpoint. On the handler side, `catch_unwind` around the *handshake recv specifically* catches the "sender dropped" panic and converts it to a typed `HandshakeError`. This is not the same as Option A's "let everything panic through to the looper boundary" — it's a targeted catch at the handshake call site that produces a typed error the caller can act on.
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn run_handshake(transport: impl Transport + 'static) 
>     -&gt; Result&lt;Welcome, HandshakeError&gt; 
> {
>     let client = bridge_client_handshake(transport);
>     let client = client.send(hello);
>     
>     match catch_unwind(AssertUnwindSafe(|| {
>         futures::executor::block_on(client.recv1())
>     })) {
>         Ok(welcome) =&gt; Ok(welcome),
>         Err(_) =&gt; Err(HandshakeError::TransportLost),
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> This happens *before* the looper starts. The caller of `run_handshake` (which is `PaneBuilder::run_with` or equivalent) gets a Result and can:
> - Try a different server
> - Print a useful error
> - Exit with `ExitReason::InfraError` (which actually means infrastructure failure, unlike Failed)
> 
> ### Summary
> 
> **D, implemented as: Transport returns Result, bridge catches errors cleanly, handshake wrapper converts par's panic-on-drop to a typed HandshakeError via targeted catch_unwind. The looper boundary catch_unwind is reserved for active-phase handler panics only.**
> 
> What you gain: handshake failure is distinguishable from handler bugs. Multi-server retry becomes possible. ExitReason semantics stay clean. Error messages are useful.
> 
> What you pay: one catch_unwind at the handshake call site. Transport trait signature changes from `fn send_raw(&amp;mut self, data: &amp;[u8])` to `-&gt; io::Result&lt;()&gt;`. Bridge thread needs a small error-handling path.
> 
> The simpler alternative is Option A (status quo, essentially). You'd pick A if you were certain pane would never need to retry a different server on handshake failure, and you were OK with transport failures during setup being indistinguishable from handler logic bugs. I don't think either of those is true given your Phase 2 plans.
> 
> Confidence: high on the direction, medium on the exact catch_unwind-at-handshake-site mechanism. If you end up vendoring par (which you might want to do anyway given it's "complete" and you're building on it), you could change recv to return Result directly and eliminate that catch_unwind entirely.</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>40710</total_tokens><tool_uses>19</tool_uses><duration_ms>107328</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-05T05:42:14.827Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a75b43a650051c97e</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01SU5TJXQFXHhwUCfpwCi4rX</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/a75b43a650051c97e.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Option A vs D for disconnect" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. I now have the complete picture. Here's the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## What BeOS/Haiku actually does
> 
> The Haiku source reveals a **three-tier failure model** for BApplication's connection to app_server. This is worth understanding in detail because it maps directly to your design question.
> 
> ### Tier 1: Server not found (infrastructure missing)
> 
> `get_app_server_port()` at `AppMisc.cpp:247` calls `find_port(SERVER_PORT_NAME)`. If app_server isn't running, this returns a negative error code. `create_desktop_connection()` propagates that as a `status_t` error. Back in `_InitData()`, this lands in `fInitError`.
> 
> What happens next depends on the **constructor form used**:
> 
> - `BApplication(const char* signature)` — no error pointer — line 537-539:
>   ```cpp
>   } else if (fInitError != B_OK) {
>       DBG(OUT("BApplication::InitData() failed: %s\n", strerror(fInitError)));
>       exit(0);
>   }
>   ```
>   **The process silently exits.** Not a crash, not a panic — `exit(0)`. This is the common case. Most apps used the single-argument constructor.
> 
> - `BApplication(const char* signature, status_t* _error)` — error pointer provided — the error is stored in `*_error` and the caller is expected to check `InitCheck()` before calling `Run()`.
> 
> ### Tier 2: Server found, handshake rejected
> 
> `_ConnectToServer()` at line 1395 does a two-phase handshake:
> 
> 1. `AS_GET_DESKTOP` request through `create_desktop_connection()` — gets a desktop-specific port
> 2. `AS_CREATE_APP` request — registers the app with the desktop and gets a dedicated server port
> 
> If step 2 fails (the `FlushWithReply` returns an error, or the response code isn't `B_OK`), line 1431-1432:
> ```cpp
> debugger("BApplication: couldn't obtain new app_server comm port");
> return B_ERROR;
> ```
> 
> This calls `debugger()` — which on BeOS drops you into the kernel debugger if one is attached, otherwise acts as a fatal assertion. Then it returns `B_ERROR`, which flows back through `_InitGUIContext()` to `fInitError`, and follows the same exit-or-return path as Tier 1.
> 
> ### Tier 3: Registrar not running
> 
> Separate from app_server. Line 418-421:
> ```cpp
> if (fInitError == B_OK &amp;&amp; registerApp
>     &amp;&amp; !BRoster::Private().IsMessengerValid(false)) {
>     printf("FATAL: be_roster is not valid. Is the registrar running?\n");
>     fInitError = B_NO_INIT;
> }
> ```
> 
> This prints a message and sets the error. Same exit-or-return flow.
> 
> ### The pattern
> 
> Be's design philosophy here was: **handshake failure is not a recoverable condition, but it's not a crash either.** The system distinguishes between:
> 
> 1. **Callers who can handle it** (pass `status_t*`) — get the error back, check `InitCheck()`, can report and exit cleanly
> 2. **Callers who can't or don't want to handle it** (no error pointer) — the process exits immediately with `exit(0)`
> 3. **Truly impossible states** (handshake partially completed then failed) — `debugger()` call, which is the BeOS equivalent of a panic
> 
> The critical observation: **even the `debugger()` call at line 1432 still returns `B_ERROR` afterward.** It's an assertion that aids debugging, but the code doesn't assume it's terminal. The actual termination decision is deferred to `_InitData`'s end, where it depends on whether the caller asked for error reporting.
> 
> ## How this maps to your options
> 
> Neither Option A nor Option B as stated is quite right. But B is much closer to the correct answer.
> 
> **Option A (accept panic during handshake) is wrong in spirit.** BeOS didn't panic on handshake failure. It distinguished between "infrastructure missing" (clean exit) and "impossible state during handshake" (debugger). The `exit(0)` path — which is what most apps hit — is deliberately not a crash. JBQ and the kernel team were very deliberate about this: a process that can't connect to the server it needs should die quietly, not dump core. The user doesn't need a crash report because there's nothing to report — the server isn't there.
> 
> Panic collapses "server not running" and "handshake corrupted" into the same bucket. BeOS kept them separate. The first is a known failure mode with a clear cause. The second is a bug.
> 
> **Option B is right in direction but needs to be simpler.** You don't need "protocol-level error framing." You need:
> 
> ### Option B': Transport returns Result, bridge propagates error, no par modification
> 
> 1. Change Transport:
>    ```rust
>    fn send_raw(&amp;mut self, data: &amp;[u8]) -&gt; Result&lt;(), TransportError&gt;;
>    fn recv_raw(&amp;mut self) -&gt; Result&lt;Vec&lt;u8&gt;, TransportError&gt;;
>    ```
> 
> 2. Bridge catches transport errors and converts them to a concrete Rust error. The bridge function itself returns `Result&lt;ClientHandshake, HandshakeError&gt;`:
>    ```rust
>    pub fn bridge_client_handshake(
>        mut transport: impl Transport + 'static
>    ) -&gt; Result&lt;ClientHandshake, HandshakeError&gt; {
>    ```
>    
>    But wait — `fork_sync` runs the bridge in a closure. The bridge thread can't return an error to the caller directly because the par session is already created by the time the closure runs. This is the structural issue.
> 
>    The real answer: **don't spawn the bridge thread until you've verified the transport is alive.** Split the handshake into two phases:
> 
>    - **Phase 1 (before par):** Transport-level connection. Open the socket, verify the server is reachable. This is synchronous and returns `Result&lt;ConnectedTransport, ConnectError&gt;`. No par involved. This is BeOS's `find_port()` + `create_desktop_connection()`.
> 
>    - **Phase 2 (with par):** Protocol-level handshake over the confirmed transport. Hello/Welcome exchange through the bridge. If the transport dies *during* the exchange, that's the "impossible state" — the bridge thread panics, par's linearity violation fires, and that's correct because a transport that was alive and then died mid-handshake is either a bug or a network failure so severe that crash reporting isn't going to help.
> 
> This is exactly what Haiku does. `create_desktop_connection()` is Phase 1 — it finds the port, creates the client port, does the `AS_GET_DESKTOP` exchange, all synchronous, all returning `status_t`. Only after that succeeds does `_ConnectToServer()` proceed to `AS_CREATE_APP`, which is the actual handshake.
> 
> ### Concrete recommendation
> 
> **Option B' — Result-returning transport + two-phase connect.**
> 
> The connect phase (`Transport::connect()` or a free function) returns `Result`. This is where "server not running" and "connection refused" are caught. The caller — your App equivalent — handles this the same way BApplication does: either propagate the error to the user via `InitCheck()`, or exit.
> 
> The handshake phase uses par. Transport errors during the handshake are panics, because a transport that connected successfully and then died during a two-message exchange is an exceptional condition. Par's linearity panic is the right behavior for this case — it's your `debugger("BApplication: couldn't obtain new app_server comm port")`.
> 
> This gives you:
> - **Server not running:** `Err(ConnectError::ServerNotFound)` — clean, reportable, no panic
> - **Server rejects connection:** `Err(ConnectError::Rejected(reason))` — clean, reportable, no panic
> - **Transport dies mid-handshake:** panic in bridge thread — equivalent to BeOS's `debugger()` call — caught by the caller's thread if needed
> - **Active phase disconnect:** calloop handler, `handler.disconnected()`, `Flow::Stop` — not par's concern at all
> 
> The spirit of Be's design: **failure is an error until it's impossible, then it's a crash.** Most failures should be errors. Only the ones that indicate broken invariants should crash.</result>
