### `2026-04-05T05:43:57.892Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a1453e496885e9f64</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_014FeSnRXRVKUu8GQHd2FSd6</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/a1453e496885e9f64.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Option A vs D for disconnect" completed</summary>
> <result>This is exactly the formal framework. Now I have everything needed for a precise answer.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: Option A. Conditionally sound.**
> 
> The handshake-abort-as-panic encoding is the correct CLL formalization. Option D is formally incorrect. Here's the full analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Is channel drop during handshake formally different from drop during active phase?
> 
> No and yes.
> 
> In CLL, a session either completes (all exchanges execute to `End`/`()`) or the session is *annihilated* — there is no partial completion. This is Wadler's cut elimination theorem (JFP 2014, Theorem 2: if both sides type-check with dual types, the cut reduces completely). A session that fails mid-protocol is not a CLL term at all — it's a configuration that violates the proof structure. CLL has no representation of "the session got halfway through."
> 
> Par encodes this faithfully. The `#[must_use]` on `Send&lt;T, S&gt;` and `Recv&lt;T, S&gt;` (exchange.rs lines 36, 64) plus the absence of Drop impls means: if you drop a par endpoint, the peer's `recv()` hits `oneshot::Canceled` and panics with `"sender dropped"` (exchange.rs line 127) or `"receiver dropped"` (exchange.rs line 107, 174). This panic IS the CLL annihilation of an incomplete proof.
> 
> The formal distinction between handshake and active phase is not CLL-internal — CLL doesn't care when in the protocol the failure occurs. The distinction is *architectural*: the handshake is a par-driven session (`Send&lt;Hello, Recv&lt;Welcome&gt;&gt;`), while the active phase uses calloop + typed enum dispatch, which is outside par's model entirely. So par's linearity enforcement (panic-on-incomplete) applies exclusively during the handshake, and the active phase has its own disconnect mechanism (`disconnected() -&gt; Flow::Stop`).
> 
> This is clean: par governs the session-typed phase, calloop governs the event-driven phase. Two distinct formal regimes, each with its own error channel.
> 
> ## 2. Does Option D violate CLL?
> 
> **Yes.** Option D introduces a protocol-level error through par's channel, meaning the session type would need to change from:
> 
> ```
> Send&lt;Hello, Recv&lt;Welcome&gt;&gt;
> ```
> 
> to something like:
> 
> ```
> Send&lt;Hello, Recv&lt;Result&lt;Welcome, TransportError&gt;&gt;&gt;
> ```
> 
> This is a legitimate CLL encoding — it's additive branching (A ⊕ B at the server, A &amp; B at the client). But it changes what the *handshake protocol* means. The error is no longer infrastructure failure; it's a *protocol outcome*. This has three problems:
> 
> **Problem 2a:** The bridge thread catches a transport error and must now *send* that error through par's channel to the handler. But the bridge is on the `ServerHandshake` side of the internal par session — it plays the dual. The current bridge architecture (bridge.rs lines 38-61) has the bridge holding `ServerHandshake = Recv&lt;Hello, Send&lt;Welcome&gt;&gt;`. If the transport fails during step 3 (reading Welcome from wire), the bridge has already consumed the `Recv&lt;Hello, ...&gt;` leg and holds a bare `Send&lt;Welcome&gt;` (or rather `Send&lt;Result&lt;Welcome, Error&gt;&gt;`). This works mechanically — the bridge sends `Err(...)` instead of `Ok(welcome)`. But...
> 
> **Problem 2b:** The bridge's transport failure is *not the peer's choice*. In CLL, ⊕ (Select) is the *sender's* choice. The error comes from a transport layer failure, not from the server choosing to reject. You're encoding an infrastructure event as a protocol decision, which conflates the error channels the architecture explicitly separates (architecture.md lines 96-101: Protocol / Control / Crash are disjoint). Transport failure is a crash-channel event. Routing it through the protocol channel violates the three-channel separation.
> 
> **Problem 2c:** If the transport dies *before* the bridge can send the error through par, you're back to a dropped par endpoint and a panic anyway. So Option D doesn't eliminate the panic path — it adds a second path (error-through-channel) alongside it, and the handler must handle both. This is strictly worse: two error mechanisms for one failure mode.
> 
> ## 3. Is "handshake abort = panic" the natural CLL encoding?
> 
> Yes. This is exactly par's design intent. Strba's documentation (lib.rs line 112): "Session end-points **must not be dropped.**" The enforcement mechanism is that dropping triggers cancellation of the internal oneshot, which becomes a panic at the peer's `recv()` site.
> 
> For a finite session like `Send&lt;Hello, Recv&lt;Welcome&gt;&gt;`, the only ways the session can terminate are:
> 
> 1. **Complete**: Hello sent, Welcome received, both endpoints consumed. Session value is `()`.
> 2. **Annihilated**: One side drops its endpoint. The other panics.
> 
> There is no third option in CLL. The architecture spec already acknowledges this — architecture.md lines 35-37: "Chan operations panic on disconnect (par's CLL model — sessions complete or are annihilated; the looper's catch_unwind boundary is the crash safety mechanism)."
> 
> The handshake is the first par session. The catch_unwind boundary in PaneBuilder or App::connect is the crash safety mechanism. This is the same pattern as the looper's catch_unwind (architecture.md lines 757-773), just applied earlier in the lifecycle.
> 
> ## 4. Does EAct have a concept of failed spawn?
> 
> Yes, but it's in the failure extension (Maty_⚡, §5 of the extended paper).
> 
> The base EAct calculus (§3-4) has `E-Spawn` (lines 2021-2036) which creates a fresh actor with empty handler and initialisation state. The spawned actor's term `M` then executes. If `M` encounters `raise` during execution, `E-Raise` (lines 3287-3293) fires:
> 
> ```
> ⟨a | E[raise] | σ | ι | ω⟩  →τ  ⚡(a) ∥ ⚡(σ) ∥ ⚡(ι)
> ```
> 
> The actor is replaced with zapper threads that propagate cancellation. Crucially, `E-Raise` works the same whether the actor is in its init phase (running `M` from spawn) or in its reactive loop (idle, receiving messages). The formalism doesn't distinguish — a raise is a raise regardless of when it occurs.
> 
> The key insight: in pane's architecture, the handshake happens *inside the actor's init phase*. Looking at the lifecycle:
> 
> 1. `App::connect()` — infrastructure, not EAct
> 2. `app.create_pane()` — infrastructure
> 3. `pane.setup::&lt;H&gt;()` → `PaneBuilder&lt;H&gt;` — actor sigma construction (pre-reactive)
> 4. `builder.open_service()` — blocking DeclareInterest, still pre-reactive
> 5. `builder.run_with(handler)` — enters reactive loop (E-React becomes available)
> 
> The handshake occurs at step 1-2. If the handshake par session panics, this is a panic *before the actor enters its reactive loop*. In EAct terms, this maps to an actor whose init term `M` raises — which is exactly `E-Raise` during the spawn-but-before-idle phase. The spawning actor (or the supervisor, in Maty_⚡) receives the zapper thread `⚡(a)` and invokes its monitor callback.
> 
> In pane, the "supervisor" is `std::process::exit` or the OS supervisor (s6/systemd). The zapper thread `⚡(a)` maps to process exit. The architecture spec explicitly endorses this (lines 619-623): "Pre-run failures (connect, create_pane) panic because they are infrastructure: the service map is wrong, the server is down, the handshake was rejected."
> 
> ## Concrete recommendation
> 
> **Option A.** With these conditions:
> 
> **C1: catch_unwind around the handshake par session.** The bridge thread spawned in `bridge_client_handshake` (bridge.rs line 42) already runs in a separate thread. If the transport fails, the bridge thread panics (via `.expect()` on transport operations). This panic propagates to the handler side as `oneshot::Canceled` → `panic!("sender dropped")` when the handler calls `recv()`. The handler's call site (in App::connect or PaneBuilder) must be wrapped in `catch_unwind` if you want to convert this to a process exit rather than unwinding through the caller's stack.
> 
> But actually — per the architecture spec, `App::connect()` panics on failure (line 610: "Panics if the server is unreachable"). So you *don't* need catch_unwind here. The panic IS the intended behavior. The process supervisor restarts.
> 
> **C2: Bridge thread join.** When the par session panics on the handler side (handler drops its endpoint), the bridge thread will get a `SendError` on the oneshot and should also terminate. Currently the bridge thread is spawned and detached (line 42: `std::thread::spawn(move || { ... })`). This is fine — the thread will either complete normally or panic, and either way it terminates. But if you want to observe the bridge thread's exit, you'd need to join it. For the handshake (a two-step protocol), this is unnecessary — the thread lifetime is negligibly short.
> 
> **C3: I1 must hold.** `panic = unwind` (architecture.md line 860). If panic = abort, par's endpoint drop doesn't propagate properly — the oneshot receiver gets a hard close instead of a cancellation, and the bridge thread may hang on `block_on(server.recv())`. This is already a system invariant, but it's load-bearing here.
> 
> **C4: Transport timeouts.** If the remote peer never sends Welcome, `transport.recv_raw()` blocks forever in the bridge thread. The handler's `block_on(client.recv())` also blocks forever. This is a liveness issue, not a safety issue, but it means a network partition during handshake results in a hung process rather than a crashed one. A transport-level read timeout converts this to a panic (via `.expect()` on the timeout error), which then propagates correctly through par's annihilation path.
> 
> ---
> 
> The formal summary: par's panic-on-incomplete IS CLL's proof annihilation (Wadler JFP 2014, the non-existence of a cut reduction for an incomplete proof). EAct's E-Raise (Fowler et al., §5, lines 3287-3293) handles actor failure at any point, including during init. The handshake is a finite binary session; its only outcomes are completion or annihilation. Option D introduces a spurious third outcome that violates the three-channel error separation and doesn't eliminate the panic path anyway.</result>

> </task-notification>

> <result>Perfect! Now I have comprehensive information. Let me create a detailed report:
> 
> ## Comprehensive fp-library Optics Analysis for pane Handler State
> 
> Based on my thorough exploration of fp-library's source code and your current pane implementation, here's what's available and how it maps to handler state access patterns.
> 
> ### 1. CURRENT PANE IMPLEMENTATION
> 
> Your `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-proto/src/property.rs` uses:
> - **Lens**: by-value view/set operations (S -&gt; A, (S, B) -&gt; T)
> - **Getter**: read-only view-only access
> - Simple closure-based construction: `Lens::from_view_set(|s| s.field, |(s, v)| EditorState { field: v, ..s })`
> 
> The optic laws (GetPut, PutGet, PutPut) ensure consistency between namespace view and handler state.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 2. AVAILABLE OPTICS IN FP-LIBRARY
> 
> fp-library provides a complete optics hierarchy. Here's the practical catalog:
> 
> #### **Lens** (What you're using)
> - **Location**: `/types/optics/lens.rs`
> - **API**:
>   ```rust
>   pub fn view(&amp;self, s: S) -&gt; A
>   pub fn set(&amp;self, s: S, b: B) -&gt; T
>   pub fn from_view_set(view: impl Fn(S) -&gt; A, set: impl Fn((S, B)) -&gt; T) -&gt; Self
>   pub fn new(to: impl Fn(S) -&gt; (A, impl Fn(B) -&gt; T)) -&gt; Self
>   ```
> - **Use case**: Focus on a single field that always exists. By-value only.
> - **Handler pattern**: `editor.buffer.len()`, `editor.cursor_pos`
> 
> #### **Prism**
> - **Location**: `/types/optics/prism.rs`
> - **For**: Sum types / Optional fields (enum variants, Option fields)
> - **API**:
>   ```rust
>   pub fn preview(&amp;self, s: S) -&gt; Result&lt;A, T&gt;  // Try to extract focus
>   pub fn review(&amp;self, b: B) -&gt; T              // Construct from focus
>   pub fn new(preview: impl Fn(S) -&gt; Result&lt;A, T&gt;, review: impl Fn(B) -&gt; T) -&gt; Self
>   ```
> - **Example**:
>   ```rust
>   let some_prism: Prism&lt;RcBrand, Option&lt;i32&gt;, Option&lt;f64&gt;, i32, f64&gt; =
>       Prism::new(
>           |o: Option&lt;i32&gt;| o.ok_or(None),  // preview: extract Some(x) -&gt; Ok(x), None -&gt; Err(None)
>           |f| Some(f)                       // review: construct back to Some
>       );
>   assert_eq!(some_prism.preview(Some(42)), Ok(42));
>   assert_eq!(some_prism.review(42.0), Some(42.0));
>   ```
> - **Handler pattern**: `editor.clipboard` (may not exist), `editor.selection` (Some/None)
> 
> #### **AffineTraversal**
> - **Location**: `/types/optics/affine.rs`
> - **For**: Optional focusing (at most one element), no construction required
> - **API**:
>   ```rust
>   pub fn preview(&amp;self, s: S) -&gt; Result&lt;A, T&gt;
>   pub fn set(&amp;self, s: S, b: B) -&gt; T
>   pub fn from_preview_set(
>       preview: impl Fn(S) -&gt; Result&lt;A, T&gt;,
>       set: impl Fn((S, B)) -&gt; T
>   ) -&gt; Self
>   ```
> - **Difference from Prism**: Can update without knowing how to construct the full structure
> - **Handler pattern**: `editor.maybe_cached_state` (present or absent, but don't need to construct from scratch)
> 
> #### **Traversal**
> - **Location**: `/types/optics/traversal.rs`
> - **For**: Collections, zero or more elements
> - **API** (uses Applicative functor):
>   ```rust
>   // Constructor requires implementing TraversalFunc trait
>   pub fn new(traversal: F) -&gt; Self where F: TraversalFunc&lt;'a, S, T, A, B&gt;
>   
>   // TraversalFunc trait:
>   trait TraversalFunc&lt;'a, S, T, A, B&gt; {
>       fn apply&lt;M: Applicative&gt;(
>           &amp;self,
>           f: Box&lt;dyn Fn(A) -&gt; Apply!(&lt;M as Kind&gt;(...)&gt;::Of&lt;'a, B&gt;) + 'a&gt;,
>           s: S,
>       ) -&gt; Apply!(&lt;M as Kind&gt;(...)&gt;::Of&lt;'a, T&gt;);
>   }
>   ```
> - **Example from docs**:
>   ```rust
>   #[derive(Clone)]
>   struct ListTraversal;
>   impl&lt;'a, A: 'a + Clone&gt; TraversalFunc&lt;'a, Vec&lt;A&gt;, Vec&lt;A&gt;, A, A&gt; for ListTraversal {
>       fn apply&lt;M: Applicative&gt;(
>           &amp;self,
>           f: Box&lt;dyn Fn(A) -&gt; Apply!(&lt;M as Kind!( type Of&lt;'b, U: 'b&gt;: 'b; )&gt;::Of&lt;'a, A&gt;) + 'a&gt;,
>           s: Vec&lt;A&gt;,
>       ) -&gt; Apply!(&lt;M as Kind!( type Of&lt;'b, U: 'b&gt;: 'b; )&gt;::Of&lt;'a, Vec&lt;A&gt;&gt;) {
>           s.into_iter().fold(M::pure(vec![]), |acc, a| {
>               M::lift2(
>                   |mut v: Vec&lt;A&gt;, x: A| { v.push(x); v },
>                   acc,
>                   f(a),
>               )
>           })
>       }
>   }
>   
>   let traversal = Traversal::&lt;'_, RcBrand, Vec&lt;i32&gt;, Vec&lt;i32&gt;, i32, i32, _&gt;::new(ListTraversal);
>   assert_eq!(
>       traversal.traversal.apply::&lt;OptionBrand&gt;(
>           Box::new(|x| Some(x + 1)), 
>           vec![1, 2]
>       ),
>       Some(vec![2, 3])
>   );
>   ```
> - **Handler pattern**: `editor.open_files`, `editor.breakpoints` (all items)
> 
> #### **IndexedLens**
> - **Location**: `/types/optics/indexed_lens.rs`
> - **For**: Single field WITH position/index tracking
> - **API**:
>   ```rust
>   pub fn iview(&amp;self, s: S) -&gt; (I, A)  // Returns (index, value)
>   pub fn set(&amp;self, s: S, b: B) -&gt; T
>   pub fn from_iview_set(
>       iview: impl Fn(S) -&gt; (I, A),
>       set: impl Fn((S, B)) -&gt; T
>   ) -&gt; Self
>   ```
> - **Example from docs**:
>   ```rust
>   let l: IndexedLens&lt;RcBrand, usize, (i32, String), (i32, String), i32, i32&gt; =
>       IndexedLens::from_iview_set(
>           |(x, _)| (0, x),                                    // index 0, value x
>           |((_, s), x)| (x, s)
>       );
>   assert_eq!(l.iview((42, "hi".to_string())), (0, 42));
>   ```
> - **Handler pattern**: Track which field in a struct, or position in a logical group
> 
> #### **IndexedTraversal**
> - **Location**: `/types/optics/indexed_traversal.rs`
> - **For**: Collections WITH index/position for each element
> - **API** (via IndexedTraversalFunc):
>   ```rust
>   trait IndexedTraversalFunc&lt;'a, I, S, T, A, B&gt; {
>       fn apply&lt;M: Applicative&gt;(
>           &amp;self,
>           f: Box&lt;dyn Fn(I, A) -&gt; Apply!(&lt;M as Kind&gt;(...)&gt;::Of&lt;'a, B&gt;) + 'a&gt;,
>           s: S,
>       ) -&gt; Apply!(&lt;M as Kind&gt;(...)&gt;::Of&lt;'a, T&gt;);
>   }
>   ```
> - **Example from docs**:
>   ```rust
>   let traversal = Traversed::&lt;VecBrand&gt;(std::marker::PhantomData);
>   let s = vec![10, 20, 30];
>   let f = Box::new(|i: usize, a: i32| -&gt; Option&lt;i32&gt; { Some(a + i as i32) });
>   
>   let result: Option&lt;Vec&lt;i32&gt;&gt; = IndexedTraversalFunc::apply::&lt;OptionBrand&gt;(&amp;traversal, f, s);
>   assert_eq!(result, Some(vec![10, 21, 32]));  // Each element gets its index
>   ```
> - **Handler pattern**: `editor.open_files.map_with_index(|i, file| ...)`, apply handlers to each with position
> 
> #### **Fold**
> - **Location**: `/types/optics/fold.rs`
> - **For**: Read-only traversal, collecting multiple values into a monoid
> - **API**:
>   ```rust
>   pub struct IterableFoldFn&lt;F&gt;(pub F);
>   impl&lt;'a, S, A, I, F&gt; FoldFunc&lt;'a, S, A&gt; for IterableFoldFn&lt;F&gt;
>   where F: Fn(S) -&gt; I, I: IntoIterator&lt;Item = A&gt;
>   {
>       fn apply&lt;R: Monoid&gt;(&amp;self, f: impl Fn(A) -&gt; R + 'a, s: S) -&gt; R {
>           (self.0)(s).into_iter().fold(R::empty(), |r, a| R::append(r, f(a)))
>       }
>   }
>   ```
> - **Example**:
>   ```rust
>   let fold = IterableFoldFn(|v: Vec&lt;i32&gt;| v);
>   let result = fold.apply::&lt;String&gt;(|x| x.to_string(), vec![1, 2, 3]);
>   assert_eq!(result, "123".to_string());
>   ```
> - **Handler pattern**: Count all open files, concatenate all buffer names, etc.
> 
> #### **Getter**
> - **Location**: `/types/optics/getter.rs`
> - **For**: Read-only focus (computed/derived)
> - **API**:
>   ```rust
>   pub fn view(&amp;self, s: S) -&gt; A
>   pub fn new(view: impl Fn(S) -&gt; A) -&gt; Self
>   ```
> - **Handler pattern**: Same as your ReadOnlyProperty
> 
> #### **Iso** (Isomorphism)
> - **Location**: `/types/optics/iso.rs`
> - **For**: Bidirectional lossless conversions
> - **API**:
>   ```rust
>   pub fn from(&amp;self, s: S) -&gt; A
>   pub fn to(&amp;self, b: B) -&gt; T
>   pub fn new(from: impl Fn(S) -&gt; A, to: impl Fn(B) -&gt; T) -&gt; Self
>   ```
> - **Example**:
>   ```rust
>   let string_chars: Iso&lt;RcBrand, String, String, Vec&lt;char&gt;, Vec&lt;char&gt;&gt; =
>       Iso::new(
>           |s: String| s.chars().collect(),
>           |v: Vec&lt;char&gt;| v.into_iter().collect()
>       );
>   assert_eq!(string_chars.from("hi".to_string()), vec!['h', 'i']);
>   ```
> - **Handler pattern**: Convert between internal representation and namespace view
> 
> #### **Indexed** (Wrapper)
> - **Location**: `/types/optics/indexed.rs`
> - **For**: Wrapping profunctors to carry index alongside focus
> - **API**:
>   ```rust
>   pub struct Indexed&lt;'a, P, I, A, B&gt; where P: Profunctor
>   pub fn new(inner: Apply!(&lt;P as Kind!(...) {
>       inner
>   }
>   ```
> - **Use**: Generally used internally by IndexedLens, IndexedTraversal
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 3. OPTIC COMPOSITION
> 
> **Composed optics exist**: `/types/optics/composed.rs`
> ```rust
> pub struct Composed&lt;'a, S, T, M, N, A, B, O1, O2&gt; {
>     pub first: O1,   // outer optic (applied second)
>     pub second: O2,  // inner optic (applied first)
> }
> 
> pub fn new(first: O1, second: O2) -&gt; Self { ... }
> ```
> 
> **Practical example** (from docs):
> ```rust
> let l1: LensPrime&lt;RcBrand, (i32, String), i32&gt; =
>     LensPrime::from_view_set(|(x, _)| x, |((_, s), x)| (x, s));
> let l2: LensPrime&lt;RcBrand, i32, i32&gt; = 
>     LensPrime::from_view_set(|x| x, |(_, x)| x);
> let composed = Composed::new(l1, l2);
> 
> let f = cloneable_fn_new::&lt;RcFnBrand, _, _&gt;(|x: i32| x * 2);
> let modifier = &lt;Composed&lt;...&gt; as Optic&lt;RcFnBrand, _, _, _, _&gt;&gt;::evaluate(&amp;composed, f);
> assert_eq!(modifier((21, "hi".to_string())), (42, "hi".to_string()));
> ```
> 
> **For nested access** like `editor.buffer.cursor`, you can compose lenses.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 4. REFERENCE-BASED VIEWING
> 
> **Critical Finding**: fp-library optics work by-value only. There is NO by-reference API (`&amp;S -&gt; &amp;A`).
> 
> Why? The library is built on profunctors for composability and polymorphism. References break this abstraction because:
> 1. Profunctors need to be applied to the focus type
> 2. References require lifetime tracking that defeats abstraction
> 3. The approach trades ownership for composability
> 
> **Workaround for borrowing**: Use indices or identifiers instead of references.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 5. FUNCTOR/APPLICATIVE/MONAD FOR HANDLER STATE
> 
> The fp-library Functor/Applicative/Monad hierarchy is general-purpose for any container type, not specialized for optics. However, you CAN use them for handler state transformations:
> 
> **Monad example** (from library docs):
> ```rust
> use fp_library::{brands::*, functions::*};
> use fp_macros::m_do;
> 
> fn safe_div(a: i32, b: i32) -&gt; Option&lt;i32&gt; {
>     if b == 0 { None } else { Some(a / b) }
> }
> 
> let result = m_do!(OptionBrand {
>     x &lt;- safe_div(100, 2);
>     y &lt;- safe_div(x, 5);
>     pure(y + 1)
> });
> assert_eq!(result, Some(11));
> ```
> 
> **Applicative example**:
> ```rust
> let result = a_do!(OptionBrand {
>     x &lt;- Some(3);
>     y &lt;- Some(4);
>     x + y
> });
> assert_eq!(result, Some(7));
> ```
> 
> **For handler state transformation**: You'd wrap state updates in Option/Result for fallible operations.
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 6. PRACTICAL PATTERNS FOR PANE HANDLERS
> 
> #### Pattern 1: Nested Field Access (editor.buffer.cursor)
> ```rust
> // Using Composed lenses
> let buffer_lens = Lens::from_view_set(
>     |s: EditorState| s.buffer.clone(),
>     |(s, buf)| EditorState { buffer: buf, ..s }
> );
> 
> let cursor_lens = Lens::from_view_set(
>     |buf: Buffer| buf.cursor,
>     |(buf, c)| Buffer { cursor: c, ..buf }
> );
> 
> let composed = Composed::new(buffer_lens, cursor_lens);
> // Use via Optic trait for composition
> ```
> 
> #### Pattern 2: Optional Field Access (editor.clipboard)
> ```rust
> let clipboard_prism = Prism::new(
>     |s: EditorState| s.clipboard.as_ref().map(|c| c.clone()).ok_or(EditorState { clipboard: None, ..s }),
>     |text| EditorState { clipboard: Some(text), ../* default */ }
> );
> 
> // Try to access
> match clipboard_prism.preview(state) {
>     Ok(clipboard_text) =&gt; { /* work with text */ },
>     Err(state_unchanged) =&gt; { /* no clipboard */ }
> }
> 
> // Update if present
> let state2 = clipboard_prism.set(state, "new content".to_string());
> ```
> 
> #### Pattern 3: Collection Traversal (editor.open_files)
> ```rust
> #[derive(Clone)]
> struct OpenFilesTraversal;
> 
> impl&lt;'a&gt; TraversalFunc&lt;'a, EditorState, EditorState, File, File&gt; 
>     for OpenFilesTraversal 
> {
>     fn apply&lt;M: Applicative&gt;(
>         &amp;self,
>         f: Box&lt;dyn Fn(File) -&gt; Apply!(&lt;M as Kind!( type Of&lt;'b, U: 'b&gt;: 'b; )&gt;::Of&lt;'a, File&gt;) + 'a&gt;,
>         s: EditorState,
>     ) -&gt; Apply!(&lt;M as Kind!( type Of&lt;'b, U: 'b&gt;: 'b; )&gt;::Of&lt;'a, EditorState&gt;) {
>         s.open_files.into_iter().fold(
>             M::pure(EditorState { open_files: vec![], ..s }),
>             |acc, file| {
>                 M::lift2(
>                     |mut state: EditorState, updated_file: File| {
>                         state.open_files.push(updated_file);
>                         state
>                     },
>                     acc,
>                     f(file),
>                 )
>             }
>         )
>     }
> }
> 
> let traversal = Traversal::&lt;'_, RcBrand, EditorState, EditorState, File, File, _&gt;::new(
>     OpenFilesTraversal
> );
> ```
> 
> #### Pattern 4: Indexed Collection Traversal (with position)
> ```rust
> let traversal = Traversed::&lt;VecBrand&gt;(PhantomData);  // VecBrand has TraversableWithIndex
> 
> let f = Box::new(|i: usize, file: File| -&gt; Option&lt;File&gt; {
>     // Update file based on position
>     Some(File { 
>         id: file.id + (i as i32),
>         ..file 
>     })
> });
> 
> let result: Option&lt;EditorState&gt; = IndexedTraversalFunc::apply::&lt;OptionBrand&gt;(
>     &amp;/* create IndexedTraversal over open_files */,
>     f,
>     editor_state
> );
> ```
> 
> #### Pattern 5: Read-Only Monoid Fold (count all errors across files)
> ```rust
> let count_errors = IterableFoldFn(|state: EditorState| {
>     state.open_files.iter().flat_map(|f| f.errors.clone())
> });
> 
> let error_count: usize = count_errors.apply::&lt;usize&gt;(
>     |_error| 1,  // monoid operation: count
>     state
> );
> ```
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 7. HANDLER STATE UPDATE AS MONADIC OPERATION
> 
> Example: Fallible sequential state updates
> ```rust
> use fp_library::{brands::*, functions::*};
> use fp_macros::m_do;
> 
> fn update_handler_state(initial: EditorState) -&gt; Option&lt;EditorState&gt; {
>     m_do!(OptionBrand {
>         // Check if we can parse the buffer
>         ast &lt;- parse_buffer(&amp;initial.buffer);
>         
>         // Only if successful, update cursor based on AST
>         new_cursor &lt;- validate_cursor(ast, initial.cursor);
>         
>         // Update diagnostic list (could fail)
>         diagnostics &lt;- compute_diagnostics(&amp;ast);
>         
>         pure(EditorState {
>             buffer: initial.buffer.clone(),
>             cursor: new_cursor,
>             diagnostics,
>             ..initial
>         })
>     })
> }
> ```
> 
> ---
> 
> ### 8. KEY LIMITATIONS AND NOTES
> 
> | Feature | Available | Notes |
> |---------|-----------|-------|
> | By-reference view | No | All optics use S by-value |
> | Composed optics | Yes | Via Composed struct, use Optic::evaluate |
> | Indexed optics | Yes | IndexedLens, IndexedTraversal with position |
> | Optional access | Yes | Prism for sum types, AffineTraversal for optional fields |
> | Collection access | Yes | Traversal + Applicative, IterableFoldFn for read-only |
> | Monad/Applicative | Yes | Use m_do!/a_do! macros for sequencing |
> | Laws guaranteed | Yes | GetPut, PutGet, PutPut maintained by profunctor encoding |
> 
> ---
>

>

> - **Prism**: `/Users/lane/.cargo/registry/src/index.crates.io-1949cf8c6b5b557f/fp-library-0.15.0/src/types/optics/prism.rs` (lines 104-189: preview/review)

> - **IndexedLens**: `/Users/lane/.cargo/registry/src/index.crates.io-1949cf8c6b5b557f/fp-library-0.15.0/src/types/optics/indexed_lens.rs` (iview with position)
> - **IndexedTraversal**: `/Users/lane/.cargo/registry/src/index.crates.io-1949cf8c6b5b557f/fp-library-0.15.0/src/types/optics/indexed_traversal.rs` (IndexedTraversalFunc)
> - **Composed**: `/Users/lane/.cargo/registry/src/index.crates.io-1949cf8c6b5b557f/fp-library-0.15.0/src/types/optics/composed.rs` (lens1 . lens2)
> - **AffineTraversal**: `/Users/lane/.cargo/registry/src/index.crates.io-1949cf8c6b5b557f/fp-library-0.15.0/src/types/optics/affine.rs` (optional without review)
> - **Fold**: `/Users/lane/.cargo/registry/src/index.crates.io-1949cf8c6b5b557f/fp-library-0.15.0/src/types/optics/fold.rs` (IterableFoldFn for monoid collection)
> - **Monad**: `/Users/lane/.cargo/registry/src/index.crates.io-1949cf8c6b5b557f/fp-library-0.15.0/src/classes/monad.rs` (m_do! macro, bind)
> - **Your current usage**: `/Users/lane/src/lin/pane/crates/pane-proto/src/property.rs` (Lens + Getter pattern)
> 
> ---
> 
> ### Summary
> 
> fp-library gives you a complete, composable optics system rooted in profunctors. Your current Lens/Getter approach is solid. To extend pane's handler state modeling:
> 
> 1. **Optional fields**: Switch from `Option&lt;T&gt;` manual handling to `Prism` or `AffineTraversal`
> 2. **Collections**: Use `Traversal` with `Applicative` for structured updates across all items
> 3. **Position tracking**: Use `IndexedLens`/`IndexedTraversal` when you need element positions
> 4. **Nested access**: Compose via `Composed::new(outer, inner)` 
> 5. **Fallible updates**: Wrap in `Option`/`Result` and use `m_do!` monad notation
> 6. **Read-only aggregation**: Use `Fold` with monoids for counts, concatenation, etc.
> 
> The by-value design is intentional—it preserves composability and safety. All optic laws are maintained via profunctor encoding, guaranteeing that your namespace view never diverges from handler state.</result>

> # pane-app Looper Design: BeOS Fidelity + EAct Verification
> 
> ## 1. The Looper: BLooper::task_looper() Step by Step
> 
> Here's exactly what Haiku's `task_looper()` does (from `/Users/lane/src/haiku/src/kits/app/Looper.cpp`, line 1162), with the pane mapping for each step.
> 
> ### BLooper::task_looper() — The Anatomy
> 
> ```
> _task0_()                         // thread entry point (line 1067)
>   looper-&gt;Lock()                  // acquire BLooper lock
>   looper-&gt;task_looper()           // the actual loop
>   delete looper                   // looper destroyed after loop exits
> ```
> 
> Inside `task_looper()`:
> 
> **Step 1: Unlock the looper (line 1168)**
> The looper arrives locked from `_task0_`. It immediately unlocks. This is a protocol detail — the lock was held to prevent messages from being dispatched before the looper was fully initialized. The unlock signals "I'm ready to receive."
> 
> **Step 2: Outer loop — block on port (line 1174-1194)**
> ```cpp
> while (!fTerminating) {
>     BMessage* msg = MessageFromPort();    // blocking read, infinite timeout
>     if (msg) _AddMessagePriv(msg);        // enqueue
> 
>     // Drain: read ALL pending messages from port (non-blocking)
>     int32 msgCount = port_count(fMsgPort);
>     for (int32 i = 0; i &lt; msgCount; ++i) {
>         msg = MessageFromPort(0);         // zero timeout = non-blocking
>         if (msg) _AddMessagePriv(msg);
>     }
> ```
> 
> This is the **batch acquisition** pattern. Block until at least one message arrives, then drain everything that's pending. This is exactly what pane needs — and it maps directly to calloop's dispatch model.
> 
> **Step 3: Inner loop — dispatch from queue (line 1199-1277)**
> ```cpp
> while (!fTerminating &amp;&amp; dispatchNextMessage) {
>     BMessage* message = fDirectTarget-&gt;Queue()-&gt;NextMessage();
>     Lock();
>     fLastMessage = message;
> 
>     if (fLastMessage == NULL) {
>         dispatchNextMessage = false;    // queue empty, back to outer loop
>     } else {
>         // 3a. Resolve handler target
>         BHandler* handler = ...;        // by token or preferred handler
> 
>         // 3b. Scripting specifier resolution
>         if (handler &amp;&amp; fLastMessage-&gt;HasSpecifiers())
>             handler = resolve_specifier(handler, fLastMessage);
> 
>         // 3c. Filter chain
>         if (handler) {
>             handler = _TopLevelFilter(fLastMessage, handler);
>             // 3d. Dispatch
>             if (handler &amp;&amp; handler-&gt;Looper() == this)
>                 DispatchMessage(fLastMessage, handler);
>         }
>     }
> 
>     // 3e. Check termination, cleanup
>     if (fTerminating) return;     // leave locked — Quit() sequence
>     fLastMessage = NULL;
>     Unlock();
>     delete message;
> 
>     // 3f. Check if port has new messages — if so, break to outer loop
>     if (port_count(fMsgPort) &gt; 0)
>         dispatchNextMessage = false;    // drain port before continuing queue
> }
> ```
> 
> **Step 3a — Handler resolution**: In BeOS, each message carried a target token (handler ID) or could use the "preferred" handler. Pane doesn't need this — there's one Handler per pane, not a chain. The routing is by protocol (ServiceId), not by handler identity.
> 
> **Step 3b — Specifier resolution**: `resolve_specifier` walked the BHandler chain for scripting. Pane replaces this with pane-fs (filesystem IS the scripting protocol). Not needed in the looper.
> 
> **Step 3c — Filter chain**: `_TopLevelFilter` applies looper-level filters first, then handler-specific filters. The filter can retarget to a different handler (handler chain), consume the message, or pass it through. Pane's filter chain is simpler: per-protocol `MessageFilter&lt;M&gt;` in registration order, no handler retargeting (single handler per pane).
> 
> **Step 3d — Dispatch**: `DispatchMessage` is a switch on `message-&gt;what`. The `_QUIT_` message sets `fTerminating`. `B_QUIT_REQUESTED` calls `QuitRequested()`. Everything else calls `handler-&gt;MessageReceived(message)`. Pane's dispatch is the fn-pointer table: each protocol's `Handles&lt;P&gt;::receive` pointer, captured at `open_service` time.
> 
> **Step 3f — Port re-check**: After dispatching the entire queue, if new messages arrived on the port during dispatch, jump back to the outer loop to drain the port again. This prevents starvation — new messages get batched rather than queuing behind old ones. Pane maps this to calloop: after processing one batch, re-poll all event sources.
> 
> ### Pane's Looper — Concrete Design
> 
> Here's the sketch. The looper is an internal struct, never pub. `run_with` constructs it and calls `run()`:
> 
> ```rust
> /// Internal to pane-app. Never exposed across the crate boundary.
> struct Looper&lt;H: Handler&gt; {
>     handler: H,
>     messenger: Messenger,
>     dispatch: Dispatch&lt;H&gt;,
>     service_table: ServiceDispatchTable,
>     filters: FilterChain,
>     // calloop EventLoop handle + sources are local to run()
> }
> 
> /// Service dispatch: fn pointer captured at open_service time.
> /// Type-erased at storage time — the fn pointer is
> /// `fn(&amp;mut H, P::Message) -&gt; Flow` wrapped in a closure that
> /// deserializes from raw bytes.
> struct ServiceDispatchEntry {
>     service_id: ServiceId,
>     session_id: u8,
>     /// Deserialize + dispatch. Takes raw postcard bytes.
>     dispatch_fn: Box&lt;dyn FnMut(&amp;mut dyn std::any::Any, &amp;[u8]) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;,
> }
> 
> impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; Looper&lt;H&gt; {
>     fn run(mut self) -&gt; ! {
>         // BeOS step 1: "unlock" — signal readiness
>         // Pane: send Ready lifecycle event to handler
>         let flow = self.dispatch_lifecycle(LifecycleMessage::Ready);
>         if flow == Flow::Stop {
>             self.shutdown(ExitReason::Graceful);
>         }
> 
>         // BeOS step 2+3: the event loop
>         // calloop replaces the port_read + queue drain pattern.
>         // EventLoop::dispatch() blocks until events, drains all
>         // ready sources into callbacks, returns.
>         //
>         // This IS the outer+inner loop collapsed: calloop's
>         // dispatch reads from all fd sources (connections, timers,
>         // signals), fires callbacks that enqueue into a local
>         // batch, then we process the batch sequentially.
> 
>         let mut event_loop: EventLoop&lt;LooperState&lt;H&gt;&gt; = /* calloop setup */;
>         let mut state = LooperState {
>             looper: self,
>             batch: Vec::new(),
>             exit: None,
>         };
> 
>         loop {
>             // Outer loop: block until at least one event
>             // (BeOS: MessageFromPort with infinite timeout)
>             event_loop.dispatch(None, &amp;mut state).unwrap_or_else(|e| {
>                 state.exit = Some(ExitReason::InfraError);
>             });
> 
>             if let Some(reason) = state.exit.take() {
>                 state.looper.shutdown(reason);
>             }
> 
>             // Inner loop: process the batch
>             // (BeOS: drain queue, dispatch each message)
>             let batch = std::mem::take(&amp;mut state.batch);
>             for event in batch {
>                 let flow = state.looper.dispatch_event(event);
>                 match flow {
>                     Flow::Continue =&gt; {}
>                     Flow::Stop =&gt; state.looper.shutdown(ExitReason::Graceful),
>                 }
>             }
> 
>             // BeOS step 3f: calloop handles this naturally —
>             // after processing the batch, we loop back to
>             // event_loop.dispatch() which re-polls all sources.
> 
>             // State snapshot for pane-fs (post-batch, not per-event)
>             state.looper.update_snapshot();
>         }
>     }
> 
>     fn dispatch_event(&amp;mut self, event: LooperEvent) -&gt; Flow {
>         match event {
>             LooperEvent::Lifecycle(msg) =&gt; self.dispatch_lifecycle(msg),
>             LooperEvent::Service { session_id, payload } =&gt; {
>                 self.dispatch_service(session_id, &amp;payload)
>             }
>             LooperEvent::Reply { connection, token, payload } =&gt; {
>                 self.dispatch_reply(connection, token, payload)
>             }
>             LooperEvent::Timer(id) =&gt; self.dispatch_lifecycle(LifecycleMessage::Pulse),
>             LooperEvent::ConnectionLost(conn_id) =&gt; {
>                 self.dispatch.fail_connection(conn_id, &amp;mut self.handler, &amp;self.messenger);
>                 self.dispatch_lifecycle(LifecycleMessage::Disconnected)
>             }
>         }
>     }
> 
>     fn dispatch_lifecycle(&amp;mut self, msg: LifecycleMessage) -&gt; Flow {
>         // BeOS step 3c: filter chain
>         let msg = match self.filters.apply_lifecycle(&amp;msg) {
>             FilterAction::Pass =&gt; msg,
>             FilterAction::Transform(m) =&gt; m,
>             FilterAction::Consume =&gt; return Flow::Continue,
>         };
> 
>         // BeOS step 3d: dispatch with catch_unwind
>         match std::panic::catch_unwind(std::panic::AssertUnwindSafe(|| {
>             &lt;H as Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt;&gt;::receive(&amp;mut self.handler, msg)
>         })) {
>             Ok(flow) =&gt; flow,
>             Err(_panic) =&gt; {
>                 // Handler is now potentially poisoned.
>                 // Drop handler (obligation compensation fires),
>                 // then exit with Failed.
>                 self.shutdown(ExitReason::Failed);
>             }
>         }
>     }
> 
>     fn dispatch_service(&amp;mut self, session_id: u8, payload: &amp;[u8]) -&gt; Flow {
>         // Look up the fn pointer from the service dispatch table
>         let entry = match self.service_table.get_mut(session_id) {
>             Some(e) =&gt; e,
>             None =&gt; return Flow::Continue, // I12: unknown discriminant
>         };
> 
>         // Filters run on the deserialized message (per-protocol typed filter)
>         // The dispatch_fn handles deserialization + filter + handler call
>         match std::panic::catch_unwind(std::panic::AssertUnwindSafe(|| {
>             (entry.dispatch_fn)(&amp;mut self.handler as &amp;mut dyn std::any::Any, payload)
>         })) {
>             Ok(flow) =&gt; flow,
>             Err(_) =&gt; self.shutdown(ExitReason::Failed),
>         }
>     }
> 
>     fn shutdown(mut self, reason: ExitReason) -&gt; ! {
>         // Destruction sequence (spec section "Destruction sequence")
>         // Step 1: fail_connection for lost connections
>         // (already done for ConnectionLost events)
> 
>         // Step 2: clear remaining dispatch entries
>         self.dispatch.clear();
> 
>         // Step 3: drop handler — obligation compensation fires
>         drop(self.handler);
> 
>         // Step 4: server notified via PaneExited
>         // (connection close sends the reason)
> 
>         std::process::exit(match reason {
>             ExitReason::Graceful =&gt; 0,
>             ExitReason::Disconnected =&gt; 1,
>             ExitReason::Failed =&gt; 101,
>             ExitReason::InfraError =&gt; 102,
>         });
>     }
> 
>     fn update_snapshot(&amp;self) {
>         // Clone handler state into the pane-fs snapshot.
>         // AttrReader&lt;S&gt; reads from this snapshot.
>         // This happens once per batch, not per-event.
>         // (No BeOS equivalent — this is pane-fs's contribution.)
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> **Key differences from BLooper, and why:**
> 
> 1. **No lock/unlock dance.** BLooper locked itself during dispatch so other threads (BMessenger::SendMessage from another thread) couldn't modify state during dispatch. Pane's model is single-threaded per actor (I6) — the calloop sources are the only entry points, and they're sequenced by calloop. No mutex needed.
> 
> 2. **No handler chain.** BLooper had a list of BHandlers, messages routed by token. Pane has one Handler with multiple `Handles&lt;P&gt;` impls. The routing is by protocol (ServiceId -&gt; fn pointer), not by handler identity.
> 
> 3. **catch_unwind per dispatch, not per loop iteration.** BLooper had no catch_unwind — a crash in MessageReceived killed the thread, and _task0_ caught it to delete the looper. Pane wraps each dispatch in catch_unwind (the E-Raise boundary).
> 
> 4. **Batch-then-snapshot.** BLooper had no concept of state snapshots. Pane takes a snapshot after each batch completes — pane-fs reads from the snapshot, not from live handler state. This is the optic discipline in action.
> 
> 5. **Port re-check maps to calloop re-poll.** BLooper's "check port_count, if &gt;0 break to outer loop" pattern maps naturally to calloop: after processing the batch, `event_loop.dispatch()` re-polls all sources.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 2. EAct Invariants — Where Each Must Be Enforced
> 
> ### System Invariants
> 
> | Invariant | Where enforced | Mechanism |
> |-----------|---------------|-----------|
> | **I1**: `panic = unwind` | **Cargo.toml** (`[profile] panic = "unwind"`) | Convention. The pane-app crate should assert this at init time via `cfg!(panic = "unwind")` compile check or a build.rs gate. Backstop: fd hangup detection on server side. |
> | **I2**: No blocking in handlers | **Convention**. Cannot be enforced by types. Documentation + runtime instrumentation (optional: track dispatch time, log warnings if handler takes &gt;Nms). This is a bet the same way BeOS bet on it. |
> | **I3**: Handlers terminate | **Convention**. Same as I2 — the handler must return `Flow`. The type system enforces the return type exists; it cannot enforce termination. |
> | **I4**: Typestate handles: `#[must_use]` + Drop | **Type system** (compile-time). Already implemented: `#[must_use]` on ServiceHandle, Pane, PaneBuilder, TimerToken. Drop impls send failure terminals. This is **fully satisfied** by the existing code in pane-proto and pane-app. |
> | **I5**: Filters see only Clone-safe Messages | **Type system** (compile-time). Already satisfied: `MessageFilter&lt;M: Message&gt;` requires `M: Clone`. Obligation handles don't implement Message (they're !Clone, !Serialize). The trait bounds enforce this at the crate boundary. |
> | **I6**: Single-thread dispatch per pane | **Architecture** (calloop). The looper owns `&amp;mut H` and never sends it across threads. calloop is single-threaded. `run_with` returns `-&gt; !`, so the caller can't regain the Handler. **Enforced by ownership** — H moves into the looper, no reference escapes. |
> | **I7**: Sequential fn-pointer dispatch | **Follows from I6**. The looper calls one dispatch function at a time, sequentially within the batch loop. No concurrency exists within the looper. |
> | **I8**: `send_and_wait` panics from looper thread | **Runtime check**. Thread-local flag set when entering looper dispatch, checked in `send_and_wait`. Must be implemented in Messenger. Sketch: `thread_local! { static IN_DISPATCH: Cell&lt;bool&gt; = Cell::new(false); }` |
> | **I9**: Dispatch cleared before handler drop | **Looper shutdown sequence** (runtime). The `shutdown()` method calls `self.dispatch.clear()` before `drop(self.handler)`. Order is structural in the shutdown code — but it's code, not a type guarantee. A `#[cfg(test)]` assertion could verify. |
> | **I10**: Chan Drop non-blocking | **pane-session convention**. Best-effort `let _ = write(...)` in Drop impl. Not enforceable by types. |
> | **I11**: ProtocolAbort checked at framing | **Framing layer** (pane-session or connection source). Before postcard deserialization, check for `[0xFF][0xFF]` magic. Implementation lives in the calloop source callback. |
> | **I12**: Unknown service discriminant | **Looper dispatch_service**. The match on `session_id` against `service_table` — the `None` arm. Already sketched above. |
> | **I13**: open_service blocks | **PaneBuilder::open_service** (existing code). Happens before the looper runs. Structural: PaneBuilder is consumed by `run_with`, so no open_service can happen after dispatch starts. **Fully enforced by the type system.** |
> 
> ### Dispatch Entry Invariants
> 
> | Invariant | Where enforced | Mechanism |
> |-----------|---------------|-----------|
> | **S1**: Token uniqueness | **Dispatch::insert** (existing code). `next_token` is a monotonic counter. Already implemented — `Token(self.next_token)` with `self.next_token += 1`. |
> | **S2**: Sequential dispatch | **Follows from I6/I7**. Dispatch callbacks share `&amp;mut H` — Rust's borrow checker prevents concurrent access. The looper calls one callback at a time. |
> | **S3**: Control-before-events | **Batch ordering**. When building the batch from calloop events, control protocol events must be sorted before service events within the same batch. This is a **runtime convention in the batch builder**. |
> | **S4**: Connection loss + destruction | **Looper::dispatch_event** for ConnectionLost + **Looper::shutdown**. `dispatch.fail_connection()` fires on_failed for matching entries. `dispatch.clear()` drops the rest. Already implemented in Dispatch. |
> | **S5**: Cancel removes without callbacks | **Dispatch::cancel** (existing code). Returns `bool`, does not fire callbacks. |
> | **S6**: `panic = unwind` | Same as I1. |
> 
> ### Summary of enforcement categories:
> 
> - **Type system (compile-time)**: I4, I5, I6, I7, I13, S2
> - **Architecture (structural)**: I9, S3, S4
> - **Runtime check needed**: I8, I11, I12
> - **Convention (documentation + discipline)**: I1, I2, I3, I10
> - **Already implemented**: I4, I5, I13, S1, S5
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 3. The Messenger Problem
> 
> ### How BeOS Did It
> 
> In BeOS, `BHandler` had a direct back-pointer to its `BLooper`:
> 
> ```cpp
> // Handler.h line 103
> BLooper* fLooper;
> ```
> 
> When `BLooper::AddHandler(handler)` was called (line 387 of Looper.cpp), it called `handler-&gt;SetLooper(this)` — installing the back-pointer. A handler could then:
> 
> - `Looper()-&gt;PostMessage(msg)` — post to its own looper
> - `BMessenger(this)` — construct a messenger targeting itself
> - `BMessenger(looper)` — construct a messenger targeting the looper
> 
> The back-pointer was set by the framework at AddHandler time and cleared at RemoveHandler time. The handler always knew who owned it.
> 
> ### The Pane Constraint
> 
> In pane, `Handler` is defined in pane-proto (no IO, no runtime). `Messenger` is defined in pane-app (has connections, calloop, IO). The handler trait can't reference Messenger — crate boundary.
> 
> But the architecture spec already has the answer (line 247-249):
> 
> &gt; The handler communicates with the framework through a Messenger stored in its own state (typically `self.messenger`), set up during the PaneBuilder phase — not passed as a dispatch parameter.
> 
> This is the right call, and here's why it's actually *better* than BHandler's back-pointer:
> 
> ### The Design
> 
> **Messenger is created by PaneBuilder and handed to the handler constructor.** The handler stores it in its own fields. The developer example in the spec already shows this:
> 
> ```rust
> struct StatusAgent {
>     messenger: Messenger,     // stored in handler state
> }
> 
> fn main() {
>     let app = App::connect("com.ops.status");
>     let pane = app.create_pane(Tag::new("Server Status")).wait();
>     let mut builder = pane.setup::&lt;StatusAgent&gt;();
>     let messenger = builder.messenger();    // &lt;-- PaneBuilder produces it
>     builder.run_with(StatusAgent { messenger })
> }
> ```
> 
> `PaneBuilder::messenger()` creates a Messenger with the pane's identity (Handle) and service router baked in. The handler stores it. During dispatch, the handler uses `self.messenger` — no parameter passing needed.
> 
> **Why this is better than BHandler::fLooper:**
> 
> 1. **Explicit, not magical.** In BeOS, `fLooper` was set behind the handler's back by AddHandler. If you forgot to add a handler to a looper, `fLooper` was NULL and you crashed at runtime. In pane, the handler stores the Messenger explicitly — if you forget to pass it, you have a compilation error (the struct field isn't initialized).
> 
> 2. **No crate boundary problem.** The Handler trait in pane-proto doesn't need to know about Messenger. The concrete handler struct does, but that's the developer's code, not the trait.
> 
> 3. **Cloneable.** Messenger is Clone + Send (per the spec). The handler can clone it and hand it to a worker thread. BLooper's back-pointer was not threadsafe — you had to construct a BMessenger from it, which was an extra step that developers constantly forgot.
> 
> ### But What About Dispatch&lt;H&gt; Callbacks?
> 
> The `Dispatch&lt;H&gt;` entries receive `&amp;Messenger` as a parameter:
> 
> ```rust
> pub on_reply: Box&lt;dyn FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, Box&lt;dyn Any + Send&gt;) -&gt; Flow + Send&gt;,
> ```
> 
> This `&amp;Messenger` is passed by the looper at fire_reply time. Two question marks:
> 
> 1. **Which Messenger?** The looper's copy. When the handler calls `self.messenger.send_request(...)`, the request passes through the looper (via LooperSender). The looper creates the DispatchEntry and stores the looper's Messenger reference for passing to callbacks.
> 
> 2. **Is this the same Messenger the handler has in self?** Yes, conceptually — same pane identity, same service router. The looper holds its own clone.
> 
> **The wiring:**
> 
> ```rust
> impl&lt;H: Handler&gt; PaneBuilder&lt;H&gt; {
>     /// Create the Messenger for this pane. Can be called once.
>     /// The Messenger contains:
>     /// - Handle (pane identity: id, connection writer)
>     /// - ServiceRouter (ServiceId -&gt; ConnectionId mapping)
>     /// - LooperSender (channel back to the looper for dispatch registration)
>     pub fn messenger(&amp;self) -&gt; Messenger {
>         Messenger {
>             handle: self.pane.handle.clone(),
>             service_router: self.service_router.clone(),
>             looper_tx: self.looper_tx.clone(),
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> When the looper runs, it also holds a Messenger (cloned from the same source). The handler's `self.messenger` and the looper's messenger are both live clones of the same identity.
> 
> ### Interaction with State Snapshots
> 
> The Messenger is NOT part of the snapshot state. The snapshot is for pane-fs attribute reads — it's the handler's data state (buffer contents, cursor position, etc.), not the framework wiring. Messenger contains channel senders and connection handles — not serializable, not meaningful to external readers.
> 
> The snapshot mechanism works on `&amp;H` (the handler's public attributes via `AttrReader&lt;S&gt;`). Messenger is a framework handle, not a data attribute. They're orthogonal.
> 
> ### Concrete Messenger Structure
> 
> ```rust
> pub struct Messenger {
>     handle: Handle,              // pane identity (id, connection writer)
>     service_router: ServiceRouter, // ServiceId -&gt; ConnectionId
>     looper_tx: LooperSender,     // channel to looper for dispatch ops
> }
> 
> impl Messenger {
>     pub fn set_content(&amp;self, data: &amp;[u8]) {
>         // Write directly to the primary connection (handle.writer)
>         // This doesn't go through the looper — it's a fire-and-forget
>         // write to the server. The content bytes are the pane's body.
>         let _ = self.handle.send_content(data);
>     }
> 
>     pub fn set_pulse_rate(&amp;self, duration: Duration) -&gt; TimerToken {
>         // Tell the looper to register a calloop timer.
>         // The looper creates the timer source and maps it to
>         // LifecycleMessage::Pulse.
>         let (tx, token_id) = /* one-shot channel for ack */;
>         let _ = self.looper_tx.send(LooperCmd::SetTimer { duration, ack: tx });
>         let id = token_id.recv();
>         TimerToken {
>             id,
>             looper_tx: self.looper_tx.clone(),
>         }
>     }
> 
>     pub fn post_app_message&lt;T: AppPayload&gt;(&amp;self, msg: T) {
>         // Send a fire-and-forget message to the looper's queue.
>         // AppPayload: Clone + Send + 'static (no obligations smuggled).
>         let _ = self.looper_tx.send(LooperCmd::AppMessage {
>             payload: Box::new(msg),
>         });
>     }
> 
>     pub fn send_request&lt;H, R&gt;(
>         &amp;self,
>         target: &amp;Messenger,
>         msg: impl Serialize + Send + 'static,
>         on_reply: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger, R) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>         on_failed: impl FnOnce(&amp;mut H, &amp;Messenger) -&gt; Flow + Send + 'static,
>     ) -&gt; CancelHandle
>     where
>         H: Handler + 'static,
>         R: DeserializeOwned + Send + 'static,
>     {
>         // E-Suspend: install dispatch entry via looper_tx
>         let _ = self.looper_tx.send(LooperCmd::SendRequest {
>             target_connection: target.handle.connection_id(),
>             payload: postcard::to_allocvec(&amp;msg).unwrap(),
>             on_reply: Box::new(move |h, m, raw| {
>                 let reply: R = postcard::from_bytes(&amp;raw).unwrap();
>                 on_reply(h, m, reply)
>             }),
>             on_failed: Box::new(on_failed),
>         });
>         // Returns CancelHandle with the token
>         CancelHandle { /* ... */ }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 4. Missing Pieces for a Minimal Viable Looper
> 
> In order of dependency:
> 
> ### Must have before the looper can run
> 
> 1. **`LooperSender` / `LooperCmd` enum.** The internal channel from Messenger to the looper. This is a calloop channel (calloop provides `channel::Channel&lt;T&gt;` as an event source). The enum:
> 
> ```rust
> enum LooperCmd {
>     SetTimer { duration: Duration, ack: oneshot::Sender&lt;TimerId&gt; },
>     CancelTimer { id: TimerId },
>     AppMessage { payload: Box&lt;dyn std::any::Any + Send&gt; },
>     SendRequest { /* fields from Messenger::send_request */ },
>     CancelRequest { connection: ConnectionId, token: Token },
>     RevokeInterest { session_id: u8 },
> }
> ```
> 
> 2. **`Handle` type.** The pane identity + connection writer. Currently a TODO in Messenger. Minimal: `{ id: Id, writer: UnixStream /* or Arc&lt;Mutex&lt;Writer&gt;&gt; */ }`.
> 
> 3. **`ServiceRouter` type.** Maps `ServiceId -&gt; ConnectionId`. One entry per service. Currently a TODO. Minimal: `HashMap&lt;ServiceId, ConnectionId&gt;`.
> 
> 4. **`ServiceDispatchTable` type.** Maps `session_id: u8 -&gt; dispatch_fn`. Built during `open_service`, frozen at `run_with`. This is the static part of sigma (EAct handler store).
> 
> 5. **`FilterChain` type.** Stores `Vec&lt;Box&lt;dyn MessageFilter&lt;LifecycleMessage&gt;&gt;&gt;` plus per-service filter lists keyed by ServiceId. The spec says filters run in registration order.
> 
> 6. **`LooperEvent` enum.** The unified batch element. Built from calloop callbacks:
> 
> ```rust
> enum LooperEvent {
>     Lifecycle(LifecycleMessage),
>     Service { session_id: u8, payload: Vec&lt;u8&gt; },
>     Reply { connection: ConnectionId, token: Token, payload: Vec&lt;u8&gt; },
>     ReplyFailed { connection: ConnectionId, token: Token },
>     Timer(TimerId),
>     ConnectionLost(ConnectionId),
>     AppMessage(Box&lt;dyn std::any::Any + Send&gt;),
> }
> ```
> 
> 7. **Connection / transport.** At minimum, a unix socket reader/writer with the framing protocol (`[length: u32][service: u8][payload]`). This can start as a single connection to a single server.
> 
> ### Can defer
> 
> - **App type.** For the minimal looper, you can hard-code a connection. App::connect() is ergonomic surface, not structural.
> - **Multi-server.** Start with one connection.
> - **Handshake.** Can be stubbed initially — hard-code Welcome parameters.
> - **Session suspension/resumption.** Deferrable.
> - **`send_and_wait`.** The async `send_request` path is the primary. Sync can wait.
> - **pane-fs snapshots.** The snapshot update point is designed (end of batch), but the actual AttrReader/snapshot machinery is pane-fs's concern. The looper just needs a hook point.
> 
> ### The Minimal Viable Build Order
> 
> 1. `LooperCmd` enum + calloop channel source
> 2. `Handle` + framing (single unix socket)
> 3. `ServiceDispatchTable` (static fn-pointer table from PaneBuilder)
> 4. `Looper&lt;H&gt;::run()` — the event loop with lifecycle dispatch only
> 5. Wire up Messenger's `set_content`, `set_pulse_rate`, `post_app_message`
> 6. Service dispatch (deserialize + fn-pointer call)
> 7. Dispatch&lt;H&gt; integration (send_request / reply correlation)
> 8. FilterChain
> 
> Step 1-4 gets you a running looper that handles lifecycle events. Step 5-6 gets you service dispatch. Step 7 gets you request/reply. Step 8 gets you filters.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Key Observations
> 
> **The calloop channel IS the port.** BLooper had `fMsgPort` (a kernel port). Pane's equivalent is the calloop channel that carries `LooperCmd`. The connection sockets are additional calloop sources. calloop unifies them into a single poll — which is exactly BLooper's "check port, drain queue, dispatch" pattern, but generalized to multiple event sources.
> 
> **catch_unwind wraps the ENTIRE dispatch, not individual steps.** In the Haiku code, there's no exception handling in task_looper — a crash kills the thread. Pane wraps each dispatch_event call in catch_unwind. This means the panic boundary is per-event, not per-batch. If event #3 in a batch of 10 panics, events 4-10 are dropped (the looper is shutting down). This is correct — a poisoned handler should not process more events.
> 
> **The batch/snapshot cadence is new.** BLooper dispatched one message at a time, checking the port between each. Pane dispatches a batch, then snapshots. This means pane-fs sees a consistent state after all coalesced events have been applied — which is strictly better for external readers than seeing intermediate states.
> 
> **Messenger in the handler is the BHandler::fLooper of pane.** But explicit, cloneable, and thread-safe. The back-pointer is replaced by a forward-pointer: the developer constructs the handler with its Messenger, rather than the framework injecting one after the fact.</result>

> </task-notification>
