> <summary>Agent "Spec pane-fs with optics agent" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have a very complete picture. Let me assemble the design.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here is the complete pane-fs namespace design, grounded in what the code and specs currently say, with Plan 9 precedent analysis for each decision.
> 
> ## 1. Directory Structure
> 
> The full tree, with read/write semantics for every entry.
> 
> ```
> /pane/
> ├── index                       # pane index (cf. acme /dev/index)
> ├── self -&gt; &lt;n&gt;/                # process-to-pane symlink (cf. /proc/self)
> ├── by-uuid/
> │   └── &lt;uuid&gt; -&gt; /pane/&lt;n&gt;/   # stable identity (cf. /dev/disk/by-uuid)
> ├── by-sig/
> │   └── &lt;signature&gt;/
> │       └── &lt;n&gt; -&gt; /pane/&lt;n&gt;/  # per-application filter (cf. BFS live query)
> ├── by-type/
> │   └── &lt;type&gt;/
> │       └── &lt;n&gt; -&gt; /pane/&lt;n&gt;/
> ├── local/                      # local-only view
> │   └── &lt;n&gt; -&gt; /pane/&lt;n&gt;/
> ├── remote/                     # remote-only view
> │   └── &lt;host&gt;/
> │       └── &lt;n&gt; -&gt; /pane/&lt;n&gt;/
> ├── &lt;n&gt;/                        # per-pane directory
> │   ├── tag                     # title text
> │   ├── body                    # semantic content
> │   ├── attrs/                  # named attributes
> │   │   ├── signature           # e.g. "com.pane.editor"
> │   │   ├── type                # e.g. "editor"
> │   │   ├── dirty               # "true" / "false"
> │   │   ├── &lt;user-declared&gt;     # handler-declared attributes
> │   │   └── ...
> │   ├── attrs.json              # bulk read: all attrs as JSON object
> │   ├── commands/               # command vocabulary (discovery)
> │   │   ├── save                # read: metadata. write: error
> │   │   ├── close
> │   │   └── ...
> │   ├── ctl                     # control file (invocation)
> │   ├── event                   # event stream (blocking read)
> │   ├── id                      # UUID (read-only, stable identity)
> │   └── wdir                    # working directory (read/write)
> └── ...
> ```
> 
> ### Per-entry semantics
> 
> | File | Read returns | Write does | Mode | Notes |
> |------|-------------|-----------|------|-------|
> | `index` | `&lt;n&gt; &lt;uuid&gt; &lt;signature&gt; &lt;tag-line&gt;\n` per pane | error | r | cf. acme(4) index: fixed-width numeric fields + name |
> | `self` | symlink target | n/a | symlink | PID-to-pane via FUSE request context; error if PID not mapped |
> | `&lt;n&gt;/tag` | title text, newline-terminated | sets title (deliver as protocol message) | rw | cf. rio(4) label |
> | `&lt;n&gt;/body` | semantic content (text) | replaces content (deliver as protocol message) | rw | cf. acme(4) body: "text written to body is always appended" — but pane uses replace semantics, not append. Append is append-to-ctl territory |
> | `&lt;n&gt;/attrs/&lt;name&gt;` | attribute value as text, newline-terminated | parses text, routes through optic setter | rw per attr | cf. Plan 9 text-based status files |
> | `&lt;n&gt;/attrs.json` | `{"signature":"com.pane.editor","dirty":"false",...}\n` | error | r | bulk shortcut; one FUSE op for N attrs |
> | `&lt;n&gt;/commands/&lt;cmd&gt;` | `description: ...\nshortcut: ...\ngroup: ...\n` | error | r | discovery only; invocation goes through ctl |
> | `&lt;n&gt;/ctl` | error (write-only) | line command; see below | w | cf. proc(3) ctl, acme(4) ctl, rio(4) wctl |
> | `&lt;n&gt;/event` | blocking JSONL stream | error (read-only) | r | cf. acme(4) event, rio(4) wctl blocking read |
> | `&lt;n&gt;/id` | UUID as text | error | r | stable cross-machine identity |
> | `&lt;n&gt;/wdir` | working directory path | sets wdir | rw | cf. rio(4) wdir: "writable so the program may update it" |
> 
> **Design note on `ctl` being write-only.** In rio and acme, ctl is readable (returns window geometry/state) and writable (accepts commands). I recommend splitting this: the state-reading part belongs in `attrs/` (where it's individually addressable and optic-backed), and ctl is purely imperative. This is cleaner than Plan 9's convention, which overloaded ctl because they didn't have a separate attrs directory. Plan 9's proc(3) status was read-only; the ctl was write-only. Acme mixed them. Pane should follow proc, not acme, here. The attrs directory gives you everything acme-ctl-read gave you, with better granularity.
> 
> **Design note on `body` semantics.** Acme appended all writes to body. That made sense for acme (a text editor where body is a buffer). For pane, body is "the semantic content at the level the pane operates" — for a shell, the output; for an editor, the file content. Replacement semantics (write replaces content) match the optic model: body is a lens, not an append-only log. If a pane wants append semantics, it exposes that through its handler (body write -&gt; handler decides how to interpret the write). The default should be replacement.
> 
> ## 2. The ctl File
> 
> Plan 9's ctl convention: text commands, one per line, each a verb optionally followed by arguments separated by spaces. The write returns success or an error string.
> 
> ### Base command vocabulary
> 
> These are commands available to every pane, regardless of handler type. They correspond to lifecycle and framework operations.
> 
> ```
> close                    # request close (delivers CloseRequested to handler)
> stop                     # force stop (delivers Disconnected, then teardown)
> focus                    # make this pane current/focused
> hide                     # hide the pane
> show                     # unhide the pane
> pulse &lt;duration&gt;         # set pulse rate (e.g. "pulse 500ms")
> pulse off                # disable pulse
> tag &lt;text&gt;               # set title (same effect as writing to tag file)
> ```
> 
> ### Handler commands (via commands/ vocabulary)
> 
> Any command listed in `commands/` can be invoked via ctl. The ctl file does not need to know the vocabulary — it forwards unknown commands to the handler as `CommandExecuted { command, args }`.
> 
> ```
> save                     # handler-specific
> save /tmp/out.txt        # handler-specific with args
> goto 42                  # handler-specific with args
> undo                     # handler-specific
> ```
> 
> ### Structured payloads
> 
> For commands that need structured data beyond simple args:
> 
> ```
> create-command {"name":"save","description":"Save file","shortcut":"Ctrl+S"}
> ```
> 
> The parsing rule is simple and lifted straight from proc(3): the first whitespace-delimited token is the command name. Everything after the first space is the args string. The handler receives `(command, args)` and parses args as it sees fit. No special JSON handling at the ctl level — the handler decides whether args is a path, a number, or JSON. This keeps the ctl parser trivial.
> 
> ### Routing
> 
> ```
> FUSE write to /pane/N/ctl
>   -&gt; pane-fs parses first token as command name
>   -&gt; if command is a base command (close, stop, focus, hide, show, pulse, tag):
>        pane-fs generates the corresponding protocol message directly
>   -&gt; otherwise:
>        pane-fs wraps as CommandExecuted { command, args }
>        sends via protocol to the pane's looper
>        looper delivers to handler
>        handler returns Flow (ctl write blocks until Flow is returned)
> ```
> 
> The ctl write is **synchronous from the FUSE perspective** — the write call blocks until the handler processes the command and returns Flow. If Flow::Stop, the ctl write succeeds (command executed, pane is stopping). If Flow::Continue, the ctl write succeeds (command executed, pane continues). If the handler panics (E-Raise), the ctl write returns EIO.
> 
> This matches proc(3) ctl: "Textual messages written to the ctl file control the execution of the process." The write completes when the action completes. No fire-and-forget for ctl — it's imperative, not advisory.
> 
> **Confidence: high** on the command vocabulary and routing. The synchronous-write-blocks-until-processed model is how proc(3) and acme(4) ctl work. The alternative (fire-and-forget, write returns immediately) creates a race condition where `echo close &gt; /pane/1/ctl &amp;&amp; cat /pane/1/tag` could read stale data. Synchronous eliminates this.
> 
> ## 3. The event File
> 
> This is the hardest file in the namespace and the place where Plan 9 has the most to teach.
> 
> ### Two precedents
> 
> **Acme event file**: structured, multiplexed, bidirectional. Reader sees all window actions (keyboard, mouse, edits). Writing an event back causes the action to be applied as if the file weren't open. Fixed-format: origin char, type char, address range, flag, text count, text. This is a co-processor model — the reader intercepts and may replay or suppress events.
> 
> **Rio wctl blocking read**: simpler. Read returns window state. A subsequent read blocks until state changes. This is a change-notification model — the reader polls for state transitions.
> 
> ### Recommendation for pane
> 
> Pane's event file should be a **one-way notification stream**, not an acme-style interception mechanism. The format is JSONL — one JSON object per line.
> 
> Rationale: acme's event file is a cooperative editing protocol. It makes sense for acme because acme IS a text-processing environment where external programs collaborate with the editor. Pane's external scripting interface is coarser-grained: scripts observe state changes and invoke commands via ctl. They don't need to intercept button clicks or edit operations at the character level. If a pane needs acme-style co-processing, it declares that as a service protocol, not through the filesystem tier.
> 
> ### Event format
> 
> ```json
> {"type":"tag_changed","value":"Editor — lib.rs","at":"2026-04-03T14:32:01Z"}
> {"type":"attr_changed","name":"cursor","value":"42","at":"..."}
> {"type":"attr_changed","name":"dirty","value":"true","at":"..."}
> {"type":"command_executed","command":"save","args":"/tmp/out.txt","at":"..."}
> {"type":"service_opened","service":"com.pane.clipboard","at":"..."}
> {"type":"service_lost","service":"com.pane.clipboard","at":"..."}
> {"type":"focused","at":"..."}
> {"type":"hidden","at":"..."}
> {"type":"close_requested","at":"..."}
> {"type":"exited","reason":"normal","at":"..."}
> ```
> 
> ### Blocking semantics
> 
> - First read after open returns the most recent event (or blocks if none has occurred since open).
> - Subsequent reads block until the next event.
> - Multiple concurrent readers each get their own read position — each reader is an independent consumer of the event stream.
> - If the reader falls behind (doesn't read fast enough), events are buffered up to a limit. Beyond the limit, old events are dropped and a `{"type":"overflow","dropped":N}` sentinel is injected. This is the practical answer to the slow-reader problem that acme and rio never had to deal with (their readers were fast Plan 9 programs on a local kernel).
> 
> ### Interaction with filter chain
> 
> Events appearing in the event file are **post-filter**. The filter chain processes events before dispatch; the event file reflects what the handler saw, not what the system generated. This means a filter that suppresses certain events also suppresses them from the event file. If you want to see pre-filter events, that's a debug/trace facility, not a scripting interface.
> 
> **Confidence: medium-high** on the format and semantics. JSONL is the right wire format for the filesystem tier — it's human-readable, machine-parseable, and `jq` works on it. The blocking-read-with-bounded-buffer model is straightforward. The open question is whether pane ever needs acme-style bidirectional event co-processing through the filesystem. I'd defer that until someone has a concrete use case.
> 
> ## 4. Per-Service Directories
> 
> ### The question
> 
> When a pane opens clipboard, does `/pane/&lt;n&gt;/clipboard/` appear?
> 
> ### My recommendation: no
> 
> Services should not appear as subdirectories of the per-pane tree. Here's why.
> 
> In Plan 9, each file server served its own tree. You composed them with bind/mount. A process didn't have a `/proc/N/network/` subdirectory — it had `/net/` from a bind of the network file server. The composition was in the namespace, not in the file server's tree.
> 
> Pane-fs is one file server projecting pane state. If clipboard state appears at `/pane/&lt;n&gt;/clipboard/`, pane-fs has to understand clipboard internals to serve those files. Every new service means pane-fs grows a new sub-tree generator. This couples pane-fs to every service protocol.
> 
> Instead: services that want filesystem exposure should register their own entries in `attrs/`. The clipboard service, when opened, contributes:
> 
> ```
> /pane/&lt;n&gt;/attrs/clipboard.content    # current clipboard text (read-only from pane-fs; write goes through ctl)
> /pane/&lt;n&gt;/attrs/clipboard.format     # MIME type
> /pane/&lt;n&gt;/attrs/clipboard.locked     # "true" / "false"
> ```
> 
> And ctl commands for clipboard operations:
> 
> ```
> clipboard-copy &lt;text&gt;
> clipboard-paste
> clipboard-lock
> clipboard-unlock
> ```
> 
> This keeps pane-fs's tree uniform. It doesn't need to know what services exist — it just serves attrs from the AttrSet and forwards commands through ctl. The handler declares clipboard attributes via `Attribute&lt;S, A&gt;` the same way it declares any other attribute. The optic machinery handles the rest.
> 
> **The exception**: if a service needs a full sub-tree (e.g., a file manager service that exposes a directory listing), it should serve its own FUSE mount or protocol endpoint. Pane-fs serves the pane abstraction; it doesn't serve arbitrary sub-filesystems. Plan 9 would have you bind the service's file server into the per-process namespace. The pane equivalent is a separate FUSE mount or a protocol-tier query, not a pane-fs subtree.
> 
> **Confidence: high.** This is the right decomposition. The alternative (pane-fs grows subtrees per service) is the path to a monolithic file server, which is the opposite of what Plan 9 taught.
> 
> ## 5. The Optics Boundary
> 
> ### Current state
> 
> `AttrReader&lt;S&gt;` takes `&amp;S` and produces `AttrValue(String)` via `Display`. This is the read path. There is no write path through pane-fs yet.
> 
> ### Assessment: the current read boundary is correct for the common case
> 
> Text-via-Display for attribute reads matches Plan 9's convention exactly. `/proc/N/status` returned fixed-width text fields. Rio's `wctl` read returned decimal geometry plus state strings. The principle from the names paper: "all files contain text, not binary."
> 
> ### Structured reads (attrs.json)
> 
> The bulk-read file (`attrs.json`) is the right answer for scripts that need multiple attributes atomically. It reads all attributes from the same snapshot, serializes as JSON. This is a pane-fs convenience, not an optic concern — pane-fs iterates the AttrSet, reads each value, wraps in a JSON object.
> 
> Implementation note: `attrs.json` should read from a single snapshot — not call each reader independently, which could observe a partially-updated state if the looper updates the snapshot between calls. Since the looper updates the snapshot atomically (one `update_state()` call), and pane-fs reads from that snapshot without holding &amp;mut, this is already safe. The snapshot is a consistent point-in-time view.
> 
> ### Binary attributes
> 
> This is where Plan 9's "all text" convention shows its limits. An image attribute, a buffer of audio samples — these don't serialize through Display meaningfully.
> 
> Recommendation: **don't serve binary data through attrs/**. Binary data belongs in `body` (if it's the pane's primary content) or in a service's own protocol endpoint (if it's ancillary). An image editor's current canvas is its body, served at the protocol tier as raw bytes and at the filesystem tier as a path to a temp file or as nothing at all.
> 
> If you must expose a binary attribute through pane-fs, use base64 and declare the encoding in the attribute's type metadata (discoverable via `supported_properties`). But I'd resist this hard — it's the beginning of reinventing MIME inside the filesystem, and that path ends poorly.
> 
> ### Writable attributes
> 
> This is the question with real design consequences.
> 
> **The write path through optics:**
> 
> ```
> echo 42 &gt; /pane/1/attrs/cursor
> ```
> 
> This needs to:
> 1. Parse "42\n" back to the attribute's type (`usize` for cursor)
> 2. Call `Attribute::set(state, 42)` to produce a new state
> 3. Deliver the new state to the looper to replace the current handler state
> 
> Step 1 requires a parser paired with each attribute — the inverse of Display. `FromStr` is the natural Rust trait.
> 
> Step 2 requires the `Attribute&lt;S, A&gt;` (the full lens, not just the getter). `AttrReader` currently captures only the getter. You need an `AttrWriter&lt;S&gt;` that captures the setter.
> 
> Step 3 requires a channel from pane-fs to the looper that says "here is a new state, apply it." This is a **state replacement**, not a message. The looper needs to atomically replace the handler's state, which means the handler can't be executing when the replacement happens. Since the looper is single-threaded (I6), this is safe: the replacement is a looper event, processed between handler dispatches.
> 
> **Proposed `AttrWriter&lt;S&gt;`:**
> 
> ```rust
> pub struct AttrWriter&lt;S&gt; {
>     pub name: &amp;'static str,
>     writer: Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;S, &amp;str) -&gt; Result&lt;S, AttrWriteError&gt; + Send + Sync&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> The closure captures the lens set function and a `FromStr` parser. It takes `(&amp;S, &amp;str)` — the current state snapshot and the text to parse — and returns either a new state or a parse error.
> 
> **AttrWriteError** should be minimal: `ParseError(String)` for unparseable input, `ReadOnly` for attributes backed by Getter (no setter). pane-fs translates these to FUSE error codes: `ParseError` -&gt; EINVAL, `ReadOnly` -&gt; EACCES.
> 
> The `AttrSet` gains optional writers alongside readers:
> 
> ```rust
> pub struct AttrEntry&lt;S&gt; {
>     pub reader: AttrReader&lt;S&gt;,
>     pub writer: Option&lt;AttrWriter&lt;S&gt;&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> When the handler declares an `Attribute&lt;S, A&gt;` (full lens), both reader and writer are registered. When it declares a `ReadOnlyAttribute&lt;S, A&gt;` (getter only), only the reader is registered. Pane-fs checks for the writer; if absent, write returns EACCES.
> 
> **The state-update channel:**
> 
> ```
> pane-fs AttrWriter produces new S
>   -&gt; sends (PaneId, S) to looper via channel
>   -&gt; looper receives on next iteration
>   -&gt; looper replaces handler state: handler.state = new_state
>   -&gt; looper updates pane-fs snapshot: entry.update_state(new_state.clone())
> ```
> 
> This is where the model gets subtle. The write goes: pane-fs reads current snapshot -&gt; applies optic setter -&gt; sends new state to looper -&gt; looper applies it. But between the snapshot read and the looper application, the handler may have changed its own state. This is a **lost update** — the classic read-modify-write race.
> 
> Plan 9 handled this pragmatically: proc(3) writes to ctl were commands, not state patches. You wrote "kill" to ctl, not a new process state. The kernel applied the command atomically. Writable attribute files in Plan 9 were rare — consctl accepted mode strings ("rawon", "rawoff"), not arbitrary state.
> 
> **My recommendation:** Writable attributes should be **commands disguised as files**, not state patches. Writing `42` to `/pane/1/attrs/cursor` should not mean "replace the cursor field in handler state." It should mean "send a SetAttr(cursor, 42) command to the handler, and let the handler decide how to apply it."
> 
> ```
> echo 42 &gt; /pane/1/attrs/cursor
>   -&gt; pane-fs parses "42" via AttrWriter
>   -&gt; sends SetAttr { name: "cursor", value: AttrValue("42") } to looper
>   -&gt; looper delivers to handler via a new Handler method or Handles&lt;Lifecycle&gt; variant
>   -&gt; handler applies it (or rejects it, or transforms it)
>   -&gt; handler state is authoritative
> ```
> 
> This eliminates the lost-update race. The handler sees the write as a request, not a fait accompli. It can validate, clamp, reject. The optic's setter is used by the handler when it *chooses* to accept the write, not imposed by pane-fs.
> 
> The tradeoff: writes are no longer guaranteed to have the PutGet property from the filesystem perspective. You write 42, read back, might get 43 if the handler clamped it. But this is the correct behavior — the handler owns its state. The filesystem is a view, not the authoritative store. Plan 9's proc files worked this way: writing "pri 15" to ctl might result in a different priority if the kernel clamped it.
> 
> **Confidence: high** on the command-not-patch model. Medium on the specific `SetAttr` delivery mechanism — that depends on decisions about the Handler trait that are the be-systems-engineer's territory.
> 
> ## 6. Cross-Pane Namespace
> 
> ### /pane/by-sig/, /pane/by-uuid/, /pane/by-type/
> 
> These are **computed views** — synthetic directories whose contents are generated at readdir time from an in-memory index.
> 
> Not symlink farms. Not precomputed directories. Not query results cached to disk.
> 
> Implementation: pane-fs maintains an in-memory `PaneIndex`:
> 
> ```rust
> struct PaneIndex {
>     /// All registered panes, by local number
>     by_number: BTreeMap&lt;u64, PaneMetadata&gt;,
>     /// UUID -&gt; local number
>     by_uuid: HashMap&lt;Uuid, u64&gt;,
>     /// signature -&gt; set of local numbers  
>     by_sig: HashMap&lt;String, BTreeSet&lt;u64&gt;&gt;,
>     /// type -&gt; set of local numbers
>     by_type: HashMap&lt;String, BTreeSet&lt;u64&gt;&gt;,
>     /// host -&gt; set of local numbers (for remote views)
>     by_host: HashMap&lt;String, BTreeSet&lt;u64&gt;&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> When pane-fs receives a readdir for `/pane/by-sig/com.pane.editor/`, it looks up the signature in the index and generates symlink entries pointing to `/pane/&lt;n&gt;/` for each match. The symlinks are synthetic — they exist only in the FUSE response, not in any backing store.
> 
> When a pane is created or destroyed, the index is updated. For local panes, the looper notifies pane-fs directly. For remote panes, pane-store's federation index sends change notifications (the BFS live query model described in distributed-pane.md).
> 
> **Why not a real symlink farm**: symlink farms need a backing directory. They persist to disk. They need cleanup when panes die. A synthetic FUSE readdir is zero-cost — no backing store, no cleanup, no stale entries. The directory doesn't "exist" — it's computed on demand.
> 
> **The `by-uuid` exception**: readlink on `/pane/by-uuid/&lt;uuid&gt;` returns a symlink target (e.g., `/pane/3/`). This is a synthetic symlink — the FUSE response to readlink includes the target path. No file is created. But the behavior to consumers is identical to a real symlink: `cat /pane/by-uuid/&lt;uuid&gt;/tag` works because the kernel follows the symlink to `/pane/3/tag`.
> 
> **Confidence: high.** This is straightforward synthetic filesystem technique. The only risk is the index falling out of sync with reality, which is the same risk Plan 9's /proc had (process dies between readdir and stat). Return ENOENT on stale entries; the consumer retries.
> 
> ## 7. The State Snapshot Model
> 
> ### Current design
> 
> The looper clones handler state S after each dispatch cycle. Pane-fs reads from the clone. No lock contention. Eventual consistency with a bound: the snapshot is at most one dispatch cycle stale.
> 
> ### Plan 9's approach
> 
> `/proc/N/status` read directly from kernel memory with spin locks. The read was atomic with respect to the process — the kernel held a process lock for the duration of the status read. This gave strong consistency: the read reflected the process state at the moment of the read, not a stale snapshot.
> 
> But Plan 9 had kernel-level control. The lock was a single-word spin lock in the Proc struct. The critical section was tens of instructions — read a few fields, format them, return. The process couldn't be scheduled while the lock was held because the lock was per-CPU on UP systems.
> 
> ### Assessment for pane
> 
> **The snapshot model is correct for pane-fs.** Here's why:
> 
> 1. **pane-fs is a FUSE server, not a kernel driver.** The FUSE round-trip (user -&gt; kernel -&gt; FUSE server -&gt; kernel -&gt; user) already introduces ~15-30us of latency. Adding "wait for looper to finish current dispatch" on top would double that in the worst case (if the looper is mid-dispatch when the FUSE read arrives). The snapshot eliminates the coordination.
> 
> 2. **The staleness bound is tight.** The looper updates the snapshot after every dispatch cycle. A dispatch cycle is one message delivery to the handler — microseconds for simple handlers. The snapshot is stale by at most one handler call. For the filesystem tier (shell scripts, inspection tools), this is invisible.
> 
> 3. **No lock contention.** The looper thread and the FUSE threads never contend. The looper writes the snapshot (owned, single-threaded). FUSE threads read it (shared reference, via Arc&lt;RwLock&lt;S&gt;&gt; or similar). The read side never blocks the looper; the looper's write is a single pointer swap.
> 
> 4. **Strong consistency isn't needed at the filesystem tier.** A script that reads `/pane/1/attrs/cursor` and gets "42" when the handler has already moved to "43" is fine. The script is operating at human speed. By the time the script processes the "42", the cursor has probably moved again anyway. This is the same argument against strong consistency that Plan 9 made for `/dev/cons` reads — what you read was already in the past by the time you processed it.
> 
> ### Implementation recommendation
> 
> Use `Arc&lt;S&gt;` with atomic swap, not `Arc&lt;RwLock&lt;S&gt;&gt;`:
> 
> ```rust
> // In the looper (single writer):
> let new_snapshot = Arc::new(handler_state.clone());
> self.snapshot.store(new_snapshot);  // atomic pointer swap
> 
> // In pane-fs (many readers):
> let snapshot = entry.snapshot.load();  // atomic pointer load
> let value = entry.attrs.read("cursor", &amp;snapshot);
> ```
> 
> This is zero-contention. The looper does one allocation + atomic store per dispatch cycle. FUSE threads do one atomic load per read. No RwLock, no Mutex, no lock at all. Old snapshots are freed when the last Arc reference drops.
> 
> The atomic swap requires `arc-swap` or equivalent (std Arc doesn't have built-in atomic swap). `arc_swap::ArcSwap&lt;S&gt;` is the standard Rust crate for this pattern.
> 
> **Confidence: very high** on the snapshot model. This is one of those cases where the simpler approach (snapshot) is also the faster and more correct approach. Direct reads with locks would be premature complexity with no benefit at the filesystem tier.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Summary of key decisions presented for review
> 
> 1. **ctl is write-only; state reads live in attrs/.** Diverges from acme's readable ctl. Follows proc(3).
> 2. **event is one-way JSONL stream, not acme-style co-processor.** Interception, if needed, is a service protocol.
> 3. **No per-service subdirectories.** Services contribute to attrs/ and commands/. Keeps pane-fs decoupled from service internals.
> 4. **Attribute writes are commands to the handler, not state patches.** Eliminates lost-update race. Handler is authoritative.
> 5. **Cross-pane views are synthetic FUSE readdir results, not symlink farms.** Computed on demand from in-memory index.
> 6. **Snapshot model with Arc-swap, not locks.** Zero-contention reads, one dispatch cycle staleness bound.
> 7. **`wdir` file (from rio(4))** — working directory, read/write. Useful for plumber message construction.
> 8. **`body` uses replacement semantics**, not acme-style append.
> 9. **Binary attributes are out of scope for pane-fs.** Text only. Binary data goes through protocol tier or separate mounts.
> 
> These are the decisions where I have a strong Plan 9-grounded opinion. The items I'd flag for the be-systems-engineer's input: the `SetAttr` delivery mechanism in the Handler trait (point 4), and whether `body` replacement vs. append semantics align with the BeOS scripting model (point 8 — BeOS's `set Value of ...` was replacement, which supports my recommendation, but worth confirming).</result>

It must snapshot only the optic-accessible `Attribute`/`ReadOnlyAttribute` values. This needs to be decided before the looper is built, because it determines whether the looper holds `H` directly or wraps it in a structure that separates optic-accessible state from obligation-carrying state.
> 
> **S3 (control-before-events) is naturally satisfied but fragile.** Because `send_request` is called from within a handler callback (during dispatch), the Dispatch entry is installed synchronously before the looper returns to its event poll. The reply arrives as a future event. This ordering holds as long as the looper never processes incoming events during an outgoing send. If the looper ever adds pipelining or concurrent I/O, S3 would need explicit sequencing.
> 
> **The `fire_failed` = affine gap compensation pattern.** This is the clean design consequence of Rust's affine-not-linear types. EAct assumes linear handler stores (every installed handler is eventually consumed). pane's affine system adds `on_failed` as the Drop-equivalent for Dispatch entries. The on_failed closure is the dynamic analog of what `#[must_use]` + Drop is for static obligation handles. The pattern is consistent across the entire design.</result>

> <summary>Agent "Design optics layer for pane state" completed</summary>
> <result>One thing I should flag explicitly before we wrap: there's an unresolved tension I noticed between the archived `optics-design-brief.md` and the current `property.rs`.
> 
> **The design brief (Section 2) explicitly decided against the profunctor encoding:**
> 
> &gt; "What we don't take: The profunctor encoding. The representation theorem (Theorem 4.4) proves forall p. Tambara p =&gt; p a b -&gt; p s t is isomorphic to the concrete optic, but this requires rank-2 polymorphism. Rust doesn't have it. The concrete encoding (split/merge pairs) is the right Rust representation."
> 
> And Section 4 designed concrete traits (`Getter&lt;S, A&gt;` with `fn get(&amp;self, &amp;S) -&gt; &amp;A`, reference-returning, dyn-compatible, ~300 lines) rather than the profunctor encoding.
> 
> **But the current `property.rs` uses fp-library's profunctor encoding** (`Lens&lt;'a, RcBrand, S, S, A, A&gt;`), which is the representation the brief explicitly rejected.
> 
> This needs a deliberate decision. The tradeoffs:
> 
> | | Profunctor encoding (fp-library, current) | Concrete encoding (design brief's proposal) |
> |---|---|---|
> | **Composition** | Free via `Composed`/`optics_compose` — type class constraint union gives the right optic | Manual: `Then&lt;L1, L2&gt;` structs, explicit trait impls per pair |
> | **By-value vs by-ref** | By-value only (S -&gt; A). Clone at the read boundary. | By-reference (fn(&amp;S) -&gt; &amp;A). Zero-copy reads. |
> | **dyn-compatibility** | Not dyn-compatible (generic over profunctor P) | Fully dyn-compatible (no generic params) |
> | **Type complexity** | High (`Composed&lt;'a, S, S, M, M, A, A, LensPrime&lt;...&gt;, LensPrime&lt;...&gt;&gt;`) | Low (`Then&lt;FieldLens&lt;S, M&gt;, FieldLens&lt;M, A&gt;&gt;`) |
> | **Dependency** | fp-library 0.15 (large FP library) | ~300 lines, no deps |
> | **Send + Sync** | Requires ArcBrand (current uses RcBrand, which is !Send) | Just `fn(&amp;S) -&gt; &amp;A`, naturally Send+Sync with fn pointers |
> 
> The **RcBrand issue** is immediate: `AttrReader` requires `Send + Sync` (it crosses threads to the FUSE thread), but `Lens&lt;'a, RcBrand, ...&gt;` uses `Rc&lt;dyn Fn&gt;` internally, which is `!Send`. The current code works because the clone happens inside the `AttrReader` closure (the `Lens` itself doesn't cross threads), but this is fragile — if you ever need to store the typed `Attribute` in a `Send` context, it won't compile.
> 
> The design brief's concrete encoding avoids this entirely because `fn(&amp;S) -&gt; &amp;A` (function pointer) is `Send + Sync` by construction.
> 
> This is the load-bearing design decision for the optics layer. Everything else follows from it. What's your read?</result>

### `2026-04-05T06:41:22.256Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>aa7cf5f20bbd8db28</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01GYSj37Zn2CYA3WgTPRZftp</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/aa7cf5f20bbd8db28.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Reconcile optics with EAct framework" completed</summary>
> <result>I have a thorough picture now. Let me compose the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Verdict: The current PaneEntry&lt;S&gt; snapshot model is conditionally sound, but S = H (the full handler type) is unsound. The separation must happen, and it should be structural (a projection type), not a trait split on the handler.**
> 
> Here's the full analysis on each of your four questions.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## 1. Does EAct's actor state A need to be separable into data state vs. framework state?
> 
> **Yes, but the separation is already implicit in your architecture — you just haven't named the projection type.**
> 
> In EAct (Fowler et al., §3.1 Fig. 5), the actor state `A` is threaded through handler invocations as a single value. The sigma store maps session endpoints to handler values of type `Handler(S_in, A)` (TH-Handler, Fig. 8 line 2732). There is no requirement that `A` be internally homogeneous — EAct is parametric in `A`. What EAct *does* require is that every E-React step receives the full `A` and returns the full `A`. The actor state is opaque to the formalism.
> 
> Your handler H already contains two categories of fields:
> 
> 1. **Data fields** — `cursor: usize`, `buffer: String`, etc. These are Clone, Display-able, and optic-accessible.
> 2. **Framework handles** — `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;`, `Messenger`, `LooperSender`. These are !Clone (affine obligation types per I4) and must not be read by pane-fs.
> 
> The architecture.md already encodes this distinction: obligation handles bypass filters (I5), are not Message variants, and are not serializable. The optic layer (Attribute&lt;S,A&gt;) takes S by value with Clone bound. ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt; is !Clone. These two constraints are already in tension — the resolution is that S in the optic layer is NOT the full H.
> 
> What you need is a *named projection*. The simplest form:
> 
> ```rust
> /// The optic-accessible portion of handler state.
> /// Derived from H by the pane macro. Contains only Clone fields.
> /// Framework handles (ServiceHandle, Messenger) are excluded.
> trait HasDataState {
>     type DataState: Clone + Send + 'static;
>     fn data_state(&amp;self) -&gt; Self::DataState;
>     fn apply_data_state(&amp;mut self, state: Self::DataState);
> }
> ```
> 
> This is not a trait split on Handler. Handler remains monolithic (per the handler debate settlement). This is a *structural projection* — a lens from H to its Clone-able substate. In optic terms: `HasDataState` is a `Lens&lt;H, H::DataState&gt;` where the getter clones the data fields and the setter writes them back. The optic laws (GetPut, PutGet, PutPut from Clarke et al.) apply to this lens, and the `Attribute&lt;DataState, A&gt;` optics compose with it.
> 
> **EAct compatibility**: E-React still receives `&amp;mut H`. The projection is a *read-side* concern only — the looper never replaces H with a reconstituted DataState + FrameworkState. The looper reads DataState after dispatch (for pane-fs snapshots) and optionally writes DataState back (for pane-fs `ctl` writes). The full H remains the actor state `A` in EAct's formalism.
> 
> ## 2. Is "snapshot the data portion of H" formally safe?
> 
> **Yes, under four conditions (already identified in the invariant verification from 2026-04-03):**
> 
> 1. Clone at quiescent point — between dispatch cycles, not during handler execution
> 2. Clone never written *back* to looper state without going through the looper's dispatch path
> 3. Clone used for reads only (pane-fs queries)
> 4. Only DataState snapshotted, not full H
> 
> Condition 4 is the one your question is really about. The current `PaneEntry&lt;S&gt;` in `crates/pane-fs/src/namespace.rs` is parameterized by `S`, and the tests use a plain `StatusState` struct — not a handler with ServiceHandle fields. This is correct. The unsound path would be instantiating `PaneEntry&lt;H&gt;` where H is the full handler type — that would require H: Clone, which it can't satisfy because of ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;.
> 
> The snapshot is formally a *read-only projection* in DLfActRiS terms (Jacobs/Hinrichsen/Krebbers, POPL 2024, §3.2): the pane-fs thread holds a value that is derived from actor state but does not confer any write capability. In DLfActRiS's separation logic, the pane-fs thread holds a *read permission* (fractional permission in their framework) — not the full ownership. The looper holds exclusive write access to H; the snapshot is a frozen copy of the data projection. No permission aliasing occurs because the snapshot is a separate value, not a reference into the actor's state.
> 
> The quiescent-point condition matters. If you snapshot mid-dispatch (while a handler method is executing), you could observe a partially-updated DataState. The calloop batch model naturally provides quiescence: after all events in a batch are dispatched and all handler methods have returned Flow, the looper snapshots. This is the EAct "idle" state between E-React steps.
> 
> **Interaction with EAct**: snapshotting is an *administrative reduction* — it doesn't change the actor state, doesn't consume session messages, and doesn't alter sigma. EAct's Progress theorem (Thm 3.10) and Global Progress corollary (Cor 3.14) are unaffected because the snapshot operation has no preconditions that could block and produces no observable protocol effects. It's a monitoring operation outside the session calculus.
> 
> ## 3. Does separating H into DataState + FrameworkState break EAct typing?
> 
> **No, because you're not separating H. You're projecting from H.**
> 
> The critical distinction: EAct's TH-Handler (Fig. 8 line 2732) requires that all sigma entries share actor state type A. If you literally split H into two types and threaded them separately through dispatch, every `Handles&lt;P&gt;` impl would need to receive both, and the type parameter explosion would cascade through Dispatch&lt;H&gt;.
> 
> What I'm recommending instead preserves H as the single EAct actor state. The projection is one-directional:
> 
> ```
> Looper owns: H (full handler = EAct actor state A)
>     |
>     | data_state(&amp;self) -&gt; DataState [Clone, after each batch]
>     v
> PaneEntry&lt;DataState&gt; (pane-fs reads from this)
>     |
>     | AttrSet&lt;DataState&gt; reads via AttrReader&lt;DataState&gt;
>     v
> /pane/&lt;n&gt;/attrs/&lt;name&gt; → AttrValue(String)
> ```
> 
> The ctl write path goes the other direction:
> 
> ```
> /pane/&lt;n&gt;/ctl write "set cursor 42"
>     |
>     | parsed into looper command
>     v
> Looper receives command, applies to &amp;mut H
>     |
>     | data_state(&amp;self) -&gt; DataState [re-snapshot]
>     v
> PaneEntry updated
> ```
> 
> H is never destructured. DataState is a derived value. `Handles&lt;P&gt;` impls receive `&amp;mut self` where self is H. Dispatch&lt;H&gt; is parameterized by H. Nothing changes in the EAct typing.
> 
> The derive macro for Handler can generate `HasDataState` automatically by collecting all `#[attr]`-annotated fields:
> 
> ```rust
> #[derive(Handler)]
> struct MyHandler {
>     #[attr("cursor")]
>     cursor: usize,        // included in DataState
>     #[attr("title")]
>     title: String,        // included in DataState
>     clipboard: ServiceHandle&lt;Clipboard&gt;,  // excluded (no #[attr])
>     messenger: Messenger,                 // excluded (no #[attr])
> }
> ```
> 
> The generated DataState would be a struct mirroring the annotated fields. This is a compile-time derivation — no runtime overhead except the Clone at snapshot time.
> 
> ## 4. Should optics be part of Protocol or Handler?
> 
> **Handler. Not Protocol.**
> 
> Three reasons:
> 
> **Formal**: In EAct, the Protocol defines the session type S — the communication structure. Attributes are properties of the actor state A, not properties of the session. A Protocol says "the clipboard service sends Changed/LockGranted/LockDenied messages." What the handler *does* with those messages — which internal fields it updates — is actor state, not protocol. Conflating them would be like putting variable declarations inside a session type.
> 
> **Practical**: Different handlers implementing the same Protocol may expose different attributes. A text editor handling Clipboard exposes `cursor` and `buffer_length`. An image editor handling Clipboard exposes `canvas_width` and `selected_region`. The attributes are handler-specific, not protocol-specific.
> 
> **Architectural**: The architecture.md already has `supported_properties(&amp;self) -&gt; &amp;[PropertyInfo]` on Handler, not on Protocol. This is the correct location. The `#[attr]` annotations on handler fields would generate both the `HasDataState` impl and the `supported_properties` list.
> 
> The one exception: a Protocol *could* declare *required* attributes that any handler implementing it must expose. For example, Display might require a `frame` attribute. This would be expressed as a supertrait bound on Handles&lt;Display&gt;:
> 
> ```rust
> pub trait Handles&lt;Display&gt;: HasAttr&lt;"frame", Frame&gt; { ... }
> ```
> 
> But this is Phase 3+ territory (it requires const generics for string parameters or a macro workaround). For Phase 1, attributes live entirely on the Handler side.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## Concrete recommendation
> 
> **The state separation model is: derive-generated projection, not architectural split.**
> 
> The type relationships:
> 
> ```
> Handler H: Send + 'static
>     impl HasDataState for H {
>         type DataState = H_DataState;  // generated struct
>     }
> 
> AttrSet&lt;H::DataState&gt;           // pane-fs reads from this
> Attribute&lt;H::DataState, A&gt;      // individual optic
> PaneEntry&lt;H::DataState&gt;         // namespace entry with snapshot
> 
> // The current AttrReader&lt;S&gt; in crates/pane-fs/src/attrs.rs
> // is already correct — S = H::DataState, not S = H
> ```
> 
> The looper's batch cycle becomes:
> 
> 1. Collect events from calloop sources
> 2. Dispatch through Handler/Handles&lt;P&gt; (EAct E-React)
> 3. After batch: `let snapshot = handler.data_state();` (quiescent point)
> 4. Update `PaneEntry` with new snapshot (pane-fs reads see updated state)
> 
> This adds one trait (`HasDataState`), one generated struct per handler, and one Clone per batch cycle. It preserves H as the single EAct actor state, keeps obligation handles out of the snapshot, and gives pane-fs a clean read surface without any lock contention with the looper thread.
> 
> The existing code in `crates/pane-fs/src/attrs.rs` and `crates/pane-fs/src/namespace.rs` is structurally correct — it's already parameterized by S, not by H. The missing piece is the `HasDataState` trait that connects the two: telling the looper *what* to snapshot and *how* to snapshot it.</result>

### `2026-04-05T06:42:00.703Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a3954d0ce28bc0875</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01DSJhHuGqXoD2Tiue8iV5yj</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/a3954d0ce28bc0875.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Reconcile optics with Plan 9 namespace" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have all the pieces. Let me give a thorough, grounded answer.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here is how Plan 9 actually served state, and what that means for pane-fs.
> 
> ## How Plan 9 served state
> 
> Three mechanisms, each with different read/write semantics:
> 
> ### 1. /proc/N/status — function from kernel state to text
> 
> The `status` file in proc(3) is a **read-only** projection. The kernel's `devproc` device driver implements `devread()` for the status qid by calling `procstatus()`, which formats twelve fixed-width fields from the `Proc` structure: name, user, state, six cputime values, memory, two priority values. Each `read()` call snapshots the current kernel `Proc` struct and formats it fresh. There is no cached copy — the kernel holds the authoritative state, and the read function projects from it.
> 
> No composition. No sub-field access. You get the entire status line. If you want one field, you parse text in user space (`awk '{print $3}'`). The file is a flat projection, not a structured tree.
> 
> The ctl file for /proc is **write-only commands**: `stop`, `start`, `startstop`, `hang`, `noattach`, `kill`, etc. Writing "stop" to `/proc/N/ctl` calls `procctlreq()` in the kernel, which modifies the `Proc` struct directly. The write handler is a switch on command strings — a command interpreter, not a setter.
> 
> ### 2. rio's wctl — combined read/write with different semantics per direction
> 
> `wctl` is the most interesting case. **Reading** returns the window's rectangle (four coordinates) plus state strings ("hidden"/"visible", "current"/"notcurrent"). A subsequent read **blocks until the window changes** — it's a combined status+event file. The read function formats from rio's internal `Window` struct.
> 
> **Writing** accepts command strings: `resize -r minx miny maxx maxy`, `move -minx n`, `top`, `bottom`, `hide`, `unhide`, `current`, `delete`, `new`. These are line-oriented commands with options, parsed by rio's `writewctl()`. Again, a command interpreter, not a lens setter.
> 
> The critical observation: **the read and write sides of wctl have different types**. Read returns structured text (coordinates + state). Write accepts imperative commands. There is no getter/setter symmetry. You don't write "0 0 128 64 visible current" to set the state — you write "resize -r 0 0 128 64".
> 
> ### 3. rio's consctl — pure mode-switching commands
> 
> `consctl` is write-only commands: `rawon`, `rawoff`, `holdon`, `holdoff`. Close reverts to defaults. The "read" of the current mode isn't exposed as a file — you infer it from behavior.
> 
> rio's `label` and `snarf` files are the only ones with true getter/setter symmetry (read returns current value, write replaces it) — and notably these are *string-valued* files, not structured state.
> 
> ### 4. rio's wsys — the closest thing to composition
> 
> `/dev/wsys/` is a **directory of window directories**, each containing per-window versions of `cons`, `consctl`, `label`, `mouse`, etc. This is structural composition (navigate to window N, then to its attribute) but **not optic composition** (you can't project sub-fields of sub-fields through a composed pipeline). Each leaf file has its own read/write implementation.
> 
> ## What this means for pane-fs
> 
> Plan 9 never used getter/setter symmetry on structured state. It used three patterns:
> 
> | Pattern | Read | Write | Example |
> |---------|------|-------|---------|
> | Status projection | Format from state | N/A (read-only) | /proc/N/status |
> | Command file | N/A or blocking event | Imperative commands | /proc/N/ctl, consctl |
> | Value file | Return current string | Replace string | label, snarf |
> | Directory structure | Walk + readdir | Create in union dir | /dev/wsys/N/ |
> 
> Now, your specific questions:
> 
> ### Q1: AttrReader takes &amp;S — by-reference at the read boundary. The underlying optic (Lens) operates by-value internally. Is this mismatch acceptable?
> 
> Yes, and it's not really a mismatch — it's the **correct boundary**.
> 
> Look at what you have in `attrs.rs`:
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn new&lt;A: fmt::Display + 'static&gt;(
>     name: &amp;'static str,
>     view: impl Fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A + Send + Sync + 'static,
> ) -&gt; Self
> ```
> 
> This closure takes `&amp;S`. The caller provides a closure like `|s: &amp;EditorState| s.cursor`. The profunctor `Lens` from fp-library takes `S` by value. You've already solved this by making `AttrReader::new` accept an `Fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A` closure, not an fp-library `Lens`. The `AttrReader` is **not wrapping an fp-library optic** — it's wrapping a plain function.
> 
> Meanwhile, `Attribute&lt;S, A&gt;` in `property.rs` *does* wrap a `Lens&lt;RcBrand, S, S, A, A&gt;` and takes `S` by value for `view()` and `set()`.
> 
> These are two separate things and they should stay separate. `AttrReader` is the filesystem's projection mechanism — it needs `&amp;S` because it's reading from a shared snapshot. `Attribute` is the typed optic for programmatic access where the by-value semantics and optic law guarantees matter. Don't try to unify them through a single abstraction. Plan 9 never tried to make `/proc/N/status` and `procstatus()` share a getter implementation — the kernel function operated on kernel state directly, the file interface was a separate formatting layer on top.
> 
> **Recommendation:** Keep `AttrReader&lt;S&gt;` as the filesystem read boundary. It takes `&amp;S`, returns `AttrValue(String)`. When constructing an `AttrReader` from an `Attribute`, the bridge is trivial — the closure clones `s` and calls `attribute.view(s.clone())`. The clone happens once per read, at the filesystem boundary. This is the same cost as Plan 9's `procstatus()` walking through a snapshot of the `Proc` struct.
> 
> ### Q2: What IS the snapshot?
> 
> The snapshot should be a separate type. Not the handler. Not a mirror of the handler's fields. A **purpose-built projection struct**.
> 
> In Plan 9, `/proc/N/status` didn't expose the `Proc` struct. It exposed twelve fields chosen by the proc device author. The `Proc` struct had dozens of fields the status file didn't show — scheduler state, page tables, locks, the list goes on. The filesystem was a curated projection, not a dump.
> 
> For pane-fs, the snapshot type should be:
> 1. `Clone + Send + Sync` — goes across the thread boundary to the FUSE thread
> 2. Contains only what the attrs need — not the full handler state
> 3. Updated by the looper after each dispatch cycle (you already have `update_state` on `PaneEntry`)
> 
> Concretely: the handler author defines a snapshot struct and a method that builds it.
> 
> ```rust
> #[derive(Clone)]
> struct EditorSnapshot {
>     cursor: usize,
>     buffer_length: usize,
>     title: String,
> }
> 
> impl EditorHandler {
>     fn snapshot(&amp;self) -&gt; EditorSnapshot {
>         EditorSnapshot {
>             cursor: self.cursor,
>             buffer_length: self.buffer.len(),
>             title: self.title.clone(),
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> Then `PaneEntry&lt;EditorSnapshot&gt;` holds the snapshot, not `PaneEntry&lt;EditorHandler&gt;`. The looper calls `handler.snapshot()` and sends the result to pane-fs. The handler never crosses the thread boundary.
> 
> This is what BeOS's scripting system did too — `BPropertyInfo` described what was accessible, and the handler formatted values on demand through `GetSupportedSuites()` / `ResolveSpecifier()`. The scripting interface never got raw access to the BHandler's internal state.
> 
> The alternative — `PaneEntry&lt;S&gt;` where `S` is the handler type — fails because `Handler: Send + 'static` but not necessarily `Clone` or `Sync`, and the handler holds `ServiceHandle` values that are `!Clone` by design.
> 
> ### Q3: Writes — command file or optic setter?
> 
> **Use the command file pattern. Do not expose the optic's setter through the filesystem.**
> 
> This is the clearest lesson from Plan 9. Here is why:
> 
> 1. **rio's wctl doesn't expose setters.** To resize a window you don't write the new rectangle to a "geometry" attribute. You write `resize -r 0 0 128 64` to the ctl file. The resize command does validation, animation, clipping to screen bounds, Z-order updates, and then sets the geometry. The write path has *side effects beyond the state change*.
> 
> 2. **The setter needs the looper thread.** The handler has `&amp;mut self`. The optic `set()` produces a new `S`, but applying that new state to the handler requires `&amp;mut handler`, which is only available on the looper thread. So a filesystem write to an attribute would need to: serialize the new value from text, construct the correct `A` type, send a "please apply this setter" message to the looper, wait for confirmation. This is a command. You're just hiding it behind a getter/setter pretense.
> 
> 3. **Optic law violations at the filesystem boundary.** PutGet says: if I set X then get, I see X. But between the filesystem write and the next read, the handler may have processed other events that changed the state. The snapshot is stale. The guarantee doesn't hold across the async boundary. Plan 9 didn't worry about this because it never promised setter semantics through the filesystem.
> 
> 4. **Text parsing ambiguity.** The optic's `A` type might not round-trip through `Display`/`FromStr`. What happens when someone writes "forty-two" to `/pane/N/attrs/cursor`? You need validation, error reporting, type parsing — all of which the command file handles naturally.
> 
> **Concrete design:**
> 
> ```
> /pane/&lt;id&gt;/tag          read: current tag          write: set tag (string value file)
> /pane/&lt;id&gt;/body         read: content              write: (maybe) replace content
> /pane/&lt;id&gt;/attrs/&lt;name&gt; read: AttrReader output    write: ERROR (read-only)
> /pane/&lt;id&gt;/ctl          read: (block for events)   write: line-oriented commands
> ```
> 
> Attribute writes go through ctl:
> 
> ```
> echo 'set cursor 42' &gt; /pane/N/ctl
> echo 'resize -dx 800 -dy 600' &gt; /pane/N/ctl
> echo 'scroll on' &gt; /pane/N/ctl
> ```
> 
> The ctl handler is a method on Handler:
> 
> ```rust
> /// Process a ctl command. Returns error string on failure (Plan 9 convention).
> fn ctl(&amp;mut self, cmd: &amp;str) -&gt; Result&lt;(), String&gt; {
>     // Parse and apply. Has full &amp;mut self access.
>     // Can validate, trigger side effects, update multiple fields atomically.
> }
> ```
> 
> The `Attribute&lt;S, A&gt;` with its `set()` method is still useful — **inside** the handler. The ctl method can use it:
> 
> ```rust
> fn ctl(&amp;mut self, cmd: &amp;str) -&gt; Result&lt;(), String&gt; {
>     let (verb, args) = parse_ctl(cmd);
>     match verb {
>         "set" =&gt; {
>             match args[0] {
>                 "cursor" =&gt; {
>                     let n: usize = args[1].parse().map_err(|e| e.to_string())?;
>                     // Use the optic internally, where state is owned
>                     self.state = self.cursor_attr.set(self.state.clone(), n);
>                     Ok(())
>                 }
>                 _ =&gt; Err(format!("unknown attribute: {}", args[0])),
>             }
>         }
>         _ =&gt; Err(format!("unknown command: {}", verb)),
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> The optic provides the lawful state update; the ctl file provides the external interface with validation and side effects. Two layers, each doing what it's good at.
> 
> ### Where PropertyInfo fits
> 
> The `supported_properties` method on Handler and the unresolved `PropertyInfo` type should describe what the ctl file accepts, not what attrs exist. The attr names come from the `AttrSet` (for readdir). The `PropertyInfo` should describe the command vocabulary: which verbs, which parameters, what types. This is the BeOS scripting heritage — `BPropertyInfo` described the suites/specifiers the handler responded to.
> 
> ### Summary
> 
> | Concern | Mechanism | Plan 9 precedent |
> |---------|-----------|-------------------|
> | Read attributes | `AttrReader&lt;S&gt;` with `&amp;S` | /proc/N/status (projection function) |
> | State snapshot | Purpose-built `Clone+Send+Sync` struct, not the handler | Proc struct never left kernel; status formatted from it |
> | Write attributes | `ctl` file, line-oriented commands | wctl, consctl, /proc/N/ctl |
> | Optic setter | Internal to handler, used by ctl implementation | No precedent (Plan 9 didn't have optics, but it chose the same architecture) |
> | Attribute listing | `AttrSet::names()` for readdir | /dev/wsys/N/ directory listing |
> | PropertyInfo | Describes ctl command vocabulary | Not a Plan 9 thing — this is the BeOS BPropertyInfo contribution |
> 
> **Confidence:** High on the read path (your current design is already correct). High on ctl-for-writes (every Plan 9 writable interface worked this way). Medium on the snapshot type — the general approach is right, but how you integrate it with the PaneBuilder/looper lifecycle needs design work I can't fully assess from the distributed systems side.</result>

> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-05T06:43:09.081Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>af399754a918f5f71</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01HJTExqyMu3H7M5uZRbbbs3</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/af399754a918f5f71.output</output-file>

> <summary>Agent "Reconcile profunctor with concrete" completed</summary>
> <result>Good. No `pane-optic` crate yet — PLAN.md lists it under "crates preserved from prototype" but it doesn't exist in the current tree. Now let me write the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> **Recommendation: Option 3 (Hybrid), but with a specific architecture that makes it almost as simple as Option 2.**
> 
> Here's the reasoning, working from the theory up.
> 
> ## The theoretical situation
> 
> The representation theorem (Clarke et al. Thm 4.4) tells us that profunctor optics `∀p. C p ⇒ p a b → p s t` are isomorphic to concrete optics (existential pairs). The two encodings carry the same information. The profunctor encoding buys you two things:
> 
> 1. **Composition is function composition** — constraint union happens automatically via the type system.
> 2. **Uniformity** — one type signature parameterized by a constraint, rather than a family of types.
> 
> The concrete encoding buys you:
> 
> 1. **Direct access to the components** — you can extract `get`, `set`, `preview`, `review` without running the optic against a specific profunctor (Forget, Tagged, etc.).
> 2. **No closure allocation** — the optic *is* its component functions.
> 3. **Compatibility with trait objects, Send/Sync, borrowing** — the components are plain functions.
> 
> ## The fp-library problem is fatal for Option 1
> 
> I traced through the implementation. `Lens::from_view_set` calls `&lt;FnBrand&lt;PointerBrand&gt; as CloneableFn&gt;::new(view)`. The `CloneableFn` trait's `Of` type for ArcBrand resolves to `Arc&lt;dyn 'a + Fn(A) -&gt; B&gt;` — no `Send + Sync` on the trait object. fp-library has `SendCloneableFn` with `SendOf` that produces `Arc&lt;dyn Fn(A) -&gt; B + Send + Sync&gt;`, but the `Lens` internals don't use it. There is no `SendLens`.
> 
> So switching `RcBrand` to `ArcBrand` gives you `Arc` wrapping but `!Send + !Sync` closures. **Option 1 is a dead end without forking fp-library.**
> 
> Even if you forked it, you'd still have:
> - By-value `view(S) -&gt; A` requiring `S: Clone` (the `from_view_set` impl clones `s` to capture it in the setter closure)
> - `!dyn` because `Lens&lt;'a, Brand, S, S, A, A&gt;` is generic over Brand
> - Profunctor machinery (Forget, Tagged, etc.) that pane never uses at runtime
> 
> ## What pane actually needs at each layer
> 
> Looking at the code:
> 
> **Typed layer** (handler declaration): The developer writes `Attribute::new("cursor", get, set)`. They want composition — if handler state has nested struct `Config { editor: Editor { cursor: usize } }`, they want to compose `config → editor` with `editor → cursor` to get `config → cursor`. This is where profunctor composition is genuinely useful.
> 
> **Type-erasure boundary** (AttrReader/AttrWriter): Takes `&amp;S → A` and `A → Display → String`. The closure is `Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;S) -&gt; AttrValue + Send + Sync&gt;`. fp-library's Lens cannot produce this because it takes `S` by value.
> 
> **Runtime layer** (pane-fs, FUSE thread): Calls `reader.read(&amp;snapshot)`. Needs `Send + Sync`. Needs `dyn`. Doesn't know about generics.
> 
> The type-erasure boundary already discards all profunctor structure. Everything above it is development-time; everything below is runtime. The question is whether the development-time layer benefits enough from fp-library to justify the dependency.
> 
> ## The key insight: pane's optics are almost always depth-1
> 
> Look at the actual use cases:
> - `Attribute::new("cursor", |s| s.cursor, |(s, c)| EditorState { cursor: c, ..s })`
> - `AttrReader::new("status", |s: &amp;StatusState| s.status.clone())`
> 
> These are direct field accesses. Composition appears when you have nested state, but the handler's "data state" (the separable portion the session-type consultant identified) is a single struct the developer controls. They're not composing optics from a library of reusable pieces — they're writing one-off lenses for their own fields.
> 
> The place where composition *would* matter is if pane provided a library of composable optic pieces (e.g., a `Rect` lens that decomposes into `x`, `y`, `width`, `height`). But even that is a flat struct.
> 
> ## Concrete recommendation: Option 2 with a composition helper
> 
> Drop fp-library from pane-proto. Define concrete optics in pane-optic. Provide a `compose` function for the rare case where nested composition is needed. The optic laws are enforced by a test harness, not by the type system — which is the same situation as today (the tests in property.rs already test the laws manually).
> 
> Here's the sketch for `pane-optic`:
> 
> ```rust
> /// A lens from S to A. The fundamental read-write optic.
> /// 
> /// Laws (enforced by assert_lens_laws):
> ///   GetPut:  set(s, get(s))     == s
> ///   PutGet:  get(set(s, a))     == a
> ///   PutPut:  set(set(s, a1), a2) == set(s, a2)
> pub struct Lens&lt;S, A&gt; {
>     pub get: fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A,
>     pub set: fn(&amp;mut S, A),
> }
> 
> /// An affine traversal — partial access. The optic for optional fields,
> /// enum variants, fallible lookups.
> ///
> /// Laws: GetPut and PutGet conditioned on preview succeeding.
> pub struct Affine&lt;S, A&gt; {
>     pub preview: fn(&amp;S) -&gt; Option&lt;A&gt;,
>     pub set: fn(&amp;mut S, A),
> }
> 
> /// A getter — read-only lens. For computed/derived values.
> pub struct Getter&lt;S, A&gt; {
>     pub get: fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A,
> }
> 
> /// Compose two lenses. get_outer then get_inner.
> /// The composed lens satisfies the laws if both components do.
> pub fn compose&lt;S, M, A&gt;(
>     outer: &amp;Lens&lt;S, M&gt;,
>     inner: &amp;Lens&lt;M, A&gt;,
> ) -&gt; Lens&lt;S, A&gt;
> where
>     M: Clone,
> {
>     // Can't use fn pointers for composition (need captures).
>     // Two options shown below.
>     todo!()
> }
> ```
> 
> Wait — `fn` pointers can't capture, so composition needs closures. This is the one place where concrete encoding requires care. Two sub-options:
> 
> **2a: `fn` pointers, no composition.** The developer manually writes the composed lens:
> ```rust
> Lens {
>     get: |s: &amp;Config| s.editor.cursor,
>     set: |s: &amp;mut Config, c| s.editor.cursor = c,
> }
> ```
> This is what everyone does in practice. No allocation, no closures, trivially Send+Sync, dyn-compatible.
> 
> **2b: `Box&lt;dyn Fn&gt;` closures, with composition.**
> ```rust
> pub struct Lens&lt;S, A&gt; {
>     get: Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A + Send + Sync&gt;,
>     set: Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut S, A) + Send + Sync&gt;,
> }
> ```
> Composition works. But every lens allocates. And `Box&lt;dyn Fn&gt;` is not `Clone` without double-boxing or `Arc`.
> 
> **2c: Dual representation.** Use `fn` for leaf lenses (zero-cost, the common case), provide a `compose` that returns a `BoxLens` (the rare case):
> ```rust
> /// Zero-cost lens from fn pointers. The default.
> pub struct Lens&lt;S, A&gt; {
>     pub get: fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A,
>     pub set: fn(&amp;mut S, A),
> }
> 
> /// Heap-allocated lens for composition results.
> pub struct BoxLens&lt;S, A&gt; {
>     get: Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A + Send + Sync&gt;,
>     set: Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut S, A) + Send + Sync&gt;,
> }
> 
> /// Trait unifying both representations at the type-erasure boundary.
> pub trait LensLike&lt;S, A&gt;: Send + Sync {
>     fn get(&amp;self, s: &amp;S) -&gt; A;
>     fn set(&amp;self, s: &amp;mut S, a: A);
> }
> 
> impl&lt;S, A&gt; LensLike&lt;S, A&gt; for Lens&lt;S, A&gt; { ... }
> impl&lt;S, A&gt; LensLike&lt;S, A&gt; for BoxLens&lt;S, A&gt; { ... }
> ```
> 
> This is my actual recommendation: **Option 2c**. Here's how it connects to the three layers:
> 
> ```
>   Handler declaration         Type-erasure boundary       pane-fs runtime
>   ─────────────────           ──────────────────────      ────────────────
>   Lens&lt;Config, usize&gt;   ───&gt;  AttrReader&lt;Config&gt;     ───&gt; read(&amp;snapshot)
>   (fn pointers)               (Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;S)-&gt;AttrValue  (string value)
>   or                           + Send + Sync&gt;)
>   BoxLens&lt;Config, usize&gt;
>   (composed, rare)
> ```
> 
> The `Attribute` struct becomes:
> ```rust
> pub struct Attribute&lt;S, A&gt; {
>     pub name: &amp;'static str,
>     lens: Box&lt;dyn LensLike&lt;S, A&gt; + Send + Sync&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> And the write path (the new piece) is:
> ```rust
> /// A named attribute with read and optional write.
> /// Write goes through the handler — pane-fs sends a command
> /// to the looper, the handler's set method is called on
> /// the looper thread (never from the FUSE thread).
> pub struct AttrWriter&lt;S&gt; {
>     pub name: &amp;'static str,
>     writer: Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut S, &amp;str) -&gt; Result&lt;(), AttrWriteError&gt; + Send + Sync&gt;,
> }
> ```
> 
> The `&amp;str -&gt; Result` parse step happens here — pane-fs sends text, the writer parses it via `FromStr` and calls the lens's set. The handler decides whether to accept the write (per the Plan 9 engineer's design: writes are commands to the handler).
> 
> ## Why not Option 3 as stated
> 
> The "hybrid" as you described it would keep fp-library as a development-time dependency and convert at the boundary. But fp-library's lenses are by-value and !Send — so the conversion requires cloning the state just to extract a value, and the fp-library Lens itself can't be stored in any Send+Sync context. The conversion isn't "use fp-library then erase"; it's "rewrite the optic as concrete functions at the boundary, discarding the fp-library lens entirely." At that point fp-library isn't providing value — it's a conceptual crutch that you pay for in compile time and API surface.
> 
> The one thing fp-library gives that concrete functions don't is automatic constraint merging on composition. But pane's optic compositions are:
> 1. Rare (most attributes are flat field access)
> 2. Statically known (the developer writes them at declaration time)
> 3. Small (2-3 levels deep at most)
> 
> Paying for a profunctor encoding to get free composition on something that barely composes is the wrong trade.
> 
> ## What about law enforcement?
> 
> The optic laws are convention in both encodings. fp-library doesn't enforce them either — `Lens::from_view_set` takes arbitrary closures and trusts the developer to satisfy GetPut/PutGet/PutPut. The concrete encoding is equally trustworthy here.
> 
> The right enforcement mechanism is the test harness:
> ```rust
> /// Assert the three lens laws for a given lens, state, and value.
> pub fn assert_lens_laws&lt;S, A&gt;(lens: &amp;impl LensLike&lt;S, A&gt;, s: S, a: A)
> where
>     S: Clone + PartialEq + std::fmt::Debug,
>     A: Clone + PartialEq + std::fmt::Debug,
> {
>     // GetPut
>     let mut s1 = s.clone();
>     let v = lens.get(&amp;s1);
>     lens.set(&amp;mut s1, v);
>     assert_eq!(s1, s, "GetPut failed");
> 
>     // PutGet
>     let mut s2 = s.clone();
>     lens.set(&amp;mut s2, a.clone());
>     assert_eq!(lens.get(&amp;s2), a, "PutGet failed");
> 
>     // PutPut
>     let mut s3 = s.clone();
>     let a2 = a.clone();
>     lens.set(&amp;mut s3, a.clone());
>     lens.set(&amp;mut s3, a2.clone());
>     let mut s4 = s;
>     lens.set(&amp;mut s4, a2);
>     assert_eq!(s3, s4, "PutPut failed");
> }
> ```
> 
> This goes in `pane-optic` as a public testing utility. Every attribute declaration should have corresponding law tests.
> 
> ## Summary of the recommendation
> 
> | Concern | Resolution |
> |---------|-----------|
> | Send + Sync | fn pointers are inherently Send+Sync; Box closures carry explicit bounds |
> | dyn-compatible | LensLike trait is object-safe |
> | By-reference | get(&amp;S), set(&amp;mut S, A) — no cloning for access |
> | Composition | Manual for fn pointers (developer writes composed accessor); BoxLens for rare programmatic composition |
> | Law enforcement | Test harness, same as current |
> | Handler !Clone | Optics operate on separable data state, not full H |
> | Write path | AttrWriter parses text, calls lens set; handler controls acceptance |
> | fp-library dependency | Removed from pane-proto |
> 
> The cost is losing fp-library's automatic constraint merging on composition. The benefit is: Send+Sync, by-reference, dyn-compatible, zero allocation for the common case, no HKT machinery, and the write path works naturally with `&amp;mut S`.</result>

> </task-notification>
