> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Reconcile optics with Be heritage" completed</summary>
> <result>Now I have the complete picture. Here's my analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> ## How BeOS Actually Did It
> 
> The BeOS scripting protocol was **not optics**. It was a message-passing protocol with dynamic dispatch. Let me walk through the actual mechanism because it's instructive for what pane should do.
> 
> ### The property_info table: a static schema declaration
> 
> ```c
> static property_info sViewPropInfo[] = {
>     { "Frame", { B_GET_PROPERTY, B_SET_PROPERTY },
>         { B_DIRECT_SPECIFIER, 0 }, "The view's frame rectangle.", 0,
>         { B_RECT_TYPE }
>     },
>     { "Hidden", { B_GET_PROPERTY, B_SET_PROPERTY },
>         { B_DIRECT_SPECIFIER, 0 }, "Whether or not the view is hidden.",
>         0, { B_BOOL_TYPE }
>     },
>     { "View", { B_COUNT_PROPERTIES, 0 },
>         { B_DIRECT_SPECIFIER, 0 }, "Returns the number of child views.", 0,
>         { B_INT32_TYPE }
>     },
>     { "View", { 0 },
>         { B_INDEX_SPECIFIER, B_REVERSE_INDEX_SPECIFIER, B_NAME_SPECIFIER, 0 },
>         "Directs the scripting message to the specified view.", 0
>     },
>     { 0 }
> };
> ```
> 
> This is pure metadata. It declares: "I have a property called 'Frame' that responds to GET and SET, takes DIRECT specifiers, and the value is a B_RECT_TYPE." It doesn't contain any access logic. It's a routing table, not an optic.
> 
> ### ResolveSpecifier: navigation, not access
> 
> The dispatch loop in `BLooper::resolve_specifier` (line 1440-1466 of Looper.cpp) does something elegant — it iterates through a specifier stack. `hey Application Window 0 View "MyButton" Frame` becomes:
> 
> 1. Looper gets the message, sees `HasSpecifiers()`, enters the resolve loop
> 2. Top specifier is `Window[0]` — BApplication's ResolveSpecifier pops it, returns BWindow
> 3. Next specifier is `View["MyButton"]` — BWindow resolves it, returns BView
> 4. Next specifier is `Frame` — BView says "I know Frame" (case 0 in FindMatch), returns `this`
> 5. Loop terminates (newTarget == target), message dispatched to BView::MessageReceived
> 
> ### MessageReceived: the actual access logic
> 
> This is where state is actually read or written:
> 
> ```cpp
> case 0:  // "Frame"
>     if (message-&gt;what == B_GET_PROPERTY) {
>         err = replyMsg.AddRect("result", Frame());
>     } else if (message-&gt;what == B_SET_PROPERTY) {
>         BRect newFrame;
>         err = message-&gt;FindRect("data", &amp;newFrame);
>         if (err == B_OK) {
>             MoveTo(newFrame.LeftTop());
>             ResizeTo(newFrame.Width(), newFrame.Height());
>         }
>     }
> ```
> 
> This is **by-reference, imperative, mutating**. `Frame()` reads directly from the object's state. `MoveTo()` and `ResizeTo()` mutate it. There's no intermediate representation, no functional update, no clone. The handler owns its state and is running on its looper's thread — the threading model guarantees exclusive access.
> 
> ### GetSupportedSuites: machine-discoverable schema
> 
> `GetSupportedSuites` adds a suite name string and a flattened BPropertyInfo to a reply message. This is introspection — `hey Application get Suites` asks an app what it can do. Each class in the chain adds its own suite (BHandler adds "suite/vnd.Be-handler", BView adds "suite/vnd.Be-view"), building up a composite description.
> 
> ### Was it by-value or by-reference?
> 
> **By-reference, always.** The handler methods (`Frame()`, `SetFrame()`, `IsHidden()`, `Hide()`, `Show()`) are direct method calls on the object. The scripting message carries data in BMessage fields (typed but dynamically), and the handler extracts it and calls its own methods. The "serialization boundary" is the BMessage itself — values go into the message as typed fields and come out the other side.
> 
> The Newsletter from Issue 2-42 (Peter Potrebic) makes the design philosophy clear:
> 
> &gt; "In keeping with BeOS philosophy the goal was to add scriptability with minimal complexity. Hopefully we've achieved that goal."
> 
> And from Issue 4-2:
> 
> &gt; "The really interesting aspect of scripting is that applications can inter-operate without necessarily having to be specially designed to work together."
> 
> ## Why fp-library's Profunctor Optics Don't Fit
> 
> Looking at the actual fp-library `Lens::from_view_set` (line 154 of lens.rs):
> 
> ```rust
> pub fn from_view_set(
>     view: impl 'a + Fn(S) -&gt; A,
>     set: impl 'a + Fn((S, B)) -&gt; T,
> ) -&gt; Self
> where
>     S: Clone,
> ```
> 
> Three problems are visible in the type signature alone:
> 
> 1. **`Fn(S) -&gt; A` — takes S by value.** The state must be cloned to read. Your handler state contains `ServiceHandle&lt;P&gt;` which is `!Clone`. You can't even construct a lens over the full handler type.
> 
> 2. **`S: Clone` bound.** This isn't just inconvenient, it's architecturally wrong. The snapshot model already handles the crossing — the looper clones state once per dispatch cycle. Adding another clone inside the optic is double-copying for reads that should be zero-copy.
> 
> 3. **`!dyn`-compatible.** The `Lens&lt;'a, RcBrand, S, S, A, A&gt;` type carries the full `S` and `A` in its type signature. You can't erase to `Box&lt;dyn Lens&gt;`. The `AttrReader&lt;S&gt;` in pane-fs already works around this by closing over the view function and erasing to `Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;S) -&gt; AttrValue&gt;`, which means the fp-library Lens is doing nothing — the actual type erasure discards it.
> 
> The profunctor encoding gives you composition (`lens1 . lens2`) and the ability to derive traversals/prisms from the same infrastructure. But look at what BeOS actually needed: flat property tables, direct name-to-accessor dispatch, no composition of accessors. `hey App Window 0 View "MyButton" Frame` composes **navigation** (Window -&gt; View -&gt; child), not **accessors** (Frame is a leaf property, never composed with another lens).
> 
> ## The Architecture of What You Already Have
> 
> Here's what's interesting: you already have the right design, split across two crates, with a false dependency connecting them.
> 
> **pane-fs/attrs.rs** has the correct runtime model:
> - `AttrReader&lt;S&gt;` — `Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;S) -&gt; AttrValue + Send + Sync&gt;` — by-reference, type-erased, dyn-compatible, zero-copy read
> - `AttrSet&lt;S&gt;` — a name -&gt; reader map, serves readdir and read
> - Snapshot-based: reads from `&amp;S`, never needs `&amp;mut S`
> 
> **pane-proto/property.rs** has the wrong model:
> - `Attribute&lt;S, A&gt;` wrapping `Lens&lt;'a, RcBrand, S, S, A, A&gt;` — by-value, requires Clone, !dyn, lifetime-infected
> - Only used in tests. Never imported by pane-fs.
> 
> The fp-library dependency (with serde and rayon features!) is pulled in for a type that is never used at the erasure boundary.
> 
> ## Recommendation: Concrete Functions, No fp-library
> 
> Drop fp-library. Replace `Attribute` with concrete function pairs. Here's the design:
> 
> ### Property (replaces Attribute)
> 
> ```rust
> /// A named read-write property on a handler.
> ///
> /// Corresponds to a property_info entry in BeOS. The name is used
> /// for namespace routing (/pane/&lt;n&gt;/attrs/&lt;name&gt;) and scripting
> /// (get/set by name).
> ///
> /// Optic laws still hold — they're enforced by convention
> /// (same as BeOS, where the property_info table was declarative
> /// and the actual access was imperative code in MessageReceived).
> pub struct Property&lt;S, A&gt; {
>     pub name: &amp;'static str,
>     pub get: fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A,
>     pub set: fn(&amp;mut S, A),
> }
> ```
> 
> Two key differences from the current Attribute:
> - **`fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A`** — by-reference read. No Clone needed on S.
> - **`fn(&amp;mut S, A)`** — by-reference write. Functional update (`Fn((S, A)) -&gt; S`) was never how BeOS did it and isn't how pane's looper will work. The handler has `&amp;mut self` during dispatch.
> 
> ### AttrReader (stays as-is, bridges the erasure boundary)
> 
> ```rust
> impl&lt;S: 'static, A: Display + 'static&gt; From&lt;&amp;Property&lt;S, A&gt;&gt; for AttrReader&lt;S&gt; {
>     fn from(prop: &amp;Property&lt;S, A&gt;) -&gt; Self {
>         let get = prop.get;
>         AttrReader::new(prop.name, move |s: &amp;S| (get)(s))
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> This is where type erasure happens. The `Property&lt;S, A&gt;` is concrete and typed; the `AttrReader&lt;S&gt;` is erased to strings. The erasure boundary is the namespace boundary. This maps exactly to BeOS: inside the handler, you work with typed values; outside (in the scripting/automation world), everything is serialized into BMessage fields.
> 
> ### AttrWriter (new, for the ctl/write path)
> 
> ```rust
> /// Type-erased writer for the ctl/command path.
> /// Parses a string value and calls the setter.
> pub struct AttrWriter&lt;S&gt; {
>     pub name: &amp;'static str,
>     writer: Box&lt;dyn Fn(&amp;mut S, &amp;str) -&gt; Result&lt;(), ParseError&gt; + Send + Sync&gt;,
> }
> 
> impl&lt;S: 'static, A: FromStr + 'static&gt; From&lt;&amp;Property&lt;S, A&gt;&gt; for AttrWriter&lt;S&gt;
> where
>     A::Err: std::error::Error + 'static,
> {
>     fn from(prop: &amp;Property&lt;S, A&gt;) -&gt; Self {
>         let set = prop.set;
>         AttrWriter {
>             name: prop.name,
>             writer: Box::new(move |s, text| {
>                 let val: A = text.parse().map_err(/* ... */)?;
>                 (set)(s, val);
>                 Ok(())
>             }),
>         }
>     }
> }
> ```
> 
> ### PropertyInfo (the suite descriptor)
> 
> ```rust
> /// Machine-readable description of a property, analogous to
> /// BeOS property_info. Used for introspection (equivalent of
> /// GetSupportedSuites) and namespace discovery.
> pub struct PropertyInfo {
>     pub name: &amp;'static str,
>     pub readable: bool,
>     pub writable: bool,
>     pub type_name: &amp;'static str,  // "u64", "String", "Rect", etc.
>     pub description: &amp;'static str,
> }
> ```
> 
> This is the pane analog of the `property_info` struct. The `commands` and `specifiers` arrays don't translate — BeOS needed those because it had multiple command verbs (GET, SET, COUNT, CREATE, DELETE) and multiple specifier forms (DIRECT, INDEX, NAME, REVERSE_INDEX). In pane, the namespace handles navigation (the specifier chain is the filesystem path), and the command verb is implicit in the operation (read = GET, write to ctl = SET).
> 
> ### What about composition?
> 
> BeOS composed **navigation**, not **lenses**. `hey App Window 0 View "MyButton" Frame` navigates through a tree of BHandlers, each of which owns its own property table. The composition is in the specifier stack, not in the accessor.
> 
> In pane, this maps to the filesystem path: `/pane/&lt;id&gt;/attrs/frame` reads a leaf property. Navigation to the right pane is done by the namespace (pane-fs), not by composing optics. If you ever need deep access (the analog of nested specifiers), it's a path traversal, not a lens composition.
> 
> The one place composition might matter is if you had computed properties that derive from multiple fields. But BeOS handled those the same way — with imperative code in MessageReceived. `BView::Frame()` wasn't composed from separate X/Y/W/H lenses; it was a method that returned a BRect.
> 
> ### The ~300-line budget
> 
> This entire layer is:
> - `Property&lt;S, A&gt;` — ~15 lines
> - `PropertyInfo` — ~10 lines  
> - `AttrWriter&lt;S&gt;` + From impl — ~30 lines
> - From&lt;Property&gt; for AttrReader — ~10 lines
> - A `PropertySet&lt;S&gt;` that holds readers + writers + info — ~50 lines
> - Tests — ~100 lines
> 
> Under 300 lines total, zero external dependencies for the optics layer.
> 
> ### What you give up
> 
> You give up:
> 1. **Free lens composition** — but you never needed it. BeOS never had it. Navigation composes (through the path), accessors don't.
> 2. **Prisms and traversals** — these matter for sum types and collections. If you need them, you add `TryGet: fn(&amp;S) -&gt; Option&lt;A&gt;` to handle the prism case. That's still concrete functions, not profunctor encoding.
> 3. **Formal optic law verification** — your existing tests verify GetPut/PutGet/PutPut manually, and they'll continue to. fp-library didn't verify laws either; it just made well-typed lenses that happen to satisfy them by construction. Your `fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A` + `fn(&amp;mut S, A)` pair satisfies the same laws if the implementations are correct, which you test.
> 
> ### What you keep
> 
> You keep everything that mattered:
> 1. **Named, typed access to handler state** — the `Property&lt;S, A&gt;` is the declaration
> 2. **Type erasure at the namespace boundary** — `AttrReader&lt;S&gt;` and `AttrWriter&lt;S&gt;` are dyn-compatible, Send+Sync, by-reference
> 3. **Machine-discoverable schema** — `PropertyInfo` serves the same role as `GetSupportedSuites`
> 4. **Snapshot-based reads** — pane-fs reads from `&amp;S`, no locking
> 5. **Command-based writes** — writes parse from string, go through the looper as commands
> 
> The spirit of the BeOS design was: properties are declared in a table, accessed through imperative methods, serialized at the IPC boundary. fp-library is a categorical abstraction over something that was always a dispatch table with getter/setter methods. The abstraction adds complexity where BeOS had simplicity.
> 
> Drop fp-library. Use concrete functions. The optic laws are real and matter — but they're properties of correct implementations, not something you need a type-level encoding to guarantee. BeOS proved this for a decade.</result>

> </task-notification>

> - From `fn_brand.rs` line 50: `type Of&lt;'a, A, B&gt; = &lt;P as RefCountedPointer&gt;::CloneableOf&lt;'a, dyn 'a + Fn(A) -&gt; B&gt;`

>

> 
> This is the complete picture. Let me write the analysis.
> 
> ---
> 
> Here's the precise breakdown after reading every relevant source file.
> 
> ## 1. What exactly is the Send+Sync blocker?
> 
> The blocker is structural, embedded in the `Lens` type definition itself. Look at line 48 of `lens.rs`:
> 
> ```rust
> pub(crate) to: Apply!(&lt;FnBrand&lt;PointerBrand&gt; as Kind!(...)&gt;::Of&lt;'a, S, (A, &lt;FnBrand&lt;PointerBrand&gt; as CloneableFn&gt;::Of&lt;'a, B, T&gt;)&gt;)
> ```
> 
> Expanding for `PointerBrand = ArcBrand`:
> 
> - `FnBrand&lt;ArcBrand&gt;`'s `Kind::Of&lt;'a, A, B&gt;` resolves to `&lt;ArcBrand as RefCountedPointer&gt;::CloneableOf&lt;'a, dyn 'a + Fn(A) -&gt; B&gt;` (fn_brand.rs:50)
> - `ArcBrand::CloneableOf&lt;'a, T&gt; = Arc&lt;T&gt;` (arc_ptr.rs:66)
> 
> So the `to` field is:
> 
> ```
> Arc&lt;dyn 'a + Fn(S) -&gt; (A, Arc&lt;dyn 'a + Fn(B) -&gt; T&gt;)&gt;
> ```
> 
> **The trait object is `dyn Fn(S) -&gt; ...`, NOT `dyn Fn(S) -&gt; ... + Send + Sync`.** Despite `Arc` being Send+Sync itself, `Arc&lt;dyn Fn(...)&gt;` is only Send when the inner trait object is Send. Since `UnsizedCoercible::coerce_fn` (arc_ptr.rs:225) produces `Arc&lt;dyn 'a + Fn(A) -&gt; B&gt;` without Send+Sync bounds, the resulting `Arc` is `!Send`.
> 
> The entire `CloneableFn` trait's associated type enforces this:
> 
> ```rust
> type Of&lt;'a, A: 'a, B: 'a&gt;: 'a + Clone + Deref&lt;Target = dyn 'a + Fn(A) -&gt; B&gt;;
> //                                               ^^^ no Send + Sync
> ```
> 
> This is not an Rc-vs-Arc issue. **Even with ArcBrand, `Lens` is `!Send` because the stored closures are `dyn Fn` not `dyn Fn + Send + Sync`.** The problem is that `Lens` hardcodes `CloneableFn::Of` (which omits Send+Sync from the trait object), and there is no mechanism to choose `SendCloneableFn::SendOf` instead.
> 
> ## 2. Does fp-library have a SendBrand or SendArcBrand?
> 
> **No.** There is no separate send-aware brand. What fp-library has is a *parallel trait hierarchy*:
> 
> | Non-send | Send-aware |
> |----------|-----------|
> | `RefCountedPointer` (`.CloneableOf`) | `SendRefCountedPointer` (`.SendOf` — requires `T: Send + Sync`) |
> | `UnsizedCoercible` (`.coerce_fn`) | `SendUnsizedCoercible` (`.coerce_send_fn`) |
> | `CloneableFn` (`.Of`) | `SendCloneableFn` (`.SendOf`) |
> 
> `ArcBrand` implements *all six* traits. It can produce both `Arc&lt;dyn Fn&gt;` (via `CloneableFn`) and `Arc&lt;dyn Fn + Send + Sync&gt;` (via `SendCloneableFn`). The brand is the same — the trait you invoke determines whether you get Send bounds.
> 
> `FnBrand&lt;ArcBrand&gt;` also implements `SendCloneableFn` (fn_brand.rs:436), producing `P::SendOf&lt;'a, dyn 'a + Fn(A) -&gt; B + Send + Sync&gt;` = `Arc&lt;dyn 'a + Fn(A) -&gt; B + Send + Sync&gt;`.
> 
> But — and this is the critical gap — **the `Lens` struct only uses `CloneableFn::Of`, never `SendCloneableFn::SendOf`**. The send-aware half of the hierarchy is available but the optics types don't use it.
> 
> ## 3. Could we define our own `SendLens` type?
> 
> Yes, it's possible in principle. You would define a `SendLens` that mirrors `Lens` but replaces every occurrence of `CloneableFn::Of` with `SendCloneableFn::SendOf`, and `CloneableFn::new` with `SendCloneableFn::send_cloneable_fn_new`. The `from_view_set` constructor would require `view: impl Fn(S) -&gt; A + Send + Sync` and `set: impl Fn((S, B)) -&gt; T + Send + Sync`.
> 
> **How much code:** Looking at what you'd need to replicate from `lens.rs`:
> 
> - The struct definition (~10 lines, trivial change to SendOf)
> - `Clone` impl (~5 lines)
> - `new()` and `from_view_set()` (~25 lines)
> - `view()`, `set()`, `over()` (~20 lines)
> - `Optic` impl, `LensOptic` impl, `AffineTraversalOptic`, `TraversalOptic`, `SetterOptic`, `GetterOptic`, `FoldOptic` impls — each ~10-20 lines
> 
> So roughly 150-200 lines, plus you need an analogous `SendShop` for the extraction profunctor.
> 
> **But there is a deep problem with composition.** The `Composed` struct itself is just `PhantomData` + two optic values — it's fine. But `Composed` implements `Optic&lt;Q&gt;` by calling `O1::evaluate` and `O2::evaluate`. The profunctor Q it's polymorphic over must be instantiated at a concrete profunctor to extract values. When you call `optics_view`, it instantiates at `Forget` (the `Shop`/`Forget` profunctor). When you call `optics_set`, it instantiates at `FnBrand&lt;PointerBrand&gt;`.
> 
> The `Forget` and `Shop` profunctors in fp-library are *also* built on `CloneableFn`, not `SendCloneableFn`. You would need `SendForget`, `SendShop`, etc. — or you'd need to change the profunctor implementations. This cascades through the entire profunctor hierarchy.
> 
> **Estimated total: 400-600 lines** of parallel send-aware types, plus the maintenance burden of keeping them in sync with fp-library upstream.
> 
> ## 4. The by-value problem
> 
> `Lens::from_view_set` takes `Fn(S) -&gt; A` — the getter consumes S by value. Looking at the actual implementation (lines 164-174):
> 
> ```rust
> to: &lt;FnBrand&lt;PointerBrand&gt; as CloneableFn&gt;::new(move |s: S| {
>     let s_clone = s.clone();
>     let set_brand = set_brand.clone();
>     (
>         view_brand(s),
>         &lt;FnBrand&lt;PointerBrand&gt; as CloneableFn&gt;::new(move |b| {
>             set_brand((s_clone.clone(), b))
>         }),
>     )
> }),
> ```
> 
> `s` is moved into `view_brand(s)`, and a *clone* `s_clone` is captured for the setter closure. The setter closure itself clones `s_clone` again each time it's called (because the closure must be `Fn`, not `FnOnce`).
> 
> **Cost analysis for pane:** If pane's State is `Clone + Send`, using this encoding means:
> - Each `view(state)` call clones the entire State once (to pass by value), then the view function extracts the field. The clone of the rest is discarded.
> - Each `set(state, value)` call clones State twice — once at the `to` invocation, once inside the setter closure.
> 
> Given that `AttrReader` snapshots occur at a type-erasure boundary (not in inner loops), a single Clone at that point is defensible. But two clones per `set` is wasteful, and this is intrinsic to fp-library's encoding — the nested closure needs its own copy.
> 
> For comparison, the concrete encoding uses `fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A` and `fn(&amp;mut S, A)` — zero clones for reads, zero unnecessary clones for writes.
> 
> ## 5. Would this give us anything the concrete encoding doesn't?
> 
> Let me be precise about what each approach provides.
> 
> **What fp-library's profunctor encoding gives you:**
> - Automatic composition via the trait system (`Composed` picks up the union of profunctor constraints)
> - Access to the full optic hierarchy (Prism, Traversal, Grate, etc.) through the same mechanism
> - Profunctor polymorphism — one optic definition works as getter, setter, modifier
> - Theoretical elegance: the optic *is* the profunctor transformer
> 
> **What it costs you (even with the SendLens work):**
> - 400-600 lines of send-aware parallel types that must track fp-library upstream
> - Two clones per set vs zero in the concrete encoding
> - The profunctor polymorphism is consumed immediately at the type-erasure boundary anyway — you instantiate at Forget/Function to produce the `fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A` / `fn(&amp;mut S, A)` that `AttrReader`/`AttrWriter` actually store
> - The HKT machinery (`Brand`, `Kind!`, `Apply!`) makes types unreadable at use sites
> - fp-library as a dependency (not small, pulls in its own macro crate)
> 
> **What the concrete encoding gives you:**
> - ~300 lines, already validated, already Send+Sync
> - Zero-clone by-reference access
> - Trivial to understand and debug
> - Composition is manual but straightforward (compose the closures)
> - No dependency beyond std
> 
> **The representation theorem** (Clarke et al., from the reference material) guarantees that the concrete encoding (get/set pair) and the profunctor encoding are isomorphic for lenses. There is no expressiveness gap — you can convert freely between them. The profunctor encoding's advantage is composition ergonomics, but at the type-erasure boundary you're converting to concrete anyway.
> 
> **My assessment:** The concrete encoding is strictly dominant for pane's use case. You cross the type-erasure boundary exactly once per optic, so composition happens at definition time (compile time), not at runtime. The profunctor encoding's composition advantage — the one thing it genuinely gives you over concrete — is consumed before it reaches the runtime boundary. You'd be paying 400-600 lines of send-aware parallel types, an external dependency, by-value cloning overhead, and HKT type noise to get a composition mechanism that you'd immediately throw away at the `dyn` boundary.
> 
> The answer to "what do we gain that justifies the dependency" is: nothing. The concrete encoding is the right call here, and it's not a compromise — it's the representation theorem telling you that the two encodings are the same optic viewed from different angles.</result>

> </task-notification>

> 
> ---
> 
> Here's the concrete assessment for each blocker, inverting the question to "what would pane need to change?"
> 
> ## Blocker 1: Send+Sync
> 
> **The actual problem is worse than stated.** Even switching from `RcBrand` to `ArcBrand` does not solve this. I traced through the fp-library source:
> 
> - `Lens::from_view_set` (lens.rs:160-161) calls `&lt;FnBrand&lt;PointerBrand&gt; as CloneableFn&gt;::new(view)`, never `SendCloneableFn::send_cloneable_fn_new`.
> - `CloneableFn::Of` for `FnBrand&lt;ArcBrand&gt;` resolves to `Arc&lt;dyn Fn(A) -&gt; B&gt;` — note the absence of `+ Send + Sync` on the trait object.
> - `SendCloneableFn` exists as a separate trait with `SendOf&lt;'a, A, B&gt; = Arc&lt;dyn Fn(A) -&gt; B + Send + Sync&gt;`, but `Lens` never uses it.
> 
> So `Lens&lt;'a, ArcBrand, S, S, A, A&gt;` is `!Send + !Sync` regardless of brand. This is not a pane design problem — it's that fp-library's Lens type is architecturally single-threaded. No amount of pane redesign fixes this without patching fp-library or doing something unsafe.
> 
> **System change that would eliminate it:** Move all optic evaluation to the looper thread. Concretely:
> 
> 1. AttrReader construction and evaluation both happen on the looper thread.
> 2. The looper thread eagerly evaluates all attributes after each dispatch cycle, producing `HashMap&lt;&amp;'static str, AttrValue&gt;` (all Strings, trivially Send).
> 3. Only the materialized `HashMap&lt;&amp;'static str, AttrValue&gt;` crosses to the FUSE thread.
> 
> **Assessment: This is a reasonable design on its own merits.** It's the snapshot model you already have (namespace.rs:21 stores `state: S`), just pushed one step further — snapshot the *attribute values*, not the *state*. The tradeoff is that you lose lazy evaluation (every attribute is computed on every state change, even if nobody reads it). For a typical pane with 5-20 attributes where each is a trivial field access, this is negligible. For a hypothetical pane with 1000 attributes where computation is expensive, it's wasteful — but that pane would be a design smell anyway.
> 
> The deeper question: does this eliminate the need for `AttrReader&lt;S&gt;` entirely? Nearly. The looper would hold the typed `Attribute&lt;S, A&gt;` (with the fp-library Lens) and the `AttrSet&lt;S&gt;` would live on the looper side. What crosses the thread boundary is just `HashMap&lt;&amp;'static str, String&gt;`. The FUSE thread becomes a dumb cache reader.
> 
> **But**: This still doesn't eliminate the `Attribute` -&gt; `AttrReader` conversion, because `AttrReader` is how you type-erase `A` to get a heterogeneous collection. The Lens is still `!dyn-compatible` even on the looper thread. You still need the closure-based `AttrReader` to put multiple attribute types in one `HashMap`. The Lens just lives outside the HashMap, used once during construction to build the `AttrReader`. Which is *exactly what the code already does* — `AttrReader::new("cursor", |s: &amp;EditorState| s.cursor)` doesn't use a Lens at all.
> 
> So eliminating blocker 1 via "keep Lens on the looper thread" is viable, but it reveals that the Lens never needs to cross thread boundaries in the first place because the type erasure happens at `AttrReader::new`, and by then you've already extracted the closure.
> 
> ## Blocker 2: By-value (S -&gt; A)
> 
> **The proposed change:** Require `S: Clone` and accept the clone cost.
> 
> fp-library's `Lens::view(s: S) -&gt; A` takes `S` by value. The `Lens::from_view_set` already requires `S: Clone` (lens.rs:159). So every `view` call either consumes or clones the state.
> 
> **What would pane's State need to look like?** Handler already requires `Send + 'static` (handler.rs:22). If you add `Clone`, the handler struct itself must be cloneable. The state snapshot model in namespace.rs already requires this — `update_state` takes `state: S` by value, which means the looper clones the handler state once per dispatch cycle.
> 
> So the question reduces to: is cloning once *more* per attribute read acceptable?
> 
> **Assessment: Reasonable in practice, but masks a real cost.** For the common case (state is a few scalars and short strings), cloning is trivial. But handler state can grow. Consider a text editor handler with a `buffer: String` of 10MB — cloning that per attribute read is clearly unacceptable. The `&amp;S` in `AttrReader::read(&amp;self, state: &amp;S) -&gt; AttrValue` exists for exactly this reason.
> 
> The current code handles this cleanly. Look at `AttrReader::new` (attrs.rs:51-58): it takes `impl Fn(&amp;S) -&gt; A`, and the comment at line 50 says "the Attribute's by-value view() is called inside the closure with a clone." This means you'd construct the AttrReader by wrapping the Lens in a closure that clones:
> 
> ```rust
> AttrReader::new("cursor", |s: &amp;EditorState| {
>     cursor_lens.view(s.clone())
> })
> ```
> 
> One clone per attribute read, per FUSE read(2) call. If the state is small, fine. If large, you're paying the Lens's by-value tax to get... what? The same field access you could write as `|s: &amp;EditorState| s.cursor`.
> 
> **Verdict:** The clone cost is acceptable for small states. But accepting it doesn't buy you anything the direct `|s: &amp;S| s.field` closure doesn't already give you. The Lens's by-value signature is a cost with no corresponding benefit at this layer — the benefits of Lens (composition, law enforcement, profunctor polymorphism) are all consumed at the type-erasure boundary and don't survive into `AttrReader`.
> 
> ## Blocker 3: !dyn-compatible
> 
> **The proposed change:** Replace `HashMap&lt;&amp;str, AttrReader&lt;S&gt;&gt;` with a derive-macro-generated match statement.
> 
> ```rust
> // Instead of:
> attrs.read("cursor", &amp;state)  // HashMap lookup
> 
> // Generate:
> match name {
>     "cursor" =&gt; Some(AttrValue(state.cursor.to_string())),
>     "buffer_length" =&gt; Some(AttrValue(state.buffer.len().to_string())),
>     _ =&gt; None,
> }
> ```
> 
> **Assessment: This is a genuinely good design change, independent of the Lens question.** A compile-time generated `AttrSet` eliminates:
> - The HashMap allocation
> - The Box&lt;dyn Fn&gt; per attribute
> - The runtime registration boilerplate (`attrs.add(AttrReader::new(...))`)
> - The possibility of name collisions at runtime
> 
> It also enables:
> - Exhaustive attribute documentation via the macro
> - Compile-time checking that attribute names are valid identifiers
> - Static dispatch for the FUSE readdir listing
> 
> **But it still doesn't make fp-library's Lens fit.** The match statement body still needs to call `lens.view(state)` — and the Lens is parameterized by `PointerBrand`, which means every arm of the match has a different type unless all attributes share the same `A`. You can't store `Lens&lt;'a, RcBrand, S, S, usize, usize&gt;` and `Lens&lt;'a, RcBrand, S, S, String, String&gt;` in the same struct without type erasure. The macro would need to inline the get/set functions, at which point the Lens is just a pair of closures generated by the macro — which is exactly what a `#[derive(Attributes)]` would produce without Lens.
> 
> **The macro approach works great. It just doesn't need Lens to work.**
> 
> ## The synthesis
> 
> All three proposed changes are independently reasonable design choices:
> 
> | Change | Reasonable? | Does it make fp-library Lens fit? |
> |--------|-------------|----------------------------------|
> | Eager attribute evaluation on looper thread | Yes — simple, matches existing snapshot model | No — Lens is still !Send even on one thread; type erasure still needed |
> | S: Clone, accept by-value cost | Yes for small states | Technically yes, but the Lens adds nothing over a direct closure |
> | Derive macro for static dispatch | Yes — better than HashMap in every dimension | No — still need type erasure for heterogeneous attributes |
> 
> Each change eliminates the *symptom* at the pane-fs boundary, but none makes the fp-library Lens the right tool for the interior. The pattern that emerges is:
> 
> 1. The handler author writes `get` and `set` functions (either manually or via derive).
> 2. Those functions are used directly in `AttrReader::new` / macro expansion.
> 3. The fp-library Lens wrapping those same functions adds six type parameters, a PointerBrand, a closure-in-closure allocation (lens.rs:164-172 allocates a closure that allocates another closure), a Clone requirement, and !Send — and is then immediately discarded at the type-erasure boundary.
> 
> The Lens is not being used as a lens (composed, polymorphically applied across profunctors). It's being used as a named pair of functions. The profunctor encoding is dead weight between `from_view_set` and the next line of code.
> 
> **The strongest version of your question — "is any of the bending reasonable?" — has a nuanced answer:** The individual bends are reasonable, but they solve problems that only exist because of the Lens. Remove the Lens and you don't need the bends. The system design you already have (AttrReader with `Fn(&amp;S) -&gt; AttrValue + Send + Sync`, AttrSet with HashMap) is the natural design for pane's access pattern. The derive macro upgrade is worth doing regardless. The eager evaluation model is worth considering regardless. Neither needs Lens to motivate it.</result>
> <usage><total_tokens>56504</total_tokens><tool_uses>35</tool_uses><duration_ms>173722</duration_ms></usage>
> </task-notification>

### `2026-04-05T08:38:29.080Z`

> can we do an analysis of how plan9 and pane-fs will involve optics discipline?

### `2026-04-05T08:41:38.587Z`

> <task-notification>
> <task-id>a8244596b5d5647ce</task-id>
> <tool-use-id>toolu_01NBk5hUAqGwcDagcJynJ3QE</tool-use-id>
> <output-file>/private/tmp/claude-501/-Users-lane-src-lin-pane/66fc8461-c45b-455e-92e2-30b68bff4458/tasks/a8244596b5d5647ce.output</output-file>
> <status>completed</status>
> <summary>Agent "Plan 9 namespace + optics discipline" completed</summary>
> <result>So pane-optic exists in build artifacts from v1 but the crate itself was removed or lives elsewhere. The v1 subsystem inventory says it's "preserved" but it's not in the current crates directory. That's fine — the question is conceptual, not about the code.
> 
> Now I have enough context to give a thorough answer. Let me structure this carefully.
> 
> ---
> 
> Good question. Let me take each of your observations seriously and say where I think you're right, where you're slightly off, and what the actual value proposition is.
> 
> ## 1. read(2) on synthetic files as a getter — mostly correct, with a caveat
> 
> Yes, `cat /proc/N/status` is a projection. The kernel's `devproc.c` (in Plan 9: `/sys/src/9/port/devproc.c`, function `procread`) switches on the Qid path component, and for `Qstatus` it formats a fixed set of fields from the `Proc` struct into a text buffer. It's a pure function from kernel state to text. In optic terms, this is a `Getter&lt;Proc, String&gt;`.
> 
> But there's a subtlety you should be precise about: **the projection is not referentially transparent**. Two reads of `/proc/N/status` can return different values even with no intervening write to that file. The process's state changes underneath you. In optic theory, a Getter `get : S -&gt; A` is a pure function on a fixed `S`. In Plan 9, the "S" is moving. The synthetic file gives you a *snapshot* — a point sample of a continuous process.
> 
> This doesn't invalidate the Getter classification, but it means the optic operates over snapshots, not stable state. For pane-fs, this matters: when you read `/pane/1/tag`, you're getting a snapshot of the title at the time of the FUSE request. If another client changes the title between your read and your next read, both reads are valid projections — they're just projections at different moments. The Getter is well-typed; the underlying state has its own dynamics.
> 
> Plan 9 never formalized this, and it never needed to. The convention was implicit: reads of synthetic files return current state, period. Nobody worried about whether two reads were consistent with each other because the protocol was simple enough that it didn't matter. Pane might need to be more careful if you're composing multiple reads (e.g., read tag AND body AND attrs atomically) — but that's a consistency question, not an optics question per se.
> 
> ## 2. ctl is not a setter — correct, and this is the key distinction
> 
> You're right, and this is the most important observation in the list. Let me be precise about Plan 9's ctl files.
> 
> `/proc/N/ctl` in Plan 9 (devproc.c, `procctlmesg`) parses write data as text commands: `kill`, `startstop`, `pri N`, `wired N`, `noswap`, etc. It's a command interpreter. The ctl file has no meaningful read — in Plan 9, reading `/proc/N/ctl` returns nothing useful (it was historically empty or undefined; the readable state is in `status`, not `ctl`).
> 
> This means ctl violates every lens law:
> - **PutGet fails**: `echo kill &gt; ctl` then `cat ctl` does not return "kill"
> - **GetPut fails**: you can't read a value from ctl and write it back
> - **PutPut fails**: writing "kill" twice doesn't compose in any meaningful way (the second write to a dead process just errors)
> 
> In optic terms, ctl is neither a Lens nor a Setter nor a Prism. It's a *sink* — a one-way channel accepting commands. The optic vocabulary doesn't have a standard term for this because optics are about state access, and command dispatch isn't state access. It's closer to a monadic action: `CtlCommand -&gt; IO ()`.
> 
> Rio's `wctl` is more interesting. Reading `wctl` returns the window's geometry and state (visible, current, hidden). Writing `wctl` sends commands (resize, move, hide, delete). So wctl is a **split interface**: read is a Getter, write is a command interpreter. The two directions don't compose as a Lens.
> 
> For pane-fs, your spec already has this right: `ctl` is write-only, `attrs/` is read (and selectively write). The optic classification confirms what the spec already decided by convention. The value of the formalism here is that it gives you a name for the design rule: **never promise Lens semantics on a command interpreter**.
> 
> ## 3. Namespace walking as optic composition — correct in structure, wrong in details
> 
> The intuition is right: walking `/proc/N/status` is a sequence of focusing operations. But the optic classification of each step needs refinement.
> 
> In Plan 9, the walk(5) operation in 9P resolves each path component by asking the file server to advance a fid from a parent to a child. The kernel's `namec()` function (in `/sys/src/9/port/chan.c`) resolves the full path by chaining walks through the mount table. Each walk step is:
> 
> - **`/proc`** — a name lookup in the mount table. This finds the device (or mounted file server) that handles `/proc`. In optic terms, this is an **affine** (partial getter): the name might not exist.
> - **`N`** — an indexed lookup. devproc's `procgen` generates the directory listing by iterating the process table; walking to `N` is a lookup by process index. This is a **PartialGetter** or **Affine**: process N might not exist.
> - **`status`** — a fixed name in a known directory structure. Once you have the process, `status` always exists. This is a total Getter.
> 
> So the composition is `Affine . Affine . Getter`, which composes to an `Affine` (the composition is as weak as the weakest component). This means: the full path `/proc/N/status` *might fail* (process doesn't exist, mount not present), but if it succeeds, you get a deterministic projection.
> 
> For `/pane/`:
> - `/pane/` — mount lookup (Affine)
> - `N/` — pane index lookup (Affine — pane might not exist)
> - `tag` — fixed field (Getter or Lens depending on read/write)
> 
> The composition `Affine . Affine . Lens` gives you an `AffineTraversal` — you might not reach the target, but if you do, you have a full lens on it. This is actually useful information for pane-fs: it tells you that FUSE error handling (ENOENT) is exactly the optic partiality, and that the lens laws (GetPut, PutGet) only need to hold *conditional on successful path resolution*.
> 
> Calling `/proc` a "traversal" (many processes) is technically imprecise. The directory listing operation — readdir — is a Fold (it iterates over all entries). Walking to a *specific* N is an Affine (indexed partial access). These are different operations in both 9P and optics.
> 
> ## 4. bind/mount as optic combinators — this is where it gets interesting
> 
> `bind(1)` in Plan 9 modifies the process's mount table. Specifically, `bind /usr/local/bin /bin` adds an entry to the mount table that says "when walking through `/bin`, also (or instead) consult `/usr/local/bin`". The semantics depend on the flags:
> 
> - `bind -b` (before): union mount, new tree checked first
> - `bind -a` (after): union mount, existing tree checked first  
> - `bind` (replace): old tree is shadowed entirely
> 
> In optic terms, this is **not** an optic combinator. It's a *modification of the resolution context*. The optics operate *within* a particular namespace configuration; bind changes the namespace configuration itself. It's more like changing which lens library is loaded than composing two lenses.
> 
> If you want to force an optic reading: bind-replace is like substituting one Getter for another at a given path. bind-before is like composing a fallback: try optic A, fall back to optic B. But this stretches the metaphor past its useful range. The mount table is a routing table for name resolution, not an optic composition system.
> 
> For pane-fs, your "Rejected" column already has the right call: no union directories, computed projections instead. The pane-fs views (`/pane/local/`, `/pane/remote/`, `/pane/by-sig/`) are precomputed filtered Folds over the indexed state. This is cleaner than bind's union semantics because there's no MBEFORE/MAFTER ambiguity. Each view is a total function from the indexed pane state to a directory listing.
> 
> ## 5. rio's per-window namespace — mixed optic classification
> 
> Rio (`/sys/src/cmd/rio/`, specifically `fsys.c` and `wind.c`) synthesizes `/dev/wsys/N/` for each window. The interesting files:
> 
> - **`label`**: read returns the window label, write sets it. This satisfies GetPut and PutGet. It's a Lens.
> - **`mouse`**: read returns the current mouse position (blocking until a mouse event occurs in that window). This is a *stream*, not a getter — you don't get a static value, you get the next event. It's closer to an `Iterator&lt;Item=MouseEvent&gt;` than a `Getter&lt;WindowState, MousePos&gt;`. Plan 9's read semantics here rely on blocking I/O to create event-stream behavior, which is outside the optic model.
> - **`wctl`**: as discussed, split read/write semantics. Read is a Getter (window geometry). Write is a command interpreter. Not a Lens.
> - **`text`**: read returns the window's content (what's displayed). Write sends input. This is *not* a Lens — writing "hello" to text sends keystrokes, it doesn't set the content to "hello". Reading after writing depends on how the application processed the input.
> - **`cons`**, **`consctl`**: console semantics. `consctl` is a mode toggle (write "rawon"/"rawoff"). It has an interesting property relevant to pane: closing the fd reverts the mode change. This is the "consctl close-to-revert" pattern I flagged as not-yet-evaluated in the reference doc.
> 
> For pane-fs's endpoints:
> - `/pane/N/tag` — Lens (GetPut and PutGet hold, assuming write takes effect synchronously)
> - `/pane/N/attrs/cursor` — if read-only, it's a Getter. If read-write for some attrs and read-only for others, you have a per-attribute classification.
> - `/pane/N/body` — this depends on semantics. If write *replaces* body content and read returns the current content, it's a Lens. If write *appends* or is interpreted as input, it's not.
> - `/pane/N/event` — a blocking-read stream (like rio's mouse). Not an optic. It's a Fold at best, but really it's an event source.
> 
> ## 6. Where optic laws matter concretely for pane-fs
> 
> For the specific endpoints you listed:
> 
> **`/pane/N/tag`**: Yes, this should be a Lens. GetPut: read the tag, write it back, tag is unchanged. PutGet: write "Editor — main.rs", read it back, get "Editor — main.rs". PutPut: write X then write Y, result is Y. All three should hold. If they don't, it's a bug. This is a testable property.
> 
> **`/pane/N/attrs/cursor`**: If writes go through `ctl`, then the filesystem endpoint is a Getter. The write path is the command channel, not a setter on this optic. You have two separate interfaces to related state — one read-only projection and one command channel — which is exactly the Plan 9 pattern (status + ctl).
> 
> **`/pane/N/body`**: If write replaces content, Lens. But the spec says "the semantic body content" — for a shell, that's command output, which isn't writeable in the same sense. For an editor, write could mean "replace buffer contents." The optic classification depends on the pane type's handler. This is where `DynOptic` earns its keep: the handler declares whether body is a Lens or a Getter, and the declaration is the contract.
> 
> ## 7. What optics buy pane that Plan 9 didn't have
> 
> This is the real question. My honest assessment:
> 
> **What Plan 9 had implicitly:**
> - A convention that synthetic files project state (implicit Getter)
> - A convention that ctl files accept commands (implicit sink)
> - A convention that some files are read-write (implicit Lens)
> - No enforcement of these conventions. A buggy file server could violate any of them.
> 
> **What formalizing as optics gives pane:**
> 
> 1. **Testable contracts.** The optic laws become property tests. For every declared Lens, you can generate random values, write them, read them back, and verify PutGet. This is mechanical and high-value. Plan 9 never had this — you tested synthetic file servers by hand.
> 
> 2. **Classification as documentation.** When pane-fs exposes an endpoint, saying "this is a Getter" or "this is a Lens" tells the consumer exactly what operations are valid. Plan 9's `proc(3)` man page described this in prose ("The read-only control file..."). Optic classification is the same information but machine-checkable.
> 
> 3. **Composition correctness.** When you compose optics (internal state -&gt; protocol message -&gt; filesystem representation), the composition laws tell you what the composed thing is. If you compose a Lens with a Getter, you get a Getter. If you compose two Lenses, you get a Lens. This means the filesystem projection's properties are *derivable* from the properties of the intermediate projections, not discovered empirically.
> 
> 4. **The DynOptic boundary.** The type-erased optic at the scripting protocol boundary solves a problem Plan 9 didn't have: runtime-polymorphic access to properties of different types. Plan 9's convention was text for everything, so the type issue didn't arise. Pane's properties are typed (int, string, bool, etc.) but accessed through a uniform protocol. DynOptic is the adapter.
> 
> **What optics do NOT buy:**
> 
> - **Performance.** The optic abstraction has zero performance implication for pane-fs. FUSE operations are FUSE operations regardless of whether you model them as optics.
> - **Failure handling.** Optic laws hold over a stable state. Network partitions, crashed panes, stale caches — none of these are optic problems. They're distributed systems problems. The "lens laws may be temporarily violated" paragraph in foundations.md is doing real work here — it acknowledges that the formalism has limits.
> - **Security.** Optics don't tell you who can access what. That's the .plan governance and Landlock layer.
> 
> **My assessment:**
> 
> The optic discipline is *above* what Plan 9 did, not inherent in it. Plan 9's synthetic filesystems used the same patterns that optics formalize, the way a carpenter uses geometry without writing proofs. The formalism gives you names, laws, and testability. Whether that's worth the conceptual overhead depends on whether you actually write the property tests and enforce the classifications.
> 
> If pane-fs just documents "tag is read-write, event is read-only, ctl is write-only" and tests those behaviors, you've gotten 80% of the value without the optic vocabulary. The remaining 20% — composition laws, DynOptic at the scripting boundary, property-test generation from optic classification — is where the formalism earns its keep. It's most valuable at the DynOptic/scripting boundary where runtime polymorphism makes informal conventions brittle.
> 
> The risk is treating the optic classification as load-bearing architecture when it's really a quality discipline. Plan 9 shipped perfectly good synthetic file servers for decades without it. The question isn't "does Plan 9 need optics" — it didn't. The question is "does pane's scripting protocol need the guarantees that optics provide" — and given that pane is recovering BeOS's `ResolveSpecifier` with typed properties across a network, I think the answer is yes, specifically at the DynOptic boundary. For plain filesystem access via FUSE, it's a testing discipline, not an architectural necessity.</result>
